Roman Lives

Five lives from the fall of the Republic: Caesar, Cato, Cicero, Brutus, Antony

by Plutarch

c. 100 AD 2026

Cato the Younger

Cicero

Brutus

Antony

Caesar

When Sulla made himself master of Rome, he wanted Caesar to divorce his wife Cornelia — the daughter of Cinna, the man who had lately held sole power in the state. He could not make it happen, not with promises and not with threats, so he settled for confiscating her dowry. The root of Sulla's grudge was Caesar's family tie to Marius. The elder Marius had married Julia, the sister of Caesar's father, which made the younger Marius Caesar's first cousin. At first, with so many people to kill and so much business to get through, Sulla simply overlooked Caesar. But the boy would not keep his head down. He presented himself to the people as a candidate for a priesthood while he was still barely more than a child, and Sulla, without opposing him openly, quietly arranged his defeat. When the question was raised of whether to have him killed outright, and someone objected that it was hardly worth the trouble to contrive the death of a boy, Sulla answered that they had no eyes at all if they could not see more than one Marius in that boy.

Word of that remark reached Caesar, and he disappeared. For a long while he kept out of sight in the Sabine country, moving from house to house — until one night, shifting quarters yet again because he was ill, he fell straight into the hands of Sulla's soldiers, who were sweeping the district for men in hiding. Caesar bribed their captain, Cornelius, with two talents to let him go, and the moment he was released he made for the sea and sailed to Bithynia. After a short stay there with King Nicomedes, he was captured on the voyage home, near the island of Pharmacusa, by pirates — who in those days controlled the seas everywhere with great fleets and swarms of smaller craft.

When the pirates demanded twenty talents for his ransom, he laughed in their faces for not knowing the value of their prisoner, and volunteered to pay them fifty. Then he sent his people off in every direction to raise the money, until he was left among the most bloodthirsty men in the world, the Cilicians, with a single friend and two attendants. He treated his captors as if they hardly existed. When he wanted to sleep, he would send someone over to order them to keep the noise down. For thirty-eight days he carried on with perfect freedom, joining their games and exercises as though they were not his keepers but his bodyguard. He wrote poems and speeches and made the pirates his audience, and anyone who failed to admire the work he called an illiterate barbarian to his face — and he would often, in the middle of the joking, promise to have them all crucified. They loved this. They put his free talk down to a kind of simple, boyish playfulness. As soon as his ransom arrived from Miletus, he paid it and was set free — and went straight to the port of Miletus, manned some ships, and sailed out after the pirates. He caught them still anchored at the island, took most of them prisoner, seized their money as his prize, and locked the men up in the prison at Pergamus. Then he applied to Junius, the governor of Asia, since punishing them fell to Junius's office as praetor. But Junius had his eye on the money — the sum was considerable — and said he would consider at his leisure what to do with the prisoners. So Caesar took polite leave of him, went back to Pergamus, had the pirates brought out, and crucified every one of them — the exact punishment he had so often promised them while he was in their hands, when they never dreamed he meant a word of it.

Meanwhile Sulla's power was fading, and Caesar's friends urged him to come home to Rome. Instead he went to Rhodes and enrolled in the school of Apollonius, the son of Molon — a famous teacher of rhetoric with a reputation as a decent man, who had counted Cicero among his students. Caesar, it is said, was fitted by nature to be a great statesman and orator, and he worked so hard at developing the gift that he could without question have claimed second place in eloquence. He aimed no higher, because he chose to be first among men of arms and power instead; and so he never reached the summit of oratory that nature had put within his range, his attention being pulled away toward the campaigns and grand designs that eventually won him the empire. He said so himself. In his reply to Cicero's tribute to Cato, he asked his readers not to compare a soldier's plain way of speaking with the polished performances of an orator who had not only brilliant gifts but a whole lifetime spent perfecting them.

When he returned to Rome, he prosecuted Dolabella for corruption in office, and many of the cities of Greece supplied evidence against him. Dolabella was acquitted; but Caesar, to repay the Greeks for their support, helped them prosecute Publius Antonius for corruption before Marcus Lucullus, the praetor of Macedonia. He pressed the case so hard that Antonius had to appeal to the tribunes at Rome, claiming that in Greece he could not get a fair trial against Greeks. At Rome, Caesar's eloquence in the courts quickly earned him a serious reputation, and the warmth and courtesy of his manner won him the people's affection — he had a tact and consideration well beyond his years. And the open house he kept, the dinners he gave, the general splendor of his way of living, all built his political influence little by little. His enemies shrugged at it at first, assuming the whole thing would collapse the moment his money ran out. But while they shrugged, it grew and flowered among the common people. By the time his power was established beyond challenge — openly aimed, now, at transforming the entire constitution — they understood, too late, that no beginning is so small that persistence will not make it great, and that a danger despised at first becomes irresistible in the end. Cicero was the first to suspect what Caesar was really after. Like a good pilot who fears the storm while the sea is still smiling, he saw the calculation underneath the charm and good humor, and said that in everything Caesar did and planned he could detect the ambition for absolute power. "But when I look at his hair, so carefully arranged," he said, "and watch him adjusting it with one finger, I cannot imagine that such a man could ever conceive of anything so enormous as overthrowing the Roman state." But more of that later.

The first proof Caesar had of the people's goodwill came when he ran for a military tribuneship and finished higher on the list than Gaius Popilius. A second and clearer proof came when he delivered a magnificent oration in the forum in praise of his aunt Julia, the wife of Marius — and at her funeral had the audacity to bring out the images of Marius himself, which no one had dared display since Sulla took power and Marius's party was declared enemies of the state. A few in the crowd started shouting against Caesar for it, but the people drowned them out with cheering and applause, delighted that he had brought the honors of Marius back from the grave, as it were, after so many years lost to the city. It had always been the custom at Rome to deliver funeral orations for elderly matrons, but never for young women — until Caesar gave one at the death of his own wife. This too won him favor: the people saw the show of grief and took him for a man of great tenderness and kindness of heart. After burying his wife, he went to Spain as quaestor under one of the praetors, a man named Vetus, whom he honored ever afterward — when Caesar himself became praetor, he made Vetus's son his own quaestor. When that service was over, he married his third wife, Pompeia; he already had a daughter by his first wife Cornelia, whom he later gave in marriage to Pompey the Great. He spent money so lavishly that before he held any public office at all he was thirteen hundred talents in debt, and many people thought he was trading a solid good for a short and uncertain return, buying popularity at ruinous cost. In truth he was buying the most valuable thing in the world, and getting it cheap. When he was made curator of the Appian Way, he poured a great deal of his own money into the work on top of the public funds; and as aedile he put on three hundred and twenty single combats of gladiators, and mounted theatrical shows, processions, and public feasts on a scale so magnificent that he washed out the memory of everyone who had come before him. The people were so taken with it all that every man of them was hunting for new offices and new honors to pay him back.

There were two factions in the city: Sulla's, which was very powerful, and Marius's, which was broken and cowed. Caesar set out to revive the latter and make it his own. At the height of his popularity from the shows of his aedileship, he had images of Marius made, along with figures of Victory carrying trophies, and had them carried up at night and set in place on the Capitol. In the morning people saw them glittering with gold, beautifully worked, their inscriptions recording Marius's victories over the Cimbrians — and they were stunned at the boldness of the man who had put them there. It was not hard to guess who that was. Word spread fast, and a great crowd came running. Some cried out that this was open rebellion against the settled government — reviving honors that had been buried by laws and decrees of the senate — and that Caesar had done it to test the temper of a people he had already softened up, to see whether they were tame enough to indulge him and let his innovations pass. Marius's people, on the other hand, took heart, and it was astonishing how many of them suddenly turned out to be, and how they came pouring into the Capitol, shouting. Many wept for joy at the sight of Marius's likeness, and Caesar was praised to the sky as the one man in Rome who was worthy of his kinship to Marius. The senate met over it, and Catulus Lutatius, one of the most respected men in Rome, rose and denounced Caesar, closing with the memorable line that Caesar was no longer tunneling under the state — he was bringing up siege engines against it in the open. But Caesar made his own defense and satisfied the senate, and his admirers were more excited than ever. They urged him to yield his ambitions to no one: with the people's favor behind him, he would beat them all in the end and be the first man in the commonwealth.

At this point Metellus, the high priest of Rome, died, and Catulus and Isauricus — men of the highest standing and great influence in the senate — competed for the office. Caesar refused to step aside for them and presented himself to the people as a rival candidate. With support running about even, Catulus, who had the most reputation to lose and was the most nervous about the outcome, sent to Caesar offering a large sum of money to withdraw. Caesar answered that he was ready to borrow an even larger sum to stay in the fight. On election day, as his mother walked him to the door in tears, he embraced her and said, "Mother, today you will see me either high priest or an exile." When the votes were counted, after a hard struggle, he won — and the senate and the nobility were seized with alarm that he would now lead the people into every kind of recklessness. Piso and Catulus blamed Cicero for letting Caesar slip away when the Catiline conspiracy had given the government such a hold on him. For Catiline had planned not merely to change the government but to overthrow the whole Roman order and throw everything into chaos; yet he had fled the city while the evidence against him was still incomplete, before his deepest designs came to light, leaving Lentulus and Cethegus behind to carry on the conspiracy in his place. Whether Caesar secretly encouraged and helped them is uncertain. What is certain is that they were overwhelmingly convicted in the senate, and when Cicero, as consul, went around the chamber asking each senator what punishment they favored, every man who spoke before Caesar said death. Then Caesar stood and delivered a prepared speech, arguing that it was without precedent, and unjust, to execute men of their birth and rank without a proper trial, unless there was absolute necessity for it. Better, he said, to hold them under guard in whatever Italian towns Cicero himself might choose, until Catiline was defeated — and then the senate could decide, in peacetime and at leisure, what should be done with them.

The proposal sounded so humane, and Caesar urged it so eloquently, that it swept the room: not only did those who spoke after him adopt it, but many who had already voted for death got up and switched sides — until the turn to speak came around to Catulus and Cato. They fought it hard, and Cato, in his speech, pointed the finger of suspicion at Caesar himself, and pressed the matter so fiercely that the conspirators were handed over for execution. As Caesar was leaving the senate house, a crowd of the young men who then served as Cicero's guard rushed at him with drawn swords. But Curio, it is said, threw his own toga over him and hustled him out, and Cicero himself, when the young men looked to him for the signal, shook his head — either out of fear of the people or because he thought the killing would be unjust and lawless. If the story is true, I cannot understand why Cicero left it out of the book he wrote about his consulship. In any case, he was blamed afterward for wasting so perfect an opportunity against Caesar — for flinching, people said, out of fear of the common people, who were extraordinarily devoted to Caesar. Indeed, a short while later, when Caesar went into the senate to clear himself of the suspicions hanging over him and the session ran long amid loud attacks on him, the crowd came surging up to the senate house, surrounded it, and shouted for Caesar, demanding that the senators let him go. That was what moved Cato, who was afraid above all of a rising among the poor — the tinder of every revolution, and men who had set all their hopes on Caesar — to persuade the senate to grant them a monthly allowance of grain. The measure added seven and a half million drachmas a year to the state's expenses, but it worked: it smothered the immediate danger, and it broke up much of Caesar's power at just the right moment, since he was about to enter his praetorship and would have been all the more formidable in office.

In fact his praetorship passed without any public disturbance. The trouble that year came inside his own house. Publius Clodius was a patrician by birth, distinguished for both wealth and eloquence, but in sheer insolence and appetite for scandal he outdid every notorious degenerate of the day. He was in love with Pompeia, Caesar's wife, and she was not unwilling. But her rooms were closely watched, and Caesar's mother Aurelia, a shrewd woman, kept so constant an eye on her that any meeting between the two was difficult and dangerous. Now, the Romans have a goddess they call Bona, the Good Goddess — the same one the Greeks call Gynaecea, the women's goddess. The Phrygians claim her as their own and say she was the mother of King Midas; the Romans say she was one of the dryads and the wife of Faunus; the Greeks say she is that mother of Bacchus whose name must never be spoken — which is why the women celebrating her festival cover their tents with vine branches, and why, in keeping with the myth, a sacred serpent sits beside the goddess. While her rites are performed, no man may be present, or even inside the house. The women conduct the sacred ceremonies by themselves, and these are said to be much like the rites of Orpheus. When the festival comes around, the host — a consul or a praetor — leaves his own house, along with every male creature in it. His wife takes the house over and puts it in order, and the main ceremonies are performed at night: a night of games and play among the women, with music of every kind.

Pompeia was hosting this festival when Clodius — who had no beard yet, and figured he could pass unnoticed — dressed himself up as a singing girl, ornaments and all, and went to the house looking like a young woman. He found the doors open and was slipped safely inside by a maid who was in on the affair, and who ran ahead to tell Pompeia. But she was gone a long time, and Clodius, too restless to wait where he had been left, went drifting through the big house, room to room, keeping away from the lights — until one of Aurelia's attendants ran into him and, taking him for a woman, invited him to play, as the women were doing among themselves. When he refused, she pulled him forward into the open and asked who he was and where he had come from. Clodius answered that he was waiting for Pompeia's own maid, Abra — which happened, in fact, to be this woman's name too — and his voice gave him away. The attendant shrieked, ran back to the lights and the company, and cried out that she had found a man in the house. The women were terrified. Aurelia covered up the sacred objects and stopped the rites, ordered the doors shut, and went through the house with torches, hunting for Clodius. He was found where he had started — hiding in the room of the maid who had let him in — and seized. The women recognized him and threw him out of the house, and that same night, before dawn, they went home and told their husbands everything. By morning the story was all over Rome: Clodius had committed sacrilege, and owed satisfaction not only to the women he had insulted but to the city and to the gods. One of the tribunes formally charged him with profaning the holy rites, and some of the leading senators joined forces to testify against him — swearing that, among a long list of other outrages, he had committed incest with his own sister, the wife of Lucullus. But the people set themselves against the nobles' campaign and defended Clodius, which counted for a great deal with the judges, who took fright and had no wish to provoke the crowd. Caesar divorced Pompeia immediately. Yet when he was called as a witness at the trial, he said he knew nothing against Clodius at all. Since that sounded absurd, the prosecutor asked him: then why had he put away his wife? "Because I wished my wife," Caesar said, "to be not so much as suspected." Some say he meant it; others, that he said it to please the people, who were determined to save Clodius. Either way, Clodius was acquitted — most of the judges turning in ballots written so illegibly that they could not be read, so as not to risk their necks with the people by convicting, nor their standing with the nobility by acquitting.

His praetorship over, Caesar was assigned the province of Spain — and found himself in serious trouble with his creditors, who descended on him as he was about to leave, loud and immovable. This drove him to Crassus, the richest man in Rome, who for his part needed Caesar's youth and fire for his own contest with Pompey. Crassus agreed to take on the most impatient and unbending of the creditors and stood surety for eight hundred and thirty talents, and with that Caesar was free to leave for his province. On the journey out, crossing the Alps, he passed a shabby little native village with a handful of wretchedly poor inhabitants, and his companions began joking among themselves: was there election campaigning here too — rivalries for first place, feuds between the local great men? Caesar answered them in complete earnest. "For my part," he said, "I would rather be first man here than second at Rome." In the same way, they say that later, in Spain, in an idle hour, he was reading from the history of Alexander and sat a long time lost in thought — and then burst into tears. His friends were astonished and asked what was wrong. "Do you not think I have cause to weep," he said, "when Alexander at my age was already king over so many nations, and I have done nothing memorable at all?" The moment he arrived in Spain he went to work. Within days he had raised ten new cohorts of infantry on top of the twenty already stationed there, marched against the Callaici and the Lusitanians, beat them, and pushed on all the way to the outer ocean, subduing tribes that had never before answered to Rome. Having managed the war so well, he did equally well at governing the peace. He worked to bring the province's communities into harmony, and took particular care to heal the running war between debtors and creditors: he ordered that each creditor take two-thirds of his debtor's annual income, with the debtor keeping the remaining third, until the whole debt was worked off. This won him a fine reputation to leave with. He departed the province a rich man himself, with soldiers he had enriched too — and they had hailed him with the honorable title of Imperator, the soldiers' salute of victory.

Roman law said that a general who wanted the honor of a triumph had to wait outside the city walls until the senate gave its answer. Another law said that a candidate for the consulship had to appear in the city in person. Caesar arrived home at the exact moment of the consular elections and found himself pinned between the two rules. So he sent word to the senate asking that, since the law obliged him to stay outside, he be allowed to stand for the consulship in absence, through his friends. Cato opposed the request, standing squarely on the letter of the law — and then, when he saw that Caesar had already talked a large part of the senate into agreeing, he switched tactics and simply played for time, speaking on and on until the whole day was wasted. At that, Caesar made his decision. He let the triumph go and went after the consulship.

He entered the city, came forward at once, and pulled off a political masterstroke that deceived everyone except Cato. He reconciled Crassus and Pompey, the two most powerful men then in Rome. There had been a quarrel between them; Caesar now patched it up, harnessed their combined strength to himself, and — under cover of an act that looked like nothing but kindness and good nature — carried out what was in reality a revolution in the government. For it was not the quarrel between Pompey and Caesar that produced the civil wars, as most people imagine. It was their union: their conspiring together, first, to pull down the aristocracy, and only afterward falling out with each other. Cato, who kept predicting exactly where this alliance would end, was written off at the time as a sour, meddling obstructionist. In the end he got the reputation of a wise counselor who was simply unlucky.

So Caesar, propped up on both sides by the interests of Crassus and Pompey, was swept into the consulship and triumphantly proclaimed, with Calpurnius Bibulus as his colleague. The moment he took office he began introducing bills that would have come more gracefully from the most reckless of the tribunes than from a consul — proposals for planting colonies and carving up public lands, designed purely to please the crowd. The best and most honorable senators opposed him, which was exactly the excuse he had been waiting for. He protested loudly that it was against all his wishes, but the senate's insults and harshness were driving him to it: there was nothing left for him now but to devote himself to the people and their cause. And out of the senate house he stormed. Presenting himself before the popular assembly, with Crassus stationed on one side of him and Pompey on the other, he asked the two men whether they approved of his bills. They said they did. He then asked them to stand by him against those who had threatened to stop the bills with their swords. They promised they would, and Pompey went further: if anyone came with swords, he said, he would bring his own sword — and a shield as well. The nobles hated this remark. It was beneath his dignity, they said, and showed none of the respect owed to the senate; it sounded like a hotheaded boy, or a madman raving. But the people loved it.

To fasten his hold on Pompey tighter still, Caesar gave him his daughter Julia — who was already engaged to Servilius Caepio — and told Servilius he could have Pompey's daughter instead, though she was not exactly free either, being promised to Sulla's son Faustus. A little later Caesar himself married Calpurnia, the daughter of Piso, and then got Piso made consul for the following year. At this Cato exploded, protesting with real heat that it was intolerable to have the government bartered away through marriages, and to watch these men trade armies, provinces, and great commands among themselves by dealing out women. Bibulus, Caesar's colleague, found that opposing the bills was useless and that he was in real danger of being murdered in the forum — as was Cato. So he shut himself up in his house and stayed there for the remainder of his term as consul. Pompey, as soon as he was married, filled the forum with soldiers, helped the people force the new laws through, and secured for Caesar the government of all Gaul — both on this side of the Alps and beyond them — together with Illyricum and the command of four legions for five years. Cato made some attempt to fight these measures, and Caesar had him seized and marched off toward prison, expecting him to appeal to the tribunes. But Cato walked along without saying a word. Caesar watched — and saw that not only were the nobles indignant, but the common people too, out of respect for Cato's virtue, were following behind him in silence with downcast faces. So Caesar quietly asked one of the tribunes, himself, to intervene and release him. As for the rest of the senators, only a few still attended the meetings; the others, disgusted, stayed away. Considius, a very old man, told Caesar one day that the senators did not come because they were afraid of his soldiers. "Then why doesn't the same fear keep you at home?" Caesar asked. Because, Considius answered, old age was his bodyguard: the little life he had left was not worth much caution. But the most disgraceful act of Caesar's entire consulship was helping win the tribuneship for Clodius — the same Clodius who had made the attempt on his wife's chastity and crashed the secret rites of the women. Clodius was elected for one purpose: to destroy Cicero. And Caesar did not leave the city to join his army until the two of them together had brought Cicero down and driven him out of Italy.

So much for Caesar's career before the wars in Gaul. From here he seems to start over — to enter a new life and a whole new field of action. The wars he now fought, and the long string of campaigns in which he conquered Gaul, proved him a soldier and a general second to none of the greatest and most admired commanders who ever led an army. Compare him with the Fabii, the Metelli, the Scipios; with the men of his own time or just before it — Sulla, Marius, the two Luculli, or even Pompey himself, whose military glory in those days seemed to reach the sky — and Caesar's achievements outstrip them all. One he surpassed in the difficulty of the country he fought in; another in the sheer extent of territory he conquered; some in the number and strength of the enemies he defeated; one in the wildness and treachery of the tribes he managed to win over; another in his humanity and mercy toward the beaten; others again in his gifts and generosity to his own soldiers; and all of them alike in the number of battles he fought and enemies he killed. For in less than ten years of war in Gaul he took more than eight hundred towns by storm, subdued three hundred nations, and — of the three million men who at one time or another stood against him — killed one million and took another million prisoner.

He commanded such devotion and loyalty from his soldiers that men who were nothing special in other campaigns became unstoppable, past defeating or even withstanding, the moment Caesar's glory was at stake. One such was Acilius, who in the sea-fight off Marseilles had his right hand sheared off by a sword — and did not drop the shield from his left. He smashed it into the enemies' faces until he had driven them all back and taken the ship. Another was Cassius Scaeva, who in the battle near Dyrrhachium had one eye shot out with an arrow, his shoulder run through with one javelin and his thigh with another, and caught one hundred and thirty darts on his shield. In that state he called out to the enemy as if he meant to surrender — and when two of them came up to take him, he sliced the shoulder off one with his sword, struck the other across the face and drove him back, and escaped with the help of his friends, who by then had reached him. In Britain, some of the leading officers blundered into a watery marsh and were attacked there by the enemy. While Caesar stood watching, a common soldier flung himself into the middle of the fight, performed one astonishing feat of courage after another, rescued the officers, and beat off the barbarians. Then, going last himself, he plunged into the marsh and struggled across, partly swimming, partly wading — but in the crossing he lost his shield. Caesar and his officers, who had seen the whole thing, came to meet him with shouts of joy and admiration. But the soldier, miserable and in tears, threw himself at Caesar's feet and begged forgiveness for letting go of his shield. And once in Africa, when Scipio captured one of Caesar's ships with Granius Petro aboard — he had just been appointed quaestor, a treasury officer — Scipio handed the other passengers over to his soldiers as plunder but offered the quaestor his life. Granius answered that Caesar's soldiers were not in the habit of taking mercy, but of giving it — and with those words he fell on his own sword and killed himself.

This appetite for honor, this passion for distinction, Caesar himself planted in his men and fed daily. He handed out money and honors without stinting, and made it plain that he was not piling up wealth from his wars for his own luxury or private pleasure: all of it was a public fund, held in trust to reward and encourage courage, and he counted every coin he gave a deserving soldier as an addition to his own riches. Beyond that, there was no danger he would not walk into willingly, no hardship he ever excused himself from. His contempt for danger did not much surprise his soldiers, because they knew how hungry he was for glory. What astonished them was his endurance of hardship — far beyond, it seemed, what his body should have been able to bear. For he was a slight man, with soft, white skin, subject to headaches and to epileptic fits, which are said to have first seized him at Corduba, in Spain. Yet he never used his frail constitution as an excuse for taking it easy. Instead he used war itself as his medicine — fighting off his ailments with relentless marches, plain food, night after night camping in the open, and constant punishing exercise, hardening his body against every attack. He usually slept in carriages or litters, putting even his rest to work. By day he had himself driven around to the forts, garrisons, and camps, with one servant riding beside him to take dictation as he traveled and a single soldier standing behind him with a drawn sword. And he drove hard: on his first departure from Rome, he reached the river Rhone in eight days. Riding had been easy for him since childhood — he used to sit with his hands clasped behind his back and put his horse to a full gallop. In this war he trained himself further still, until he could dictate letters from horseback and keep two secretaries busy at once — or, as Oppius says, even more. It is thought he was also the first to work out a way of communicating with friends in cipher, when the press of business, or the sheer size of the city, left no time to meet face to face on urgent matters. As for how little he cared about his food, one story tells it all. At Milan his host Valerius Leo served him a dinner at which the asparagus came dressed not with olive oil but with sweet perfumed ointment. Caesar ate it without a flicker of disgust — and scolded his friends for complaining. "It was enough not to eat it if you didn't like it," he said. "But a man who points out another man's bad manners shows a shortage of his own." Another time a storm drove him and his companions to shelter in a poor man's cottage on the road. Finding it held a single room, and that one barely fit to lodge one person, he told his friends that places of honor should go to the greater men, but necessities to the weaker — and so he ordered that Oppius, who was in poor health, should sleep inside, while he and the rest bedded down under a shed by the door.

His first war in Gaul was against the Helvetii and the Tigurini, who had burned their own twelve towns and four hundred villages and set out to march through the part of Gaul that belonged to the Roman province, just as the Cimbri and Teutones had done before them. They were not inferior to those invaders in courage, and in numbers they were their equals: three hundred thousand in all, of whom one hundred and ninety thousand were fighting men. The Tigurini Caesar did not engage in person; Labienus, acting under his orders, routed them near the river Arar. But the Helvetii caught Caesar by surprise, falling on him unexpectedly as he was marching his army toward an allied town. He managed to pull back to a strong position, and there, once he had mustered and drawn up his men, his horse was brought to him. "When I've won the battle," he said, "I'll use my horse for the chase. For now — let's go at the enemy." And he charged them on foot. After a long, hard fight he drove their main army off the field, but the toughest work came at their wagons and ramparts, where not only the men stood and fought but the women and children defended themselves too, until they were cut to pieces; the fighting was barely finished by midnight. To this victory Caesar added something finer still. He gathered up all the barbarians who had escaped the battle — more than one hundred thousand of them — and compelled them to return to the country they had abandoned and the cities they had burned. He did this because he feared that if the land lay empty, the Germans would cross over and take it.

His second war he fought in defense of the Gauls against the Germans — even though, not long before, he had himself arranged for their king Ariovistus to be recognized at Rome as an ally. But the Germans had become insufferable neighbors to the peoples under his government, and it was obvious that at the first opportunity they would tear up the existing arrangements and march in to seize Gaul. Caesar found his officers frightened, though — especially the young nobles who had come along on the campaign imagining it would be a road to pleasure and profit. So he called them together and told them to march off home: there was no need for men with such weak and unmanly feelings to risk a battle against their inclinations. "I will take the tenth legion alone," he said, "and march against the barbarians. I don't expect these enemies to prove more formidable than the Cimbri — and I don't think you will find me a general inferior to Marius." At this, the tenth legion sent a deputation to thank him, while the other legions turned on their own officers in shame, and the whole army, full of fire and eagerness now, followed him for many days' march, until they camped about twenty-five miles from the enemy. Their mere approach took some of the fight out of Ariovistus. He had never expected the Romans to attack the Germans — he had assumed they would not dare stand against them even in defense of their own subjects — and Caesar's boldness stunned him; he could see, too, that his own army was rattled. The Germans were discouraged even more by the prophecies of their holy women, who read the future in the eddies of rivers and took signs from the windings and murmur of streams, and who now warned them not to fight before the new moon appeared. Caesar got word of this, and seeing the Germans sitting idle, he judged it far better to attack them while they were unnerved than to wait around for their chosen moment. So he pressed assaults on the strongholds and hills where they lay camped, and stung and goaded them until at last they came storming down in a fury to fight. He won a crushing victory, and pursued them fifty miles, all the way to the Rhine, the whole distance strewn with spoils and the bodies of the dead. Ariovistus with a small remnant managed to get across the Rhine; the dead are said to have numbered eighty thousand.

After this campaign, Caesar left his army in winter quarters in the country of the Sequani and, in order to keep a hand on affairs at Rome, came down into the part of Gaul that lies along the Po, which belonged to his province — for the river Rubicon divides Gaul on this side of the Alps from the rest of Italy. There he settled in and worked at courting popularity. Crowds came to him constantly, and every man got what he asked for; no one left without something in hand and greater hopes for the future. And through this whole period of the Gallic war, Pompey never noticed what was happening: that Caesar was using the arms of Rome to conquer Gaul, and using the wealth of those conquests to buy Rome. But when Caesar heard that the Belgae — the most powerful of all the Gauls, holding a third of the country — had revolted and assembled many thousands of men in arms, he turned back immediately and moved with tremendous speed. Falling on the enemy as they plundered the lands of Rome's Gallic allies, he quickly shattered and scattered the largest and most concentrated body of them. Their numbers were huge, but they fought so feebly that the Roman infantry crossed marshes and deep rivers on the sheer mass of the dead. After that, all the rebel tribes along the ocean submitted without a fight, and Caesar led his army against the Nervii, the fiercest and most warlike people in that whole region. The Nervii live in a country of dense unbroken woods. Having stowed their families and property deep in the forest, out of harm's way, they fell on Caesar with sixty thousand men while he was still building his camp, before he was ready for them. They routed his cavalry, surrounded the twelfth and seventh legions, and killed every one of the officers. If Caesar had not snatched up a shield himself, forced his way through the press of his own men, and thrown himself at the barbarians — and if the tenth legion, seeing his danger from the hilltops where they were posted, had not come running down and smashed through the enemy ranks to reach him — in all likelihood not one Roman would have survived. As it was, with Caesar's boldness in front of them, the men fought, as the saying goes, beyond human courage. Even so they could not drive the Nervii from the field; they cut them down where they stood, still fighting. Of sixty thousand men, it is said that no more than five hundred survived the battle — and of their four hundred senators, no more than three.

When news of this reached Rome, the senate voted sacrifices and festivals to the gods, to be strictly observed for fifteen days — a longer celebration than any victory had ever earned before. The danger had been real, they felt: so many nations rising at once. And because Caesar was the people's favorite, his success shone all the brighter. He himself, after settling everything in Gaul, came back and again spent the winter in the country along the Po, working at his designs on Rome. Candidates for office lined up for his help and drew on his money to bribe the people and buy votes, and once elected, they did everything they could to increase his power. More significant still, the most eminent and powerful men in Rome came to visit him at Lucca — Pompey, and Crassus, and Appius the governor of Sardinia, and Nepos the proconsul of Spain — until there were one hundred and twenty lictors in the town at one time, and more than two hundred senators. In the conference held there, it was settled that Pompey and Crassus should be consuls again the following year, and that Caesar should have a fresh grant of money and his command renewed for five more years. Thinking people found the arrangement grotesque: the very men who had taken so much money from Caesar were now urging the senate to give him more, as if he were poor. Though in truth "urging" is the wrong word — it was compulsion, and the senators groaned over their own votes as they passed the measures. Cato was not there; they had taken care to ship him off to Cyprus in good time. But Favonius, a devoted imitator of Cato, finding his opposition useless, burst out of the senate house and loudly denounced the whole business to the people. No one listened. Some brushed him off out of deference to Crassus and Pompey; most did it to please Caesar, on whom all their hopes now hung.

Caesar then returned to his forces in Gaul, where he found the country in the grip of a dangerous war: two powerful German nations, the Usipetes and the Tencteri, had just crossed the Rhine to conquer land. Of the war with them, Caesar's own account in his commentaries is this: the barbarians sent ambassadors to negotiate with him, and then, in the middle of the truce, attacked him on the march — which is how eight hundred of their horsemen routed five thousand of his cavalry, who suspected nothing. They then sent a second embassy to try the same trick again; Caesar held the ambassadors in custody and led his army against the barbarians, judging it plain foolishness to keep faith with men who had so faithlessly broken their own agreements. But Tanusius adds this: when the senate was decreeing festivals and sacrifices for the victory, Cato rose and declared that in his opinion Caesar ought to be handed over to the barbarians — so that the guilt of his broken faith might be purged from the city and the curse carried onto the one man responsible for it. Of the Germans who had crossed the Rhine, four hundred thousand were cut down; the few who escaped found shelter with the Sugambri, a people of Germany. Caesar seized on this as his pretext to invade the Germans — and besides, he coveted the honor of being the first man ever to cross the Rhine with an army. He began throwing a bridge across it, though the river was very wide, and at that point especially the current ran full, strong, and violent, driving down tree trunks and debris that battered the bridge's foundations. But Caesar drove great piles of timber into the riverbed upstream of the crossing to catch and hold whatever came floating down — and so, setting a bridle on the stream itself, he brought his bridge to completion. It was a sight no one who saw it could believe was the work of just ten days.

The crossing itself met no resistance. Even the Suevi, the most warlike people in all Germany, would not face him; they gathered up their belongings and vanished into the deepest, most thickly wooded valleys they could find. Caesar burned the enemy's countryside, encouraged the tribes that had taken Rome's side, and after eighteen days in Germany marched back into Gaul. But it was his expedition to Britain that became the most famous proof of his daring. He was the first commander ever to bring a fleet into the western ocean, the first to sail an army out onto the Atlantic to make war. And the island he invaded was so remote, and the reports of its size so extravagant, that historians had actually argued over whether it existed at all — whether "Britain" was a real place or just a name, a fiction. By landing there, you could fairly say, Caesar carried the Roman empire beyond the limits of the known world. He crossed twice from the part of Gaul that faces the island, and in several battles he did the enemy more damage than he did himself any good, for the islanders were so miserably poor that they had nothing worth plundering. Unable to finish the war the way he wanted, he settled for taking hostages from the king, imposing a tribute, and leaving.

Back in Gaul, he found letters waiting for him, ready to be carried across the water — letters from his friends in Rome, telling him that his daughter was dead. Julia had died giving birth to Pompey's child. Caesar and Pompey were both shattered by her death, and their friends were hardly less shaken, because everyone understood that the alliance which had kept the ailing republic at peace was now broken. The baby, too, died within a few days of its mother. As for Julia's body, the people took it — over the objections of the tribunes — carried it to the Field of Mars, and held her funeral there. That is where her remains lie.

Caesar's army had by now grown so large that he had to scatter it among a number of camps for the winter, and he himself went off to Italy, as he usually did. In his absence, all of Gaul erupted at once. Great armies swept across the country, attacked the Roman winter quarters, and tried to storm the forts where the legions lay. The largest and strongest body of rebels, under the command of Ambiorix, wiped out Cotta and Titurius and every man with them, and then a force sixty thousand strong laid siege to the legion commanded by Cicero and very nearly took it by storm — every Roman soldier wounded, all of them spent, holding on by an effort beyond their natural strength. Caesar was far away when the news reached him. He instantly scraped together seven thousand men and raced to relieve Cicero. The besiegers knew he was coming and marched out to meet him, fully confident of swallowing so small a force. Caesar, to feed their overconfidence, kept seeming to avoid battle, falling back and back until he found ground suited for a few men to fight many, and there he made camp. He forbade his soldiers to attack. He ordered them instead to build the ramparts higher and barricade the gates, so that everything about the camp said fear — and the enemy's contempt grew accordingly. At last, swaggering and disordered, sure of themselves, they came on to assault the walls. Then Caesar came out, threw them into flight, and killed a great many of them.

That settled most of the uprisings in this part of Gaul, and through the winter Caesar went everywhere in person, watching sharply for any new trouble. Three fresh legions had arrived to replace the men he had lost — two of them lent by Pompey from his own command, the third newly raised in the Gaul along the Po. But the seeds of war had been quietly sown and scattered long before, by the most powerful men among those warrior nations, and now they broke out into the greatest and most dangerous war ever fought in that country. Consider the scale of it: the sheer numbers of young men in their prime, gathered and armed from every quarter; the vast sums of money collected to sustain the fight; the strength of the fortified towns; the near-impossibility of the terrain. It was winter. The rivers were frozen, the forests buried in snow, the plains flooded; in some places the roads disappeared under snowdrifts, in others the overflowing of marshes and streams made every route uncertain. All of it seemed to make a move against the rebels simply impossible. Many tribes had risen together, chief among them the Arverni and the Carnutes, and supreme command of the war belonged to Vercingetorix — a man whose own father the Gauls had put to death on suspicion of aiming at absolute power.

Vercingetorix divided his forces into several armies, set officers over each, and brought over to his side all the surrounding country as far as the river Arar. He had intelligence, too, of the opposition Caesar was now facing in Rome, and his plan was to raise all Gaul at once. Had he done it only a little later, when Caesar was tied down in the civil wars, Italy would have known a terror not felt since the Cimbri. But Caesar had, above all men who ever lived, the gift of using every advantage a war offered — and above all the gift of seizing the right moment. The instant he heard of the revolt he turned around and went back the way he had come, and the sheer speed of that march, through such a brutal season, showed the barbarians exactly what kind of army was coming for them: an unstoppable one. In the time it seemed scarcely believable that a courier could have arrived with a message from Caesar, Caesar himself arrived with his whole army — ravaging the country, reducing strongholds, taking towns, receiving the tribes that came over to him. So it went until even the Aedui declared against him. They had always called themselves brothers of the Roman people and had been showered with honors, and when they joined the rebels, Caesar's army felt the discouragement badly. He pulled out of that region and crossed the country of the Lingones, making for the territory of the Sequani, who were friendly and who stood like a rampart between Italy and the rest of Gaul. There the enemy fell on him and surrounded him with many tens of thousands of men. Caesar was eager enough to fight, and after a long and bloody struggle he won, on the whole, a complete victory — though at first he seems to have taken a beating, for the Arverni still show visitors a short sword hanging in one of their temples which they claim was captured from Caesar. Caesar himself saw it there later, and smiled, and when his friends urged him to have it taken down, he refused. It had been dedicated to the gods, and he considered it sacred.

After the defeat, most of the survivors fled with their king into the town of Alesia. Caesar besieged it, though the height of the walls and the number of defenders made the place look impregnable — and meanwhile, from outside the siege lines, he faced a danger greater than words can express. The best fighting men of Gaul, picked from every nation and well armed, were converging on Alesia to relieve it: three hundred thousand of them. And inside the town were no fewer than a hundred and seventy thousand more. Caught and squeezed between two such forces, Caesar had to build two walls — one facing the town, the other facing the relief army — because he knew that if the two enemies ever joined hands, he was finished. The danger he ran at Alesia earned him fame, and deservedly, on many counts; no other contest ever gave him such scope to display his boldness and his generalship. One marvels that he could engage and defeat so many thousands outside the town without the men inside ever noticing. But stranger still: even the Romans guarding the inner wall, the one facing the town, knew nothing of the battle. They learned of the victory only when they heard the crying of the men and the wailing of the women in Alesia, who had looked out and seen, in the distance, the Romans carrying into their camp great numbers of shields decorated with gold and silver, breastplates soaked in blood, drinking cups, and tents of Gallic workmanship. That is how quickly so vast an army dissolved and vanished — like a ghost, like a dream — with most of them killed where they stood. At last, after giving Caesar and themselves plenty of trouble, the defenders of Alesia surrendered. And Vercingetorix, the mainspring of the whole war, put on his finest armor, decked out his horse, and rode out through the gates. He made a circle around Caesar, who sat waiting; then he dismounted, stripped off his armor, and sat down quietly at Caesar's feet, and there he stayed until he was led away — a prisoner reserved for the triumph.

Caesar had long since decided to bring Pompey down, and Pompey, for that matter, had decided the same about him. Crassus, whom they had both feared, and whose existence had kept the peace between them, was now dead in Parthia. From that point the logic was simple: if either man wanted to be the greatest in Rome, he had only to destroy the other; and if either wanted to avoid being destroyed, his only move was to strike first. Pompey had come to this fear only recently. Until lately he had despised Caesar, figuring it would be no great trouble to put down a man he himself had raised up. But Caesar had been planning for this contest from the beginning. Like a champion wrestler, he had withdrawn to train for the match in private. The Gallic wars were his training ground: there he hardened his soldiers and piled up glory with his victories, until men spoke of him as a rival worthy of comparison with Pompey. And he let no advantage slip — not the openings Pompey gave him, not the openings the times gave him, not the rot in Rome's own government. In Rome, candidates for office bought votes in the open, shamelessly, with cash on the table; and the people, once paid, fought for their paymasters not with ballots but with bows, swords, and slings. More than once the voting ground was left stained with blood and littered with the dead, and the city drifted into pure anarchy, like a ship swept along with no one at the helm. Sensible people could only hope that after such a storm of madness and disorder, things might end in nothing worse than a monarchy. Some said it openly: only a monarchy could cure the state now, and the remedy ought to be taken from the gentlest physician available — meaning Pompey. Pompey made a show of declining, but in reality he worked with everything he had to be named dictator. Cato saw through him, and persuaded the senate to make him sole consul instead — offering him a more legal sort of monarchy, so that he would stop demanding the dictatorship. On top of this they voted to extend his provinces. He had two, Spain and all of Africa, which he governed through deputies while maintaining armies at the public expense — a thousand talents a year out of the treasury.

At this Caesar sent to Rome and applied, in his turn, for a consulship and an extension of his own provinces. Pompey at first said nothing, but Marcellus and Lentulus — men who had always hated Caesar — opposed him, and did everything, fair or foul, to disgrace and insult him. The people of New Comum, a colony Caesar had recently planted in Gaul, were stripped of their Roman citizenship; and Marcellus, who was then consul, had one of that town's senators, who happened to be in Rome, publicly whipped — telling him the lashes were a mark to prove he was no Roman citizen, and that he should go home and show them to Caesar. After Marcellus's consulship ended, Caesar opened the floodgates of the wealth he had taken in Gaul and let it pour over the public men of Rome. He paid off the enormous debts of the tribune Curio. He gave the consul Paulus fifteen hundred talents, with which Paulus built the splendid basilica beside the forum, the courthouse that replaced the old Fulvian one. Now Pompey was alarmed. Openly, in his own person and through his friends, he pushed to have a successor appointed to Caesar's command, and he sent to demand back the soldiers he had lent him for the Gallic wars. Caesar sent them back — with a parting gift of two hundred and fifty drachmas to every man. But the officer who conducted them home to Pompey spread ugly and dishonest reports about Caesar among the people, and fed Pompey himself on flattering delusions: Caesar's own army longed for Pompey, he said; and while envy and bad government made Pompey's position difficult at home, out there the army was his for the asking — let them once cross into Italy and they would declare for him at once, so sick were they of Caesar's endless campaigns, and so suspicious that he aimed at monarchy. On this diet Pompey grew fat with confidence. He neglected every military preparation, since he feared no danger, and fought Caesar with nothing but speeches and votes — which Caesar cared nothing about. The story goes that one of Caesar's captains, sent on an errand to Rome, was standing outside the senate house when he heard that the senate refused to give Caesar an extension of his command. The captain clapped his hand on the hilt of his sword and said, "But this shall."

And yet the demands Caesar was making had the fairest look of justice imaginable. He proposed that he lay down his arms — provided Pompey did the same — and that both become private citizens and let the public reward each man's services as it saw fit. To disarm Caesar while confirming Pompey in all his power, he argued, was simply to establish one man in the very tyranny they accused the other of plotting. When Curio laid these proposals before the people in Caesar's name, they cheered him to the sky; some threw garlands at him and sent him off crowned with flowers, like a winning athlete. Antony, who was tribune, produced a letter from Caesar on the question and read it aloud, though the consuls did all they could to prevent it. But Scipio, Pompey's father-in-law, moved in the senate that if Caesar did not lay down his arms by a fixed day, he should be declared an enemy of the state. And when the consuls put the questions — should Pompey dismiss his soldiers? should Caesar disband his? — hardly anyone voted yes to the first, and nearly everyone voted yes to the second. But then Antony moved that both men give up their commands together, and all but a very few agreed to that. Scipio raged; the consul Lentulus shouted that against a robber you need weapons, not votes; and the senate adjourned, its members putting on mourning clothes in grief at the quarrel.

Then more letters came from Caesar, and their terms seemed more moderate still. He offered to give up everything else and keep only Gaul-within-the-Alps, Illyricum, and two legions, until he could stand for his second consulship. Cicero the orator, just back from Cilicia, worked hard for a reconciliation and softened Pompey up; Pompey was ready to yield on everything but the soldiers. At last Cicero persuaded Caesar's friends to settle for the provinces and a mere six thousand men, and close the quarrel there. Pompey was inclined to accept. But the consul Lentulus would not hear of it, and went so far as to drive Antony and Curio out of the senate house with insults — handing Caesar the most plausible grievance he could have asked for, and one perfectly designed to inflame his soldiers: look, he could say, at two officers of rank and authority forced to flee Rome in a hired carriage, dressed as slaves. For that is how they had to disguise themselves to get out of the city.

Caesar at that moment had no more than three hundred cavalry and five thousand infantry with him. The rest of his army was still beyond the Alps, to be brought up later by officers already under orders. But he judged that the opening move of his enterprise did not need large forces — what it needed was to be made suddenly, with a boldness that would stun his enemies. It would be easier, he reckoned, to throw them into panic by doing what they never expected than to beat them in a fair fight after his preparations had put them on guard. So he ordered his captains and officers to take Ariminum — a large city of his province, the first inside Italy — with their swords alone, no other weapons, and with as little disturbance and bloodshed as possible. He gave the command of the force to Hortensius. He himself spent the day in public, standing around watching gladiators exercise. A little before evening he bathed and dressed, went into the hall, and chatted for a while with the guests he had invited to dinner. When it was beginning to get dark, he rose from the table, politely asked the company to wait for him until he came back, and left — having already told a few close friends, privately, to follow him, not all together but by different roads. He climbed into one of the hired carriages and drove off at first in a different direction entirely, then turned toward Ariminum.

When he came to the river Rubicon, which divides Gaul-within-the-Alps from the rest of Italy, his thoughts began to work on him. Here was the edge of the danger. He wavered, turning over in his mind the sheer size of what he was about to attempt. He slowed, then ordered a halt, and sat a long while in silence, revolving the decision this way and that, changing his mind and changing it again. This was the moment his purpose hung most in the balance. Then he began to talk it through with the friends who were with him — Asinius Pollio was one — reckoning up the calamities that his crossing of that river would let loose on mankind, and what the men of the future would say of it. At last, on a kind of passion, he threw calculation aside and flung himself at whatever was coming. Speaking the words men always use on the brink of a desperate gamble — "The die is cast." — he took the river. Once across, he moved at full speed, and before daybreak he reached Ariminum and took it. It is said that the night before he crossed, he had a monstrous dream: he dreamed that he lay with his own mother.

With Ariminum taken, it was as if wide gates had swung open to let war flood in over every land and sea alike; with the border of the province, the boundaries of the laws had been crossed too. And the panic that followed was not the usual kind, with men and women fleeing in terror from one Italian town to another. This time, you would have said, the towns themselves tore loose from their foundations and fled to one another for shelter. Rome itself was overrun, as if by a flood, with people pouring in from all the surrounding country. No magistrate could govern the city, no orator's eloquence could quiet it; it came within an inch of wrecking itself on the violence of its own storm. Everywhere the most violent and contradictory passions collided. Those who welcomed the coming upheaval made little effort to hide it, and when they ran into the frightened and dejected men of the other party — as in so great a city they constantly did — their loud confidence in what was coming started quarrels on the spot. Pompey, unsettled enough on his own account, was battered from every side. Some told him he was only getting what he deserved for arming Caesar against himself and against the state. Others blamed him for letting Lentulus insult Caesar just when Caesar was making generous concessions and offering reasonable terms of peace. Favonius told him to go ahead and stamp his foot — because once, talking big in the senate, Pompey had told the senators not to trouble themselves with preparations for war: he himself, with one stamp of his foot, would fill all Italy with soldiers. Even then, Pompey still had more forces than Caesar. But he was never allowed to think his own thoughts. Buried under false reports and alarms — the enemy at the gates, the enemy sweeping all before him — he gave way and let himself be carried off by the general panic. He issued an edict declaring the city in a state of anarchy, and left it, ordering the senate to follow and declaring that anyone who stayed behind was choosing tyranny over country and liberty.

The consuls fled at once, without even performing the sacrifices custom required before a journey. Most of the senators fled too, snatching up whatever of their own property came to hand, in as much haste as if they were looting their neighbors' houses. Some who until then had been warm supporters of Caesar's cause lost their heads in the general terror and let themselves be swept along in the current, though it did them no good at all. It was a pitiful sight: the city tossed in the tumult like a ship abandoned by her pilots, left to run on any rock in her path as chance might steer her. And yet, miserable as the flight was, people counted exile itself as home because Pompey was there, and fled from Rome as if Rome had become Caesar's camp. Even Labienus deserted — Labienus, who had been one of Caesar's closest friends and his lieutenant, who had fought at his side through all the Gallic wars — he now abandoned him and went over to Pompey. Caesar sent his money and his baggage after him. Then he sat down before Corfinium, which was held by thirty cohorts under the command of Domitius. Domitius, despairing of the defense, asked a physician among his attendants for poison, took the dose, and drank it, expecting it to finish him. But shortly afterward, when he heard that Caesar was treating his prisoners with extraordinary clemency, he began to lament his misfortune and curse the haste of his decision. His physician then set his mind at rest: what he had swallowed was a sleeping draught, not poison. Overjoyed, Domitius got up from his bed, went straight to Caesar, and gave him his hand in pledge — and then, later, went over to Pompey again anyway. When news of all this reached Rome, it calmed the people who remained there, and some of those who had fled came back.

Caesar folded Domitius's soldiers into his own army, and he did the same in every town where he found men enlisted for Pompey's service. Now strong and formidable, he marched against Pompey himself. Pompey did not wait for him. He fell back to Brundisium, sent the consuls ahead to Dyrrhachium with a body of troops, and shortly afterward, as Caesar approached, put to sea — as will be told in more detail in his own Life. Caesar wanted to pursue at once, but he had no ships, so he turned back to Rome. In sixty days, without shedding a drop of blood, he had made himself master of all Italy.

He found the city quieter than he expected, and many senators still in it. He addressed them with courtesy and deference, and asked them to send envoys to Pompey with reasonable terms for a peace. Nobody took him up on it — whether from fear of Pompey, whom they had deserted, or because they suspected Caesar did not mean a word of it and was simply saying what sounded good. When the tribune Metellus tried to stop him from taking money out of the public treasury, citing the laws against it, Caesar answered that arms and laws each had their own season. "If what I am doing displeases you, leave. War has no room for free speech. When I have laid down my arms and made peace, come back and give all the speeches you please. And in telling you even this much," he added, "I am giving up my own just rights — for you, and everyone else who took sides against me and is now in my power, may be dealt with exactly as I please." With that he walked to the doors of the treasury. The keys could not be found, so he sent for smiths to break the doors open. Metellus objected again, and some of the bystanders cheered him on — at which Caesar raised his voice and told him he would put him to death if he made any more trouble. "And you know very well, young man," he said, "that saying this is more disagreeable to me than doing it." The words sent Metellus away in fear, and from then on everything Caesar demanded for the war was supplied promptly and without argument.

Next he set out for Spain. He meant to crush Afranius and Varro first — Pompey's lieutenants there — and take over the armies and provinces they held, so that he could then move against Pompey with no enemy left at his back. In Spain his life was in constant danger from ambushes and his army from hunger, but he never let up: pursuing the enemy, daring them to fight, walling them in with his siege-works, until by main force he made himself master of their camps and their armies. Only the generals got away, and fled to Pompey.

When Caesar came back to Rome, his father-in-law Piso urged him to send envoys to Pompey and negotiate a peace; Isauricus, angling for Caesar's favor, argued against it. The senate then made him dictator, and he used the office to call home the exiles, restore citizen rights to the children of those who had suffered under Sulla, and ease the debtors by canceling part of the interest on their debts — that, and a few other measures of the same kind, but not many. Within eleven days he resigned the dictatorship, had himself declared consul alongside Servilius Isauricus, and hurried back to the war.

He marched so fast that he outran his own army. With only six hundred picked horsemen and five legions, he put to sea in the dead of winter, at the beginning of January — which corresponds roughly to the Athenian month Posideon — crossed the Ionian Sea, took Oricum and Apollonia, and sent the ships back to Brundisium for the soldiers still on the road behind him. Those soldiers, as they marched — men past the first vigor of youth, worn down by so many wars — grumbled openly against Caesar. "When will this Caesar finally let us rest, and where? He hauls us from place to place as if we could never wear out, as if we felt nothing. Even iron gets used up by hammering. Our shields and breastplates have served so long they deserve some pity. Our wounds, if nothing else, ought to remind him that the men he commands are mortal, and feel pain and suffering like any other human beings. The gods themselves cannot force the winter season or hold back the storms — yet he pushes on as if he were running from an enemy instead of chasing one." So they talked, strolling toward Brundisium at their ease. But when they got there and found Caesar already gone, the mood flipped in an instant. Now they cursed themselves as traitors to their general, railed at their officers for the slow pace, and perched on the heights facing Epirus, scanning the sea for the ships that would carry them across to Caesar.

Caesar, meanwhile, sat at Apollonia without an army strong enough to fight, and the forces from Brundisium were so long in coming that he was in an agony of suspense over what to do. At last he settled on a desperate experiment. Without telling anyone, he would cross back to Brundisium himself in a twelve-oared boat, though the sea was covered with the enemy's fleets. He went aboard at night dressed as a slave, threw himself down like a passenger of no account, and lay flat in the bottom of the boat. The river Anius was to carry them down to the sea. Most mornings a gentle breeze off the land smoothed the waves at the river's mouth, driving them outward; but that night a strong wind had blown in from the sea and overpowered the land breeze, so that where the river met the incoming seawater and the push of the waves, it turned rough and angry. The current was beaten back with such a violent swell that the master of the boat could not force the passage, and ordered his sailors to come about and turn back. At this Caesar revealed himself, took the astonished man by the hand, and said, "Go on, my friend, and fear nothing — you carry Caesar and his fortune in your boat." The sailors forgot the storm. They threw all their strength into their oars and fought to force their way down the river. But it was no use; the boat kept taking on water, and Caesar, finding himself in real danger at the river's very mouth, very reluctantly allowed the master to turn back. When he came ashore, his soldiers ran to him in a crowd, reproaching him and demanding to know why he thought he could not win with their help alone — why he had to torment himself and risk his life for the absent, as if he could not trust the men he had.

After this, Antony came over with the forces from Brundisium, and Caesar felt strong enough to offer Pompey battle. Pompey was superbly encamped, with supplies flowing in by land and sea, while Caesar was short of everything at the start and desperately pinched before the end. His soldiers were reduced to digging up a local root, mixing it with milk, and eating that. Sometimes they baked it into a kind of bread, and they would run up to the enemy's outposts and throw the loaves over, shouting that as long as the earth produced roots like these, they would never give up the blockade of Pompey. Pompey did what he could to keep both the loaves and the words from reaching his men, who were already disheartened — terrified of the enemy's ferocity and toughness, as if they were fighting some breed of wild beast. There was constant skirmishing around Pompey's outworks, and Caesar got the better of all of it except once — one fight in which his men were routed so badly that he nearly lost his camp. Pompey's sortie swept everything before it. Not a man stood his ground; the trenches filled with the slain; men fell at their own ramparts and palisades, driven onto them in headlong flight. Caesar met the fugitives and tried to turn them around, and could not. When he grabbed at the standards, the bearers dropped them and ran — the enemy captured thirty-two of them. He himself barely escaped with his life. Seizing a big, powerful soldier who was running past, he ordered him to stand and face the enemy; and the man, out of his mind with panic, raised his sword to strike him — before Caesar's armor-bearer sliced off his arm. That day Caesar's cause was so far gone that when Pompey — whether from over-caution or plain bad luck — failed to deliver the finishing blow, and pulled back after driving the routed enemy inside their camp, Caesar said to his friends as he watched the withdrawal, "Today the victory belonged to the enemy — if they had had a general who knew how to win it." Then he went to his tent and lay down, and spent the most miserable night of his life, turning it all over and concluding that he had managed the war badly. With fertile country in front of him, and all the rich cities of Macedonia and Thessaly, he had failed to carry the war inland and had instead planted himself by the sea, where the enemy's fleet ruled — so that in truth he was less besieging his enemy with arms than being besieged himself by hunger. Wrestling with the difficulty and distress of his position, he broke camp, resolved to march against Scipio, who lay in Macedonia. Either he would draw Pompey after him into country where Pompey would have to fight without his seaborne supplies, or he would crush Scipio unaided.

The move set Pompey's whole army and officer corps on fire to chase Caesar, whom they took for a beaten man on the run. But Pompey was afraid to stake everything on a single battle. He was stocked and supplied for a long war, and he meant to let time grind down Caesar's army, which could not last. The best of Caesar's men had enormous experience and an irresistible courage in any engagement; but the endless marching, the shifting of camps, the assaults on fortifications, the long night watches, were wearing them out and breaking them down. They were getting old. Their bodies were less fit for labor, and their spirit was starting to sag as their strength failed. On top of that, an infectious disease, bred by their wretched diet, was said to be running through Caesar's army — and worst of all, he had neither money nor provisions, so that in a little while he must simply collapse on his own.

For all these reasons Pompey refused battle, and only one man thanked him for it: Cato, who rejoiced at the prospect of sparing the lives of fellow citizens. Cato had seen the bodies of the thousand men who fell on Caesar's side in the last battle, and had turned away, covered his face, and wept. Everyone else berated Pompey for ducking the fight and needled him with nicknames — Agamemnon, King of Kings — as if he were in no hurry to lay down his supreme command, and enjoyed the sight of so many commanders dancing attendance at his tent. Favonius, who liked to copy Cato's blunt way of speaking his mind, complained bitterly that thanks to Pompey's love of command they would not taste figs at Tusculum even this year. Afranius, just back from Spain and suspected — given how badly things had gone there — of having sold his army for a bribe, demanded to know why they were not fighting this merchant who bought up provinces. Goaded by talk like this, Pompey was driven, against his own judgment, into offering battle, and set off after Caesar. Caesar's march had been hard going. Nobody would supply him with provisions; since his recent defeat, his reputation had fallen everywhere. But once he took Gomphi, a town in Thessaly, he found not only food for his army but medicine too. The place was full of wine, and the men drank it freely; heated with it, romping and reveling along the march like one long festival of Bacchus, they shook off the disease entirely, their whole condition washed clean and made new.

The two armies came into the plain of Pharsalia and encamped facing each other. Pompey's thoughts swung back to their old track, away from fighting — all the more because of unlucky omens and a vision that came to him in a dream. But the men around him were so confident of success that Domitius, and Spinther, and Scipio quarreled, as if they had already won, over which of them would succeed Caesar as high priest; and many sent ahead to Rome to rent houses suitable for consuls and praetors, sure of stepping into those offices the moment the battle was over. The cavalry above all were spoiling for a fight. They were splendidly armed and superbly mounted, proud of their fine horses and of their own handsome persons — and of their numbers: five thousand of them against Caesar's one thousand. The infantry odds were just as lopsided: forty-five thousand on Pompey's side against twenty-two thousand.

Caesar called his soldiers together and told them that Corfinius was on the way with two legions, and that fifteen more cohorts under Calenus were posted at Megara and Athens. Did they want to wait for these to join them, he asked, or hazard the battle by themselves? They shouted back at him not to wait — to do whatever it took to bring on an engagement as soon as possible. When he sacrificed to the gods for the purification of his army, the first victim was no sooner killed than the augur told him that within three days matters would come to a decisive action. Caesar asked whether he saw anything in the entrails that promised a happy outcome. "That," said the priest, "you can answer better than I can. The gods announce a great change from the present state of things. If you think yourself well off now, expect worse fortune; if badly off, hope for better." The night before the battle, as Caesar walked the rounds about midnight, a light appeared in the sky, bright and flaming, and seemed to pass over his camp and fall into Pompey's. And when his soldiers came to relieve the watch at dawn, they could hear a panicked confusion in the enemy's lines. Even so, he did not expect to fight that day, and began breaking camp to march for Scotussa.

The tents were already down when his scouts rode up with word that the enemy was coming out to give battle. The news delighted him. He performed his devotions to the gods and drew up his army in three divisions. Domitius Calvinus took the center, Antony commanded the left wing, and Caesar himself the right, where he meant to fight at the head of the tenth legion. But when he saw the enemy's cavalry taking position opposite him — struck by their gleaming turnout and their sheer numbers — he quietly ordered six cohorts up from the rear of the army and posted them behind his right wing, with exact instructions for what to do when the enemy horse charged. On the other side, Pompey commanded his own right wing, Domitius the left, and Scipio, Pompey's father-in-law, the center. The whole weight of the cavalry was massed on the left, with one purpose: to swing around the enemy's right wing and rout the very part of the line where the general himself stood. No block of infantry, they reckoned, could be packed solid enough to take that shock; under the charge of so immense a force of horse, it must be broken and smashed to pieces. When both sides were ready to sound the signal, Pompey ordered his front ranks to stand their ground — to hold formation and quietly absorb the enemy's first attack until they came within javelin range.
