Here too Caesar finds fault with his generalship: Pompey did not understand that the first collision, delivered on the run, puts weight and force behind every blow and sets the men's spirits blazing, the roar of the whole mass fanning them to full heat. Caesar himself was just setting his lines in motion and advancing to the action when he noticed one of his captains, a steady and experienced soldier, urging his men to give their utmost. Caesar called him by name. "What are our hopes, Gaius Crassinius? What are our grounds for confidence?" Crassinius flung out his hand and shouted, "We shall conquer nobly, Caesar — and today, alive or dead, I will earn your praise." So he said, and he was the first man to run in upon the enemy, the hundred and twenty soldiers of his command at his back. He tore through the first rank and was still driving forward, cutting men down, when at last he was stopped by a sword-thrust that entered his mouth with such force that it came out at the back of his neck.

While the infantry ground against each other in the center, Pompey's cavalry rode up confidently on the flank, spreading their ranks wide to envelop Caesar's right wing. Before they could charge, Caesar's cohorts sprang out at them — and they did not hurl their javelins from a distance, or stab at thighs and legs as men usually do in close fighting. They aimed at the faces. Those were Caesar's orders. These young gentlemen, he reasoned, had seen little of battle or wounds; they wore their hair long, in the flower of their youth and the height of their good looks; and a blow like that they would dread twice over — the danger now, and the disfigurement forever. It worked exactly as he hoped. Far from standing up to the javelins, they could not even bear the sight of them. They twisted away and covered their faces to protect them; and once in disorder, they turned and fled — and most shamefully ruined everything. For the men who had beaten them wheeled at once around the flank of the infantry, fell on it from behind, and cut it to pieces. When Pompey, from the other wing, saw his cavalry scattered and flying, he was no longer himself. He no longer remembered that he was Pompey the Great. Like a man robbed of his senses by some god, he walked to his tent without a word and sat there, waiting to see how it would end, until the whole army was routed and the enemy was on the ramparts of the camp, fighting hand to hand with the men posted to defend it. Then at last he seemed to come back to himself, and saying — so it is reported — only these words, "What, into the camp too?" he took off his general's dress, put on clothes better suited to a fugitive, and stole away. What became of him afterward — how he took refuge in Egypt, and was murdered there — we tell in his own Life.

When Caesar came to look over Pompey's camp and saw some of his opponents lying dead on the ground and others still dying, he groaned and said, "They would have it so. They drove me to this. After winning so many wars, I — Gaius Caesar — would have stood condemned in their courts if I had dismissed my army." Pollio says Caesar spoke these words in Latin at the time, and that he himself later wrote them down in Greek; he adds that most of those killed in the taking of the camp were servants, and that no more than six thousand soldiers fell. Most of the infantry taken prisoner Caesar folded into his own legions, and he granted a free pardon to many distinguished men — among them Brutus, who would later kill him. Brutus did not appear right away when the battle was over, which is said to have thrown Caesar into great anxiety for him; and his pleasure was just as great when Brutus presented himself alive.

Many prodigies were said to have foretold this victory, but the most remarkable on record happened at Tralles. In the temple of Victory there stood a statue of Caesar. The ground it stood on was naturally hard and solid, and the stone paving on top of it harder still — yet a palm tree, they say, shot up beside the statue's pedestal. And at Padua, one Gaius Cornelius, a man with the reputation of a good augur — a fellow citizen and acquaintance of Livy the historian — happened to be taking the auguries on the very day the battle was fought. First, as Livy tells it, he marked the exact moment of the fight, announcing to those around him that just then the battle had begun and the men were engaging. When he looked a second time and read the signs, he leapt up as if inspired and cried out, "Caesar, you are victorious!" The bystanders were astonished; but he took the garland from his head and swore he would never wear it again until the outcome had proved his art true. Livy insists this really happened.

As a memorial of his victory, Caesar gave the Thessalians their freedom, and then went in pursuit of Pompey. When he reached Asia, he made the Cnidians a free people as a favor to Theopompus, the collector of fables, and cut the taxes of the whole province of Asia by a third. He came to Alexandria to find Pompey already murdered. When Theodotus presented him with the head, Caesar turned away and would not look at it; he took only the signet ring, and wept. Every one of Pompey's friends the king of Egypt had seized as they wandered the country, Caesar took under his protection and offered his own friendship. He wrote to his friends at Rome that the greatest and sweetest pleasure of his victory was this: that he could go on saving the lives of fellow citizens who had fought against him.

As for the war in Egypt, some say it was dangerous, inglorious, and entirely unnecessary — fought for no reason but his passion for Cleopatra. Others blame the king's ministers, above all the eunuch Pothinus, the power behind the throne, who had just had Pompey killed and Cleopatra banished, and was now secretly plotting Caesar's murder — which is why, from that time on, Caesar took to sitting up whole nights, under the pretense of drinking parties, to guard his own life. In the open, meanwhile, Pothinus was insufferable, insulting Caesar in everything he said and did. When Caesar's soldiers were issued moldy, foul grain, Pothinus told them to be content with it, since they were eating at another man's expense. He had the royal table set with wooden and earthenware dishes, claiming Caesar had carried off all the gold and silver plate against a supposed debt. In fact, the reigning king's father had owed Caesar seventeen and a half million; Caesar had earlier forgiven the king's children the greater part of it, but now demanded ten million to maintain his army. Pothinus advised him to go away for the present and attend to his great affairs elsewhere — he would receive his money later, with thanks. Caesar replied that he had no need of Egyptians as his advisers, and shortly afterward, in secret, he sent for Cleopatra from her exile.

She took a small boat and a single companion — Apollodorus the Sicilian — and came ashore near the palace at dusk. There was no way to get in unseen, until she hit on the idea of rolling herself up full-length inside a bed-sack; Apollodorus tied up the bedding, hoisted it onto his back, and carried it straight through the gates to Caesar's rooms. That piece of bold wit was what first captivated Caesar; the charm of her company did the rest. He arranged a reconciliation between Cleopatra and her brother, on the condition that she rule beside him as his colleague in the kingdom. A festival was held to celebrate the settlement, and at it Caesar's barber — a busy, listening fellow whose sheer timidity kept him poking his nose into everything — discovered that a plot was under way against Caesar, laid by Achillas, the general of the king's forces, and Pothinus the eunuch. The moment Caesar learned of it, he threw a guard around the banquet hall and had Pothinus killed. Achillas escaped to the army and raised a troublesome, dangerous war — no easy thing for Caesar to manage with his handful of soldiers against so powerful a city and so large an army. His first difficulty was water: the enemy had blocked the canals. The second came when they tried to cut him off from the sea. To head off that danger he set fire to his own ships, and the flames, after consuming the docks, spread onward and destroyed the great library. The third came in the fighting near the Pharos, when he leaped from the mole into a small boat to help his soldiers, who were in trouble. The Egyptians pressed in on him from every side, so he threw himself into the sea and swam clear only with great difficulty. This was the moment, the story goes, when he had a bundle of manuscripts in his hand and refused to let them go: though missiles rained down on him the whole way and he was forced again and again to duck his head under the water, he held the papers up dry in one hand and swam with the other, while his little boat sank behind him. At last, once the king had gone over to Achillas and his party, Caesar attacked and beat them. Many died in that battle, and the king himself was never seen again. Caesar left Cleopatra on the throne as queen of Egypt — soon afterward she bore him a son, whom the Alexandrians called Caesarion — and then set out for Syria.

From Syria he crossed into Asia, where he learned that Domitius had been beaten by Pharnaces, son of Mithridates, and had fled Pontus with a handful of men — and that Pharnaces, pressing his victory hungrily, had already made himself master of Bithynia and Cappadocia, had designs on Lesser Armenia besides, and was calling on all the kings and princes of the region to rise. Caesar marched against him at once with three legions, fought him near Zela, drove him out of Pontus, and destroyed his army entirely. Reporting the action to Amantius, a friend of his in Rome, he caught its whole speed and suddenness in three words: I came, I saw, I conquered. In Latin — veni, vidi, vici — the three words share the same clipped cadence, and the brevity could not be more fitting.

From there he crossed to Italy and reached Rome at the close of the year for which he had been chosen dictator a second time — though that office had never before lasted a whole year — and he was elected consul for the year to come. People spoke badly of him because, after mutinous soldiers killed two men of praetorian rank, Cosconius and Galba, he gave the mutineers nothing worse than the mild rebuke of addressing them as "citizens" instead of "fellow soldiers" — and then handed each man a thousand drachmas and a share of land in Italy besides. He was blamed, too, for the behavior of his lieutenants: Dolabella's extravagance, Amantius's greed, Antony's debauchery, and Corfinius, who pulled down Pompey's house and rebuilt it on the grounds that it was not magnificent enough. The Romans were disgusted by all of this. Caesar knew exactly what these men were, and disapproved of them — but for the sake of his own scheme of government he was forced to use the instruments that would serve him.

After the battle of Pharsalia, Cato and Scipio had fled to Africa, and there, with the help of King Juba, they assembled a considerable army. Caesar resolved to fight it. He crossed to Sicily around the winter solstice, and to knock every hope of delay out of his officers' heads he pitched his camp right on the beach; the moment he had a fair wind he put to sea with three thousand infantry and a few horsemen. Once he had landed them, he slipped back secretly, anxious about the larger part of his army — but he met those troops already at sea and brought the whole force into the same camp. There he learned that the enemy were leaning on an ancient oracle: the family of the Scipios would always be victorious in Africa. Now, there happened to be in his own army a man of the house of the Africani — an otherwise obscure and unimpressive fellow — whose name was Scipio Sallutio. This man Caesar put at the head of his troops, as though he were the general, in every one of the frequent battles he was compelled to fight. Whether he did it in mockery, to ridicule the enemy's Scipio, or in earnest, to steal the omen for his own side, is hard to say. And he was compelled to fight often, because he was desperately short of food for his men and forage for his horses — so short that he fed the horses on seaweed, rinsed thoroughly to take off the salt and mixed with a little grass to make it more palatable. The Numidians, numerous and superbly mounted, commanded the countryside wherever he went. One day Caesar's cavalry, having nothing to do, were amusing themselves watching an African man who danced and played the flute at the same time, marvelously well. They were so absorbed that they dismounted and handed their horses to some boys — and at that moment the enemy swept around them, killed some, and chased the rest headlong into their own camp. If Caesar himself, with Asinius Pollio, had not rushed to help and stopped the rout, the war would have ended right there. In another engagement, too, the enemy got the better of the fight, and Caesar, it is said, seized a fleeing standard-bearer by the neck, spun him around, and said, "Look — that is the way to the enemy."

Scipio, flushed with these early successes, decided to risk one decisive battle. Leaving Afranius and Juba in two separate camps not far off, he marched to Thapsus and began building a fortified camp above a lake, to serve as a center for operations and a refuge if things went wrong. While Scipio was busy with the work, Caesar moved with unbelievable speed. He made his way through thick woods and country everyone thought impassable, cut off one body of the enemy, and struck another head-on. Having routed them, he rode his opportunity and the current of his good fortune: at the first onset he carried Afranius's camp and sacked the camp of the Numidians, whose king, Juba, was glad to escape with his life. In a small part of a single day he had made himself master of three camps and killed fifty thousand of the enemy, at the cost of only fifty of his own men. That is one account of the battle. Others say Caesar was not in the action at all — that his old sickness, the epilepsy, took hold of him just as he was drawing up his army. He felt it coming, and before it could scramble his senses completely — he was already beginning to shake under it — he had himself carried into a nearby fort and rested there. Of the men of consular and praetorian rank captured after the battle, Caesar put several to death; others saved him the trouble by killing themselves.

Cato had undertaken the defense of Utica, and for that reason was not in the battle. Caesar wanted badly to take him alive, and hurried toward the city — and when word came that Cato had killed himself, he was visibly shaken, though exactly why is not agreed. What he certainly said was this: "Cato, I grudge you your death, as you grudged me the honor of saving your life." Yet the treatise he wrote against Cato after his death hardly suggests kindness, or any inclination to be reconciled. How likely is it that he would have spared the living man when he poured such bitterness on the dead one's memory? Then again, from his clemency to Cicero, to Brutus, and to many others who had fought against him, people have concluded that the book was written not so much out of hatred of Cato as in Caesar's own defense. It came about like this. Cicero had written a tribute to Cato and given it his name; and a composition by so great a master, on so excellent a subject, was bound to end up in everyone's hands. That stung Caesar, who regarded praise of his dead enemy as nothing less than an indictment of himself. So he wrote his Anti-Cato, a collection of everything that could be said against the man. The two works — like Cato and Caesar themselves — have each their own admirers to this day.

On his return to Rome, Caesar did not fail to stand before the people and deliver a magnificent account of his victory: he had subdued a country, he told them, that would supply the public every year with two hundred thousand Attic bushels of grain and three million pounds of olive oil. He then celebrated three triumphs — for Egypt, for Pontus, and for Africa — the last of them, officially, for the victory over King Juba, not over Scipio. Juba's little son was carried in that triumph, and he proved the luckiest captive who ever walked in one: from a barbarian Numidian prince, he went on to earn a place among the most learned historians of Greece. After the triumphs, Caesar distributed rewards to his soldiers and treated the people to feasts and shows. He banqueted the entire population at a single sitting, at twenty-two thousand dining couches, and put on displays of gladiators and mock naval battles in honor, as he said, of his daughter Julia — though she had been dead for years. When the shows were over, a count was taken of the people. Where there had been three hundred and twenty thousand, there were now one hundred and fifty thousand. So great was the wound the civil war had cut into Rome alone — to say nothing of what the rest of Italy and the provinces had suffered.

He was now chosen consul a fourth time, and went into Spain against Pompey's sons. They were young, but they had gathered a very large army and showed they had the courage and the skill to command it, and they put Caesar in extreme danger. The great battle came near the town of Munda. Caesar, seeing his men hard pressed and putting up a feeble resistance, ran through the ranks shouting at his soldiers, asking whether they were not ashamed to hand him over to boys. With enormous difficulty, and the best effort he had in him, he forced the enemy back, killing thirty thousand of them — though at the cost of a thousand of his own best men. Coming back from the fight, he told his friends that he had often fought for victory, but this was the first time he had ever fought for his life. He won the battle on the feast of Bacchus, the very day on which, four years earlier, Pompey had set out for the war. The younger of Pompey's sons escaped; a few days after the battle, Didius brought Caesar the head of the elder. This was the last war he ever fought — and the triumph he celebrated for it displeased the Romans beyond anything he had done. This time he had not defeated foreign generals or barbarian kings; he had destroyed the children and the family of one of the greatest men Rome had produced, however unfortunate. It did not look well to lead a parade celebrating his country's calamities — to rejoice in things whose only possible excuse, before gods or men, was that they had been absolutely necessary. And until then he had never sent so much as a letter or a messenger to announce a victory over his fellow citizens; he had seemed ashamed of such actions, not eager for credit.

Nevertheless the Romans bowed to his fortune and accepted the bit, hoping that one-man rule would give them room to breathe after so many civil wars and calamities: they made him dictator for life. This was tyranny openly admitted — his power was now not only absolute but permanent. Cicero made the first proposals in the senate for conferring honors on him, and those at least might be said to stay within the limits of ordinary human moderation. But others competed to outdo one another, and piled the honors so extravagantly high that they made Caesar odious even to mild and indifferent men, by the sheer pretension and excess of the titles they decreed him. His enemies are thought to have had a hand in this along with his flatterers: it gave them ammunition against him, a ready justification for whatever move they might make — for now that the civil wars were over, there was nothing else to charge him with. And in fairness, the senate had good reason to decree a temple to Clemency in thanks for the mildness with which he used his victory. He not only pardoned many of the men who had fought against him — to some he gave honors and offices, notably Brutus and Cassius, who both became praetors. Pompey's statues had been thrown down; Caesar set them up again, which drew from Cicero the remark that by raising Pompey's statues, Caesar had fixed his own in place. When his friends urged him to keep a bodyguard, and several volunteered for the duty, he refused to hear of it: better to suffer death once, he said, than to live always in fear of it. The affection of the people, he believed, was the best and surest guard a man could have; so he courted them again with public feasts and general distributions of grain, and to satisfy his army he sent out colonies to several places. The most remarkable were Carthage and Corinth — two cities that had been destroyed at the same time, and now, together, were rebuilt and repeopled.

As for the men of high rank, he promised some of them future consulships and praetorships, consoled others with different offices and honors, and gave everyone hope of his favor by the eagerness he showed to rule with the general good will. When the consul Maximus died with a single day of his term remaining, Caesar made Caninius Revilius consul for that one day. Crowds set off to pay the customary compliments and attentions to the new consul, and Cicero said, "Let us hurry — or the man will be out of office before we get there."

Caesar was born to do great things, and his passion was for honor. The many brilliant exploits behind him did not incline him to sit still and enjoy the fruit of his labors; they were fuel and provocation instead, kindling ideas of still greater actions and an appetite for fresh glory, as though the present supply were all used up. The feeling was really a kind of contest with himself, as if with a rival: a struggle to make his future outdo his past. His plan was to make war on the Parthians; once they were subdued, to pass through Hyrcania and march along the Caspian Sea to the Caucasus; then to swing around the Black Sea into Scythia, overrun the countries bordering Germany and then Germany itself, and return through Gaul to Italy — closing the circle of his intended empire and bounding it on every side by the ocean. While preparations for this expedition went forward, he proposed to cut a canal through the isthmus at Corinth, and appointed Anienus to superintend the work. He planned to divert the Tiber into a deep channel running straight from Rome to Circeii and into the sea near Tarracina, giving the merchants who traded to Rome a safe and easy passage. Beyond that, he intended to drain the marshes at Pomentium and Setia, reclaiming enough ground from the water to employ many thousands of men in farming; to raise great seawalls on the shore nearest Rome, to keep the sea from breaking in on the land; to clear the coast at Ostia of the hidden rocks and shoals that made it unsafe for shipping; and to build ports and harbors fit to receive the great number of vessels that would crowd into them.

All these designs stayed on paper. But his reform of the calendar, undertaken to fix the irregularity in the reckoning of time, was not only worked out with great scientific ingenuity but carried through to completion — and it proved enormously useful. From ancient times the Romans had lacked any reliable rule for keeping the cycle of their months in step with the course of the year, so their festivals and days of sacrifice drifted little by little until they were finally being kept in seasons opposite to the ones intended. Even in Caesar's day the people had no way of computing the solar year at all; only the priests could say what time it was, and they, at their own pleasure and without any notice, would slip in the extra month they called Mercedonius. Numa had been the first to insert that month, but his expedient was a poor patch, entirely inadequate to correct the errors that piled up in the annual cycles — as we have shown in his Life. Caesar called in the best philosophers and mathematicians of his time to settle the question, and out of the systems laid before him he built a new and more exact method of correcting the calendar — the one the Romans use to this day, and it serves them better than any other nation in avoiding the errors of the cycles. Yet even this gave offense to the men who looked on his position with an evil eye and felt the weight of his power. When someone in Cicero's company happened to remark that the constellation Lyra would rise the next morning, the orator answered, "Yes — by edict," as if even the stars now rose on compulsion.

But what brought down on him the most open and deadly hatred was his desire to be king. It gave the common people their first real grievance against him, and it handed the men who had long been his secret enemies their most plausible excuse. Those who wanted to procure him the title spread a story that, according to the Sibylline books, the Romans could conquer the Parthians only when they fought under the command of a king — and not before. And one day, as Caesar was coming down from Alba to Rome, some in the crowd were bold enough to hail him as king. Seeing that the people took it badly, he put on an air of being offended himself, and said his name was Caesar, not King. A general silence followed, and he passed on looking far from pleased. Another time, the senate voted him some extravagant honors, and the consuls and praetors came to deliver the message — the whole body of the senate attending them — while he sat on the rostra. He did not rise. He received them as though they were private citizens, and remarked that his honors wanted trimming back rather than adding to. This offended not only the senate but the common people too, who felt that an insult to the senate fell on the whole republic; and everyone who could decently leave walked away, deeply upset. Caesar, realizing the false step he had made, went straight home, bared his throat, and told his friends he was ready to offer it to anyone who wanted to strike. Later he made his illness the excuse for staying seated: those who suffer from it, he said, lose command of their senses if they talk long on their feet — they grow dizzy, fall into convulsions, and lose their reason entirely. But that was not the truth. He had been perfectly willing to rise for the senate, and was held back, the story goes, by Cornelius Balbus — a friend, or rather a flatterer — who said, "Will you not remember that you are Caesar, and claim the honor that is owed to your merit?"

Then came his affront to the tribunes, and fresh fuel for the resentment. The feast of the Lupercalia was being celebrated — a festival that at its founding belonged, some writers say, to the shepherds, with some connection to the Arcadian Lycaea. Many young noblemen and magistrates run through the city with their upper garments off, striking everyone they meet with strips of hide, all in sport; and many women, even of the highest rank, deliberately put themselves in the way and hold out their hands to the lash, the way schoolboys do to a master — believing that it grants an easy delivery to those who are pregnant, and children to those who are barren. Caesar, dressed in his triumphal robe, seated himself in a golden chair at the rostra to watch the ceremony. Antony, as consul, was one of the runners, and when he came into the forum the crowd parted for him. He went up to Caesar holding out a diadem — the band of kingship — wreathed with laurel. At this there was a shout, but a thin one, raised by the few men planted in the crowd for the purpose; and when Caesar pushed the diadem away, the whole forum applauded. Antony offered it a second time, and again only a few cheered; Caesar refused a second time, and again everyone applauded. The experiment had plainly failed. Caesar rose and ordered the crown carried up to the Capitol. But afterward his statues were found wearing royal diadems on their heads. Two tribunes of the people, Flavius and Marullus, went and tore the diadems off, then hunted down the men who had first hailed Caesar as king and marched them to prison. The people followed the two tribunes with cheers, calling them by the name of Brutus — because it was Brutus, long ago, who had ended the succession of kings and transferred the power once lodged in one man into the hands of the senate and the people. Caesar was stung. He stripped Marullus and Flavius of their office, and in pressing his charges against them he ridiculed the people into the bargain, calling the men over and over "Bruti" — brutes — and "Cumaeans," a byword for dullness.

All this talk turned people's thoughts toward Marcus Brutus. On his father's side he was believed to descend from the first Brutus, the man who had driven out the kings; on his mother's side he came from the Servilii, another noble house; and he was Cato's nephew and son-in-law besides. But the honors and kindnesses Caesar had heaped on him had dulled whatever appetite he might have had for overthrowing the new monarchy himself. Caesar had not only pardoned him after Pompey's defeat at Pharsalia, and pardoned many of his friends at his request — he trusted him, personally and particularly. Brutus held the most honorable praetorship of the year, and was marked out for the consulship four years later, chosen over Cassius, his rival for the post. When the choice was being weighed, Caesar is said to have remarked that Cassius had the stronger claim — but he could not pass over Brutus. Later, when the conspiracy was already in motion and people came to him with accusations against Brutus, he refused to listen. Laying a hand on his own body, he told the informers, "Brutus will wait for this skin of mine" — meaning that Brutus deserved power for his virtue, but would never be base and ungrateful to get it. Meanwhile, the people who wanted a change, and who looked on Brutus as the only man — or at least the likeliest man — to bring it about, did not dare speak to him directly. Instead they came at night and left notes on the tribunal chair where he sat to hear cases: "You are asleep, Brutus." "You are no longer Brutus." When Cassius saw Brutus's ambition beginning to stir at this, he pressed him harder than before. Cassius had a private grudge of his own against Caesar, for reasons we have mentioned in the Life of Brutus. And Caesar suspected him too. He once said to his friends, "What do you think Cassius is aiming at? I don't like him — he looks so pale." Another time, when he was told that Antony and Dolabella were plotting against him, he said it was not the fat, luxurious men he feared, but the pale, lean ones — meaning Cassius and Brutus.

Fate, it seems, is not so much unexpected as unavoidable. Strange signs and apparitions were reported in the days before the event. Lights in the sky, crashing noises in the night, wild birds settling in the forum — perhaps those are not worth much notice in a case as great as this. But Strabo the philosopher records that crowds of men were seen fighting one another, all of them seemingly ablaze; and that flame streamed from the hand of a soldier's servant, so that the onlookers were sure he was burning — yet when it passed, he was unhurt. When Caesar himself offered sacrifice, the victim's heart was missing — a very bad omen, since no living creature can exist without a heart. And many writers tell us that a soothsayer had given him warning of grave danger to come: "Beware the Ides of March." When the day arrived and Caesar was on his way to the senate, he met the soothsayer and greeted him with a joke: "The Ides of March have come." "Yes," the man answered calmly, "they have come — but they have not gone." The evening before the assassination, Caesar dined with Marcus Lepidus, and as he reclined at the table signing letters, as his habit was, the conversation turned to the question of which kind of death is best. Before anyone else could speak, Caesar answered, "A sudden one."

That night, as he lay in bed with his wife, every door and window in the house flew open at once. Startled by the noise and by the light that broke into the room, he sat up, and by the moonlight he could see Calpurnia fast asleep — but murmuring broken words and groaning in her dreams. She was dreaming, in fact, that she was weeping over Caesar, holding his butchered body in her arms. Others say her dream was different: that the ornamental gable the senate had voted to raise on Caesar's house as a mark of grandeur — Livy tells us this — was tumbling down, and that this was what made her weep and cry out. Either way, when morning came she begged Caesar not to go out if he could possibly avoid it, and to postpone the senate to another day. If he thought nothing of her dreams, she begged him at least to consult his fate by sacrifices and every other kind of divination. And Caesar himself, it seems, was not free of suspicion and fear. He had never before known Calpurnia to show any womanish superstition, and here she was in real terror. When the priests reported that they had killed several victims and found every one of them inauspicious, he made up his mind to send Antony to dismiss the senate.

At this critical moment Decimus Brutus, surnamed Albinus — a man Caesar trusted so completely that he had named him his second heir, and who was nevertheless in the conspiracy with the other Brutus and with Cassius — grew afraid that if Caesar put the senate off to another day, the plot might leak out. So he scoffed at the diviners and told Caesar he was handing the senate a perfect excuse to say he had insulted them. They had assembled at his summons; they were ready to vote unanimously that he be declared king of all the provinces outside Italy, free to wear a diadem anywhere by land or sea except in Italy itself. If someone were sent now to tell them to go home and come back whenever Calpurnia happened to have better dreams, what would his enemies say? Who would listen patiently while his friends tried to argue that his rule was not arbitrary and tyrannical? And if he was truly determined to treat the day as unlucky, then it was more gracious to go in person and adjourn the senate himself. With these words Brutus took Caesar by the hand and led him out. He had not gone far from the door when another man's servant tried to reach him, but could not get through the crowd pressing around him. So the servant forced his way into the house and put himself in Calpurnia's hands, begging her to keep him safe until Caesar came home — he had matters of great importance to tell him.

Artemidorus, a Cnidian who taught Greek logic, and who through that profession had come to know Brutus and his circle well enough to learn the secret, brought Caesar a small written scroll containing the essentials of what he had to reveal. He had noticed that Caesar handed every paper he received straight to the attendants around him; so he pushed in as close as he could and said, "Read this one, Caesar — alone, and quickly. It contains matters of great importance that concern you personally." Caesar took it and tried several times to read it, but was blocked at every attempt by the crush of people who came to speak with him. It was the one paper he kept in his own hand, and he was still holding it when he entered the senate. Some say it was another man who gave Caesar this note, and that Artemidorus never got near him at all — the crowd kept him off the whole way.

All of that, you might say, could have happened by chance. But the place appointed for the scene of the murder — the hall where the senate met that day — held a statue of Pompey, and was one of the buildings Pompey himself had raised and dedicated, along with his theater, for public use. It is hard not to see some higher power guiding the action and calling it to that particular spot. Cassius, just before the act, is said to have turned toward Pompey's statue and silently asked for its help — Cassius, who inclined to the doctrines of Epicurus, which leave no room for such prayers. But the occasion, and the danger of the instant, carried him past all his reasoning and filled him with something like inspiration. As for Antony, who was loyal to Caesar and a physically powerful man, Decimus Brutus Albinus kept him outside the hall, detaining him with a long conversation invented for the purpose.

When Caesar entered, the senate rose to its feet in respect. Some of Brutus's confederates gathered around his chair and stood behind it; others came forward to meet him, pretending to add their voices to the petition of Tillius Cimber on behalf of his exiled brother, and they trailed Caesar with their pleas all the way to his seat. He sat down and refused the request, and when they kept pressing, he began to scold them one by one for their persistence — at which Tillius seized his toga with both hands and yanked it down from his neck. That was the signal. Casca struck the first blow, a cut to the neck, neither deep nor dangerous — the stroke of a man badly shaken, no doubt, at the opening of so enormous a piece of daring. Caesar spun around, seized the dagger, and held on. In the same instant both men cried out — Caesar, in Latin, "Casca, you villain, what are you doing?" and Casca, in Greek, to his brother: "Brother, help!"

At that first assault, the senators who knew nothing of the plot were so stunned, so frozen with horror at what they were watching, that they could neither run nor come to Caesar's aid — they could not even make a sound. But the men who had come prepared closed around him on every side, naked daggers in their hands. Whichever way he turned he met a blow. The blades were leveled at his face and his eyes; he was hemmed in like a wild animal in the hunters' nets. For they had agreed that every one of them would strike him, every one would flesh his blade in the blood — which is why even Brutus gave him one thrust, in the groin. Some say Caesar fought all the rest of them, twisting his body away from the blows and shouting for help; but when he saw Brutus's sword drawn, he pulled his toga over his face and gave up the struggle. He fell — whether by chance, or pushed that way by his killers — against the pedestal of Pompey's statue, which was soaked with his blood. So Pompey himself seemed to preside over the vengeance taken on his old enemy, who now lay at his feet, breathing out his life through his wounds. He had received, they say, twenty-three. And many of the conspirators wounded one another, so many blows aimed at a single body.

When Caesar was dead, Brutus stepped forward to give the reasons for what they had done, but the senate would not hear him. The senators bolted for the doors, and their flight filled the city with such alarm and confusion that some men barred up their houses while others abandoned their counters and shops. Everyone was running — some toward the place, to see the terrible sight; others away from it, having seen. Antony and Lepidus, Caesar's most faithful friends, slipped off quietly and hid in the houses of friends. But Brutus and his followers, still hot from the deed itself, marched in a body from the senate house up to the Capitol with drawn swords — not like men trying to escape, but with an air of confidence and assurance — calling on the people as they went to take back their liberty, and inviting the distinguished men they met to join them. And some did join the procession and walk up with them, as if they too had been in the conspiracy and could claim a share of the glory — Gaius Octavius, for instance, and Lentulus Spinther. Their vanity cost them dearly afterward: Antony and the young Octavian put them to death, and they lost the honor they had claimed along with their lives, since no one ever believed they had actually been involved. Even the men who punished them were not avenging the deed — they were punishing the wish. The next day Brutus and the rest came down from the Capitol, and Brutus made a speech to the people, who listened without showing either pleasure or resentment. Their silence said plainly enough that they pitied Caesar and respected Brutus. The senate passed a general amnesty for what was past and set about reconciling all parties. It decreed that Caesar should be worshipped as a god, and that nothing he had enacted while in power, down to the smallest measure, should be revoked; at the same time it gave Brutus and his followers provinces and other important posts. So everyone now assumed that matters were settled, and settled as happily as they could possibly be.

But then Caesar's will was opened, and it was found that he had left a generous legacy to every single Roman citizen. And then his body was carried through the forum, mangled with wounds. After that the crowd could no longer be kept within any bounds of order. They heaped up benches, railings, and tables into a pyre, laid the corpse on it, and burned it where it stood. Then, snatching burning brands from the pile, some ran to set fire to the conspirators' houses, while others ranged up and down the city hunting for the men themselves, to tear them to pieces. They found not one of them; the conspirators had taken good care to secure themselves.

One of Caesar's friends, a man named Cinna, happened to have a strange dream the night before. He dreamed that Caesar invited him to dinner, and that when he declined, Caesar took him by the hand and pulled him along, forcing him though he hung back. Now, hearing that Caesar's body was burning in the forum, Cinna got up and went there to pay his respects, though the dream gave him misgivings and he was running a fever. Someone in the crowd saw him and asked a neighbor who he was; the name, once learned, was passed from mouth to mouth; and in a moment it was taken for certain that this was one of Caesar's murderers — for there was in fact a conspirator named Cinna, another man entirely. Believing this was he, the mob seized him and tore him limb from limb on the spot.

That, more than anything, frightened Brutus and Cassius, and within a few days they withdrew from the city. What they afterward did and suffered, and how they died, is told in the Life of Brutus. Caesar died in his fifty-sixth year, having outlived Pompey by little more than four. The empire and the power he had chased his whole life through so much danger he did at last achieve, with enormous difficulty — and reaped from it nothing but the name itself, and a glory that drew envy from his fellow citizens. But the great genius that had watched over him all his life remained, even after his death, as the avenger of his murder, pursuing the men who had a hand in it across every sea and every land, letting not one escape — reaching every man who had either joined in the killing or in any way encouraged the plot.

Of the merely human coincidences, the most remarkable was the one that befell Cassius: defeated at Philippi, he killed himself with the same dagger he had used against Caesar. Of the supernatural signs, the greatest were the great comet, which blazed bright for seven nights after Caesar's death and then disappeared, and the dimming of the sun, whose disk stayed pale and dull for that whole year, rising without its usual radiance and giving only a weak and feeble heat. The air hung damp and heavy for want of stronger rays to clear it, and the fruits of that year never properly ripened — they withered and dropped half-formed for lack of warmth. But above all, the phantom that appeared to Brutus showed that the murder was not pleasing to the gods. The story goes like this.

Brutus was about to take his army across from Abydos to the mainland on the other side. The night before, he lay in his tent as usual — not asleep, but thinking over his situation and what might be coming. Of all the men who have ever commanded armies, they say, he needed sleep the least, with the greatest natural capacity for staying awake and working without rest. He thought he heard a noise at the door of the tent, and looking that way by the light of his lamp, which was almost out, he saw a terrifying figure — shaped like a man, but unnaturally tall, with a grim and forbidding face. He was frightened at first; but when the thing neither did nor said anything, only stood silently at his bedside, he asked who it was. The specter answered, "I am your evil genius, Brutus. You will see me at Philippi." And Brutus answered steadily, "Then I will see you," and the apparition vanished at once. When the time came, he drew up his army at Philippi against Antony and Octavian, and in the first battle he won the day, routing the enemy in front of him and plundering Octavian's camp. The night before the second battle, the same phantom came to him again. It said nothing this time; but Brutus understood that his hour had arrived, and threw himself into the thickest danger of the fighting. Yet he did not die in the battle. When he saw his men beaten, he climbed to the top of a rock, set his naked sword against his breast — helped, they say, by a friend who drove the thrust home — and so met his death.

Cato the Younger

The Cato family first became famous through this Cato's great-grandfather — the elder Cato, whose virtue won him such standing and authority among the Romans that I have told his story in a life of its own.

Our Cato lost both his parents and was left an orphan, along with his brother Caepio and his sister Porcia. He also had a half-sister on his mother's side, Servilia. All of these children lived together and were raised in the house of Livius Drusus, their uncle on their mother's side, who at that time carried real weight in the government. He was a powerful speaker, a man of exceptional self-control, and in dignity second to no Roman alive.

They say that even as a small child, Cato showed — in the way he spoke, in the look on his face, even in his games — a nature that would not bend. Nothing swayed him; he was steady in everything. Once he set himself a purpose, he pushed through to the end with a determination far beyond his years. He was gruff and cold toward anyone who flattered him, and even more immovable toward anyone who threatened him. It was hard to make him laugh; his face rarely relaxed even into a smile. He was not quickly or easily provoked to anger — but once his anger was up, it was just as hard to bring it down again.

When he began his lessons, he proved a dull student, slow to take things in — but whatever he did take in, his memory held with a remarkable grip. And this, in fact, is generally how nature works. Quick, brilliant minds recall things readily, but those who learn with the most pain and difficulty remember best; everything new they learn is, so to speak, burned and branded into their minds. Cato's natural stubbornness, his resistance to being persuaded, probably also made him harder to teach. For to learn is to submit — to let something be done to you — and persuasion works fastest on those with the least strength to resist it. That is why the young are more easily persuaded than the old, and the sick more easily than the healthy. In short, wherever there is the least doubt and resistance standing in the way, a new impression settles in most easily. Still, they say Cato was very obedient to his tutor and would do whatever he was told — but he would also ask the reason, and want to know the why of everything. His teacher, to be fair, was a cultivated man, more inclined to explain than to beat his students. His name was Sarpedon.

While Cato was still a child, Rome's Italian allies were petitioning to be made full Roman citizens. One of their delegates, Pompaedius Silo, a brave soldier and a man of great reputation, was a friend of Drusus and stayed at his house for several days. In that time he became familiar with the children. "Well," he said to them, "will you ask your uncle to help us in our cause?" Caepio smiled and agreed, but Cato said nothing at all — he just stared at the strangers, hard and fierce. So Pompaedius pressed him: "And you, young sir — what do you say? Won't you plead with your uncle for us, like your brother?" Cato still gave no answer, and his silence and his expression seemed to refuse the whole petition. So Pompaedius snatched him up and held him out the window, as if he meant to drop him, and told him to consent or down he would go. He put on a harsher voice, dangled the boy's body out over the drop, and shook him — more than once. Cato endured all of it, unmoved and unafraid, until at last Pompaedius set him down and murmured to his friend, "What a blessing for Italy that he is only a child. If he were a man, I don't believe we would win a single vote among the people."

Another time, a relative celebrating his birthday invited Cato and some other children to dinner. The children went off to play in another part of the house, older and younger together, and their game was to act out trials before the judges — accusing one another and hauling the convicted off to prison. In the middle of this, a very beautiful young boy was bound and dragged off to "prison" by an older one, and he cried out to Cato. The moment Cato saw what was happening, he ran to the door, shoved aside the boys standing guard, pulled the child out, and marched home in a fury, with several of his companions trailing after him.

In time Cato grew so admired among the young that when Sulla decided to stage the sacred equestrian game called Troy — a ceremonial cavalry exercise for well-born boys — he gathered the youth of good families and appointed two leaders over them. One of the two the boys accepted for his mother's sake, since he was the son of Metella, Sulla's wife. But the other — Sextus, Pompey's nephew — they flatly refused. They would not follow him or drill under him. When Sulla asked whom they wanted instead, they all shouted, "Cato!" — and Sextus willingly gave up the honor to him, as the worthier of the two.

Sulla, an old friend of their family, would send for Cato and his brother from time to time, to see them and talk with them — a favor he showed to very few once he had seized his great power. Sarpedon, alert to the advantage of this — for his students' honor and for their safety — often brought Cato to pay his respects at Sulla's house. And that house, in those days, crowded with people being dragged in and tortured, looked like nothing so much as a place of execution. Cato was then in his fourteenth year. Watching the heads of reportedly distinguished men being carried in, and noticing the stifled groans of those standing by, he asked his tutor, "Why does nobody kill this man?" "Because, child," said Sarpedon, "they fear him even more than they hate him." "Then why," Cato answered, "didn't you give me a sword, so that I could stab him and free my country from this slavery?" When Sarpedon heard that — and saw the boy's face swelling with anger and resolve — he watched him closely from then on, afraid he might attempt something desperate.

While Cato was still very young, some people asked him whom he loved best. "My brother," he answered. And whom next? "My brother," again. And the third time the same, until they gave up asking. As he grew older, this love for his brother only grew stronger. By the time he was about twenty, he never ate supper, never left town, never went into the forum without Caepio. But when his brother took to using expensive oils and perfumes, Cato refused them; in all his habits he was strict and austere. So when Caepio was praised for his moderation and self-restraint, he would admit that yes, he might count as moderate — compared with most men. "But," he said, "when I compare myself with Cato, I find I'm hardly any different from Sippius" — naming a man notorious at the time for his soft and luxurious living.

When Cato was made a priest of Apollo, he moved to a house of his own, took his share of the family inheritance — a hundred and twenty talents — and began to live even more simply than before. He became close to Antipater of Tyre, the Stoic philosopher, and devoted himself above all to the study of ethics and politics. He pursued every virtue as if driven by a kind of inspiration, but the one that seized his devotion most of all was justice — the rigid, unbending kind, the kind that cannot be worked on by favors or by pity. He also trained himself in the art of public speaking and debate, reasoning that political philosophy, like a great city, ought to keep a fighting force on hand for its defense. But he would never practice his exercises in front of company, and no one ever heard him declaim. When someone told him that people blamed him for his silence, he replied, "As long as they don't blame my life. I will begin to speak when I have something to say that is better said than left unsaid."

The great Porcian Hall, as it was called, had been built and dedicated to public use by the old Cato during his term as aedile. The tribunes of the people conducted their business there, and because one of the pillars seemed to be in the way of their seats, they debated whether to move it elsewhere or remove it altogether. This was the occasion that first drew Cato — very much against his will — into the forum. He opposed the tribunes' demand, and in doing so gave a first display of both his courage and his power as a speaker, which won him great admiration. There was nothing youthful or polished about his speech; it was direct, packed with substance, and rough. And yet there was a certain grace in his rough statements that held people's attention, and the speaker's character, showing through everything he said, gave his severe language something that people found naturally pleasing and interesting. His voice was full and carrying, strong enough to reach that whole crowd, and its stamina was simply inexhaustible — he could often speak an entire day and never stop.

Once he had won this case, he went back to his studies and his quiet life. He hardened his body with labor and strenuous exercise. He trained himself to go bareheaded in the hottest weather and the coldest, and to travel on foot in every season. When he journeyed with friends who rode on horseback while he walked, he would drop back and move up along the line, joining first one and then another to talk with them on the road. In sickness, his patience in enduring an illness — and the strict self-denial he used to cure it — were remarkable. When he had a fever, he would stay alone and let no one see him until he began to recover and knew the attack had passed. At supper, when the dice were thrown for choice of portions and he lost, and the company offered him his pick anyway, he declined — he would not, as he put it, argue with the ruling of Venus. At first he was in the habit of drinking a single cup after supper and then leaving; but over time he came to drink more, so much so that he would often sit over the wine until morning. His friends explained this by saying that public business and affairs of state took up his whole day, and that Cato, hungry for knowledge, liked to spend his nights over wine in the company of philosophers. So when a certain Memmius remarked in public that Cato spent whole nights drinking, Cicero shot back, "You should add that he spends whole days gambling."

Cato, in general, considered the customs and manners of his era so corrupt, and so badly in need of reform, that he thought it necessary to go against the ordinary way of the world in many things — deliberately. Seeing that the lightest, brightest purple was then the height of fashion, he always wore the shade nearest to black. He would often go out after his morning meal with no shoes and no tunic. He was not fishing for attention with these oddities. He was training himself to be ashamed only of what truly deserves shame, and to shrug off every other kind of disgrace.

When the estate of a cousin — another Cato — fell to him, worth a hundred talents, he turned all of it into ready cash and kept it on hand for any friend who found himself in need, lending it out without interest. For some of them, he even allowed his own land and slaves to be pledged to the public treasury as security.

When he judged himself old enough to marry — having never yet been with any woman — he was engaged to Lepida. She had previously been engaged to Metellus Scipio, but Scipio had backed out, the contract had been dissolved, and she was free. Yet afterward Scipio changed his mind, did everything he could to win her back before Cato's marriage could take place — and succeeded. Cato was furious. At first he resolved to take the matter to court, but his friends talked him out of it. Still, so hot was his young blood that he wrote a stream of iambic verses against Scipio, in the biting, sarcastic style of Archilochus — though without that poet's license and vulgarity. After this he married Atilia, the daughter of Soranus. She was the first woman he ever knew — the first, but not the only one. In that he was less fortunate than Laelius, the friend of Scipio Africanus, who through the whole course of a very long life knew only the one woman he wed in his first and only marriage.

In the slave war — the one named for Spartacus, its ringleader — Gellius held the command, and Cato went along as a volunteer for the sake of his brother Caepio, who was serving as a tribune in the army. The general's incompetence gave Cato no real opening to show his zeal or prove his courage. But amid all the corruption and disorder of that army, he showed such devotion to discipline, such bravery when the moment called for it, and such steady good judgment in everything, that it seemed clear he was in no way inferior to the old Cato. Gellius offered him substantial rewards and would have voted him the first honors; Cato refused them all, saying he had done nothing to deserve them. Which made people think him a strange, eccentric character.

There was, moreover, a law that candidates standing for office could not use prompters in their campaigns — hired men who whispered them the names of the citizens they greeted. When Cato ran for military tribune, he was the only candidate who obeyed it. He took great pains to learn, from his own memory, how to greet the voters he had to address and call each of them by name. Yet even the people who praised him for this could not do it without a touch of envy and resentment — for the more they reflected on how fine the thing was, the more it stung them how hard it would be to do the same.

Elected military tribune, he was sent to Macedon to join Rubrius, the general there. The story goes that his wife was distressed and in tears at his leaving, and Munatius, one of Cato's friends, said to her, "Don't worry, Atilia — I promise to watch over him for you." "By all means," said Cato. And after they had traveled one day's journey together, he turned to Munatius as soon as they had finished supper. "Now," he said, "so that you're sure to keep your promise to Atilia, you must not leave me day or night." From then on he ordered two beds made up in his own chamber so that Munatius would sleep there — and so it continued, Cato making a running joke of it: it was he who kept watch over Munatius. He traveled with fifteen slaves, two freedmen, and four friends. They rode on horseback; Cato always went on foot — yet he kept pace beside them, talking with each in turn as they went.

When he reached the army, which consisted of several legions, the general gave him command of one. Cato considered it a small thing, hardly worthy of a commander, to put his own personal bravery on display; what he resolved to do was make his soldiers, as far as he could, like himself. He did not soften the fear his office carried, but he joined reason to his authority. He persuaded and instructed every man individually, and handed out rewards and punishments strictly by what each deserved. In the end his men were so well disciplined that it was hard to say whether they were more peaceable or more warlike, more brave or more just. They were formidable to their enemies and courteous to their allies, afraid to do wrong and eager to earn honor. And Cato himself won, in the fullest measure, exactly what he had least set out to seek — glory and a great name. He was highly esteemed by everyone and completely beloved by his soldiers. Whatever he ordered done, he took part in doing himself. In his dress, his food, and his manner of traveling, he was more like a common soldier than an officer — but in character, in high purpose, and in wisdom, he towered over everyone who carried the names and titles of commander. And so, without knowing it, he made himself the object of universal affection. For a true love of virtue grows, in all of us, out of love and respect for the person who teaches it. People who praise good men without loving them may honor their reputation, but they do not truly admire their virtue — and they will never imitate it.

There lived at that time in Pergamus a man named Athenodorus, surnamed Cordylio, of high repute for his mastery of Stoic philosophy. He was now old, and he had always firmly refused the friendship and acquaintance of princes and great men. Cato understood this. Figuring he would never win the man over by messages or letters, and being allowed by law two months' leave from the army, he resolved to sail to Asia and see him in person, trusting that his own qualities would keep the trip from being wasted. He talked with Athenodorus, succeeded in arguing him out of his old resolutions, and brought him back to camp — as delighted and as proud of this conquest as if he had pulled off some heroic feat greater than anything done by Pompey or Lucullus, who at that very moment were subduing whole nations and kingdoms with their armies.

While Cato was still in the service, his brother, traveling toward Asia, fell sick at Aenus in Thrace. Letters with the news were sent to Cato at once. The sea was running very rough and no decent-sized ship could be found, so Cato boarded a small trading vessel with only two friends and three servants and set sail from Thessalonica. He very nearly drowned on the crossing — and arrived at Aenus just as Caepio died. On this occasion, people felt, he showed himself more a devoted brother than a philosopher: not only in the extravagance of his grief, weeping and embracing the dead body, but in the enormous cost of the funeral — the huge quantity of rich perfumes and costly garments burned with the corpse, and the monument of Thasian marble he raised, at a cost of eight talents, in the public square of Aenus. Some people made a point of carping at all this as inconsistent with his usual calm restraint. What they failed to see was that for all his steel — firm and unbending against pleasure, fear, and shameless begging — Cato was full of natural tenderness and brotherly affection. Various cities and princes of the region sent him many gifts to honor his brother's funeral. He took none of their money; he accepted only the perfumes and ornaments, and paid for those. And afterward, when the inheritance was divided between him and Caepio's daughter, he did not ask for any share of the funeral expenses to be paid out of it. Despite all this, it has been claimed that he had his brother's ashes passed through a sieve, to sift out the gold that had melted in the fire with the body. But the man who wrote that, it seems, assumed that his pen, like his sword, stood above all question and criticism.

When Cato's term of service ended, he left the army carrying not just the prayers and praises of the soldiers, but their tears and embraces. They spread their cloaks under his feet and kissed his hand as he passed — an honor the Romans of that day rarely paid even to their generals and commanders-in-chief. Having left the army, he resolved that before he went home and threw himself into public life, he would travel through Asia and study the customs, the character, and the strength of every province. He was also reluctant to refuse the kindness of Deiotarus, king of Galatia, who had been a close friend of his father's and was very eager for a visit. Cato's travel arrangements ran like this. Early in the morning he would send his baker and his cook ahead to the town where he planned to spend the next night. They would enter quietly and without fuss, and if Cato had no friend or family acquaintance in the place, they arranged for him at an inn and bothered no one. If there was no inn — then, and only then — they went to the magistrates, asked for help finding lodgings, and accepted without complaint whatever they were given. Because his servants approached the magistrates without bluster or threats, they were often not believed, or simply ignored, so that Cato frequently arrived to find nothing prepared for him. And it was worse when he appeared in person; he counted for even less. Seeing him sit down on his baggage without a word, the officials wrote him off at once as a nobody who wouldn't dare demand anything. On such occasions he would sometimes call them over and say, "You foolish people — drop this inhospitality. Not all your visitors will be Catos. Use courtesy to blunt the sharp edge of power. There are plenty of men who want nothing but an excuse to take from you by force what you give so grudgingly."

While traveling this way he had a comic misadventure in Syria. As he approached Antioch, he saw a great crowd outside the gates, lined up in order on both sides of the road — young men in long cloaks here, children neatly dressed there, and others in garlands and white robes, the priests and the magistrates. Cato, assuming all this could only be a ceremony of welcome in his honor, grew angry with the servants he had sent ahead for allowing it. He made his friends dismount, and walked in with them on foot. But as soon as he neared the gate, an elderly man who seemed to be the master of ceremonies, wand and garland in hand, came up to Cato and — without so much as a greeting — asked where he had left Demetrius, and how soon he would arrive. Demetrius was Pompey's servant; and since at that moment the whole world, so to speak, had its eyes fixed on Pompey, even this man was treated with high honor because of his influence with his master. Cato's friends collapsed into such laughter that they couldn't compose themselves as they walked through the crowd. Cato himself, ashamed and mortified, said only, "Unfortunate city!" — and not another word. Afterward, though, it always made him laugh, whenever he told the story or was reminded of it.

Pompey himself soon made the people of Antioch ashamed of their ignorance and foolishness in slighting Cato that way. For when Cato's journey brought him to Ephesus, he went to pay his respects to Pompey — the older man, loaded with honors, and then the general of a great army. Yet Pompey would not receive him sitting down. The moment he saw him he rose, went to meet him as the more distinguished of the two, gave him his hand, and embraced him with a great show of warmth. He praised Cato's virtue lavishly to his face at the reception, and even more lavishly after he had gone. And so at once everyone began to show Cato open respect — discovering, in the very habits they had despised before, an admirable mildness of temper and greatness of spirit. It was clear enough, though, that Pompey's own courtesy came from a man who respected Cato rather than loved him. The general impression was that Pompey admired him while he was present and was not sorry when he left. When other young men came to visit, Pompey usually pressed and begged them to stay on with him. Cato he did not invite to stay at all — as if his own power shrank in the man's presence, he was quite willing to let him go. And yet, of all those departing for Rome, Cato was the only one to whom Pompey entrusted his children and his wife — who was, it is true, a relation of Cato's.

After that, every city he passed through on the way home competed with its neighbors in showing him respect and honor. There were feasts and receptions at every stop — so many that Cato asked his friends to keep strict watch over him, in case he ended up proving Curio right. Curio was a friend of his, but he disliked Cato's austere temper, and he had once asked him whether he planned to see Asia when he left the army. "Yes, by all means," Cato answered. "Good," said Curio. "You'll come back with a better temper and pleasanter manners." Those were pretty nearly his exact words.

Deiotarus, the Galatian king, was an old man by now, and he had sent for Cato to place his children and his household under Cato's protection. The moment Cato arrived, the king began pressing gifts of every sort on him, begging and pleading with him to accept. All that pleading annoyed Cato so much that although he had come in the evening, he stayed only that one night and left early the next morning. After a single day's travel he reached Pessinus — and found an even bigger store of presents waiting for him there, along with letters from Deiotarus entreating him to take them, or at least to let his friends take them, since they deserved some reward for his sake and Cato could hardly provide for them out of his own means. Cato refused even that, though he could see that some of his friends were quite willing to accept and were ready to grumble at his severity. Corruption, he answered, would never lack an excuse; and his friends would share with him in whatever he obtained justly and honestly — nothing more. He sent the presents back to Deiotarus.

When he was about to take ship for Brundisium, his friends urged him to put his brother's ashes aboard a different vessel. He said he would sooner part with his life than with those ashes, and set sail. And as it happened, we are told, his crossing was a genuinely dangerous one, while other ships making the passage at the same time came through safely enough.

Back in Rome, he spent his time for the most part either at home, in conversation with Athenodorus, or in the forum, in the service of his friends. It was now time for him to stand for the quaestorship — but he refused to run for the office until he had studied the laws governing it, questioned men of experience, and gained a precise understanding of its duties and its authority. Armed with that knowledge, he began a sweeping reform of the treasury's clerks and under-officers the moment he took office. These were men with long practice and total familiarity with the public records and the laws; and since the new magistrates who came in year after year were so ignorant and unskilled that they positively needed someone to teach them their own jobs, the clerks never submitted to them or gave way. They kept the real power in their own hands. They were, in effect, the treasurers themselves. Until Cato. He threw himself squarely into the work and showed that he possessed not just the title and honor of a quaestor but the knowledge, the understanding, and the full authority of the office. He treated the clerks and under-officers as what they were — servants — exposing their corrupt practices and correcting their ignorance. They were bold, shameless men, and they flattered the other quaestors, Cato's colleagues, and tried through them to keep up an opposition against him. But Cato convicted the chief of them of a breach of trust in the handling of an inheritance and threw him out of his place. He brought a second to trial for dishonesty — a man defended by Lutatius Catulus, the censor at that time, a man of great weight because of his office and of greater weight because of his character, for he stood above all the Romans of his age in his reputation for wisdom and integrity. Catulus was also close to Cato and admired his way of living. Seeing that he could not get his client off in a fair trial, he began openly begging him off. Cato objected to his doing it. Catulus kept pleading. At last Cato said, "It would be shameful, Catulus, if the censor — the judge of all our lives — had to be removed from court by our officers." Catulus looked as if he wanted to make some answer; but he said nothing, and — whether from anger or from shame — walked away silent and out of countenance. Even so, the man was not convicted: the votes to acquit fell just one short of the votes to condemn, and Marcus Lollius, one of Cato's colleagues, who was absent sick, was sent for by Catulus and begged to come and save the man. So Lollius was carried into court in a chair and cast his vote for acquittal. Cato, for his part, never employed that clerk again, never paid him his salary, and refused to count Lollius' vote for anything.

Having humbled the clerks and brought them to heel, he managed the books and registers as he saw fit, and within a short time he had given the treasury a higher name than the Senate house itself. Everyone said that Cato had made the office of quaestor equal to the dignity of a consul. When he found that many people owed the state money on old accounts, and that the state likewise owed money to many private citizens, he made sure the public would no longer either commit wrongs or suffer them: he exacted what was due to the treasury strictly and punctually, and paid the state's creditors promptly and in full. People watched with awe and respect as men who had expected to slip away with their plunder were made to pay, and men who had despaired of ever seeing their money received every bit of it. And whereas those who showed up with false claims and pretended senate orders had usually managed, through favoritism, to get them accepted, Cato was never once taken in. In one particular case, when a question arose over whether an order had actually passed the senate, he refused to believe the many witnesses who attested to it, and would not admit it until the consuls came and swore to it on oath.

There were at that time a great many men Sulla had used as his agents in the proscriptions — men he had paid twelve thousand drachmas apiece for their service in putting the condemned to death. Everybody hated them as wicked, polluted wretches, but nobody dared take revenge on them. Cato called every one of them to account as wrongful holders of public money, exacted repayment, and in the same breath denounced them for their lawless and unholy actions. Immediately after this they found themselves charged with murder — already, in a sense, prejudged as guilty — and they were easily convicted, and paid the penalty. The whole people rejoiced at it, feeling that the old tyranny had finally been abolished and that Sulla himself, so to speak, had at last been brought to punishment.

His sheer diligence won the people over too — the man never stopped. He was always the first of his colleagues to arrive at the treasury and the last to leave. He never missed an assembly of the people or a sitting of the senate, always anxious, always on the watch for those who would casually, or as a favor to someone, vote to remit a debt or grant away customs owed to the state. And in the end, having kept the exchequer clean of cheats and informers while filling it with treasure, he showed that the state could be rich without oppressing the people. Some of his colleagues found him irritating at first, but after a while they were well contented with him, since he was perfectly willing to take all the blame on himself when they declined to hand out public money to their friends or to pass dishonest accounts. When petitioners pressed them hard, they could simply answer that nothing could be done unless Cato consented. On the last day of his term, nearly the whole people escorted him home in honor — but on the way he learned that several powerful men were at the treasury with Marcellus at that very moment, using all their influence to get him to record a certain debt to the public revenue as if it were a gift. Marcellus had been one of Cato's friends since childhood, and so long as Cato was at his side he was one of the best colleagues in the office; but on his own he could not resist the pressure of petitioners, and was prone to do anybody a kindness. Cato turned straight around, and finding that Marcellus had given in and passed the entry, he took the book and — while Marcellus stood silently by, watching — struck it out. That done, he led Marcellus out of the treasury and took him home with him. And Marcellus never complained of it, not then and not afterward, but remained his friend and intimate ever after.

Even after he laid down the office, Cato did not stop watching the treasury. He kept servants who continually copied out the details of public expenditure, and he always had by him a set of account books containing the state revenues from Sulla's time down to his own quaestorship — books he had bought for five talents.

He was always the first man into the senate and the last one out; and often, while the others were slowly gathering, he would sit and read quietly by himself, holding his gown up in front of his book. He was never once out of town when the senate was due to meet. Later, when Pompey and his party found that Cato could be neither persuaded nor pressured into favoring their unjust designs, they tried to keep him away from the senate by tangling him up in business for his friends — pleading their cases, arbitrating their disputes, and so on. Cato saw through the trick quickly, and to defeat it he told all his acquaintances flatly that he would never touch private business while the senate was in session. For it was not in hope of honor or riches, nor on impulse, nor by chance that he had gone into politics; he had taken up the service of the state as the proper business of an honest man, and he considered himself as bound to his public duty as a bee to its honeycomb. To that end he arranged to have friends and correspondents everywhere, sending him reports of the edicts, decrees, judgments, and all the important proceedings in the provinces. Once, when Clodius, the seditious agitator, was pushing his violent, revolutionary projects by slandering some of the priests and priestesses before the people — Fabia, sister of Cicero's wife Terentia, stood in real danger — Cato stepped in boldly, made Clodius look so disgraceful that he was forced to leave town, and, when it was over, was approached by Cicero, who came to thank him for what he had done. "You must thank the commonwealth," Cato said — for the commonwealth's sake, he insisted, was the only reason he did anything. Out of all this grew a great and remarkable reputation. An advocate in one case, facing a single witness on the other side, told the judges they ought not to rely on one witness even if it were Cato himself. And it became a kind of proverb with many people, when some wildly unlikely claim was asserted, to say they would not believe it even if Cato himself affirmed it. One day, while a debauched and extravagant liver was lecturing the senate on frugality and temperance, Amnaeus jumped up and cried, "Who can stand this, sir — you feast like Crassus, you build like Lucullus, and you talk like Cato!" And in the same spirit, men whose lives were vicious and dissolute but whose language was grave and severe were mockingly called Catos.

At first, when his friends urged him to stand for tribune of the people, he thought it a bad idea: the power of so great an office, he felt, should be held in reserve, like the strongest medicines, for occasions of last necessity. But later, during a court vacation, as he was traveling with his books and his philosophers to Lucania, where he had lands and a pleasant house, they met on the road a long train of horses, carriages, and attendants — belonging, they learned, to Metellus Nepos, who was heading to Rome to stand for the tribunate.
