Cato stopped. He was silent for a moment; then he gave orders to turn back at once. His companions looked amazed, and he said, "Don't you know how dangerous the madness of Metellus is all by itself? Now he comes armed with Pompey's backing, and he will fall on the state like lightning and throw everything into disorder. This is no time for holidays and leisure. We must get ahead of this man and stop his designs — or die honorably in defense of our liberty." At his friends' urging he did go on first to his country house, but he stayed only a very short time before returning to town.

He arrived in the evening, and first thing the next morning he went down to the forum and began campaigning for the tribuneship, in opposition to Metellus. The power of that office lies more in blocking business than in doing it: even if every other tribune agreed to a measure, the refusal of a single one could stop the whole thing cold. At first Cato had few supporters; but as soon as his intention became known, all the good and distinguished men of the city quickly came forward to encourage and support him. They saw him not as a man asking a favor of them, but as a man offering a great favor to his country and to every honest citizen — a man who had refused this same office many times when he could have had it without trouble, and who now sought it at real risk so that he might defend their liberty and their government. It is reported that so great a crowd pressed around him that he was nearly crushed, and could barely make his way through. He was declared tribune, along with several others — Metellus among them.

Once elected, Cato saw that the election of consuls had become a matter of open purchase, and he rebuked the people sharply for the corruption, ending his speech with a vow to prosecute whomever he found handing out money — with one exception, Silanus, on account of their close connection: Silanus was married to Servilia, Cato's sister. So he let Silanus alone and instead accused Lucius Murena, who had won the consulship by bribery alongside him. There was a law allowing the accused to appoint a man to keep watch on his accuser, so that he could know fairly how the prosecution was being prepared. The watcher Murena set on Cato followed him closely at first, observing everything — and never once caught him doing anything unfair or underhanded. Cato went about the accusation generously and candidly, by the just and open methods. The man came to admire his great spirit so much, and to trust his integrity so completely, that he would simply walk up to Cato in the forum, or call at his house, and ask whether he planned to do anything on the case that day; and if Cato said no, he went away satisfied, relying on his word. When the case was pleaded, Cicero — consul that year, and defending Murena — spent a good deal of the trial being witty at Cato's expense, poking fun at the Stoic philosophers and their so-called paradoxes until the judges were roaring with laughter. Cato smiled and remarked to the bystanders, "What a pleasant consul we have, my friends." Murena was acquitted — and afterward showed himself neither resentful nor a fool, for as consul he took Cato's advice in the weightiest affairs, and treated him with honor and respect throughout his entire term. The credit for that belongs to Murena's good sense, but also to Cato's own behavior: terrible and severe as he was in matters of justice, in the senate and at the bar, once the fight was over his manner toward everyone was perfectly friendly and humane.

Before he entered on the tribunate, he supported Cicero, then consul, in many of the struggles of his office — above all in his great and noble handling of Catiline's conspiracy, which owed its final successful outcome to Cato. Catiline had plotted the total overthrow of the Roman state by sedition and open war, but Cicero exposed and convicted him, and he was forced to flee the city. Yet Lentulus and Cethegus remained behind, with several others, to carry the plot forward; and blaming Catiline for cowardice and timid, small-scale thinking, they resolved to set the whole town on fire, overthrow the empire outright, rouse whole nations to revolt, and stir up foreign wars. But Cicero discovered the design — as I have written in his life — and laid the matter before the senate. Silanus, speaking first, gave his opinion that the conspirators should suffer the ultimate punishment, and every speaker after him agreed — until it came to Caesar. Caesar was an excellent speaker, and he regarded every upheaval and commotion in the state as raw material for his own purposes — better fed than put out. He rose and delivered a very merciful, very persuasive speech: the men ought not to be put to death without fair trial according to law, and he moved that they be held instead under guard in whatever Italian towns Cicero himself might choose. The house swung almost entirely to Caesar's side, dreading also the anger of the people; even Silanus backtracked and explained that he had never meant death, only imprisonment — since that was the utmost a Roman could suffer.

The whole senate was tipping toward the milder, more merciful opinion when Cato stood up. He tore at once, with passion and vehemence, into Silanus for changing his position — and then he turned and pointed the finger of suspicion at Caesar himself. Here was a man, he said, working to ruin the commonwealth with soft words and crowd-pleasing speeches; a man trying to frighten the senate when he himself ought to be afraid, and grateful if he walked out unpunished and unsuspected, since he so openly and boldly dared to protect the enemies of the state. For his own native country, glorious as it was and brought to the very edge of destruction, this man could find no compassion; yet he could be full of pity for men who should never have been born, and whose deaths would deliver the commonwealth from bloodshed and ruin. Of all Cato's speeches, it is said, this is the only one preserved — because Cicero, as consul, had stationed around the senate house a number of exceptionally quick writers whom he had taught to make small strokes standing for many words. Up to that time Rome had no shorthand writers, as we call them; this occasion, it is said, was the first real example of the art. Cato carried the day, and turned the house around once more, and it was decreed that the conspirators should be put to death.

I will not leave out the small things that show Cato's temper and add a stroke or two to the portrait of his mind. At the very height of this duel, it is reported, with the whole senate watching the two of them, a little note was brought in and handed to Caesar. Cato declared it suspicious — some piece of sedition in motion, he said — and demanded the letter be read aloud. Caesar handed the paper straight across to Cato. And Cato found himself holding a love letter from his own sister Servilia to Caesar, who had seduced her. He flung it back. "Keep it, you drunkard," he said — and went on with his speech. Indeed, Cato seems to have had poor luck in the women of his family. This sister got an ugly name for her intimacy with Caesar; and the other Servilia, also Cato's sister, conducted herself worse still: married to Lucullus, one of the greatest men in Rome, and having borne him a son, she was eventually divorced for infidelity. Worst of all, Cato's own wife Atilia was not free from the same fault; after she had given him two children, he was forced to divorce her for her misconduct. He then married Marcia, the daughter of Philippus, a woman of good reputation — and yet a woman about whom there has been endless talk; for the life of Cato, like a stage play, contains this one scene full of perplexity and doubtful meaning.

Here is how Thrasea tells it, on the authority of Munatius, Cato's friend and constant companion. Among the many who loved and admired Cato, some stood out more than others. One was Quintus Hortensius, a man of high reputation and proven character, who wanted not merely Cato's friendship and company but some tie of marriage that would unite their two houses into one. So he set about persuading Cato that his daughter Portia — already married to Bibulus, and the mother of his two children — might nevertheless be given over to him, like a fine plot of land, to bear fruit for him as well. "In the opinion of the world," he argued, "this may seem strange; but in nature it is honorable, and good for the public, that a woman in the prime of her youth should not sit idle and let the fruit of her womb go to waste — nor, on the other side, wear down and impoverish one man by bearing him more children than he needs. If worthy men shared their families in this way, virtue would increase and spread through their bloodlines, and the commonwealth would be knit and cemented together by their alliances." And if Bibulus was altogether unwilling to give up his wife, Hortensius would return her as soon as she had borne him a child, so that he would then be bound to both families. Cato answered that he loved Hortensius well and thoroughly approved of uniting their houses — but it was a strange thing to talk of marrying a daughter who was already given to another man. At that, Hortensius simply shifted ground, and did not hesitate to speak openly and ask for Cato's own wife; for Marcia was still young and fertile, and Cato had children enough. And it cannot be supposed that Hortensius did this because he imagined Cato indifferent to Marcia — she was, we are told, pregnant at the time. Cato, seeing how much in earnest he was, did not refuse; but he said that Philippus, Marcia's father, would have to be consulted as well. So Philippus was sent for and came; and finding the two men agreed, he gave his daughter Marcia to Hortensius in Cato's presence — with Cato himself assisting at the wedding. This happened at a later time; but since I was on the subject of the women, I thought it best to tell it now.

Lentulus and the rest of the conspirators were put to death. But Caesar, finding so much insinuated and charged against him in the senate, turned to the people, and began stirring up the most corrupt and rotten elements of the state to form a party behind him. Cato, alarmed at what might follow, persuaded the senate to win over the poor, unprovided-for multitude with a distribution of grain — an act of humanity and kindness that cost the state twelve hundred and fifty talents a year, and that unquestionably dissipated the immediate danger. But then Metellus, entering on his tribunate, began holding riotous assemblies, and had prepared a decree that Pompey the Great should be summoned into Italy at once, with all his forces, to preserve the city from the danger of Catiline's conspiracy. That was the fair-sounding pretext. The real design was to hand everything over to Pompey and give him absolute power. The senate was assembled, and Cato — who so often went straight at Metellus — did not do it this time. He urged his advice in the most reasonable and moderate tone he could manage; at last he descended even to entreaty, praising the house of Metellus for having always stood with the nobility. This only made Metellus more insolent. Concluding that Cato was yielding out of fear, he let himself run on into the most audacious threats, openly declaring that he would do whatever he pleased in spite of the senate. At that, Cato's expression changed, and his voice, and his language; and after many sharp exchanges he ended with the bold declaration that while he lived, Pompey would never enter the city armed. The senate, watching the two of them, concluded that neither man was entirely in his right and sober senses. Metellus' course seemed sheer rage and frenzy — an excess of malice dragging everything toward ruin and confusion — while Cato's virtue looked like a kind of ecstasy of contention in the cause of everything good and just.

When the day came for the people to vote on the decree, Metellus got to the forum first and packed it with armed men — foreigners, gladiators, slaves. A good part of the crowd was for Pompey too, hoping a change of regime would pay off for them, and there was powerful help besides from Caesar, who was praetor that year. The best and most distinguished men of the city were every bit as disgusted by all this as Cato was, but they looked more likely to suffer alongside him than to be of any use to him. Meanwhile Cato's whole household was in an agony of fear for him. Some of his friends never ate or slept that night; they passed the hours arguing in circles and getting nowhere. His wife and sisters wept and pleaded. He himself, without a trace of fear, was the one comforting everyone else, steadying them with his talk. After supper he went to bed at his usual hour, and the next morning had to be shaken out of a deep sleep by Minucius Thermus, one of his fellow tribunes. The two of them walked down to the forum together, accompanied by only a handful of people but met by a great many more, who kept urging them to be careful. When Cato saw the temple of Castor and Pollux ringed with armed men, the steps held by gladiators, and Metellus and Caesar seated together at the top, he turned to his friends. "Look at this bold coward," he said, "who has recruited a regiment of soldiers against one unarmed man." And he walked on with Thermus. The men holding the approaches let those two through and no one else — though Cato caught Munatius by the hand and with a good deal of trouble hauled him along too. Then he went straight up to Metellus and Caesar and sat down between them, cutting off their conversation, which left them both staring in confusion. The honest citizens, watching Cato's face and marveling at his spirit and audacity, pressed closer and shouted to him to have courage, and called out to one another to stand together and not betray their liberty, or the man defending it.

Then the clerk produced the bill, and Cato forbade him to read it. Metellus took it to read it out himself; Cato snatched the document out of his hands. Metellus knew the decree by heart and started reciting it from memory — and Thermus clapped a hand over his mouth. Seeing the two of them determined to block him by any means, and the people cowed but leaning toward the better side, Metellus sent to his house for armed men. They came rushing in with a roar, and in the terror everyone scattered — everyone but Cato, who stood alone while the other party pelted him with sticks and stones from above, until Murena, the very man he had once prosecuted, came up to protect him, holding his own gown in front of him as a shield and shouting at them to stop throwing. In the end, half persuading and half dragging, Murena got him into the temple of Castor and Pollux. Metellus, seeing the ground clear and the whole opposition fled from the forum, thought the thing was as good as done. He ordered his soldiers to withdraw, and resuming in proper form, began to put the decree to the vote. But the other side had recovered themselves, and they came back with a bold, deafening shout — at which Metellus's followers panicked, took the noise for the arrival of armed reinforcements, and fled the place to the last man. With them scattered, Cato came forward again, praising the people's courage and cheering on their resolve, and now the majority were for stripping Metellus of his office by any means available. The senate, too, assembled and gave orders once again to back Cato and resist the bill as a measure designed to breed sedition in the city — perhaps civil war.

Metellus himself remained bold and unbending; but he could see that his followers were thoroughly frightened of Cato, whom they regarded as unbeatable, so he rushed out of the senate into the forum, called the people together, and gave a bitter, poisonous speech against him — crying out that he was fleeing from Cato's tyranny and from this conspiracy against Pompey, and that the city would soon be sorry for having dishonored so great a man. And with that he set off for Asia, to lay before Pompey, everyone assumed, the full catalogue of his injuries. Cato was showered with praise for having delivered the state from a dangerous tribuneship and for having, in the person of Metellus, dealt a blow to the power of Pompey himself. But he was admired even more for what came next: when the senate moved to disgrace Metellus and formally depose him, Cato opposed the whole proceeding and finally talked them out of it. The common people admired his moderation and humanity — he would not trample a beaten enemy for the pleasure of it — while shrewder men recognized the policy in it: he was not going to provoke Pompey needlessly.

Soon after this, Lucullus came home from the war in Asia — a war whose finishing stroke, and with it the glory of the whole campaign, had to all appearances been lifted out of his hands by Pompey. He came close to losing his triumph as well, because Gaius Memmius attacked him before the people and threatened prosecution — more out of devotion to Pompey than any real grudge of his own. But Cato was tied to Lucullus, who had married his sister Servilia, and he thought the whole business a gross injustice besides, so he stood up to Memmius — and exposed himself to a storm of slander for it. They came near to throwing him out of his office, on the pretense that he was wielding his power like a tyrant. Yet in the end Cato got the better of Memmius, who was forced to drop the charges and abandon the fight. Lucullus got his triumph, and from then on cultivated Cato's friendship all the more carefully, regarding it as his best wall and defense against the power of Pompey.

And now Pompey himself came home from the war in glory. The splendor of his reception convinced him the people would refuse him nothing, so he sent word to the senate asking them to postpone the consular elections until he could be there in person to campaign for Piso, who was standing for the office. Most of the senators were ready to go along. Cato was not — and not because he thought the delay itself mattered much. He wanted to cut down Pompey's swelling expectations at a stroke. He opposed the request and swung the senate against it. This shook Pompey more than a little. He could see he was going to fail, and fail often, unless he could bring Cato over to his side. So he sent for Munatius, Cato's friend, and made an offer: Cato had two nieces of marriageable age, and Pompey proposed to marry the elder himself and take the younger for his son. (Some say they were not his nieces but his daughters.) Munatius laid the proposal before Cato in the presence of his wife and sisters, and the women were overjoyed at the prospect of an alliance with so great and important a man. Cato, without a pause and without a moment's deliberation, made up his mind on the spot. "Go, Munatius," he said, "go tell Pompey that Cato cannot be captured by way of the women's rooms. I am grateful for the kindness he intends, and so long as his conduct is honest, I promise him a friendship more solid than any marriage alliance. But I will not hand over hostages to Pompey's glory against my country's safety." The women were badly disappointed by this answer, and even his friends thought it harsh and high-handed. But later, when Pompey was trying to get the consulship for one of his friends and paid the people outright for their votes — a bribery so notorious that the money was counted out in Pompey's own gardens — Cato said to the women that they would necessarily have shared in the dirt of these dealings if they had been joined to Pompey's family; and they admitted he had judged best in refusing. And yet, if we go by what actually happened, Cato was badly at fault in rejecting that alliance — because it fell instead to Caesar. That was the match that fused Caesar's power with Pompey's, all but ruined the Roman empire, and did destroy the republic. None of it, perhaps, would have come to pass if Cato had not been so alarmed by Pompey's small faults that he drove him into handing another man the opportunity for the greatest ones.

But all that lay in the future. In the meantime Lucullus and Pompey fell into a great dispute over their rival arrangements in Pontus, each fighting to have his own ordinances stand. Cato took the side of Lucullus, who was plainly the wronged party; and Pompey, finding himself the weaker in the senate, went over its head to the people and proposed a law dividing lands among his soldiers to buy their votes. Cato opposed him there too, and the bill was thrown out. At that, Pompey attached himself to Clodius, the most violent demagogue of the day — and struck up his friendship with Caesar. And for that, too, Cato was in a way responsible. Here is how. Caesar, back from his governorship in Spain, wanted to stand for the consulship and keep his claim to a triumph at the same time. But the law required candidates for office to be present in the city, while a man awaiting a triumph had to remain outside the walls. So Caesar asked the senate for permission to canvass through his friends in his absence. Many senators were willing to allow it. Cato was not — and seeing them inclined to oblige Caesar, he spoke for the entire day and talked the session into the ground, so that the senate could reach no decision at all. Caesar thereupon let the triumph go, came into the city, and immediately made his alliance with Pompey and stood for the consulship. The moment he was declared consul-elect, he married his daughter Julia to Pompey. And having leagued themselves together against the commonwealth, the one proposed laws distributing land to the poor, and the other stood by to push the proposals through. Lucullus, Cicero, and their friends lined up with Bibulus, the other consul, to block the laws — and at the front of them all stood Cato, who already saw the friendship and alliance of Pompey and Caesar for the dangerous thing it was. What he objected to, he declared, was not the advantage the poor would get from the land — it was the price these men meant to collect for it, this courting and cozening of the people. On that argument he carried the senate with him, along with many outside it who were disgusted at Caesar's conduct — that a consul should flatter the mob so basely, using tricks that had always belonged to the rashest and most rebellious tribunes. So Caesar and his party, afraid they could not win by fair means, turned to open force. First a basket of dung was dumped on Bibulus on his way to the forum. Then they set on his lictors and broke their rods. Finally darts flew and men were wounded, and everyone who opposed the laws fled the forum — the rest at a dead run, and Cato last of all, walking out slowly, turning back again and again to call down the vengeance of heaven on them.

So the other party not only carried their land law, but tacked on a decree that the whole senate should swear to uphold it and defend it against any attempt at repeal, with heavy penalties for refusing the oath. The senators all swore — necessity staring them in the face, and with it the memory of the elder Metellus, long ago, who had refused an oath in just such a case and been driven out of Italy for it. As for Cato, his wife and children begged him with tears, and his friends and familiars pressed and pleaded with him to give way and swear. But the man who prevailed with him above all was Cicero, the orator, who urged that it was perhaps not even right in itself for one private citizen to set himself against what the public had decreed; that with the thing done and past altering, it was folly and madness to throw himself into danger when it could do his country no good at all; and that the worst of all evils would be to seize the excuse, as it were, to abandon the commonwealth he did everything for, and hand it over to the men bent on its ruin — as if he were glad to be relieved of the trouble of defending it. "For though Cato may have no need of Rome," Cicero said, "Rome has need of Cato — and so do all his friends." Of these, Cicero professed, he himself was the first, since Clodius was even then taking aim at him, openly threatening to come after him the moment he got the tribuneship. So Cato, they say, worn down by the entreaties and arguments of his friends, went unwillingly and took the oath — last of all, except only Favonius, one of his closest acquaintance.

Riding high on this success, Caesar brought in another law, this one distributing almost the whole territory of Campania among the poor and needy citizens. No one dared speak against it but Cato — so Caesar had him pulled off the rostra and dragged toward prison. Even that did not check his freedom of speech: as he went along he kept on denouncing the law, and advised the people to put down every legislator who proposed the like. The senate and the best of the citizens followed after him with sad, dejected faces, their grief and indignation showing in their silence, so Caesar could hardly miss how offended they were; but for pure contention's sake he pressed on, waiting for Cato to beg off or file an appeal. When he saw that Cato would not so much as consider doing either, ashamed of what he was doing and of what the city thought of it, Caesar himself quietly asked one of the tribunes to intervene and release him. For all that, with these laws and gratifications the multitude was won, and they decreed Caesar the government of Illyricum and all Gaul, with an army of four legions, for five years — while Cato cried out that they were voting, with their own hands, to install a tyrant in their citadel. Publius Clodius, who had gotten himself illegally transferred from patrician to plebeian, was declared tribune of the people, having promised to do everything according to Caesar's and Pompey's pleasure on one condition: that he be allowed to banish Cicero. And for consuls they set up Calpurnius Piso, the father of Caesar's wife, and Aulus Gabinius — one of Pompey's creatures, as we are told by the people best acquainted with his life and habits.

Yet even with everything locked down — one half of the city mastered by favors, the other by fear — they were still afraid of Cato. They remembered, with some bitterness, what pains and trouble their victories over him had cost, and what shame and disgrace besides, when they had been driven at last to lay violent hands on him. Clodius, for one, despaired of forcing Cicero out of Italy while Cato remained in Rome. So, with his scheme laid in advance, the first thing he did on entering office was send for Cato and tell him that he regarded him as the most incorruptible man in Rome, and was ready to prove he meant it. "Plenty of men," he said, "have applied for the commission to Cyprus in the case of Ptolemy, and lobbied for the appointment. But you are the only man worthy of it, and I am pleased to give you the favor." Cato cried out at once that the thing was a trap — no favor, but an injury. To which Clodius answered, proud and fierce: "Then if you will not take it as a kindness, you shall make the voyage as a punishment," and going straight out to the assembly of the people, he had them pass a decree that Cato be sent to Cyprus. So Cato was shipped off — and they voted him neither ship, nor soldier, nor any staff except two secretaries, one of whom was a thief and a scoundrel, and the other a hanger-on of Clodius. And as if Cyprus and Ptolemy were not work enough, he was ordered to restore the refugees of Byzantium into the bargain. Clodius meant to keep him far away for as long as his own tribuneship lasted.

Forced to go, Cato advised Cicero — next on the list — to make no resistance, for fear of throwing the state into civil war and chaos, but to bow to the times, and so become his country's preserver a second time. He sent his friend Canidius ahead to Cyprus to persuade Ptolemy to yield without a fight, promising that if he did he would lack for neither riches nor honor, for the Romans would give him the priesthood of the goddess at Paphos. Cato himself waited at Rhodes, making his preparations and expecting an answer from Cyprus. While he waited, the other Ptolemy — the king of Egypt, who had abandoned Alexandria after a quarrel with his subjects and was sailing for Rome in hopes that Pompey and Caesar would send an army to restore him — asked, on his way, to see Cato, and sent word to him, assuming Cato would come to him. Cato had taken a purgative that morning, and sent back the answer that Ptolemy was welcome to come to him, if he saw fit. When the king came, Cato neither went out to meet him nor so much as rose from his seat; he greeted him like any ordinary visitor and told him to sit down. This threw Ptolemy off balance at first — he was startled to meet such stern, lofty manners in a man of so plain and unassuming an appearance. But when they began to talk over his affairs, he was still more astonished at the wisdom and the bluntness of what he heard. For Cato criticized the whole course he had taken. He pointed out what honor and happiness the king was walking away from, and what humiliations and miseries he was walking into — the bribing he would have to do, the greed he would have to feed when he reached the leading men of Rome, whom all Egypt turned into silver would scarcely satisfy. His advice was to sail home and be reconciled with his subjects — and he offered to go along himself and help settle the differences. Brought back to his senses by this talk, as if out of a fit of madness or delirium, and seeing the truth and good sense in what Cato said, Ptolemy resolved to follow his advice. But his friends talked him around again, and he went on to Rome as he had first planned. When he got there, and found himself cooling his heels at the door of one magistrate after another, he began to curse his own folly — feeling he had refused not the counsel of a good and wise man so much as the oracle of a god.

Meanwhile the other Ptolemy, the one in Cyprus, very conveniently for Cato, poisoned himself. He was reported to have left great riches; so Cato, who meant to go first to Byzantium, sent his nephew Brutus to Cyprus, since he did not entirely trust Canidius. Then he reconciled the refugees with the people of Byzantium, left that city quiet and at peace, and sailed for Cyprus, where he found a royal treasure — plate, furniture, precious stones, purple — all of which had to be turned into ready money. Determined to do everything with perfect exactness, and to drive the price of every item to the top, he attended the sales in person and went carefully through all the accounts. He would not trust the customs of the market; he looked with suspicion on everyone alike — the officers, the criers, the bidders, even his own friends — and ended by talking with the buyers himself, pressing them to bid higher, and running the greater part of the sales in that fashion.

This distrust gave offense to a number of his friends, and one of them in particular — Munatius, the closest of them all — was left almost beyond reconciling. When Caesar came to write his book against Cato, this affair supplied him with his most savage passages. Munatius himself, though, says the quarrel came not so much from Cato's distrust as from Cato's neglect of him — and from his own jealousy of Canidius. For Munatius also wrote a book about Cato, and it is the main authority Thrasea follows. Munatius tells it this way: he arrived in Cyprus after the others, and found miserable lodgings provided for him; he went to Cato's house and was turned away at the door, because Cato was closeted with Canidius. He later complained about this, in quite mild terms — and got a very harsh answer: too much love, according to Theophrastus, often turns to hatred. "And you," Cato told him, "because you bear me so much love, think you receive too little honor, and so you lose your temper. I employ Canidius for his industry and his fidelity. He has been with me from the first, and I have found him worthy of trust." All this was said privately, between the two of them; but Cato afterward repeated it to Canidius — and when Munatius found that out, he refused to come to Cato's suppers anymore, and refused to come when he was invited to give counsel. Cato then threatened to seize his goods, the customary penalty for the disobedient; Munatius ignored the threats, went back to Rome, and nursed the grievance a long time. But later, when Cato had come home too, Marcia — who at this time was still living with him — arranged for the two men to be invited to supper together at the house of one Barca. Cato arrived last, when the rest were already reclining, and asked where he should take his place. Wherever he pleased, said Barca. Cato looked around the room, said he would be near Munatius — and went and placed himself on the couch beside him. But that was the only mark of kindness he showed him all through the meal. Another time, at Marcia's urging, Cato wrote to Munatius that he wished to speak with him. Munatius came to the house in the morning, and Marcia kept him there until all the other company had gone. Then Cato came in, threw both arms around him, embraced him warmly, and the two were friends again. I have told this little story at full length because I think the characters and tempers of men show themselves more clearly in things of this kind than in great and famous actions.

Cato collected not much less than seven thousand talents of silver. Anxious about what might happen on so long a sea voyage, he had a great many chests made, each holding two talents and five hundred drachmas; to every chest he fastened a long rope, and to the far end of the rope a piece of cork, so that if the ship went down, the floats would mark where the chests lay under water. And in fact the money, all but a very little, was carried over safely. But he had also kept two account books, in which every transaction of his commission was carefully written out — and neither of them survived. One was in the charge of his freedman Philargyrus, who sailed from Cenchreae and went down with the ship and all its freight. The other Cato kept safe himself as far as Corcyra, where he pitched his tent in the marketplace; the sailors, bitterly cold in the night, built a great many fires, some of the tents caught, and the book was burned. He had brought along several of Ptolemy's stewards, who could vouch for his integrity and shut the mouths of enemies and false accusers; even so, the loss gnawed at him, and he was angry with himself over it — for he had intended those books not so much as proof of his own honesty, but as a pattern of exactness for other men to follow.

Word that he was coming up the river reached Rome ahead of him. Every magistrate, the priests, the whole senate, and a great part of the people went out to meet him; both banks of the Tiber were lined with spectators, so that his arrival had all the solemnity and honor of a triumph. But it struck many as strange — willful, even proud — that when the consuls and praetors appeared, Cato neither disembarked nor paused to salute them, but swept on upstream in a royal galley of six banks of oars and did not stop until he had brought his vessels to the dockyard. Still, when the money was carried through the streets, the people marveled at the sheer quantity of it, and the senate, once assembled, decreed him in handsome terms an extraordinary praetorship, along with the privilege of watching the public games in a purple-bordered robe. Cato declined every one of these honors. Instead, telling the senate what diligence and honesty he had found in Nicias, Ptolemy's steward, he asked them to grant the man his freedom.

Philippus, Marcia's father, was consul that year, and the power and authority of the office rested, in effect, with Cato — for the other consul deferred to him for his virtue's sake no less than Philippus did on account of the family connection. Cicero, meanwhile, had returned from the exile into which Clodius had driven him and had recovered great standing with the people; and while Clodius was away, he went to the Capitol and carried off by force the records of Clodius's tribuneship that had been stored there. The senate assembled, Clodius made his complaint, and Cicero answered that Clodius had never legally been tribune at all, so that everything done in his tribuneship was void and carried no authority. But Cato interrupted him as he spoke, and at last stood up and declared that he in no way justified or approved of Clodius's conduct — but if they questioned the validity of everything done in his tribuneship, they might just as well question everything Cato himself had done at Cyprus, since that expedition was unlawful if the man who sent him held no lawful office. For his own part, he thought Clodius had been legally made tribune, since the law allowed a patrician to be adopted into a plebeian family. If he had done wrong in office, he could be called to account for it — but the authority of the magistracy ought not to suffer for the faults of the magistrate. Cicero took this hard, and for a long time dropped his friendship with Cato; later, though, the two were reconciled.

Pompey and Crassus, in agreement with Caesar — who crossed the Alps to meet them — had now formed a plan: the two of them would stand for a second consulship, and once in office they would extend Caesar's command by five more years and take for themselves the greatest provinces, with armies and money to hold them. This looked like nothing less than an open conspiracy to subvert the constitution and carve up the empire. Several men of high character had intended to run for the consulship that year, but at the sight of these enormous competitors they all withdrew — all except Lucius Domitius, the husband of Cato's sister Porcia, whom Cato persuaded to stand his ground and not abandon the contest, which, he said, was not about winning a consulship but about saving the liberty of Rome. Meanwhile the more sober part of the citizens were telling one another that the power of Pompey and Crassus must not be allowed to combine — united, it would swell past all bounds and become dangerous to the state — and that one of the two must therefore be refused. So they took Domitius's side, urging and encouraging him to press on and assuring him that many who were afraid to support him openly would help him in private. Pompey's party feared exactly that, and laid an ambush for Domitius as he went down before daybreak, by torchlight, to the Field of Mars. The torchbearer walking just ahead of Domitius was struck down first and killed; then others were wounded, and everyone fled except Cato and Domitius. Cato held him there — though he himself had taken a wound in the arm — crying out, imploring the rest to stay and not abandon, while they had breath in their bodies, the defense of their liberty against tyrants who showed plainly enough, by grasping at power through this kind of violence, how moderately they were likely to use it once they had it. But in the end Domitius, unwilling to face the danger any longer, fled to his own house, and Pompey and Crassus were declared consuls.

Even then Cato refused to give up. He resolved to stand for praetor himself that year, to have some footing for his campaign against them — he did not want to be a private citizen contending with public magistrates. Pompey and Crassus saw the danger, and fearing that the praetorship in Cato's hands might carry as much weight as a consulship, they convened the senate suddenly, without notice to a great many of the senators, and pushed through an order that the praetors-elect should take office immediately, without waiting out the usual interval during which, by law, they could be prosecuted for buying votes. Having thus licensed themselves to bribe freely without being called to account, they put up their own friends and dependents as candidates for the praetorship, handing out money and standing watch over the people as they voted. Even so, Cato's virtue and reputation were on the point of triumphing over all these stratagems, for the people generally felt it was shameful that a price should be paid to reject Cato, when he was the man who ought rather to be paid to accept the office. He carried the first tribe. At that, Pompey immediately manufactured a lie, crying out that he had heard thunder, and abruptly broke up the assembly — for the Romans held thunder a matter of religion and a bad omen, and would conclude no business after it. Before the next election they distributed still larger bribes, drove the best men from the Field, and by these foul means got Vatinius chosen praetor instead of Cato. The story goes that those who had sold their votes so corruptly and dishonestly hurried out of the Field the moment it was done, as if in flight. The rest stayed, gathered together and loudly indignant; one of the tribunes kept the assembly in session, and Cato stood up and, as if by inspiration, foretold all the miseries that were afterward to befall the state. He urged the people to beware of Pompey and Crassus, men whose guilt and whose secret designs were exactly what made them so afraid of having Cato for praetor. When he had finished, a larger crowd followed him home than escorted all the new praetors-elect put together.

Gaius Trebonius now proposed the law assigning provinces to the consuls: one was to have Spain and Africa, the other Egypt and Syria, with full power to make war by land and sea however they saw fit. Once it was proposed, everyone else despaired of stopping it, and neither did nor said anything against it. But Cato, before the voting began, mounted the speaker's platform and demanded a hearing; with great difficulty he was allowed two hours. He spent them informing the people, reasoning with them, foretelling much of what was to come — and then he was permitted to speak no longer. As he pressed on, an officer came up and pulled him down; and once down, he went on shouting, and found plenty of listeners ready to share his outrage. The officer seized him again and forced him out of the forum; the moment Cato broke loose, he turned back toward the platform, shouting to the people to stand by him. After this had happened several times, Trebonius lost his temper and ordered him hauled to prison — but the crowd followed, listening to the speech Cato made as he went, until Trebonius took fright again and ordered him released. That whole day was used up, and Cato had staved the business off. But in the days that followed, some of the citizens were overawed by fears and threats, and others were won with gifts and favors. Aquillius, one of the tribunes, they penned inside the senate house with armed men; Cato, crying out that it was thundering, they drove from the forum; a number of people were wounded, and some were killed; and at last, by open force, they passed the law. Feeling ran so high that a crowd gathered to pull down the statues of Pompey — until Cato went and turned them from the attempt.

Then another law was proposed, this one concerning Caesar's provinces and legions. This time Cato did not appeal to the people; he went to Pompey himself. He told him he did not realize it yet, but he was hoisting Caesar onto his own shoulders; and when Caesar grew too heavy for him, Pompey would find he could neither set the burden down nor bear it any longer, and would come crashing with it — burden and bearer together — onto the commonwealth. Then he would remember Cato's advice, and remember that it had been as much to Pompey's own advantage as it was just and honest in itself. Pompey heard warnings like this again and again, and every time he shrugged them off and let them go, never suspecting that Caesar could change, and trusting always in his own power and good fortune.

Cato was made praetor the following year — and, it must be said, he did the office no more honor and credit by his conspicuous integrity than he did it disgrace and damage by his strange behavior. He would often come to court without shoes, sit on the bench with no tunic under his toga, and in that attire pass judgment in capital cases on men of the highest rank. It was said, too, that he used to drink wine after his morning meal and then transact the business of his office — but that report was false. What was true was this: the people at that time had been thoroughly corrupted by the handouts of office-seekers, and most of them treated the selling of their votes as a regular trade. Cato was determined to root this corruption out of the commonwealth altogether, so he persuaded the senate to order that men elected to any office, even if no one accused them, must come into court and give an account under oath of their conduct during the election. This was intensely hateful to the candidates, and more hateful still to the vast numbers who took the bribes. So it happened that one morning, as Cato was on his way to the tribunal, a great mob crowded around him, shouting curses and abuse and throwing stones. Everyone around the tribunal fled at once; Cato himself was forced off it and jostled through the crush, barely escaping the stones, until with great difficulty he caught hold of the Rostra. There he stood up, bold-faced and unshaken, and mastered the tumult at once; the clamor died, and addressing the crowd in words fit for the occasion, he was heard in complete attention and quelled the disturbance entirely. When the senate afterward praised him for it, he answered, "But I do not commend you — for abandoning your praetor in danger, and bringing him no help."

Meanwhile the candidates for office were in an agony of perplexity: each man dreaded to spend money himself, yet dreaded even more that his rivals would. At last they agreed that each should put down 125,000 drachmas, and then all canvass fairly and honestly, on the condition that anyone caught using bribery forfeited his deposit. The bargain struck, they chose Cato to hold the stakes and judge the matter; they brought him the agreed sums and signed the agreement in his presence. He chose not to take the actual money, but accepted securities from men who stood bound for the candidates. On election day he stationed himself beside the tribune who took the votes and watched everything that passed; he caught one candidate breaking the agreement and promptly ordered him to pay his forfeit to the rest. But they, loudly praising Cato's justice, let the penalty go, judging the exposure punishment enough. The affair brought Cato as much envy as reputation, and many resented his taking upon himself, single-handed, the whole authority of the senate, the courts, and the magistracies. For there is no virtue whose honor and credit earn a man more ill-will than justice — and this because justice, more than any other virtue, wins a man power and standing with the common people. Men merely honor the brave and admire the wise, but the just they also love, and trust, and rely on completely. The bold man they fear and the clever man they suspect; and besides, they credit courage and cleverness to a man's natural makeup rather than to any goodness of his will — courage being a kind of natural vigor of the mind, and wisdom a built-in sharpness. But any man can be just if he only wills it; and that is why injustice is held the most dishonorable of vices — because it is the least excusable.

For exactly this reason all the great men opposed Cato: his virtue felt like a standing rebuke. Pompey in particular regarded the growth of Cato's credit as the ruin of his own power, and kept putting men up to rail against him. Among them was the agitator Clodius, now back in league with Pompey, who openly charged that Cato had pocketed a great deal of the treasure found in Cyprus, and that he hated Pompey only because Pompey had refused to marry his daughter. Cato answered that although they had given him neither a horse nor a single soldier, he had brought home more treasure from Cyprus alone than Pompey had from all his wars and triumphs over the ransacked world; and that he had never sought an alliance with Pompey — not because he thought him unworthy of the connection, but because they differed so much in matters that concerned the commonwealth. "I laid down the province that was offered me when my praetorship ended," he said. "Pompey, on the contrary, keeps many provinces for himself and hands out many more to others. Just now he lent Caesar a force of six thousand men for Gaul — which Caesar never asked the people for, and which Pompey never got the people's consent to give. Men, horses, and arms by the thousand have become the private gifts that private men make to one another. Pompey keeps the titles of commander and general while handing over the armies and provinces to others to govern, and he himself stays home to preside over the contests of the canvass and to stir up riots at the elections — and out of the anarchy he creates among us, we can all see well enough, he is seeking a monarchy for himself." So he paid Pompey back in kind.

Cato had a devoted friend and admirer named Marcus Favonius — much what Apollodorus of Phalerum is said to have been, long before, to Socrates: a man whose master's words went to his head like strong wine and threw him into raptures bordering on frenzy. This Favonius stood for the aedileship and was on the point of losing when Cato, there to help him, noticed that all the ballots were written in a single hand; he exposed the fraud and appealed to the tribunes, who stopped the election. Favonius was afterward elected, and Cato, who assisted him in everything the office involved, also took charge of the shows exhibited in the theater. He gave the actors crowns not of gold but of wild olive, such as they give at the Olympic games; and in place of the usual magnificent presents he offered the Greeks beetroot, lettuces, radishes, and pears, and the Romans earthenware jars of wine, pork, figs, cucumbers, and little bundles of firewood. Some ridiculed the economy of it; others watched with respect this gentle unbending of Cato's usual rigor and austerity. In the end Favonius himself slipped into the crowd, took a seat among the spectators, and applauded Cato — shouting to him to reward the performers who did well, and calling on the people to give their honors to Cato, since he for his own part had placed his whole authority in Cato's hands. Meanwhile, in another theater, Curio, Favonius's colleague, was giving magnificent entertainments; but the people left his shows for Favonius's, and joined heartily in the fun, delighted to watch Favonius playing the private citizen and Cato the master of the games. Cato did all this, in truth, to mock the great sums that others spent, and to teach a lesson: that in amusements men ought to seek amusement and nothing more — the display of a decent cheerfulness, not elaborate productions and costly magnificence that demand endless care and trouble over things of no importance at all.

After this, Scipio, Hypsaeus, and Milo stood for the consulship — and not merely with the by-now familiar and accepted disorders of bribery and corruption, but with weapons and slaughter, driving their recklessness and desperation to the very edge of open civil war. It was proposed that Pompey be empowered to preside over the election. Cato at first opposed this, saying that the laws ought not to look to Pompey for protection — Pompey should look for protection to the laws. But the confusion dragged on, three armies in effect besieged the forum day after day, and when no possible end to the disorder appeared, Cato at last decided that rather than let things reach the final extremity, the senate should freely confer everything on Pompey: better to use a lesser illegality as the cure for the greatest of all, and better to set up a more legal sort of monarchy themselves than to let the chaos run on into a sedition that could only end in one anyway. So Bibulus, a friend of Cato's, moved in the senate that Pompey be created sole consul — for either he would restore lawful government, or Rome would at least be serving its best master. Cato stood up and, against all expectation, seconded the motion, concluding that any government was better than pure confusion, and that he did not doubt Pompey would deal honorably with the commonwealth committed to his charge. Pompey, declared consul, invited Cato to visit him at his house in the suburbs. When he came, Pompey greeted and embraced him warmly, acknowledged what Cato had done for him, and asked for his counsel and support in managing the office. Cato answered that nothing he had said in the past had been said out of hatred for Pompey, and nothing he had done now had been done out of love for him: all of it was for the good of the commonwealth. In private, if Pompey asked, he would freely give his advice; in public, whether he asked or not, he would always speak his mind. And he was as good as his word. First, when Pompey drew up severe laws with heavy fines for those who had bribed the people, Cato advised him to let the past alone and provide for the future — if he went digging up old misdemeanors, it would be hard to know where to stop, and if he decreed new penalties for past acts, it would be unreasonable to punish men under a law they had never had the opportunity of breaking. Later, when a number of prominent men, some of them Pompey's own relations, stood accused, and Pompey went slack and lost his appetite for prosecution, Cato reproved him sharply and pushed him on. Pompey had also made a law forbidding the custom of delivering speeches in praise of men on trial; yet he himself wrote one for Munatius Plancus and sent it in while the case was being pleaded. Cato, who was sitting as one of the judges, clapped his hands over his ears and refused to hear it read. Plancus objected to him before the verdict was given — and was condemned anyway. In fact Cato had become a great trouble and perplexity to defendants in general: they dreaded to have him among their judges, yet did not dare demand his removal. Many men had been condemned precisely because, by refusing Cato, they seemed to admit they could not trust their own innocence; and there were some whose enemies threw it in their teeth as a reproach that they had not accepted Cato for a judge.

In the meantime, Caesar kept close with his forces in Gaul and stayed in arms — while at the same time using his gifts, his riches, and above all his friends to build up his power in the city. And now Cato's old warnings began to rouse Pompey out of the careless security he had been lying in, into a kind of dawning sense of danger at hand. But seeing him slow, unwilling, and timid about taking any step to head Caesar off, Cato resolved to stand for the consulship himself and force Caesar's hand at once: either lay down his arms or show his intentions. Both of Cato's competitors were men of good standing; one of them, Sulpicius, owed a great deal to Cato's credit and authority in the city, and people thought it ungracious and ungrateful of him to run against him. Cato himself took no offense. "It is no wonder," he said, "if a man will not yield to another the thing he counts the greatest good." He had persuaded the senate to order that candidates must ask the people for their votes in person — no soliciting through go-betweens, no bringing others along to speak for them on their rounds. This made the common people bitterly hostile to him: he was taking from them not only the money, but the chance of putting important men in their debt — leaving them, at a stroke, both poorer and less courted. On top of that, Cato was by nature completely unsuited to the business of canvassing; he cared more about sustaining the dignity of his life and character than about winning the office. So he solicited in his own fashion, would not let his friends do the things that win over a crowd, and was rejected: he lost the consulship.

Now, the custom on such occasions was for the loser — and his friends and relations with him — to feel disgraced and humiliated, and to observe a kind of mourning for days afterward. Cato took it so lightly that he oiled himself and played ball in the Field of Mars, ate his breakfast, and went down to the forum as usual, shoeless and tunicless, to stroll about with his friends. Cicero faulted him for this: at a moment when affairs demanded such a consul, he said, Cato would neither exert himself nor condescend to pay the people a little court — and then he gave up and never tried again, though he had stood a second time for the praetorship. Cato answered that he had lost the praetorship the first time not by the honest vote of the people but by the violence and corrupt dealing of his adversaries, whereas in the consular election there had been no foul play at all. He could see perfectly well that the people simply did not like his manners; and an honest man ought not to change his manners for their sake — nor would a wise man attempt the same thing again while the same prejudices stood in his way.

Caesar, all this while, was off fighting warlike nations and subduing them at great risk to himself. Among other things, it was widely believed that he had attacked the Germans during a truce and slaughtered three hundred thousand of them. Some of his friends moved that the senate declare a public thanksgiving for the victory. Cato's counter-proposal was that they hand Caesar over to the people he had wronged, and so wash away the offense rather than bring its curse down on the city. "Still, we do have reason to thank the gods," he added, "for sparing the commonwealth — for not taking vengeance on the army for the madness and folly of its general." Caesar answered with a letter to the senate, which was read aloud, and which was packed with insults and accusations against Cato. Cato stood up looking entirely unconcerned. Without heat, without passion — in a calm and, you might say, rehearsed manner — he made all of Caesar's charges sound like common scolding and abuse, a kind of joke Caesar was amusing himself with. Then he got down to business. He went through Caesar's whole political career, explaining and exposing his conduct and his purpose from the very beginning — not like a lifelong opponent, but like a fellow conspirator letting out the secrets — and he closed by telling the senators that it was not the sons of the Britons or the Gauls they needed to fear, but Caesar himself, if they had any sense. The speech so stirred and awakened the senate that Caesar's friends were sorry they had ever had the letter read, since it had handed Cato the opportunity to say so many reasonable things and so many hard truths. Yet nothing was actually decided. The only suggestion made was that it might be well to send Caesar a successor. At that, Caesar's friends demanded that Pompey also lay down his arms and give up his provinces — or else Caesar should not be required to do either. Now, Cato cried out, exactly what he had foretold had come to pass: it was plain that Caesar was using his army to force their judgment, turning against the state the very forces he had wheedled out of it by imposture and trickery. But outside the Senate house Cato could accomplish little. The common people were always eager to build Caesar up, and the senate, though Cato had convinced it, was afraid of the people.

Then the news arrived that Caesar had seized Ariminum and was marching on Rome with his army — and suddenly everyone, Pompey and the common people included, turned their eyes to Cato, the one man who had seen Caesar's intentions from the start and said so plainly. "If you had believed me," he told them, "or taken my advice, you would not now be reduced to fearing one man — or to pinning all your hopes on one man either." Pompey admitted that Cato had spoken like a prophet, while he himself had acted too much like a friend. And Cato advised the senate to put everything into Pompey's hands. "Those who can raise up great evils," he said, "are best at putting them down."

Pompey, finding his forces insufficient and the men he could raise halfhearted, abandoned the city. Cato, resolved to follow him into exile, sent his younger son to Munatius, who was then in Bruttium, and took his eldest with him. But he needed someone to keep his house and look after his daughters, so he took Marcia back — she was a rich widow now, for Hortensius had died and left her his whole estate. Caesar later used this, too, as ammunition, charging Cato with greed and with trafficking in marriage for profit. "If he needed a wife," Caesar asked, "why did he give her away? And if he didn't, why did he take her back? Unless he offered her to Hortensius as bait from the beginning — lent her out young to get her back rich." To that, the fair answer is the line from Euripides:

  Some things are past discussing; and the first
  is calling Hercules a coward.

Charging Cato with greed is about as sensible as charging Hercules with cowardice. Whether the marriage itself was entirely rightly handled is another question, and open to dispute. In any case, as soon as he had taken Marcia back, he put his house and his daughters in her charge, and himself followed Pompey. And it is said that from that day forward he never cut his hair or shaved his beard or wore a garland, but carried the same sadness, grief, and dejection over his country's calamities to the very end, whichever side happened to be winning or losing.

The government of Sicily fell to him by lot, and he crossed over to Syracuse. Learning that Asinius Pollio had landed at Messena with a force from the enemy, Cato sent to him demanding the reason for his coming; Pollio replied by demanding that Cato explain the reason for the whole upheaval of the times. And when word came at the same moment that Pompey had abandoned Italy altogether and lay encamped at Dyrrhachium, Cato remarked on how strange and unfathomable the divine government of the world is: "When Pompey did nothing wisely or honestly, he was always successful; and now that he wants to save his country and defend her liberty, nothing goes right for him." As for Asinius, Cato said he could drive him out of Sicily easily enough — but larger forces were on their way to reinforce him, and Cato would not plunge the island into war. So he advised the Syracusans to join the winning side and look to their own safety, and sailed away.

When he reached Pompey, his advice never varied: drag the war out. He always hoped matters could still be settled, and he had no wish to see things come to battle, for the commonwealth would suffer terribly and would be the author of its own ruin no matter whose sword won. In the same spirit he persuaded Pompey and his council to decree that no city subject to Rome should be sacked, and that no Roman should be killed except in the heat of battle. This won him great honor, and it drew many people over to Pompey's side, attracted by such moderation and humanity. Afterward he was sent into Asia, to help those who were raising men and fitting out ships there, and he took along his sister Servilia and the little boy she had borne to Lucullus. Since her widowhood she had lived with her brother, and she had recovered much of her reputation by placing herself under his care, following him on his voyages, and accepting his severe way of life. Caesar, naturally, did not fail to sling mud at Cato on her account as well.

As it turned out, Pompey's officers in Asia had little need of Cato; but he did talk the people of Rhodes over to the cause, and, leaving Servilia and her child on the island, he returned to Pompey — who by now had assembled very great forces by land and sea. And here, more than in any other single act, Pompey betrayed what he was really thinking. His first intention was to give Cato command of the navy, which counted no fewer than five hundred warships, besides a huge number of light galleys, scouts, and open boats. But then it occurred to him — or his friends pointed out — that Cato's one guiding aim was to free his country from all usurpation, and that if Cato held forces on that scale, the moment Caesar was beaten he would call on Pompey, too, to lay down his arms and submit to the laws. So Pompey changed his mind, though he had already mentioned the appointment to Cato, and made Bibulus admiral instead. For all that, he had no reason to think Cato's zeal for the cause had cooled. Before one of the battles at Dyrrhachium, we are told, Pompey himself addressed the soldiers and ordered his officers to do the same, and the men listened coldly, in silence — until Cato, last of all, came forward and spoke to them as a philosopher would, saying what the occasion demanded about liberty, and manly virtue, and death, and a good name. He delivered it all with strong natural feeling, and he finished by calling on the gods directly, addressing them as though they stood present to watch men fight for their country. At that the army raised such a shout, and showed such fire, that the officers led them into the danger full of hope and confidence. Caesar's men were routed and driven off — but Caesar's presiding fortune used Pompey's caution and self-distrust to leave the victory incomplete. That story, though, has been told in the life of Pompey. And while everyone else was celebrating and glorying in the success, Cato alone turned away, covered his face, and wept for his country, cursing the fatal ambition that had set so many brave Romans to murdering one another.

After this, when Pompey followed Caesar into Thessaly, he left behind at Dyrrhachium a mass of munitions, money, and stores, along with many of his own household and relations, and put the whole charge of it in Cato's hands — with a command of only fifteen cohorts. He trusted Cato greatly, but he feared him too, knowing perfectly well that if things went badly, Cato would be the last man to desert him; but that if he conquered, Cato would never let him use the victory however he pleased. A good many men of high rank also stayed with Cato at Dyrrhachium. When they heard of the disaster at Pharsalia, Cato settled his course in his own mind: if Pompey were dead, he would lead the men in his charge back to Italy, and then withdraw as far from Caesar's tyranny as he could and live in exile; if Pompey were alive, he would hold the army together for him. On that resolution he crossed over to Corcyra, where the fleet lay, and there he offered to hand his command to Cicero, on the grounds that Cicero had been consul and he himself only a praetor. But Cicero refused it, and was setting out for Italy. Pompey's son, furious at this, was ready in his rashness and heat to punish everyone who was quitting the cause — starting by laying hands on Cicero. Cato spoke with the young man privately and talked him out of it. So he plainly saved Cicero's life, and rescued several others from rough treatment besides.

Guessing that Pompey the Great had fled toward Egypt or Africa, Cato resolved to hurry after him, and he put all his men aboard and set sail — but first he gave free leave to depart to everyone whose heart was not in continuing the fight. On the African coast they fell in with Sextus, Pompey's younger son, who told them of his father's death in Egypt. The grief was overwhelming, and to a man they declared that after Pompey they would follow no leader but Cato. Out of compassion, then, for so many worthy men who had given such proof of their loyalty — men he could not for shame abandon in a desert country in the middle of so many dangers — he took the command upon himself, and marched toward the city of Cyrene, which opened its gates to him at once, though not long before it had shut them against Labienus. There he learned that Scipio, Pompey's father-in-law, had been received by King Juba, and that Attius Varus, whom Pompey had made governor of Africa, had joined them with his forces. Cato resolved to march to them overland, though it was now winter. He collected a train of donkeys to carry water, laid in plenty of every other provision, and gathered a number of wagons. He also brought along some of the people called Psylli, who cure snakebite by sucking the venom out with their mouths, and who have charms besides, with which they drug the serpents into a stupor.

They marched seven days straight, Cato on foot the whole time at the head of his men, never once using a horse or a chariot. Ever since the battle of Pharsalia, moreover, he had taken his meals sitting upright — adding this to his other habits of mourning, that he never lay down except to sleep.

The winter passed in Africa, and Cato led out his army, which came to little short of ten thousand men. The affairs of Scipio and Varus were going badly, because of the quarrels and dissensions between them and their fawning submission to King Juba, whose vanity, and whose swollen pride in his own power and riches, made him insufferable. The first time he came to a conference with Cato, Juba had his own seat placed in the middle, between Scipio and Cato. Cato saw it, picked up his chair, and set himself down on the far side of Scipio — putting Scipio in the place of honor in the middle, though Scipio was his enemy and had once published a pamphlet full of slanders against him. There are people who treat that moment as a trifle, and who nonetheless criticize Cato because in Sicily one day, walking with Philostratus, he gave the philosopher the middle place, to show his respect for philosophy. However that may be, he now succeeded both in humbling Juba, who had been treating Scipio and Varus like a pair of satraps under his orders, and in reconciling the two Romans with each other. All the troops wanted Cato for their general; Scipio and Varus, too, gave way and offered him the command. But he said he would not break the very laws he was fighting to defend: he was only a propraetor, and a propraetor had no business commanding in the presence of a proconsul — for Scipio had been made proconsul. Besides, people took it as a good omen to see a Scipio in command in Africa, and the mere name put hope of victory into the soldiers.

No sooner had Scipio taken up the command than he resolved — Juba urging him on — to put the whole population of Utica to the sword and level the city, on the charge that it had taken Caesar's side. Cato would not stand for it. Invoking the gods, crying out and protesting in the council of war, he managed with great difficulty to save the poor people from that cruelty. Afterward, at the request of the inhabitants and at Scipio's own instance, Cato took over the government of Utica himself, so that the city should not fall into Caesar's hands one way or another — for it was a strong place, and valuable to either side. Under Cato it became stronger and better supplied still. He brought in vast stores of grain, repaired the walls, raised towers, and ran deep trenches and palisades around the town. The young men of Utica he quartered in these outworks, having first taken their weapons from them; the rest of the inhabitants he kept inside the town, and he took the greatest care that the Romans did them no injury and offered them no insult. From this base he sent great quantities of arms, money, and provisions to the camp, and the city became the war's chief magazine.

His advice to Scipio was the same he had once given Pompey: do not stake everything on a battle against a man experienced in war and formidable in the field, but play for time — for time gradually wears down the fury of a crisis, and fury is what usurpation lives on. Scipio, out of pride, rejected the counsel, and wrote Cato a letter taunting him with cowardice: apparently it was not enough for him to sit safe himself behind walls and trenches; he had to stop other men from boldly using their own good judgment to seize the right opportunity. Cato wrote back offering to take the horse and foot he had brought to Africa and cross into Italy, to make a diversion there and pull Caesar off after him. Scipio ridiculed that proposal too. Then Cato let it be seen openly that he was sorry he had yielded the command — sorry because Scipio would clearly not conduct the war with any wisdom, and because, if against all likelihood he somehow succeeded, he would use his success just as unjustly at home. Cato had made up his mind by then, and said as much to his friends: he could have only slender hopes of generals with so much boldness and so little judgment; and yet if anything happened beyond all expectation, and Caesar were overthrown, he for his part would not stay in Rome, but would get away from the cruelty and inhumanity of Scipio, who was already flinging fierce, arrogant threats at many men.

What Cato had been bracing for came sooner than he expected. Late one evening a man arrived from the army, three days on the road, with word that a great battle had been fought near Thapsus; that everything was utterly lost; that Caesar had taken the camps; that Scipio and Juba had escaped with only a handful, and all the rest of the army was destroyed. Arriving in wartime, and at night, news like that drove the people nearly out of their minds; they could scarcely be kept inside the city walls. But Cato came out to meet them in their uproar and confusion, and did everything he could to comfort and steady them, easing their terror and bewilderment a little by suggesting that things were probably not as bad as the reports made out — rumor always exaggerates. For the moment, the panic subsided. The next morning he sent for the three hundred men he used as his council — Romans who were in Africa on business, in trade and money-lending — along with the senators who were present, and their sons, summoning them all to the temple of Jupiter. While they were gathering, Cato walked about quietly, perfectly composed, as though nothing had happened, reading a book he carried in his hand. It was his inventory of war supplies: armor, grain, ammunition, soldiers.

When they had assembled, he began. First he addressed the three hundred themselves, praising warmly the courage and loyalty they had shown, and their good service to their country with their persons, their money, and their counsel. Then he begged them on no account to scatter, as if any man alone could find safety by deserting his companions. On the contrary: as long as they held together, Caesar would have less reason to despise them if they fought, and more readiness to pardon them if they submitted. So he urged them to deliberate among themselves, and said he would find no fault with either choice. If they decided to bow to fortune, he would put their change of course down to necessity. But if they resolved to stand firm and face the danger for liberty's sake, he would do more than commend them — he would admire their courage, and he would make himself their leader and their companion, until they had tried the last fortune of their country. And their country was not Utica or Adrumetum: it was Rome, which had often, by her own sheer greatness, lifted herself up from worse disasters than this. Besides, many things argued for their safety, and chiefly this: they would be fighting a man whose affairs were dragging him urgently in every direction at once. Spain had already gone over to the younger Pompey; Rome itself, unused to the bridle and chafing under it, was ready to rise at any turn of events. As for danger — they should not flinch from it, and they could take a lesson there from their enemy, who spent his own life so freely to accomplish the most unrighteous ends, but who could never hope for the ending they could promise themselves. For all the uncertainty of war, they were sure of one or the other: a most happy life if they won, or a most glorious death if they failed. Still, the decision was theirs to make among themselves, he said; and for his part he joined them in praying that the gods, in payment for their past courage and goodwill, would prosper whatever they now determined. When Cato finished, some of his hearers were moved and heartened by the arguments themselves; but the greater part, watching his fearlessness, his generosity, his goodness, all but forgot the danger they were in. Here, they felt, was the one leader no fortune could conquer; and they begged him to use their persons, their arms, and their estates however he saw fit — better to meet death following his counsel than to save their skins by betraying such virtue. Someone in the assembly proposed a decree freeing the slaves, and most of the rest approved the motion. Cato refused: it was neither just nor lawful, he said; but if individual masters chose to free their slaves willingly, those of military age should be accepted for service. Many promised to do so; he ordered their names enrolled, and withdrew.

Soon afterward letters reached him from Juba and Scipio. Juba, with a few of his men, had withdrawn to a mountain, and was waiting to hear what Cato would decide — intending to wait there for him if he chose to abandon Utica, or to march to his relief if he were besieged. Scipio was aboard ship off a promontory not far from Utica, waiting for an answer on the same question. Cato thought it best to hold the messengers until the three hundred should come to some resolution.

The senators, for their part, showed real spirit: they freed their slaves at once and armed them. But the three hundred were businessmen and moneylenders, with much of their wealth held in slaves, and the enthusiasm Cato's speech had kindled in them did not last long. Just as some substances take heat easily and lose it the moment the fire is removed, so these men glowed while Cato was in front of them; but when they began reasoning among themselves, their fear of Caesar soon overpowered their reverence for Cato and for virtue. "Who are we, after all," they said, "and who is it we are refusing to obey? Isn't this the Caesar who now holds the whole power of Rome? And which of us is a Scipio, a Pompey, a Cato? Now, when every man is letting fear outweigh honor, shall we of all people take up the fight for the liberty of Rome — shall we, in Utica, declare war on the man before whom Cato and Pompey the Great fled out of Italy? Shall we free our slaves to fight Caesar, when we ourselves have exactly as much liberty as he chooses to allow us? No — poor creatures that we are, let us know ourselves, submit to the victor, and send envoys to beg his mercy." That was the moderate faction talking. The greater part were for seizing the senators and holding them, hoping to appease Caesar's anger by handing him prisoners. Cato saw the change come over them, and gave no sign of it; but he wrote to Juba and Scipio to keep away from Utica, because he did not trust the three hundred.

Now a considerable body of cavalry that had escaped from the lost battle came riding toward Utica, and sent three men ahead to Cato — who did not, between them, carry a single coherent message, for one party in the squadrons was for going to Juba, another for joining Cato, and some were simply afraid to enter Utica at all. On hearing this, Cato ordered Marcus Rubrius to keep an eye on the three hundred — quietly taking down the names of those who freed their slaves of their own accord, and forcing no one. Then, taking the senators with him, he went out of the town to meet the cavalry's senior officers.
