He pleaded with them not to abandon so many Roman senators, and not to take Juba for their commander in place of Cato, but to think of the common safety and come into the city — a city impregnable, and stocked with grain and every other provision enough for years. The senators added their own pleas, with tears. The officers went off to consult their soldiers, and Cato sat down with the senators on an embankment to await the answer. At that moment Rubrius came up in great agitation, crying out that the three hundred were all in commotion, stirring up revolt and tumult in the city. At this the rest gave way to despair, weeping and lamenting their condition; Cato did what he could to comfort them, and sent word to the three hundred asking them to be patient. Then the cavalry officers came back with demands that were anything but reasonable. They said they did not want to serve Juba for his pay, and they would not fear Caesar so long as they followed Cato; but they dreaded being shut up inside with the men of Utica — a treacherous breed, they said, of Carthaginian blood; quiet enough for now, but certain, the moment Caesar appeared, to conspire together and betray the Romans. If Cato expected them to join him, he must first drive every Utican out of the town, or kill them, and receive his cavalry into a place cleansed of enemies and barbarians alike. Cato thought this utterly cruel and barbarous; but he answered mildly that he would consult the three hundred.

Then he rode back into the city, where he found the three hundred no longer making excuses or hiding their feelings out of respect for him. Now they said it openly: no one was going to force them into a war against Caesar. They were not able to fight him, they said, and they were not willing. Some of them were even muttering about holding the senators in the city until Caesar arrived — a hostage offering. Cato acted as though he had not heard it; he had, in fairness, the excuse of being slightly deaf. At that moment a man came running to tell him the cavalry were riding away. Afraid that the three hundred, left to themselves, might do something desperate to the senators, Cato immediately went out with a few of his friends, and seeing that the horsemen had already gone some distance, he took a horse and rode after them. They were delighted when they saw him coming, welcomed him warmly, and begged him to save himself along with them. And it was then, they say, that Cato wept — pleading for the senators, stretching out his hands in supplication. He turned some of their horses around bodily; he grabbed hold of the men by their armor; until at last, out of sheer compassion, they gave in and agreed to stay one day — just one — to cover the senators' escape. So they rode back with him, and he posted some of them at the town gates and gave others charge of the citadel. Now it was the three hundred who grew frightened, sure they would be made to pay for their wavering, and they sent to Cato begging him to come to them by any means. But the senators crowded around him and refused to let him go. They would not, they said, hand their guardian and savior over to a pack of traitors.

There was never, perhaps, a moment when Cato's virtue showed more plainly. Every class of person in Utica could see it, with sorrow and with admiration: nothing he was doing carried the slightest trace of hidden motive or self-interest. Here was a man who had long since settled on his own death, taking infinite pains, labor, and care — entirely for the sake of others — so that once he had made their lives safe, he could put an end to his own. For it was easy enough to perceive that he had determined to die, though he never let it show.

So, after calming the senators, he did what the three hundred asked and went to them alone, without a single attendant. They thanked him over and over, and begged him to use them and trust them in whatever came next. If they were not Catos, they said — if they could not rise to his greatness of soul — then let him at least pity their weakness. They had decided to send to Caesar and plead with him, first and above all, for Cato; and if they could not win his case, they would not accept a pardon for themselves, but would fight in his defense as long as they had breath in their bodies. Cato thanked them for their good intentions and advised them to send quickly, for their own safety's sake — but to ask nothing whatsoever on his behalf. "Entreaties are for the conquered," he said, "and begging pardon is for those who have done wrong. As for me, I have never been beaten in my life. So far as I chose to be, I have been the winner — I have conquered Caesar on every point of justice and honesty. It is Caesar who has been caught and defeated. The designs against his country that he denied for so long — he now stands convicted of them all." With that he left the assembly. Told that Caesar was already approaching with his whole army, he said, "Ah — he expects to find us brave men." Then he went to the senators and urged them not to delay another hour, but to get away while the horsemen were still in the city. He ordered every gate shut except the one facing the sea, assigned ships to those who were leaving, and handed out money and provisions to those in need — all of it done with perfect order and exactness, keeping down every disturbance, making sure no one did the townspeople any wrong.

Marcus Octavius now arrived with two legions, camped near Utica, and sent to Cato to settle the question of who held supreme command. Cato sent him no answer at all; to his friends he said, "And we wonder why everything has gone to ruin — when even here, in the wreck itself, our love of office survives." Meanwhile word came that the cavalry, on their way out, had begun to loot and plunder the citizens. Cato ran straight at them. From the first man he reached, he snatched back what had been taken; the rest threw down their spoils and rode off in silence, ashamed of themselves. Then he called together the people of Utica and asked them, for the three hundred's sake, not to inflame Caesar against them, but to join with them in seeking safety for everyone together. After that he went down to the port again to watch over those who were embarking, and there he embraced the friends and companions he had persuaded to go, and sent them on their way. His own son he did not advise to leave; he did not think it was his place to make a son abandon his father. But there was a young man named Statyllius, in the very flower of his age, high-spirited and burning to imitate Cato's iron constancy. Cato pressed him to leave — he was a known enemy of Caesar — and got nowhere. So Cato turned to Apollonides the Stoic philosopher and Demetrius the Peripatetic. "This one is your job," he said. "Cool this young man's fever, and teach him what is good for him." And so, seeing his friends off and dispatching the business of everyone who came to him, he spent that night and the greater part of the following day.

Lucius Caesar, a kinsman of Caesar's, had been chosen to go as envoy for the three hundred, and he came to Cato asking for help in preparing a persuasive speech on their behalf. "And for you yourself," he added, "it will be an honor for me to kiss Caesar's hands and fall at his knees, pleading your cause." Cato would not hear of it. "If I were willing to be saved by Caesar's favor," he said, "I should go to him myself. But I will not be indebted to a tyrant for his acts of tyranny. And tyranny is what it is — it is usurpation for him to play the rightful lord, graciously sparing the lives of men he has no title to rule at all. But come — if you like, let us work out what you should say for the three hundred." They spent some time at it together, and as Lucius was leaving, Cato commended his son and the rest of his friends to his care, took his hand, and said goodbye.

Then he withdrew to his house again and gathered his son and his friends around him. He talked with them about many things — among the rest, he forbade his son ever to go into politics. To act in public life as a Cato should, he said, had become impossible; and to act any other way would be shameful. Toward evening he went to his bath. As he was bathing, he remembered Statyllius and called out loudly, "Apollonides! Have you tamed that high spirit of Statyllius'? Has he gone, without even saying goodbye to us?" "No," said Apollonides. "I have said a great deal, and it has done very little good. He is resolute and immovable. He declares he is determined to follow your example." At this, they say, Cato smiled, and answered, "That will soon be tried."

After his bath he went to supper with a large company. He sat upright at the table, as he had done ever since the battle of Pharsalia; from that day on he never lay down except to sleep. At supper with him were all his own circle and the magistrates of Utica.

After the meal, the wine brought on a great deal of lively, agreeable conversation, and one philosophical question after another went around the table. At length they came down to the strange Stoic doctrines known as the paradoxes — and to one in particular: that only the good man is free, and every wicked man is a slave. The Peripatetic, as you might expect, objected — and Cato came down on him hard. Raising his voice, he argued the point at astonishing length and with such passionate vehemence that it became obvious to everyone at the table: he had resolved to put an end to his life, and set himself free. When he finished speaking, there was a long silence, and every face fell. Cato saw it, and to draw them off from suspecting his design, he turned the conversation back to the present — worrying aloud over the men now at sea, and over the others who were traveling by land across a dry and barbarous desert.

When the company broke up, he took his usual after-supper walk with his friends, gave the necessary orders to the officers of the watch, and went to his chamber — but first he embraced his son, and then each of his friends, with more than his usual warmth, which set them all suspecting again. Then he lay down and took up Plato's dialogue on the soul — the Phaedo. He had read more than half the book when he glanced up and noticed his sword was missing; his son had carried it off while he was at supper. He called a servant and asked who had taken his sword. The servant said nothing, and Cato went back to his reading. A little later — not pressing, not seeming in any hurry, as if he merely wanted to know what had become of it — he told the man to bring it. He waited a while; he finished the whole book; still no one brought the sword. At that he called up all his servants and demanded it in a louder voice. One of them he struck across the mouth so hard that he bloodied his own hand — and now his anger rose in earnest, and he shouted that his son and his servants were betraying him, handing him over naked to the enemy. Then his son came running into the room with the rest of his friends, and they fell at his feet, weeping and begging. But Cato rose up and fixed them with a terrible look. "When did I lose my mind," he said, "and how, without my noticing? No one argues with me. No one shows me a better course, if my judgment is supposed to be so bad — I am simply to be disarmed, and prevented from using my own reason. Why don't you go further, young man, and tie your father's hands behind his back, so that when Caesar comes he'll find me unable to defend myself? To kill myself I need no sword. I need only hold my breath a while, or dash my head once against the wall."

At this his son went out of the chamber weeping, and all the rest with him, except Demetrius and Apollonides. Left alone with those two, Cato began to speak more calmly. "And you," he said. "Do you also propose to keep a man of my age alive by force — to sit here and watch me in silence? Or have you brought me arguments — some proof that it is not base and unworthy, when Cato can find safety nowhere else, to go begging for it from his enemy? If so, make your case. Show me why we should unlearn everything we were ever taught — why we should throw over every conviction we have lived by, so that Caesar can make us wiser, and we can owe him even more than our lives. Not that I have decided anything about myself. But when I do decide, I must have the power to carry out my own resolution. And I will decide it, be assured, in counsel with you — in counsel with the doctrines your own philosophy teaches. So set your minds at rest. And go tell my son that he should not try to compel his father where he cannot persuade him." They gave him no answer, but went out weeping. Then the sword was carried in by a small boy. Cato took it, drew it, and looked it over; and when he saw the point was true, he said, "The sword is now my own." He laid it down, picked up his book again, and — so it is related — read it through twice from the beginning. After that he slept so soundly that the men outside the door could hear him snoring.

About midnight he called up two of his freedmen — Cleanthes, his physician, and Butas, his chief agent in public business. Butas he sent down to the port, to see whether all his friends had gotten safely to sea. To the physician he gave his hand to be dressed, swollen as it was from the blow he had struck his servant. At this everyone in the house took heart, hoping it meant he intended to live.

After a while Butas returned with word that all had sailed except Crassus, who had been delayed by some business but was on the point of departing; the wind, he added, was high, and the sea running very rough. Cato sighed when he heard it, out of pity for the men on the water, and sent Butas down again — in case anyone had been driven back and needed something, he was to come and report it.

By now the birds were beginning to sing, and Cato drifted off into a short sleep. At last Butas came back and told him all was quiet in the port. Then Cato lay down as if to sleep out what remained of the night, and told him to shut the door behind him. But the moment Butas was gone, he took his sword and drove it into his chest. The swollen hand robbed the blow of its force, and he did not die of it at once; struggling, he fell from the bed, knocking over a small geometrical table that stood beside it, and the crash brought the servants crying out, and his son and all his friends running into the chamber. They found him lying in his blood, with a great part of his bowels forced out of the wound — but still alive, still able to look at them — and they stood frozen in horror. The physician went to him and, finding the bowels unpierced, tried to put them back and sew up the wound. But Cato came to himself, and understood what the man intended. He thrust the physician away, tore at his own bowels with his hands, ripped the wound wide — and died.

In less time than you would think his own household could have learned what had happened, the three hundred were at the door. A little later the people of Utica came flooding in, all crying out with one voice that he was their benefactor and their savior — the only free man, the only man undefeated. And this at the very hour word arrived that Caesar was coming. Yet neither fear of the danger at hand, nor any urge to flatter the conqueror, nor their own quarrels and divisions could turn them from honoring Cato. They laid out his body magnificently, gave him a splendid funeral, and buried him by the seashore — where a statue of him now stands, sword in hand. Only when all this was done did they turn to the business of saving themselves and their city.

Caesar had been told that Cato was staying in Utica and making no attempt to escape — that he had sent the other Romans away while he himself, with his son and a handful of friends, remained there entirely unconcerned — and he could not fathom what the man intended. Holding him in great consideration, he hurried toward the city with his army. When he heard of the death, this, it is related, is what he said: "Cato, I grudge you your death, as you grudged me the honor of saving your life." And in truth, had Cato allowed himself to owe his life to Caesar, he would not so much have tarnished his own honor as added to Caesar's glory. What would actually have happened, of course, we cannot know — though from Caesar's usual clemency, we may guess it would have been the kindest outcome.

Cato was forty-eight years old when he died. His son suffered no harm from Caesar; but he grew idle, it is said, and got a reputation for wasting himself on women. In Cappadocia he stayed at the house of Marphadates, a member of the royal family there who had a very beautiful wife; and he stretched the visit so far past what was decent that he became the butt of epigrams — such as this one:

  Tomorrow — the thirtieth day — they say,
  Cato at last will go away.

and this:

  Porcius and Marphadates, friends so true —
  one Soul, they say, suffices for the two.

For Soul — Psyche — was the wife's name. And again:

  To Cato's greatness all confess:
  a royal Soul he does possess.

But every one of these stains was wiped away by the bravery of his death. At Philippi, fighting for his country's liberty against Octavian and Antony, when the ranks were breaking, he scorned to run and scorned to slip away unnoticed. He shouted his defiance at the enemy, showed himself out in front where they could see him, rallied those of his side who stood their ground — and fell at last, leaving his enemies in awe of his courage.

Nor was Cato's daughter Portia one whit behind the rest of her family in sober living and greatness of spirit. She married Brutus, the man who killed Caesar; she was in on the conspiracy; and she ended her life in a manner worthy of her birth and her virtue. All of which is told in the life of Brutus.

And Statyllius — the young man who swore he would imitate Cato — was stopped by the philosophers at the time, when he wanted to end his life. Afterward he followed Brutus, served him with complete loyalty and great usefulness, and died on the field of Philippi.

Cicero

Cicero's mother, Helvia, is generally agreed to have been well born and to have lived an honorable life. About his father, the reports run to opposite extremes. Some say he was the son of a fuller — a cloth-worker — and raised in that trade; others trace the family all the way back to Tullus Attius, a famous king of the Volscians who fought the Romans with some distinction. Whatever the truth, the first man of the family to carry the surname Cicero must have been somebody worth remembering, because his descendants refused to drop the name — in fact they were fond of it — even though people made fun of it. The Latin word cicer means chickpea, and that first Cicero apparently had a notch or dimple at the tip of his nose that looked like the cleft in a chickpea. Hence the name.

The Cicero whose story I am telling had spirit on this subject. When he first stood for office and got into politics, some of his friends advised him to drop the name or trade it for a better one. "No," he told them. "I intend to make the name Cicero more glorious than Scaurus or Catulus ever were." And later, when he was quaestor in Sicily and dedicated a piece of silver plate to the gods, he had his first two names, Marcus and Tullius, engraved on it — and then, as his little joke, told the craftsman to carve a chickpea where the third name should go. So much for the name.

As for his birth, the story goes that his mother delivered him without pain or labor, on the third of January — the same day on which, now, the magistrates of Rome offer prayers and sacrifices for the emperor. It is also said that a vision appeared to his nurse and foretold that the baby at her breast would grow up to be a great blessing to Rome. Omens like these can usually be written off as fancy and idle talk, but Cicero himself soon made them look like genuine prophecy. The moment he was old enough for lessons, his talent was so obvious, and his name among the schoolboys so great, that their fathers used to drop in at the school just to lay eyes on young Cicero and be able to say they had personally witnessed the quickness in learning he was famous for. The cruder ones got annoyed with their own sons when they saw the boys walking along with Cicero respectfully placed in the middle. He had exactly the temperament Plato asks of the scholar and philosopher — hungry for every kind of learning, turning up his nose at no branch of knowledge — but his special pull was toward poetry, and a poem he wrote as a boy, in tetrameter verse, called Pontius Glaucus, still survives. As he grew and worked at these arts more seriously, he came to be considered not just the best orator in Rome but the best poet too. The glory of his oratory holds up to this day, despite all the new fashions in speaking that have come along since; but his poetry has been forgotten and fallen completely out of favor, so many gifted poets having come after him.

When he finished his boyhood studies, he attached himself to Philo the Academic, the student of Clitomachus the Romans admired most for his eloquence and loved best for his character. At the same time he kept company with the Mucii, distinguished statesmen and leaders of the senate, and picked up his knowledge of the law from them. For a short stretch he even served in the army under Sulla, in the Marsian war — Rome's war with its Italian allies. But when he saw the state splitting into factions, and the factions dragging everything toward one-man rule, he withdrew into a quiet, contemplative life, keeping company with learned Greeks and devoting himself to his books, until Sulla got control of the government and things settled into some sort of order.

Around this time Chrysogonus, a freed slave of Sulla's, had put in a claim about the estate of a man said to have been executed in the proscriptions, and had bought it himself for 2,000 drachmas. Roscius, the dead man's son and heir, protested, and demonstrated that the estate was actually worth 250 talents. Sulla was furious at having his dealings questioned, and — with Chrysogonus managing the evidence — brought a prosecution against Roscius for the murder of his own father. Not one advocate would help the man; every one of them, dreading Sulla's cruelty, backed away from the case. Abandoned on all sides, the young man came to Cicero for refuge. Cicero's friends urged him on, telling him he would never have a finer or more honorable entry into public life. So he took the defense, won the case, and was widely admired for it.

But he was afraid of Sulla, so he traveled to Greece, putting the word out that he went for his health. And in fairness, there was something to that. He was thin and undernourished, with a stomach so weak that he could keep down only a light, plain diet, and that only late in the evening. His voice was loud and strong but harsh and untrained, and in the heat and vehemence of speaking he always drove it up to such a pitch that people genuinely worried for his health.

When he got to Athens he attended the lectures of Antiochus of Ascalon, and was charmed by the fluency and elegance of the man's language, though he did not approve of his novelties in doctrine. For Antiochus had by now broken away from the so-called New Academy and abandoned the school of Carneades — maybe won over by the argument from the plain evidence of the senses, maybe, as some say, pushed by rivalry and spite against the followers of Clitomachus and Philo into changing his views and adopting, on most points, the doctrine of the Stoics. Cicero preferred the New Academy and held to it, and he made himself a plan: if he found no place in public life, he would retire here, away from the courts and politics, and live out his days quietly in the study of philosophy.

Then came the news of Sulla's death. By now Cicero's body, rebuilt by exercise, had come into vigorous condition; his voice had been trained until it was sweet and full on the ear and properly matched to his constitution. His friends at Rome were writing letter after letter begging him to come back, and Antiochus too kept urging him toward public affairs. So he set to work again on rhetoric — the orator's instrument — and summoned up his political faculties, drilling himself in practice speeches and attending the most celebrated rhetoricians of the day. He sailed from Athens for Asia and Rhodes. Among the Asian masters he worked with Xenocles of Adramyttium, Dionysius of Magnesia, and Menippus of Caria; at Rhodes he studied oratory with Apollonius, the son of Molon, and philosophy with Posidonius. Apollonius, the story goes, knew no Latin, so he asked Cicero to declaim in Greek. Cicero was glad to do it, figuring his faults would be more easily pointed out that way. When he finished, the rest of the audience was astonished, and they fell over one another competing to praise him. But Apollonius, who had shown no excitement while listening, now sat lost in thought for a long while, saying nothing at all. And when Cicero began to look crushed, Apollonius said, "You have my praise and admiration, Cicero — and Greece has my pity. The only glories she had left were learning and eloquence, and now you are carrying those off to Rome as well."

So Cicero, full of expectation, was once more set on a political career — when an oracle took the edge off his ambition. He asked the god at Delphi how he could win the most glory, and the priestess answered: by making his own nature, and not the opinion of the crowd, the guide of his life. Because of that he went cautiously at first in Rome, hanging back from public office, and so was little regarded, and picked up the labels that Rome's crude and ignorant sorts hand out so freely — "the Greek," "the Scholar." But when his own appetite for fame, and his father's and relatives' eagerness, drove him seriously into pleading, he did not rise to the top by slow degrees. He blazed out at full brilliance all at once and left every other advocate at the bar far behind. Yet at first, they say, he was as weak in delivery as Demosthenes had been, and so he studied carefully under Roscius the comic actor and Aesop the tragic one. Of this Aesop they tell a story: playing Atreus on stage, plotting his revenge on Thyestes, he was so carried out of himself in the heat of the scene that he struck a servant running across the stage with his scepter — and killed him on the spot. That was the kind of intensity Cicero learned, and his delivery became no small part of what made his eloquence so persuasive. He used to mock orators who bellowed. "They shout because they cannot speak," he said, "the way lame men get on horseback because they cannot walk." That readiness with a jab — that gift for the witty line — was thought to suit a courtroom advocate perfectly, and audiences loved it; but he overused it, offended a good many people, and got a reputation for spite.

He was appointed quaestor at a time of severe grain shortage, and drew Sicily as his province. At first he made himself unpopular there by forcing the Sicilians to ship their grain to Rome, but once they had experience of his diligence, fairness, and mildness, they honored him beyond any governor they had ever had. And when a number of young Romans from good and noble families were brought before the praetor in Sicily on charges of indiscipline and misconduct on military service, Cicero took their defense, argued it superbly, and got them all acquitted. So he headed back to Rome feeling very pleased with himself — and then, as he tells the story on himself, something absurd happened. In Campania he ran into a prominent citizen he counted as a friend, and asked him what Rome was saying about his achievements — what Rome thought of them — as if the whole city must be ringing with the glory of what he had done. His friend replied, "So where have you been all this time, Cicero?" That flattened him completely. He saw that the news of his deeds had sunk into the city of Rome as into a vast ocean, without leaving one visible ripple of reputation. Later, thinking it over, he concluded that the glory he was chasing was an infinite thing — there was no fixed finish line, no way to measure done — and he scaled back his ambitions considerably. All the same, he remained excessively delighted by his own praise, and stayed passionately hungry for glory to the very end of his life; and that hunger often got in the way of his soundest judgment.

As he threw himself more seriously into public business, one thought struck him as absurd: a craftsman working with tools and vessels — dead objects — knows the name, the place, and the use of every one of them; should a statesman, whose instruments for public work are human beings, be careless and lazy about knowing people? So he memorized not only the names but everything else: exactly where each of the more prominent citizens lived, what land he owned, who his friends were, who his neighbors were; and traveling any road in Italy, Cicero could point out his friends' and acquaintances' estates and country houses as he passed. His own fortune was small — enough to live on, no more — and so people marveled that he took neither fees nor gifts from his clients, and marveled most of all when he took on the prosecution of Verres. This Verres had been praetor of Sicily, and the Sicilians charged him with a long list of crimes committed during his governorship. Cicero got him convicted not by speaking, but, in a sense, by declining to speak. The praetors favored Verres and had stalled the trial with adjournment after adjournment until the very last day, when it was obvious there wouldn't be time for the advocates to be heard and the case brought to a verdict. So Cicero stood up and said there was no need for speeches; he simply produced and examined his witnesses, and then called on the judges to vote. Even so, the record preserves plenty of Cicero's wit from that case. When a man named Caecilius, a former slave who was said to keep Jewish practices, tried to shove the Sicilians aside and take over the prosecution of Verres himself, Cicero asked, "What does a Jew have to do with a pig?" — verres being Latin for a boar. And when Verres started sneering that Cicero lived like a woman, he shot back, "You ought to save that lecture for your sons, at home" — Verres having a son who had gone down a disgraceful road. The orator Hortensius did not dare defend Verres outright, but he was persuaded to appear for him at the assessment of the fine, and took an ivory sphinx as his fee. When Cicero worked an oblique dig at him into one of his speeches and Hortensius protested that he was no good at solving riddles, Cicero said, "Really? And you with the Sphinx in your house."

So Verres was convicted — though Cicero, who set the fine at 750,000 denarii, fell under suspicion of having been bribed to keep the figure low. Still, the grateful Sicilians came to him when he was aedile bringing all sorts of presents from the island; he took no personal profit from any of it, using their generosity only to bring down the public price of food.

He had a very pleasant place at Arpinum, plus a farm near Naples and another near Pompeii, neither worth much. His wife Terentia's dowry came to 100,000 denarii, and he received a bequest worth 90,000 denarii; on this he lived generously but temperately, surrounded by the learned Greeks and Romans of his circle. He rarely if ever sat down to eat before sunset — not because of the press of business so much as for his health and that weak stomach. He was altogether careful and precise about his body, keeping to a fixed regimen of walks and massages, and by managing his constitution this way he built it up, in time, into something healthy, equal to serious strains and hardships. His father's house he turned over to his brother; he himself lived near the Palatine hill so that the people who came to pay court to him would be spared a long walk. And come they did — every day, as many at his door as ever came to Crassus for his riches or to Pompey for his standing with the soldiers, and those two were then the most admired and powerful men in Rome. In fact, Pompey himself used to pay court to Cicero, and Cicero's political work did a great deal to build up Pompey's authority and reputation in the state.

A crowd of distinguished candidates stood with him for the praetorship, and he was elected ahead of them all; and he handled the cases before him with justice and integrity. There is a story that Licinius Macer, a man of real power in the city with Crassus backing him besides, was tried before Cicero for extortion, and was so confident in his own influence and his friends' efforts that while the judges were still voting he went home, got a quick haircut, put on a clean toga as if already acquitted, and was setting off for the Forum again — when at his front door he met Crassus, who told him he had been convicted by every single vote. Macer went back inside, lay down on his bed, and died on the spot. The verdict brought Cicero great credit for running an honest court. Another time a man named Vatinius — rough-mannered, often insolent to the magistrates in court, with his neck covered in swollen tumors — came before the tribunal with some request, and when Cicero said he needed time to consider it, Vatinius remarked that he wouldn't hesitate over such a thing if he were praetor. Cicero turned on him instantly: "Ah, but I don't have your neck."

With only two or three days left in his term, Manilius was brought before him, charged with embezzlement. Manilius had the goodwill and support of the common people, who believed he was being prosecuted only to get at Pompey, whose particular friend he was. So when he asked for time before his trial and Cicero granted him just one day — the very next — the people were furious, because the custom was for praetors to give the accused ten days at least. The tribunes hauled Cicero before the assembly and accused him; he asked to be heard, and said that he had always treated defendants with as much fairness and humanity as the law allowed, so he thought it would be hard to deny the same to Manilius — and that he had deliberately assigned the one day of which, as praetor, he was still master, since it was hardly the move of a man trying to help Manilius to toss his case into another praetor's court. These words produced a wonderful change in the crowd. Loud in his praise, they asked him to take on Manilius' defense himself. He willingly agreed — chiefly for the sake of Pompey, who was away — and taking his place before the people again, he delivered a bold attack on the oligarchical party and on the men who envied Pompey.

Yet when it came to the consulship, the nobles pushed him forward as eagerly as the common people did, for the good of the city, and both parties worked together for his election — and here is why. The new order Sulla had imposed on the state had seemed senseless at first, but with time and habit most people had come to regard it as a settlement they could live with. There were men, however, who wanted to shake and overturn the whole existing arrangement — not from any honorable motive, but for their own private gain — and with Pompey off fighting the kings of Pontus and Armenia, there was no force at Rome sufficient to put down a revolution. These men had for their leader Lucius Catiline, a bold, daring, restless character who stood accused, among other enormous crimes, of taking his own virgin daughter's honor and of killing his own brother — and, fearing prosecution for that murder, he had persuaded Sulla to enter his brother's name, as though the man were still alive, on the proscription lists of those to be executed. With this man as their captain, the city's worst citizens pledged themselves to one another, sealing the pact, among other rites, by sacrificing a man and tasting his flesh. Catiline corrupted a large part of the young men of Rome, keeping each supplied with pleasures, drink, and women, and freely covering the bills for all of it. Beyond the city, all Etruria had been stirred to the edge of revolt, along with much of Gaul this side of the Alps. And Rome itself was primed for upheaval by the lopsided distribution of wealth: men of the highest rank and proudest spirit had bankrupted themselves on shows, banquets, election campaigns, and extravagant building, and the city's riches had drained into the hands of low-born nobodies. It would have taken only a slight push to bring the whole thing down — any man with enough daring could have toppled a commonwealth that sick.

Catiline, wanting a strong base from which to launch his plans, stood for the consulship, and his hopes were high: he expected to share the office with Gaius Antonius, a man fit to lead in neither a good cause nor a bad one, but a useful addition to someone else's power. Most of the good and honest citizens saw what was coming, and they put Cicero up for the consulship. The people embraced him gladly; Catiline was rejected; and Cicero and Gaius Antonius were elected — even though, of all the candidates, Cicero was the only one whose father belonged to the equestrian order rather than the senatorial.

Though Catiline's plot was not yet public knowledge, Cicero's consulship ran into serious preliminary trouble at once, from two directions. On one side, the men whom Sulla's laws had barred from holding office — no small group, in numbers or in influence — came forward as candidates and courted the people, saying much about Sulla's tyranny that was perfectly true and just, except that they were shaking the government at the wrong and most dangerous moment. On the other side, the tribunes were pushing laws to the same effect, proposing a commission of ten men with unlimited powers: as supreme governors they would have the right to sell the public lands of all Italy and Syria, and everything Pompey had newly conquered; to try and to banish whomever they pleased; to plant colonies; to draw money from the treasury; and to levy and pay whatever soldiers they saw fit. Several of the nobles favored this law — none more than Gaius Antonius, Cicero's colleague, who hoped to be one of the ten. What frightened the nobles most was that Antonius was thought to know about Catiline's conspiracy and, buried in debt as he was, not to mind it.

Cicero dealt with that danger first. He arranged a decree assigning Antonius the province of Macedonia, while declining for himself the province of Gaul that was offered to him. That single favor so completely bought Antonius that he was ready, like a hired actor, to play the supporting part in whatever Cicero said for the good of the country. With his colleague tamed and manageable, Cicero could go after the plotters with more confidence. In the senate he delivered a speech against the law of the ten commissioners that left its sponsors with nothing to say for it. They tried again, and, with everything prepared beforehand, summoned the consuls before the popular assembly; Cicero, entirely unafraid, walked out first, ordered the senate to follow him, and not only got the law thrown out but so overwhelmed the tribunes with his oratory that they dropped the rest of their projects altogether.

For Cicero, more than any man, showed the Romans how much charm eloquence can lend to what is good; that justice is invincible if it is well spoken; and that a statesman who means to govern skillfully must, in his actions, always choose what is honest over what is popular, and, in his speaking, know how to present the right and useful course stripped of whatever might give offense. One incident in the theater during his consulship shows what his speaking could do. In the old days the knights of Rome sat mixed in among the common people in the theater, taking whatever seats they found; Marcus Otho, as praetor, was the first to separate them out and assign them their own section, a mark of distinction they hold to this day. The common people took this as an insult, and when Otho appeared in the theater they hissed him, while the knights received him with loud applause. The people hissed harder; the knights clapped louder. Then the two sides turned on each other with insults, and the whole theater fell into chaos. When Cicero heard of it, he came to the theater himself, called the people out to the temple of Bellona, and there scolded and shamed them so effectively that they went straight back into the theater and applauded Otho thunderously, competing with the knights over who could show him the greater honor and respect.

Catiline's fellow conspirators had been cowed at first, even discouraged. But before long their nerve came back. They began meeting again, urging one another to strike boldly and get the thing done before Pompey returned — for word had it that he was already marching his army home toward Rome. The sharpest goads driving Catiline forward, though, were Sulla's old soldiers. They had been discharged and scattered all over Italy, and the largest and fiercest concentration of them lay seeded through the towns of Etruria, passing their days in daydreams of fresh plunder — of looting the piled-up riches of Italy all over again. With Manlius for their leader, a man who had served with real distinction in Sulla's campaigns, they attached themselves to Catiline and came into Rome to back him with their votes at the election. For he was standing for the consulship a second time, and his plan was to kill Cicero in the chaos of election day itself. Even the gods seemed to be signaling the trouble ahead, with earthquakes, lightning strikes, and strange apparitions. Human informants were not lacking either — reliable enough in themselves, but not yet enough to convict a man as noble and as powerful as Catiline. So Cicero postponed the election, summoned Catiline before the senate, and questioned him directly about the reports. Catiline, believing that plenty of senators secretly wanted a revolution — and wanting to give the conspirators in the room a taste of what he was made of — gave back an answer of pure audacity. "What harm am I doing," he said, "if I see two bodies — one lean and wasting, but with a head; the other big and strong, but with no head at all — and I simply give a head to the body that lacks one?" The riddle meant the senate and the people, and it deepened Cicero's alarm considerably. He put on a breastplate, and the leading citizens escorted him from his house in a body, with a crowd of young men accompanying him down to the Field of Mars, where the voting was held. There, quite deliberately, he let his tunic slip from his shoulder so that the armor underneath showed, putting his danger on display for everyone watching. The crowd was outraged on his behalf and packed in close around him to protect him. In the end Catiline was voted down once again, and Silanus and Murena were elected consuls.

Not long afterward, Catiline's soldiers began assembling in Etruria and forming themselves into companies, for the day fixed for the uprising was drawing near. And around midnight, some of the most prominent and powerful men in Rome — Marcus Crassus, Marcus Marcellus, and Scipio Metellus — came to Cicero's house, pounded on the gate, roused the porter, and told him to wake his master and say who was waiting. Here was the story. After dinner, Crassus's porter had handed him a bundle of letters delivered by a stranger. They were addressed to various men, but one — unsigned — was for Crassus himself. That one alone he read. It told him that a great massacre was coming at Catiline's hands, and advised him to slip out of the city. The others he did not open. He came straight to Cicero with the lot, shaken by the danger, and eager to clear himself of the suspicion he lay under because of his friendship with Catiline. Cicero weighed the matter and summoned the senate at daybreak. He brought the letters with him, handed each to the man it was addressed to, and ordered them read aloud. Every one of them told the same story of conspiracy. And when Quintus Arrius, a former praetor, reported to the house that soldiers were mustering in companies across Etruria, and that Manlius was said to be on the move with a large force, hovering near those towns and waiting for news from Rome, the senate passed its decree: everything was placed in the hands of the consuls, who were to take charge of affairs and do whatever they could to save the state. This was no routine measure. The senate reserved it for moments of imminent danger.

Once Cicero had received this power, he handed the situation outside the city to Quintus Metellus and kept the management of Rome itself in his own hands. So enormous was the escort that guarded him whenever he went out that his train filled most of the forum the moment he entered it. Catiline, unable to bear any more delay, resolved to break out and join Manlius himself — but first he ordered Marcius and Cethegus to take their swords, go to Cicero's gates early in the morning as if merely paying a courtesy call, and there fall on him and cut him down. This plot a noblewoman named Fulvia carried to Cicero by night, warning him to be on his guard against Cethegus and Marcius. They came at daybreak, and when they were refused entry they made such an angry scene at the gates that they only deepened the suspicion against them. Cicero then went out and summoned the senate to the temple of Jupiter Stator, which stands at the top of the Sacred Street on the way up to the Palatine. Catiline arrived with the rest, as though he meant to make his defense — and not one senator would sit beside him. Every man on his bench got up and moved away. When he began to speak, they shouted him down. At last Cicero rose and ordered him to leave the city: since one of them did his work with words, he said, and the other with weapons, there needed to be a wall between them. So Catiline marched straight out of Rome with three hundred armed men, surrounded himself — as though he were a lawful magistrate — with the rods, the axes, and the military standards, and made his way to Manlius. With a force now numbering nearly twenty thousand, he moved from town to town, working to talk or terrify them into revolt. It was open war now, and Antonius was sent out to fight him.

The conspirators Catiline had left corrupted inside the city were held together and kept in heart by Cornelius Lentulus, surnamed Sura — a man of noble family but a dissolute life, who had once been expelled from the senate for his debaucheries and was now serving his second term as praetor, the customary route for men trying to win back senatorial rank. The story goes that he picked up the surname Sura like this. As quaestor in Sulla's day, he had run through and squandered an enormous amount of public money. Sulla, furious, called him before the senate to account for it. Lentulus strolled in with perfect coolness and contempt and announced that he had no accounting to give — but they were welcome to this, and he stuck out the calf of his leg, the way boys do at ball when they miss a catch. And since sura is the Latin word for the calf of the leg, the name stuck. On another occasion he was on trial and had bribed some of the jurors; when he was acquitted by a margin of only two votes, he complained that he'd wasted his money on the second one — a single vote would have done the job. This was the man, such by his own nature, whom Catiline had set aflame — and false prophets and fortune-tellers had finished the corruption with empty hopes. They fed him invented verses and oracles, claiming to prove from the Sibylline prophecies that three men named Cornelius were fated to rule Rome as monarchs; that two of them, Cinna and Sulla, had already fulfilled the decree; and that destiny was now hurrying toward the third and last Cornelius with the gift of monarchy in her hands. He must seize it by every means, they said, and not ruin his chance by dawdling, the way Catiline had.

What Lentulus had in mind, then, was nothing small or trivial. He had resolved to kill the entire senate and as many other citizens as he could manage, to burn the city to the ground, and to spare nobody — nobody except Pompey's children, whom he intended to seize and hold as hostages to secure his peace with Pompey. For by now the report was loud and everywhere that Pompey was on his way home from his great eastern campaign. The night chosen for the strike was during the Saturnalia, the December festival. Swords, flax, and sulfur were carried into Cethegus's house and hidden there. They assigned a hundred men to a hundred districts, the city carved up so that with that many fires kindled at once, all of Rome would go up in a single blaze. Others had their orders too: block the aqueducts, and kill anyone who tried to carry water to the flames. While all this was in preparation, it happened that two ambassadors from the Allobroges — a Gallic people then in miserable straits and chafing badly under Roman rule — were staying in Rome. Lentulus and his circle judged them the perfect instruments for stirring Gaul to revolt, and admitted them to the conspiracy. They gave them letters for their own magistrates and letters for Catiline: to the Gauls they promised liberty; Catiline they urged to proclaim freedom for the slaves and march them on Rome. And they sent along with the ambassadors, to carry the letters to Catiline, a man named Titus, a native of Croton.

Such were the schemes of careless men who did their plotting over wine and among women — and Cicero tracked them with sober patience, hard thinking, and truly remarkable intelligence. He had agents everywhere outside, following and tracing every move, and he kept up secret communication with many men who pretended to be in the conspiracy. So he knew all their dealings with the strangers; and lying in wait by night, he took the Crotonian, letters and all — the Allobrogian ambassadors secretly working with him the entire time.

At daybreak he summoned the senate to the temple of Concord, where he read out the letters and examined the informers. Junius Silanus added that several people had heard Cethegus say three consuls and four praetors were marked for death; Piso, an ex-consul, testified to more of the same; and Gaius Sulpicius, one of the praetors, was sent to Cethegus's house, where he found a stockpile of javelins and armor, and a still larger number of swords and daggers, every one freshly sharpened. In the end the senate voted immunity for the Crotonian in exchange for a full confession, and Lentulus stood convicted. He resigned his office — he was still praetor — stripped off his purple-bordered robe there in the senate, and exchanged it for clothing better suited to his new circumstances. Then he and his confederates were handed over to the praetors to be held in open custody.

By evening the common people were massed outside, waiting. Cicero went out to them, told them what had been done, and then, with the crowd escorting him, went to the house of a friend and near neighbor — for his own house was occupied by the women, who were celebrating their secret rites in honor of the goddess the Romans call Bona, the Good Goddess, and the Greeks the Women's Goddess. Her sacrifice is performed every year in the consul's house by his wife or his mother, with the Vestal Virgins attending. Slipping quietly into his friend's house, with only a few men present, Cicero began to turn over what he should do with these prisoners. The severest penalty — the only one that fit crimes so enormous — he shrank from, hesitant and uneasy. Partly it was the gentleness of his own nature; partly he feared looking like a man drunk on his new authority, crushing too harshly men of the noblest families, with the most powerful friends in the city. Yet if he dealt with them mildly, the danger ahead looked dreadful. If they suffered anything short of death, there was no chance they would be reconciled; far more likely they would add fresh rage to their old wickedness and hurl themselves into every kind of recklessness — while he himself, whose reputation for courage did not stand especially high with the crowd as it was, would be written off as the worst sort of coward.

While Cicero sat wavering, a sign came to the women at their sacrifice. On the altar, where the fire seemed to have gone completely out, a great bright flame burst up from the ashes of the burnt wood. The other women were terrified, but the holy virgins told Terentia, Cicero's wife, to go quickly to her husband and tell him to carry out what he had resolved for his country's good — for the goddess had sent a great light on the road to his safety and his glory. Now Terentia was by no nature soft-hearted or timid; she was an ambitious woman who, as Cicero himself says, was far more likely to push her way into his public business than to share her domestic affairs with him. She delivered the message and sharpened him against the conspirators. So did his brother Quintus, and so did Publius Nigidius, one of his philosophical friends, whose counsel he used in his greatest and weightiest affairs of state.

The next day the senate took up the question of punishment. Silanus, asked his opinion first, said the men should be taken to the prison and there suffer the ultimate penalty. One after another, every senator agreed — until it came to Gaius Caesar, the man who was afterward dictator. He was young then, at the very start of his rise, but he had already set his hopes and his policy on the road by which he would eventually turn the Roman state into a monarchy. Others saw none of this coming. Cicero had strong suspicions, though he had never obtained solid proof; and some indeed said that Caesar came within a whisker of exposure and only just slipped clear of him. Others hold that Cicero deliberately looked away from the evidence against him, out of fear of his friends and his power — since it was obvious to everyone that if Caesar were charged alongside the conspirators, they were likelier to be saved with him than he was to be punished with them.

So when Caesar's turn came to speak, he stood up and proposed that the conspirators not be put to death, but that their property be confiscated and the men held instead under guard in whatever Italian towns Cicero himself might choose, until Catiline was defeated. The proposal was the moderate one, and the man delivering it was a formidable speaker — and Cicero himself added no small weight to it, for when he rose he argued the scale both ways, speaking partly for the first motion and partly for Caesar's. His friends all judged Caesar's motion the safer course for Cicero — he would carry less blame afterward if the conspirators were not executed — and so they came down on that side; and Silanus too changed course and walked his own opinion back, saying he had never meant capital punishment, only the "ultimate penalty," which for a Roman senator was imprisonment — the utmost a Roman could suffer. The first man to speak against Caesar's motion was Catulus Lutatius. Cato followed, and in his speech he pointed the finger of suspicion at Caesar himself with such vehemence, and so filled the senate with anger and resolve, that a decree was passed for the conspirators' execution. But Caesar fought the confiscation of their property, thinking it hardly fair that the senators who had thrown out the mildest part of his proposal should help themselves to the harshest. When many insisted, he appealed to the tribunes, who would do nothing; and Cicero himself gave way and dropped that part of the sentence.

Then Cicero went out with the senate to fetch the conspirators. They were not being held together in one place; each praetor had one or another of them in custody. First he took Lentulus from the Palatine and led him down the Sacred Street and through the middle of the forum, a ring of the most eminent citizens encircling and guarding him. The people, frightened by what was happening, filed past in silence — the young men especially, as if, trembling and afraid, they were being initiated into some ancient sacred mystery of aristocratic power. When he had crossed the forum and reached the prison, he handed Lentulus to the executioner and gave the order. Then Cethegus, and after him each of the rest in turn, he brought down and delivered to death. And when he saw many of the conspirators still standing about the forum in knots, ignorant of what had been done and waiting for nightfall, still supposing the men were alive and might yet be rescued, he called out to them in a loud voice: "They have lived." That is how Romans speak of the dead when they want to avoid words of ill omen.

It was evening by the time he made his way home through the forum, and now the citizens no longer escorted him in silence and good order. Instead they received him, as he passed, with shouts and applause, hailing him as the savior and founder of his country. The streets blazed with light from the lamps and torches set up at every doorway, and the women held lights out from the rooftops in his honor, to watch him going home in a magnificent procession of the city's greatest men. Among them were many who had commanded great wars, celebrated triumphs, and added to the Roman empire by land and by sea — and as they walked with him they admitted to one another that although the Roman people owed many commanders of that age a debt for riches, spoils, and power, they owed their safety — the security of everything else — to Cicero alone, for delivering them from a danger so great and so near. Not that stopping the plot and punishing the plotters seemed so wonderful in itself; the extraordinary thing was that the greatest conspiracy ever formed had been crushed with so little disturbance, so little tumult, so little cost. For most of the men who had flocked to Catiline deserted him the moment they heard the fate of Lentulus and Cethegus; and Catiline himself, giving battle to Antonius with the forces that remained to him, was destroyed along with his army.

And yet there were men quite ready to speak ill of Cicero for all this, and to do him harm as well — with leaders among the incoming magistrates of the following year: Caesar, now one of the praetors, and Metellus and Bestia, the tribunes. Taking office a few days before Cicero's consulship expired, they refused to let him address the people, and threw their benches in front of the Rostra to prevent it, telling him that he could, if he liked, take the oath of withdrawal from office and then come straight down again. Cicero accepted the terms and came forward to lay down his consulship; and when silence fell, he recited his oath — not in the usual form, but in a new one all his own: that he had, in truth, saved his country and preserved the empire. And the whole people swore the oath after him, confirming it. This only exasperated Caesar and the tribunes further, and they set about brewing new trouble for him, proposing a law to recall Pompey and his army to put an end to what they called Cicero's tyranny. But it was an enormous piece of luck for Cicero, and for the whole commonwealth, that Cato was a tribune that year. Holding equal power with the others and greater public standing, Cato could block their designs. He swept aside their other projects easily, and in a speech to the people he praised Cicero's consulship so highly that the greatest honors ever given were voted him, and he was publicly proclaimed Father of his Country. He seems to have been the first man ever to receive that title, when Cato gave it to him before the assembled people.

His authority in the city stood very high at this moment. But he made himself widely envied, and offended a great many people — not by any wicked act, but simply because he never stopped praising and glorifying himself. No senate, no assembly of the people, no court of law could convene without being treated to another round of Catiline and Lentulus. He went on to fill his books and writings with self-congratulation, until a prose style that was in itself delightful — a pleasure to hear — became tiresome and grating to his audiences; the ugly habit clung to him like a chronic disease. Still, however greedy he was for his own glory, he was remarkably free of envy toward anyone else's, and was in fact lavishly generous in praising both the ancients and his own contemporaries, as his writings show on every page. Many of these sayings are still remembered. He called Aristotle "a river of flowing gold," and said of Plato's dialogues that "if Jupiter were to speak, he would speak in language like theirs." Theophrastus he liked to call "my own special luxury." Asked which of Demosthenes's orations he thought the best, he answered, "The longest." True, certain would-be imitators of Demosthenes have made a fuss over a line in one of his letters to the effect that Demosthenes sometimes nods off in his speeches — conveniently forgetting the extravagant praise Cicero heaped on the man throughout his career, and the compliment he paid him in naming his own most labored orations, the ones against Antony, his "Philippics." As for the eminent men of his own day, in eloquence or in philosophy, there was not one of them whom he did not make more famous by speaking or writing kindly about him. He obtained Roman citizenship from Caesar, when Caesar was in power, for Cratippus the Peripatetic philosopher, and got the court of the Areopagus to pass a public decree asking Cratippus to remain in Athens to teach their young men and adorn their city. There are letters from Cicero to Herodes, and others to his own son, in which he directs the boy to study philosophy under Cratippus. There is one letter in which he blames Gorgias the rhetoric teacher for luring his son into drinking and high living, and bans the man from his company. That letter, and one other to Pelops of Byzantium, are the only two of his Greek letters written in anger — and rightly enough in the case of Gorgias, if he really was the dissolute character he was reputed to be; but with Pelops, Cicero comes off rather small, whining that the man had failed to arrange certain honors for him by decree of the Byzantines.

Here is another illustration of his hunger for praise: his habit, in the heat of making a speech more striking, of trampling right over decorum and dignity. When Munatius, who had escaped conviction thanks to Cicero's advocacy, promptly turned around and prosecuted Cicero's friend Sabinus, Cicero snapped in the heat of his resentment, "Do you imagine you were acquitted on your own merits, Munatius? Wasn't it rather that I threw so much darkness over the case that the court couldn't see your guilt?" From the Rostra he once delivered a eulogy of Marcus Crassus to great applause — and then, within a few days, publicly abused him from the same spot. "Didn't you stand in this very place two days ago," Crassus called out, "and praise me?" "Yes," said Cicero. "I was exercising my eloquence on a poor subject." On another occasion Crassus declared that no member of his family had ever lived past sixty, then later denied saying it, and demanded, "What could have put it into my head to say such a thing?" "You knew how delighted the people would be to hear it," Cicero answered. "You were fishing for their favor." When Crassus once professed admiration for the Stoic doctrine that the good man is always rich, Cicero said, "Are you sure you don't mean their doctrine that everything belongs to the wise?" — Crassus being notorious for his greed. One of Crassus's sons was thought to look so exactly like a certain Axius that it cast a shadow on his mother's honor; when the young man made a successful speech in the senate and Cicero was asked what he thought of it, he answered in Greek: "Axios Crassou" — worthy of Crassus, and of Axius too.

When Crassus was about to leave for Syria, he preferred to have Cicero as a friend rather than an enemy, so one day he greeted him warmly and said he would come and dine with him — and Cicero received him just as graciously. A few days later, some of his acquaintances came to intercede for Vatinius, then his enemy, saying the man wanted reconciliation and friendship. "What," said Cicero, "does Vatinius want to come to dinner too?" So much for his way with Crassus. As for Vatinius, who had swellings on his neck, Cicero once heard him pleading a case and called him "the swollen orator." And having been told once that Vatinius was dead, then learning shortly afterward that he was alive, Cicero said, "Then may the rascal perish — for bringing news that wasn't true."

When Caesar brought forward a law to divide the farmland of Campania among his soldiers, many senators opposed it. One of the oldest men in the house, Lucius Gellius, declared that it would never pass while he lived. "Then let's postpone it," said Cicero. "Gellius isn't asking us to wait long." There was a man named Octavius, rumored to be of African descent. When he complained during one of Cicero's pleadings that he couldn't hear him, Cicero answered, "Strange — you have holes in your ears." When Metellus Nepos told him he had ruined more men as a witness than he had ever saved as an advocate, Cicero agreed: "I admit it. I have more truth in me than eloquence." A young man suspected of serving his father a poisoned cake was talking loudly about the abuse he planned to heap on Cicero. "Better that," Cicero replied, "than one of your cakes." Publius Sextius hired Cicero as one of his advocates in a case, then insisted on doing all the talking himself and wouldn't let anyone else get in a word. When it was clear the judges were about to acquit him and the ballots were already being cast, Cicero called out, "Speak fast, Sextius — use your time. Tomorrow you'll be a nobody again." He once summoned Publius Cotta as a witness — a man who liked to pass for a lawyer, though he was ignorant and unread. When Cotta answered, "I know nothing about the matter," Cicero said, "You think, perhaps, that we're asking you about a point of law." When Metellus Nepos, in the middle of a quarrel, kept demanding, "Who was your father, Cicero?" he shot back, "In your case, your mother has made that question much harder to answer" — for Nepos's mother had a scandalous reputation. The son was cut from flighty cloth himself. At one point he abruptly threw up his office of tribune and sailed off to Syria to join Pompey — then sailed straight back again, with just as little reason. He buried his tutor Philagrus with far more ceremony than the occasion called for, and set a stone crow on top of the tomb. "That, at least, is fitting," said Cicero. "The man didn't teach you to speak — he taught you to fly around." And when Marcus Appius opened a courtroom speech by saying that his friend had begged him to bring industry, eloquence, and loyalty to the case, Cicero asked, "Then how did you have the heart to supply not one of the three?"

Sharp needling of opponents and rival advocates counts as fair courtroom rhetoric. But Cicero would attack anyone at all for the sake of a joke, and that earned him real hatred. A few samples will show the habit. Marcus Aquinius, who had two sons-in-law in exile, was dubbed King Adrastus, after the legendary king whose daughters likewise both married exiles. Lucius Cotta, a devoted lover of wine, happened to be censor when Cicero was running for the consulship. Cicero, thirsty at the election, took a drink of water while his friends crowded around to screen him. "You're right to be worried," he told them. "The censor might be angry with me — for drinking water." Meeting Voconius one day with his three strikingly ugly daughters, he quoted the verse:

  He raised a family without Apollo's blessing.

When Marcus Gellius, who was reputed to be the son of a slave, read out some letters in the senate in a piercing, carrying voice, Cicero remarked, "Don't be surprised. He comes from a family of criers." And when Faustus Sulla, son of Sulla the dictator — the man whose posted bills had proscribed and condemned so many citizens during his dictatorship — had so wasted his estate and sunk into debt that he was forced to post bills of his own advertising his goods for sale, Cicero observed that he liked the son's bills much better than the father's. Habits like these made him hateful to many people.

And it was over just such grievances that Clodius's faction formed against him. Here is how it began. Clodius was a man of noble family, in the flower of his youth, bold and headstrong. Being in love with Pompeia, Caesar's wife, he slipped secretly into Caesar's house dressed as a music-girl. The women were celebrating there the sacrifice of Bona, the Good Goddess — the rite no man is permitted to see — and there was no man in the house. Clodius, still young and beardless, hoped to pass among the women and reach Pompeia unnoticed. But finding himself inside a great house at night, he lost his way in the corridors, and a maidservant of Aurelia, Caesar's mother, spotted him wandering about and asked his name. Forced to speak, he said he was looking for one of Pompeia's maids, a girl named Abra — and the servant, hearing that this was no woman's voice, shrieked and called the women together. They barred the gates and searched every corner, and at last found Clodius hiding in the room of the very maid who had let him in. The scandal ran through the whole city. Caesar divorced Pompeia, and Clodius was prosecuted for profaning the sacred rites.

Cicero at this time was Clodius's friend, for during the Catiline conspiracy Clodius had been one of his most energetic supporters and protectors. But when Clodius rested his defense on the claim that he had not even been in Rome that day, but far away in the country, Cicero testified that Clodius had come to his own house that very day and talked with him about a number of matters. Which was true — though people believed Cicero testified not so much for the truth's sake as to keep the peace with his wife Terentia. She bore a grudge against Clodius on account of his sister Clodia, who, it was alleged, wanted to marry Cicero and had been working at it through Tullus, one of Cicero's closest friends. Cicero's frequent visits and attentions to Clodia, who lived in their neighborhood, had roused Terentia's suspicions; and being a woman of fierce temper who held the upper hand over Cicero, she pushed him into taking sides against Clodius and giving his testimony. Many other good and honest citizens gave evidence against Clodius as well — for perjuries, for public disorders, for bribing the people, for seducing women. Lucullus produced women-servants who testified that Clodius had slept with his own youngest sister while she was Lucullus's wife; and it was widely believed he had done the same with his two other sisters — Tertia, married to Marcius Rex, and Clodia, married to Metellus Celer. This Clodia was nicknamed Quadrantia, because one of her lovers had slipped her a purse of small copper coins instead of silver, and the smallest copper coin was called a quadrant. It was on this sister's account, above all, that Clodius's name became a byword. Yet for all this, when the common people massed against the accusers and witnesses and the whole prosecution, the judges panicked. A guard had to be posted around them, and most of them scrawled their ballots so that no one could read the letters. The count showed a majority for acquittal, and bribery was said to have done the work. Catulus, when he next met the judges, remarked, "You were quite right to ask for a guard — someone might have taken your money." And when Clodius taunted Cicero that the judges hadn't believed his testimony, Cicero answered, "On the contrary. Twenty-five of them believed me — they voted against you. And the other thirty didn't believe you, because they wouldn't vote to acquit until they had your money in hand."

Caesar, though summoned, gave no testimony against Clodius. He declared that he was not convinced of his wife's adultery, but that he had put her away because Caesar's house had to be clear not only of guilt but of rumor. "Because I wished my wife," Caesar said, "to be not so much as suspected."

Clodius escaped the danger, got himself elected tribune, and immediately turned on Cicero, piling up every charge and stirring up every person he could against him. He won over the common people with crowd-pleasing laws; he decreed rich provinces to each of the consuls — Macedonia to Piso, Syria to Gabinius; he organized the poorest citizens into a standing party for his purposes, and kept a bodyguard of armed slaves around him. Of the three men then holding the greatest power, Crassus was Cicero's open enemy, Pompey played coy with both sides, and Caesar was about to march for Gaul with an army. It was to Caesar — no friend of his, for the suspicions born in the days of the conspiracy still stood between them — that Cicero applied, asking to go along as one of his lieutenants in the province. Caesar accepted him. But Clodius, seeing that Cicero was about to slip beyond the reach of his power as tribune, put on a show of wanting reconciliation. He laid the whole blame on Terentia, spoke of Cicero always in generous terms, and addressed him kindly, as a man who felt no hatred — only a few complaints to raise, in a moderate, friendly way. He so completely talked Cicero out of his fears that Cicero resigned his post with Caesar and returned to public life. At this Caesar was exasperated. He joined Clodius's party against him, turned Pompey away from him entirely, and declared before a public assembly that in his own view the execution of Lentulus and Cethegus and their accomplices without trial had been neither fair nor legal. For this was the charge against Cicero, and this was the accusation he was summoned to answer. So, as a defendant in danger of the verdict, he changed into mourning clothes and went about with his hair untrimmed, in the dress of a suppliant, begging the people for mercy. But Clodius met him at every corner with a gang of insolent ruffians who jeered at his mourning dress and his humiliation, and often broke up his appeals to the people by pelting him with mud and stones.

Even so, nearly the whole equestrian order changed into mourning with him, and no fewer than twenty thousand young men followed him about with untrimmed hair, joining his supplications to the people. Then the senate met to decree that the people should put on mourning as in a time of public grief. When the consuls opposed it, and Clodius surrounded the senate-house with armed men, many senators ran out into the open, crying aloud and tearing their clothes. But even that sight stirred neither shame nor pity. Cicero had to flee, or settle it with Clodius by the sword. He begged Pompey for help — but Pompey had deliberately gotten out of the way and was staying at his country house in the Alban hills. First Cicero sent Pompey's son-in-law Piso to intercede; then he set out to go himself. Pompey, hearing of it, could not bring himself to face the man. He was ashamed, remembering the great battles Cicero had fought for his sake in the commonwealth and all the policy he had shaped for his advantage; but he was Caesar's son-in-law now, and at Caesar's bidding he had set aside every old kindness. He slipped out by another door and dodged the interview. Deserted by Pompey and left standing alone, Cicero turned to the consuls. Gabinius was harsh with him, as usual. Piso spoke more gently: give way, he urged, step aside for a while before the fury of Clodius, wait for the times to change — and be, once again, his country's savior from the storms Clodius was raising.

Cicero took this answer and consulted his friends. Lucullus advised him to stay, certain he would win in the end; others said to flee, because the people would want him back soon enough, once they had had their fill of Clodius's rage and madness. Cicero chose flight. But first he took a statue of Minerva that had long stood honored in his house, carried it up to the capitol, and dedicated it there with the inscription, "To Minerva, Patroness of Rome." Then, with an escort of friends, he left the city in the middle of the night and traveled by land through Lucania, making for Sicily.

The moment his flight became public, Clodius put a decree of exile to the vote and posted an edict barring him from fire and water — the Roman formula of outlawry — and forbidding anyone within five hundred miles of Italy to take him under a roof. Out of respect for Cicero, most people ignored the edict entirely, showing him every courtesy and escorting him along his way. But at Hipponium, a city of Lucania now called Vibo, a certain Vibius — a Sicilian by birth who owed Cicero many favors, among them an appointment as head of the state engineers during his consulship — refused to receive him into his house, sending word instead that he would assign him a spot somewhere in the countryside. And Gaius Vergilius, the praetor of Sicily, who had been on the closest terms with him, wrote telling him to stay out of Sicily. Disheartened by these snubs, Cicero went to Brundisium and put out with a fair wind — but a gale blowing in from the sea carried him back to Italy the next day. He put to sea again and reached Dyrrachium, and it is reported that just as he came ashore, an earthquake struck and the sea convulsed at the same moment — signs, the diviners said, that his exile would not last long, for such things foretell change. Many people visited him out of respect, and the cities of Greece competed over which could honor him most; yet he stayed dejected and inconsolable, gazing back toward Italy like a heartbroken lover. The misfortune had left him so small-spirited, so humbled and crushed, as no one would have expected in a man who had spent so much of his life with study and philosophy. He had often asked his friends, after all, to call him not an orator but a philosopher — philosophy, he said, was his real business; rhetoric was merely the tool he used to get things done in public life. But the hunger for glory has great power to wash the tinctures of philosophy out of a man's soul. Spend enough time governing the crowd and you catch the crowd's passions, by sheer custom and contact — unless a politician engages with public business very carefully indeed, taking on the problems themselves without taking on the passions that ride along with them.

Clodius, having driven Cicero out, burned his farms and country villas, and then his city house, and built a temple to Liberty on the empty site. The rest of his property he put up for auction, advertising it day after day; nobody came to buy. All this made him terrifying to the nobles, and with the common people — whom he had filled with insolence and license — trailing behind him, he finally began testing his strength against Pompey himself, attacking some of the settlements Pompey had made in his conquered territories. The disgrace of this stung Pompey into reproaching himself for his cowardice in abandoning Cicero, and, reversing course, he set himself and his friends to work for a recall. When Clodius blocked it, the senate voted to ratify no public business of any kind until Cicero was brought home. In the consulship of Lentulus the violence over the question rose so high that tribunes were wounded in the Forum, and Cicero's brother Quintus was left for dead, lying unnoticed among the corpses.
