At that the people's mood began to turn. Annius Milo, one of the tribunes, was the first to find the nerve to summon Clodius to trial for violence. Crowds of ordinary citizens, from Rome and the neighboring towns, formed up behind Pompey, and with them at his back he drove Clodius out of the Forum and called the people to vote. It is said the people never passed any measure more unanimously. The senate, competing to outdo the people, sent letters of thanks to the cities that had cared for Cicero in his exile, and decreed that his house and his country estates, which Clodius had destroyed, should be rebuilt at public expense.

So Cicero came home, sixteen months after his exile began. The cities were so joyful, and people so eager to meet him, that his own boast afterward — that Italy had carried him home to Rome on her shoulders — actually fell short of the truth. Even Crassus, his enemy before the exile, went out to meet him voluntarily and made peace — to please his son Publius, as he said, who was one of Cicero's devoted admirers.

Cicero had not been back long when, seizing a day Clodius was out of town, he went up to the capitol with a large following and tore down and destroyed the tribunes' tablets, on which the official acts of Clodius's term were recorded. When Clodius called him to account for it, Cicero answered that Clodius, a patrician, had gotten himself made tribune against the law, so that nothing done in his term was valid. Cato was displeased at this and spoke against Cicero — not that he had any praise for Clodius; he detested the man's whole administration — but he argued that it was an irregular and violent step for the senate to vote so many decrees and acts null, including the acts of Cato's own commission in Cyprus and at Byzantium. The clash opened a breach between Cato and Cicero. It never came to open enmity, but their friendship was cooler and more guarded afterward.

After this, Milo killed Clodius. Arraigned for the murder, he secured Cicero as his advocate. The senate, fearing that the trial of a man as prominent and hot-spirited as Milo might set off riots in the city, gave Pompey the oversight of this and the other trials, to keep both the city and the courts secure. Pompey moved in the night before, posting soldiers on the high ground and ringing the whole Forum with troops. Milo, worried that so unusual a sight would rattle Cicero and hurt his performance, persuaded him to be carried into the Forum in a covered litter and to wait there quietly until the judges were seated and the court was full. For Cicero, it seems, was timid not only in arms but in speaking too: he began nearly every speech in a fright, and in many cases he had barely stopped shaking and trembling by the time his oratory hit its full current and force. Once, defending Licinius Murena against Cato's prosecution and burning to outdo Hortensius, who had made his argument to great applause, he took so little rest the night before, and wore himself so ragged with worry and lying awake, that he spoke well below his usual level. So now, at the trial of Milo, when he stepped out of his litter and saw Pompey posted on the heights as if encamped for war, and arms glittering all around the Forum, he fell apart. He could barely begin his speech; his body shook and his voice caught — while Milo himself sat through the proceedings bold and fearless, having disdained to grow his hair out or put on mourning. That bearing, in fact, seems to have contributed as much as anything to his condemnation. Still, in Cicero's case, people judged it less cowardice for himself than sheer anxiety for his friend.

He was made one of the priests the Romans call augurs, taking the place of the younger Crassus, who had died in Parthia. Then the lot assigned him the province of Cilicia, and he sailed out to it with twelve thousand infantry and two thousand six hundred cavalry. His orders included restoring Cappadocia to the allegiance of its king, Ariobarzanes — which he settled completely without lifting a weapon. Seeing that the Cilicians, watching the great Roman losses in Parthia and the upheavals in Syria, were leaning toward revolt, he steadied them back into loyalty by governing gently. He would accept none of the presents the local kings offered him. He canceled the public banquets the province owed him, and instead entertained its educated and accomplished men daily at his own table — not lavishly, but generously. His house had no doorkeeper, and no visitor ever found him in bed: from early morning he stood or walked in front of his door, receiving whoever came to pay respects. He is said never once to have ordered a man under his authority beaten with rods or stripped of his garments; he never hurled insults in anger, never attached humiliation to punishment. He uncovered an embezzlement of public money on a large scale, and by recovering it relieved the cities of their burdens — while letting those who paid back what they had taken keep their full rights as citizens, with no further penalty. He got a taste of war, too: he defeated the bandits who infested Mount Amanus, and for it his army saluted him imperator. When Caecilius the orator asked him to send panthers from Cilicia for the shows in the theater at Rome, Cicero wrote back, in a spirit of self-congratulation, that there were no panthers left in Cilicia: they had all fled into Caria, indignant that in a province so entirely at peace, they were the only creatures anyone still hunted. Leaving his province, he touched at Rhodes and lingered a while at Athens, savoring the memory of his old studies. He called on the leading men of learning, saw his old friends and companions, and, after receiving from Greece the honors due to him, returned to Rome — where everything was now, as it were, in flames, tipping into civil war.

When the senate would have voted him a triumph, he said he would rather walk behind Caesar's triumphal chariot, if only terms could be made. Privately, he gave advice to both men — writing letter after letter to Caesar, entreating Pompey face to face — doing his best to soothe each of them and talk them back from the edge. But when the thing became incurable, and Caesar was marching on Rome, and Pompey did not dare stay but abandoned the city with many good men beside him, Cicero did not join the flight, and people counted him as siding with Caesar. It is quite clear he was torn, and swung painfully between the two. In his letters he writes, "Which way should I turn? Pompey has the honorable case for war; but Caesar has managed his affairs better, and is more able to protect himself and his friends. I know whom to flee — I just don't know whom to flee to." Then Trebatius, one of Caesar's friends, wrote to him: Caesar's view was that Cicero's best course was to join his side and share his hopes — but if he thought himself too old for that, he should go to Greece and sit quietly there, out of everyone's way. Cicero, offended that Caesar had not written himself, sent back an angry answer: he would do nothing unworthy of his past life. That, at least, is what can be gathered from his letters.

But the moment Caesar marched for Spain, Cicero sailed off to join Pompey. Everyone welcomed him — except Cato, who took him aside privately and scolded him for coming. For his own part, Cato said, it would have been dishonorable to abandon the side he had chosen in politics from the beginning; but Cicero could have been more useful to his country and his friends if he had stayed neutral at home and used his influence to soften whatever came, instead of sailing out here to make himself Caesar's enemy — without reason, without necessity — and a partner in enormous danger. The argument shook Cicero's resolve; so did the fact that Pompey made no real use of him. Though, in truth, he had himself to blame for that. He didn't deny that he was sorry he had come. He belittled Pompey's resources, sniped behind hands at his plans, and could not stop cracking jokes at the expense of his fellow soldiers. He went around the camp wearing a gloomy, sour face himself, yet was forever raising laughs in others whether they wanted to laugh or not. A few examples are worth giving. When Domitius promoted a man with no soldiering in him and defended the choice by saying he was modest and prudent, Cicero asked, "Then why didn't you keep him to tutor your children?" Hearing people praise Theophanes the Lesbian, the army's chief of engineers, for how admirably he had consoled the Rhodians on the loss of their fleet, he said, "What a thing it is to have a Greek in command!" When Caesar was getting the better of it and all but had Pompey under siege, Lentulus remarked that he'd heard Caesar's friends were downcast. "You mean," said Cicero, "that they wish Caesar ill." To a man named Marcius, newly arrived from Italy with word that a rumor was running around Rome that Pompey was blockaded, he said, "And you sailed all the way out here to see it with your own eyes?" After one defeat, Nonius tried to raise spirits by pointing out that seven eagles still remained in Pompey's camp. "That would be very encouraging," said Cicero, "if we were fighting jackdaws." When Labienus kept insisting on certain prophecies that promised Pompey the victory, Cicero said, "Yes — and by that strategy we have opened the campaign by losing our camp."

Cicero was not at the battle of Pharsalia. His health had kept him away. And when it was over, and Pompey had fled, Cato — who still held a considerable army and a great fleet at Dyrrachium — offered Cicero the supreme command. The law was on his side: Cicero had been consul, and his rank came first. But Cicero refused the command, refused, in fact, to have anything more to do with the war at all — and the refusal nearly killed him. Young Pompey and his friends called him a traitor and drew their swords on him, and it was only Cato, throwing himself between them, who plainly saved Cicero's life and got him out of the camp.

He made his way to Brundusium and waited there a long while for Caesar, who was tied up with his affairs in Asia and Egypt. When word came that Caesar had landed at Tarentum and was coming overland toward Brundusium, Cicero set out to meet him — not without hope, but not without dread, either, at the prospect of testing the temper of a conquering enemy in front of a crowd of witnesses. As it turned out, he never had to say or do anything unworthy of himself. The moment Caesar saw him coming, well ahead of the rest of the company, he got down, went to meet him, greeted him, and walked on with him for a good mile or more, talking with him alone. From that day forward Caesar treated him with honor and respect. Even when Cicero published his tribute to Cato, Caesar's reply — praising Cato was hardly something Caesar could let stand — took care to compliment Cicero's own life and eloquence, comparing him to Pericles and Theramenes. Cicero's essay was called the Cato; Caesar's, the Anti-Cato.

There is a story from the same period. When Quintus Ligarius was prosecuted for having borne arms against Caesar, and Cicero took up his defense, Caesar remarked to his friends, "Why not hear a speech from Cicero once more? Ligarius is a guilty man and an enemy — there's no question about that." But once Cicero began to speak, he was extraordinarily moving, and as the speech went on — shifting register after register of feeling, with that charm of language no one else commanded — the color came and went in Caesar's face, and it was plain that every passion in his soul was being stirred in turn. At last, when the orator touched on the battle of Pharsalia, Caesar was so shaken that his body trembled and some of the papers he was holding dropped from his hands. He was simply overpowered — and he acquitted Ligarius.

After that, with the republic changed into a monarchy, Cicero withdrew from public life. He spent his leisure teaching philosophy to any young men who wanted it, and since these tended to be from the noblest and highest-ranking families, the connection made him once again a man of great influence in the city. His real project now was to compose and translate philosophical dialogues, and to find Roman words for the technical terms of Greek logic and physics. It was Cicero, they say, who first — or at least principally — gave Latin names to phantasia, syncatathesis, epokhe, catalepsis, atomon, ameres, kenon, and the rest of that vocabulary, working by metaphor or whatever other device would serve, until the ideas could actually be said, and understood, in Latin. For recreation he turned his facility to poetry; when he set himself to it he could produce five hundred verses in a night. He spent most of his time at his country house near Tusculum, and wrote to his friends that he was living the life of Laertes — Odysseus' old father, digging his garden far from power. Perhaps he was only joking, as was his habit; more likely it was ambition talking, an appetite for public life that made him restless under the new order of things. He rarely went into the city except to pay his court to Caesar. He was usually first among those voting Caesar new honors, and forever hunting for some fresh turn of praise for the man and his works. Take his remark about the statues of Pompey, which had been torn down and which Caesar ordered set up again: Cicero said that by this act of generosity Caesar had raised Pompey's statues — and fixed his own in place.

He had a plan, it is said, to write a history of his country, weaving into it much of the history of Greece and all the stories and legends of the past he had collected. But the plan was overtaken by troubles public and private, misfortunes for most of which he had himself to blame. First, he divorced his wife Terentia. She had neglected him during the war — sent him off short of even the necessities for his journey — and she was no kinder when he returned to Italy. She never joined him at Brundusium in all his long stay there, and when their young daughter made that whole long journey to him, Terentia provided her with neither a decent escort nor the money for her expenses. She had also stripped Cicero's house bare and emptied it, while loading him with many large debts. These were the most respectable reasons given for the divorce. But Terentia denied every one of them, and her husband promptly handed her the most unanswerable defense imaginable: not long afterward he married a young girl. For love of her beauty, Terentia said scornfully. For her money, said Tiro, Cicero's freedman — to clear his debts. The girl was very rich, and Cicero was trustee of her estate, having been left her guardian; and being many millions in debt, he was persuaded by his friends and relations to marry her, disparity of age notwithstanding, and use her fortune to satisfy his creditors. Antony, who brings up this marriage in his answer to the Philippics, jeers at him for putting away a wife he had grown old with — and adds some well-aimed sarcasm about Cicero's habits: domestic, idle, nothing of the soldier about him. Not long after the marriage, Cicero's daughter died in childbirth at the house of Lentulus, whom she had married after the death of her former husband, Piso. Philosophers came from every quarter to console Cicero, but his grief was beyond all bounds — so far beyond that he put away his new bride, because she seemed pleased at Tullia's death. So stood Cicero's domestic affairs.

In the plot now forming against Caesar he had no part at all, although he was among Brutus' closest confidants, and although no man alive was more aggrieved at the present state of things or longed harder for the old republic. The conspirators simply did not trust him: not his temperament, which lacked daring, and not his age — for even the boldest natures grow timid when they are old.

So the deed was done by Brutus and Cassius. And with Caesar's friends gathering, and the city facing the fear of a new civil war, Antony, as consul, convened the senate and made a short speech recommending concord. Cicero followed with a longer one, suited to the moment, and persuaded the senate to imitate the Athenians of old: to decree an amnesty for everything done in Caesar's case, and to assign provinces to Brutus and Cassius. Neither measure came to anything. For the common people were already tender-hearted on their own account, and when they saw Caesar's dead body carried through the forum — when Antony held up the dead man's clothes, soaked in blood and stabbed through in every part — they went mad with rage, hunted the marketplace for the murderers, and ran with firebrands to burn their houses down. The conspirators, forewarned, escaped that danger; but expecting more and worse to come, they left the city.

Antony was instantly riding high, and everyone feared he meant to make himself sole ruler — Cicero more than anyone. Antony, for his part, watching Cicero's influence revive in the state, and knowing how close he stood to Brutus, did not want him in the city. There was old bad blood between them anyway; their characters could hardly have been less alike. Cicero, afraid of what was coming, first thought of going out to Syria as a lieutenant under Dolabella. But Hirtius and Pansa, the consuls-elect who would succeed Antony — good men, and fond of Cicero — begged him not to abandon them, and undertook to put Antony down if only he would stay in Rome. Cicero, neither quite trusting them nor quite distrusting them, let Dolabella go without him and promised Hirtius he would spend the summer in Athens and return when the new consuls took office. So he set out. But his crossing was delayed, and in the meantime — as so often happens — fresh news came suddenly from Rome: Antony had undergone an astonishing change, was doing everything and managing all public business according to the will of the senate, and nothing was wanting but Cicero's presence to settle affairs completely. Cursing his own cowardice, he turned back to Rome. And his first hopes were not disappointed: such crowds poured out to meet him that the greetings and compliments at the gates and along his entrance into the city took up nearly an entire day.

The next day Antony convened the senate and summoned Cicero to it. Cicero did not come. He kept to his bed, claiming to be worn out from his journey; but the true reason seems to have been fear of a plot, based on a warning that had reached him on the road. Antony took the affront badly and sent soldiers with orders to bring Cicero or burn his house down. Many people interceded and pleaded, and Antony settled for taking sureties. After that, when the two men met, they passed each other in silence, each on his guard — until the young Caesar arrived from Apollonia, took up the elder Caesar's inheritance, and fell into a dispute with Antony over twenty-five million in money that Antony was withholding from the estate.

At this point Philippus, who had married the young man's mother, and Marcellus, who had married his sister, brought him to Cicero, and a bargain was struck: Cicero would lend the young man his eloquence and his political influence with the senate and the people, and the young man would give Cicero the protection of his wealth and his arms — for he already had a large following among the dead Caesar's veterans. But there was said to be a stronger reason still for Cicero's readiness. Back when Pompey and Caesar were both alive, Cicero had dreamed that someone was summoning the sons of the senators to the Capitol, because Jupiter intended to declare one of them the future ruler of Rome. The citizens came running and stood around the temple, and the boys sat waiting in their purple-bordered robes, in silence. Suddenly the doors opened, and the boys rose one by one and filed past the god, who reviewed each of them and, to their sorrow, dismissed them — until this one boy passed, and the god stretched out his right hand and said, "Romans, this young man, when he is lord of Rome, shall put an end to all your civil wars." They say the dream left Cicero with a distinct image of the boy's face, which he retained perfectly, though he did not know who he was. The next day, going down to the Campus Martius, he met the boys just leaving their exercises — and the first of them was the very face from his dream. Astonished, Cicero asked who his parents were. And he turned out to be this young Caesar: son of Octavius, a man of no great eminence, and of Atia, daughter of Caesar's sister — which was why Caesar, having no children of his own, had made the boy heir to his house and property by his will. From then on, they say, Cicero made a point of greeting the boy whenever they met, and the boy took the attention kindly. And by one more turn of fortune, he had been born in the very year of Cicero's consulship.

Those were the reasons people talked about. But what really bound Cicero to Octavian was, first, his hatred of Antony, and then his nature, which could never resist an honor. He meant to fold Octavian's power into his own political designs — and the young man courted him so far as to call him Father. Brutus was disgusted. In his letters to Atticus he said flatly that Cicero, by courting Octavian out of fear of Antony, had shown he was not working for his country's liberty at all — he was bidding for a kinder master. All the same, Brutus took up Cicero's son, then studying philosophy at Athens, gave him a command, and employed him in various services, with good results. Cicero's own power in the city now stood at its greatest height. He did whatever he pleased. He engineered Antony's defeat and expulsion, and sent out the two consuls, Hirtius and Pansa, with an army to finish him — and meanwhile persuaded the senate to vote Octavian the lictors and insignia of a praetor, as though the young man were the republic's defender. But then Antony was beaten in battle, both consuls died of it, and the two armies combined and went over to Octavian. The senate, now frightened of this young man and his extraordinary luck, tried with honors and gifts to draw the soldiers away from him and cut his power down — arguing that with Antony in flight, the state had no further need of armies.

That alarmed Octavian, and he privately sent friends to beg Cicero to procure the consulship for the two of them together. Cicero, they said, should run things however he pleased once they were in office; he would hold the real power and govern the young man, who wanted nothing but the name and the glory of it. Octavian himself admitted afterward that this was exactly what it had been: afraid of ruin and in danger of being abandoned, he had made timely use of Cicero's ambition, coaxing him to stand for the consulship with his help and support.

And here, more than at any other moment of his life, Cicero let himself be carried away — an old man taken in by the flattery of a boy. He canvassed for him; he brought the senate around for him; and his friends blamed him for it even at the time. Soon enough he saw what he had done: he had ruined himself and betrayed the liberty of his country. For the young man, once established in the consulship, said his farewells to Cicero — and reconciled himself with Antony and Lepidus, pooled his power with theirs, and divided up the government among the three of them like a piece of family property. Together they drew up a list of more than two hundred men who were to die. The bitterest fight in all their bargaining was over Cicero. Antony would agree to nothing unless Cicero was the first man killed; Lepidus sided with Antony; Octavian held out against them both. They met secretly, just the three of them, for three days, near the town of Bononia, at a spot not far from the camp, ringed round by a river. For the first two days, it is said, Octavian fought for Cicero's life. On the third day he yielded, and gave him up.

The exchange ran like this: Octavian abandoned Cicero; Lepidus abandoned his own brother Paulus; Antony abandoned Lucius Caesar, his uncle on his mother's side. So completely did rage and fury strip them of their humanity — proof, if any were needed, that no beast is more savage than man when his power is as great as his anger.

While all this was being arranged, Cicero was with his brother at his country house near Tusculum. When news of the proscriptions reached them, they decided to move to Astura, a seaside villa of Cicero's, and take ship from there for Macedonia, to join Brutus — for word had already come of his strength in that province. They traveled in separate litters, crushed with grief, stopping again and again along the road so the litters could be brought together and the two of them could grieve side by side. Quintus was the more despairing of the two, for he had begun to think about how little he had for the journey — he had brought, as he said, nothing from home — and even Cicero's provision was slender. Better, they decided, for Cicero to hurry on ahead while Quintus went home to gather what they needed. So it was resolved; and they embraced, weeping, and parted.

Within a few days Quintus was betrayed by his own servants to the men searching for him, and was killed, together with his young son. Cicero was carried on to Astura, found a vessel there, boarded at once, and sailed as far as Circeii with a favorable wind. The pilots wanted to put straight out to sea again; but Cicero — whether he feared the sea, or had not yet wholly given up his faith in Octavian — went ashore, and walked a dozen miles inland, as if making for Rome. Then his resolution failed him. He changed his mind, turned back to the coast, and passed the night there in fearful, tangled thoughts. At moments he resolved to slip secretly into Octavian's house and kill himself on the altar of the household gods, to fasten a divine vengeance upon him; but fear of torture drove him off that course too. And so, after churning through one confused and broken plan after another, he finally let his servants carry him by sea to Caieta, where he had a house — a pleasant retreat in the heat of summer, when the cool seasonal winds come in off the water.

There was a temple of Apollo at that place, a little way up from the shore. As Cicero's vessel rowed in toward land, a flight of crows rose from the temple with a great racket and made straight for the ship, settling along both ends of the yard, some croaking, some pecking at the ends of the ropes. Everyone took it for an evil omen. Cicero went ashore anyway, entered his house, and lay down on his bed to rest. But many of the crows settled at the window, cawing dismally, and one of them flew down onto the bed where Cicero lay wrapped up, and with its beak, little by little, dragged the covering away from his face. At the sight of that, his servants turned on themselves: were they going to stand there as spectators at their master's murder, doing nothing to defend him, while the very birds came to help him and keep watch over his undeserved suffering? Partly by pleading, partly by force, they got him up and carried him in his litter down toward the sea.

But by now the assassins had arrived with a squad of soldiers — Herennius, a centurion, and Popillius, a tribune whom Cicero had once defended when he was prosecuted for the murder of his own father. Finding the doors barred, they broke them open. Cicero was not there, and the people in the house said they did not know where he was; but a young man, it is stated — a freedman of Cicero's brother Quintus, educated by Cicero himself in liberal learning, Philologus by name — told the tribune that the litter was being carried down to the sea through the walks, where the trees grew close and shady. The tribune took a few men and ran round to the spot where it would come out. Cicero saw Herennius running through the walks, and he ordered his servants to set the litter down. Then, stroking his chin with his left hand, the old familiar gesture, he looked steadily at his murderers — covered in dust, his hair and beard untrimmed, his face worn down with his troubles — and most of the men standing there covered their own faces while Herennius killed him. He stretched his neck out of the litter to take the blow. He was in his sixty-fourth year. Herennius cut off his head, and, on Antony's orders, his hands too — the hands that had written the Philippics. For that was Cicero's own name for his speeches against Antony, and the Philippics they are called to this day.

When these remnants of Cicero were brought to Rome, Antony was holding an assembly for the election of officials. Hearing the news, and seeing the head and hands, he cried out, "Now let there be an end of our proscriptions." He ordered the head and hands fastened up over the Rostra, the speakers' platform — a sight that made the Roman people shudder, for they believed they were looking not at the face of Cicero but at the image of Antony's own soul. In all of this he did do one just thing: he handed Philologus over to Pomponia, the wife of Quintus. And she, once she had the man in her power, among other terrible punishments made him cut off his own flesh piece by piece, and roast it, and eat it — so, at least, some writers have related. But Tiro, Cicero's own freedman, never so much as mentions Philologus' treachery.

A long time afterward, I have been told, Octavian — Augustus by then — was visiting one of his daughter's sons, and found the boy with a book of Cicero's in his hands. The boy, frightened, tried to hide it under his gown. Augustus saw, took the book from him, and stood there reading a great part of it; then he handed it back and said, "An eloquent man, my child — an eloquent man, and a lover of his country." And as soon as he had finally defeated Antony, being then consul himself, he chose Cicero's son as his colleague in the office. It was under that consulship that the senate took down Antony's statues, canceled every other honor he had held, and decreed that from then on no man of his family should ever bear the name Marcus. Thus the final acts of Antony's punishment were handed, by the divine powers, to the family of Cicero.

Brutus

Marcus Brutus was descended from the famous Junius Brutus — the man the ancient Romans honored with a bronze statue on the Capitol, set among the images of their kings with a drawn sword in his hand, in memory of the courage and resolve with which he drove out the Tarquins and destroyed the monarchy. But that ancient Brutus was hard by nature, inflexible, like steel tempered too far, and his character had never been softened by study and reflection. His rage and hatred against tyrants carried him so far that when his own sons conspired with the Tarquins, he had them executed. The Brutus whose life we are now writing was a different kind of man. To a naturally good disposition he added the improvements of learning and the study of philosophy, and a temperament that was by nature serious and gentle he roused into action by throwing himself into business and public affairs — so that he seemed a character framed, piece by piece, for virtue. Even his bitterest enemies, the people who hated him on account of the conspiracy against Caesar, granted him this much: whatever was honorable or generous in that whole affair they credited entirely to Brutus, and whatever was savage and cruel they laid at the door of Cassius — his kinsman and close friend, but not his equal in honesty or purity of purpose.

His mother, Servilia, came from the family of Servilius Ahala. When Spurius Maelius was whipping the people into rebellion and scheming to make himself king, this Ahala tucked a dagger under his arm, walked out into the marketplace, and, on the pretense of having some private business with him, came up close. As Maelius bent his head to hear what he had to say, Ahala struck him with the dagger and killed him. That much — the descent on his mother's side — everyone admits. As for his father's family, those who bore Brutus any hatred or ill will over Caesar's murder claimed he was no descendant of the Brutus who expelled the Tarquins at all: that line, they said, was wiped out with the execution of the two sons, and this man's ancestor was a commoner, the son of a steward named Brutus, whose family rose to office and dignity only in recent times. But the philosopher Posidonius writes that the history is accurate about the two grown sons being put to death — and that a third son, still an infant, was left alive, and from him the family came down to Marcus Brutus. He adds that in his own day there were several distinguished men of the house whose faces bore a striking resemblance to the statue of Junius Brutus. But enough on that subject.

Cato the philosopher was the brother of Servilia, Brutus's mother, and of all the Romans it was Cato whom his nephew most admired and studied to imitate; later he married Cato's daughter Portia. As for the Greek schools of philosophy, there was hardly one Brutus had not heard out and made some progress in, but his deepest loyalty went to the Platonists — and to the ancient Academy at that, for he never much approved of what is called the modern, or middle, Academy. All his life he was a great admirer of Antiochus of the city of Ascalon, and he took Antiochus's brother Aristus into his own house as a friend and companion — a man inferior to many philosophers in learning, but the equal of the best of them in evenness of temper and steadiness of conduct. As for Empylus, whom Brutus and his friends mention often in their letters as one of the household, he was a rhetorician, and he left behind a short but well-written history of the death of Caesar, titled Brutus.

In Latin, Brutus had trained himself well enough to deliver public addresses and plead a case. But in Greek he cultivated the terse, pointed, Spartan manner of speaking — the famous Laconic style — and many passages of his letters show it. At the beginning of the war he wrote to the people of Pergamum: "I hear you have given Dolabella money. If you gave it willingly, admit that you have wronged me; if unwillingly, prove it by giving willingly to me." Another time, to the Samians: "Your counsels are careless and your performance slow. What do you suppose the end will be?" And of the Patareans: "The Xanthians distrusted my kindness and made their country the grave of their despair. The Patareans trusted themselves to me and enjoy their liberty in every particular. You may choose: the judgment of the Patareans, or the fortune of the Xanthians." That is the style for which some of his letters are remembered.

While he was still a very young man he accompanied his uncle Cato to Cyprus, on the mission against Ptolemy. When Ptolemy killed himself, Cato — detained on the island of Rhodes by some necessary business — had already sent one of his friends, a man named Canidius, to take charge of the king's treasure. But soon, not feeling sure of Canidius's honesty, he wrote to Brutus to sail for Cyprus at once from Pamphylia, where Brutus was resting after just recovering from an illness. Brutus obeyed, but with real reluctance — partly out of respect for Canidius, who was being tossed out of the job by Cato with such disgrace, and partly because he considered the whole commission beneath him: petty accounting work, unsuited to a young man in the prime of life who lived for books and study. Still, he applied himself to the business and handled it so well that Cato praised him highly. Having converted all of Ptolemy's goods into ready money, he sailed with the greater part of it in his own ship to Rome.

But when the state split into two great factions — when Pompey and Caesar took up arms against each other and the whole empire dissolved into confusion — everyone assumed Brutus would choose Caesar's side. For his father, years before, had been put to death by Pompey. Brutus, however, held that it was his duty to put the public interest ahead of his private feelings, and judging Pompey's to be the better cause, he took Pompey's part — this, though formerly he would not so much as greet Pompey or acknowledge him if they happened to meet, counting it a pollution to exchange a single word with his father's murderer. Now, looking on him as the general of his country, he placed himself under Pompey's command and sailed for Cilicia as lieutenant to Sestius, who governed that province. But he found no opportunity there for any great service, and hearing that Pompey and Caesar were now closing on each other and preparing for the battle on which everything depended, he came of his own accord to Macedonia to share the danger. At his arrival, it is said, Pompey was so surprised and so pleased that he rose from his chair in full view of everyone around him and saluted and embraced Brutus as one of the chief men of his party. All the time he was in the camp, apart from the hours he spent in Pompey's company, he gave to reading and study — and he did not neglect them even on the day before the great battle. It was the middle of summer, the heat was crushing, the camp had been pitched beside marshy ground, and the men carrying Brutus's tent took forever to arrive. Though he was worn out and out of sorts on every count, and had barely managed by midday to anoint himself and eat a sparing meal, he spent his time until evening — while most of the others were asleep, or lost in dread of what the battle would bring — writing a condensed edition of Polybius.

It is said that Caesar had such regard for him that he ordered his commanders on no account to kill Brutus in the battle, but to spare him: if he surrendered willingly, they were to bring him in safe; if he resisted, they were to let him escape rather than do him any violence. And this, it is believed, Caesar did out of tenderness for Servilia, Brutus's mother. In his youth Caesar had been intimate with her, and she passionately in love with him, and since Brutus was born about the time their love was at its height, Caesar had some belief that the boy was his own child. There is a story told about this. When the great matter of Catiline's conspiracy — the plot that came near destroying the state — was being debated in the senate, Cato and Caesar were both on their feet, arguing opposite sides, when a little note was carried in and handed to Caesar, who read it silently to himself. At this Cato cried out that Caesar was corresponding with and receiving letters from the enemies of the state; and when a number of other senators joined the outcry, Caesar handed the note, just as he had received it, to Cato — who read it and found it to be a love letter from his own sister Servilia. He threw it back at Caesar with the words, "Keep it, you drunkard," and returned to the business of the debate. That is how public and notorious Servilia's love for Caesar was.

After the great defeat at Pharsalia, with Pompey escaped to the sea and Caesar's army storming the camp, Brutus slipped out privately by one of the gates that led to marshy ground, thick with water and reeds, and traveling through the night got safely to Larissa. From Larissa he wrote to Caesar, who expressed great joy to hear he was safe, told him to come, and not only forgave him freely but honored him and esteemed him among his closest friends. At that moment no one could say with any certainty which way Pompey had fled; so Caesar took a short journey alone with Brutus and sounded out his opinion, and after some discussion between them, concluding that Brutus's guess was the right one, he dropped every other consideration and set out straight for Egypt in pursuit. Pompey had indeed reached Egypt, just as Brutus guessed he would — and there he met his fate.

Brutus meanwhile obtained Caesar's forgiveness for his friend Cassius. He also pleaded the cause of the king of Libya, and though the man was buried under the sheer weight of the charges against him, by entreaties on his behalf Brutus preserved for him a great part of his kingdom. It is reported that Caesar, hearing Brutus speak in public for the first time, said to his friends, "I don't know what this young man intends — but whatever he intends, he intends it intensely." For that was Brutus's nature: a firmness of mind that did not easily bend or grant favors to everyone who asked, but which, once set in motion by reasoned judgment and deliberate moral choice, drove toward its object in whatever direction it took and was almost certain not to fail. To unjust petitions he was unmovable; no flattery could reach him. He held that being conquered by shameless, fawning importunity — which some people dress up with the name of modesty or soft-heartedness — was the worst disgrace a great man could suffer. And he used to say that people who could deny nothing had, he suspected, not behaved very well in the flower of their youth.

When Caesar was about to cross to Africa on his expedition against Cato and Scipio, he entrusted Brutus with the government of Cisalpine Gaul — to the great good fortune of that province. For while the people of other provinces groaned under the violence and greed of their governors, suffering as much oppression as slaves or captives of war, Brutus governed so mildly that he actually made his subjects amends for their miseries under previous rulers — and he directed all their gratitude for it to Caesar himself. So it was a most welcome and pleasant spectacle for Caesar, passing through Italy on his return, to see the cities under Brutus's command flourishing, and Brutus himself adding to his honor and traveling agreeably in his company.

Now several praetorships fell vacant, and everyone expected that the most distinguished of them — the one called the praetorship of the city — would go either to Brutus or to Cassius. Some say the two men, who already had certain small differences between them, were set much further at odds by this competition, even though they were connected by family: Cassius had married Junia, Brutus's sister. Others say the rivalry was Caesar's own doing — that he had privately encouraged each of them with hopes of his favor until he led them on and provoked them into open competition. Brutus had only his reputation for honor and virtue to set against Cassius's many brilliant exploits against the Parthians. Caesar heard both sides, and deliberating on the matter among his friends, said, "Cassius has the stronger claim — but Brutus must have the first praetorship." So another praetorship was given to Cassius, who was less grateful for the one he gained than furious over the one he lost. And in all other respects Brutus had as large a share of Caesar's power as he cared to take. Had he wished, he could have been first among all Caesar's friends, with authority and command beyond any of them. But Cassius, and the company Cassius kept, kept pulling him away from Caesar. He had not yet fully reconciled with Cassius after their rivalry, but he listened to Cassius's friends, who were forever warning him not to be so blind as to let Caesar soften and tame him — to shun the kindness and favors of a tyrant, which Caesar showed him, they hinted, not to honor his merit but to unstring his strength and sap the vigor of his purpose.

Nor was Caesar entirely without suspicion of Brutus; there was no shortage of informers accusing him. But while Caesar feared the man's high spirit, his great name, and his powerful friends, he trusted in his character. When he was told that Antony and Dolabella were plotting some disturbance, he said, "It's not the fat, long-haired men I'm afraid of — it's the pale, lean ones," meaning Brutus and Cassius. And later, when people were running Brutus down to him and warning him to beware, he took hold of his own flesh with his hand and said, "What — do you think Brutus cannot wait out the time of this little body?" — as if to say that no one but Brutus was fit to succeed him in his power. And indeed it seems beyond doubt that Brutus could have been the first man in Rome if he had only had the patience to be second to Caesar for a little while — to let Caesar's power pass its peak and decline, and the fame of his great deeds fade by degrees. But Cassius — a violent-tempered man, and one who hated Caesar out of private grievance rather than love of the public, hated the man and not the tyranny — kept firing him up and driving him on. Brutus resented the rule; Cassius hated the ruler. Among the grudges Cassius counted against Caesar was the loss of his lions, which he had procured when he was aedile-elect: Caesar found them at Megara when Calenus took that city, and seized them for himself. Those beasts, they say, proved a calamity for the Megarians. When the city was on the point of falling, the Megarians broke open the lions' dens and struck off their chains, meaning to loose them on the enemy coming through the gates — but the lions turned on the Megarians themselves, tearing apart crowds of unarmed people as they ran, so that even the enemy pitied the sight.

Some say this was the chief provocation that drove Cassius to conspire against Caesar — but they are quite wrong. From his boyhood Cassius carried a natural hatred and bitterness toward the whole race of tyrants, and he showed it while still a schoolboy, when he attended the same school as Faustus, the son of Sulla. Faustus was boasting among the boys, glorifying his father's absolute power, and Cassius stood up and boxed his ears — two or three good blows. Faustus's guardians and relatives meant to take the matter to law, but Pompey forbade it, and sending for both boys, examined the business himself. And Cassius is reported to have said, "Go on, then, Faustus — dare to repeat, right here, the words that provoked me, and I'll hit you again just as I did before." That was the temper of Cassius.

Brutus, though, was roused and pushed toward the undertaking by many voices: the urging of his close friends, and letters and appeals from citizens he did not know. Under the statue of his ancestor Brutus — the Brutus who overthrew the kings — people wrote: "If only we had a Brutus now!" and "If only Brutus were alive!" And Brutus's own tribunal, the platform where he sat as praetor, was found covered every morning with writings like these: "You are asleep, Brutus." and "You are no longer Brutus." The ones responsible for all this were Caesar's flatterers, who among the other invidious honors they kept fastening on him crowned his statues at night with diadems, hoping to lead the people into hailing him king instead of dictator. But exactly the opposite came to pass — as I have told more fully in the life of Caesar.

When Cassius went around sounding out friends for the plot against Caesar, every man he approached agreed readily — on one condition: that Brutus lead it. What the enterprise needed, in their view, was not hands or daring but the reputation and authority of a man like Brutus, to consecrate the deed, as it were, by his mere presence — to justify it simply by being part of it. Without him, they felt, they would go into the act with less heart and lie under heavier suspicion afterward; for if the cause had been just and honorable, people would be certain Brutus would not have refused it. Cassius thought all this over, and then paid Brutus his first visit since their falling-out. When the courtesies of reconciliation had been exchanged and their old friendship renewed, he asked whether Brutus intended to be present in the senate on the first of March; for word was going around, he said, that Caesar's friends meant to move on that day that he be made king. When Brutus answered that he would not be there, Cassius pressed him: "But what if they send for us?" "Then it will be my business," Brutus replied, "not to hold my peace, but to stand up boldly and die for the liberty of my country." And Cassius, stirred, answered: "But what Roman will let you die? Do you not know yourself, Brutus? Do you imagine the writings on your praetor's tribunal were left there by weavers and shopkeepers, and not by the first and most powerful men of Rome? From other praetors they expect handouts and shows and gladiators — but from you they claim, as a debt of your inheritance, the destruction of tyranny. They are ready to suffer anything for your sake, if you will only show yourself to be the man they believe you are, and expect you to be." With that he threw his arms around Brutus and embraced him; and so, agreed, they parted, each to sound out his own friends.

Among Pompey's old friends there was a certain Gaius Ligarius, whom Caesar had pardoned after he was accused of bearing arms against him. This man — feeling not so much gratitude for the pardon as resentment of the power that had made a pardon necessary — hated Caesar, and was one of Brutus's most intimate friends. Brutus visited him and found him sick. "Ligarius," he said, "what a time you've picked to be sick!" At those words Ligarius raised himself on his elbow, took Brutus by the hand, and said, "But Brutus — if you are on any design worthy of yourself, I am well."

From that time on they tested the inclinations of every acquaintance they dared trust, shared the secret, and drew into the plot not only their close friends but every man they believed bold, brave, and unafraid of death. That is why they hid the plot from Cicero, though they all trusted him fully and loved him well: they feared that his naturally timid disposition, with the caution of old age now added to it — his habit of weighing every particular, refusing to take a single step without absolute security — would blunt the edge of their resolve in a business that demanded all possible speed. There were two other companions of Brutus whom he likewise left out: Statilius the Epicurean, and Favonius, the devoted admirer of Cato. His reason was this. One day, in conversation with them, testing them at a safe distance, he floated a question of the kind philosophers dispute, to see where they stood; and Favonius gave it as his judgment that civil war was worse than the most lawless monarchy, while Statilius held that a wise and sensible man ought not to plunge himself into danger and turmoil on account of wicked or foolish people. But Labeo, who was present, contradicted them both; and Brutus, as though the question were a knotty one and hard to settle, held his peace for the moment — and afterward revealed the whole design to Labeo, who readily took it up. The next step, they agreed, was to win over the other Brutus — Decimus Brutus, surnamed Albinus — a man of no great daring or courage in himself, but valuable for the number of gladiators he was maintaining for a public show, and for the great confidence Caesar placed in him. When Cassius and Labeo raised the matter with him, he gave them no answer; but he sought out a private interview with Brutus himself, and on learning that Brutus was their captain, he readily agreed to take part. Among the rest, too, it was the name of Brutus that won over the most men and the best. And though they swore no oath of secrecy and used no sacred rite to bind their loyalty to one another, they all kept the design so close, so warily, so silently among themselves, that although the gods gave warning of it through prophecies and apparitions and signs in the sacrifices, no one could believe it was real.

Brutus now carried the knowledge that the best men in Rome — the noblest in birth, in character, in courage — were staking everything on him, and as he ran through all the dangers they were walking into, the weight of it began to tell. In public he mastered himself as well as he could, keeping his uneasiness locked away and his face composed. But at home, and above all at night, he was a different man. Sometimes the working of his cares jolted him out of sleep against his will; sometimes he lay awake for hours, turning his difficulties over and over. His wife, lying beside him, could not help noticing that he was full of some unusual trouble, and that he was wrestling with a question both dangerous and hard to resolve.

Portia, as was said before, was the daughter of Cato. Brutus, her cousin, had married her when she was still very young — not a girl, though, but a widow, for her first husband had died leaving her a son named Bibulus; a little book of his, the Memoirs of Brutus, survives to this day. Portia was devoted to philosophy, deeply in love with her husband, and full of a courage that had thought behind it, and she resolved not to pry into Brutus's secrets until she had first put herself to the test. She sent all her attendants out of her room, took a little knife of the kind used for trimming nails, and gave herself a deep gash in the thigh. Blood poured out, and soon afterward came violent pain and a shivering fever from the wound. Brutus was beside himself with worry for her, and at the very height of her pain she spoke to him: "I am Cato's daughter, Brutus, and I was brought into your house not like a concubine, to share only your bed and your table, but to be your partner in everything — your good fortune and your bad alike. On your side of the marriage I have no complaint. But on mine, what proof of my love, what satisfaction can I offer you, if I may not share your hidden griefs, or be admitted to the counsels that call for secrecy and trust? I know women are supposed to be too weak by nature to be trusted with a secret. But surely, Brutus, a good birth, a good upbringing, and the company of honorable people count for something in the forming of character. I can say this: I am the daughter of Cato and the wife of Brutus. Before now I put less confidence in those titles than I do today — for I have tested myself, and I find I can defy pain." With that she showed him her wound and told him of the trial she had made of her own constancy. Brutus was astonished. He lifted his hands to heaven and begged the gods to stand by his enterprise, so that he might show himself a husband worthy of such a wife as Portia. Then he set about comforting her.

A meeting of the senate was called at which Caesar was expected to appear, and the conspirators agreed this was their opportunity. There they could all gather without drawing suspicion, and there, they hoped, the noblest and leading men of the state, assembled in one place, would stand up the moment the great deed was done and claim the common liberty. Even the setting seemed heaven-sent. The senate was to meet in a portico attached to the theater, with a large recess in which stood a statue of Pompey, put up by the state when Pompey beautified that quarter of the city with the porticos and the theater. To this place the senate was summoned for the middle of March — the Ides of March, as the Romans call the day — as if some power greater than human were steering Caesar there, to meet his punishment for the death of Pompey.

As soon as it was light, Brutus went out with a dagger that no one but his wife knew he carried. The rest met at Cassius's house, and together they escorted Cassius's son down to the forum — this was the day the boy was to put on the toga of manhood, as it is called. From there they all went to Pompey's porch and waited, expecting Caesar to come to the senate without delay. And here, more than anywhere, anyone who had known what these men intended would have marveled at their unshaken calm and their steady nerve on the edge of so dangerous an undertaking. Several of them were praetors, obliged by their office to hear and decide cases, and they not only listened patiently to everyone who came before them pleading against one another, as if they had nothing else on their minds — they decided the cases with as much precision and good judgment as they had shown attention in hearing them. When one man refused to accept Brutus's ruling and appealed loudly to Caesar, with much shouting and calling of witnesses, Brutus looked around at the bystanders and said, "Caesar does not hinder me, nor will he hinder me, from acting according to the laws."

Yet a whole string of unnerving accidents was thrown in their way by pure chance. First and worst was Caesar's long delay: the day was wearing on, and he was being kept at home by his wife, forbidden to go out by the soothsayers because of some defect in his sacrifice. Then a man came up to Casca, one of the company, took him by the hand, and said, "You hid the secret from us, but Brutus has told me everything." While Casca stood there stunned, the man laughed and went on, "How did you get rich so fast, that you can afford to stand for aedile?" That was how close Casca came to letting the secret slip, on nothing more than the ambiguity of a phrase. Next Popilius Laenas, a senator, greeted Brutus and Cassius more warmly than usual, leaned in, and whispered, "My prayers are with you, that you may accomplish what you have in mind — and my advice is not to delay, for the thing is no secret now." Then he walked away, leaving them deeply afraid that the plot had leaked. Meanwhile a messenger came running from Brutus's house with the news that his wife was dying. For Portia, strung tight with waiting for the event and unable to bear the weight of her anxiety, could hardly keep herself indoors. At every little noise or cry she would start up like a woman in a bacchic frenzy, demanding of everyone who came in from the forum what Brutus was doing, and sending out one messenger after another. At last, after the long waiting, her body simply gave out. Her mind was overwhelmed by doubt and fear; she lost her hold on herself and began to faint. There was no time even to reach her room — sitting where she was among her women, she was seized by a sudden swoon and a great stupor; her color drained away and her speech failed entirely. At the sight her women screamed, neighbors came running to Brutus's door to ask what was wrong, and the report quickly spread that Portia was dead — though in fact, with her women's help, she revived after a little while and came to herself again. When the news reached Brutus he was badly shaken, and no wonder; but he did not let his private grief carry him away from his public purpose.

For now came word that Caesar was on his way, carried in a litter. Discouraged by the bad omens of his sacrifice, he had decided to take up no serious business that day but to put everything off, pleading illness. The moment he stepped out of the litter, Popilius Laenas — the same man who a little earlier had wished Brutus success in his undertaking — went up to him and talked with him at length, Caesar standing still all the while and appearing to listen closely. The conspirators (to give them that name) could not hear what was said; but guessing, from what their own consciences told them, that this conversation was the betrayal of their plot, they lost heart again, and looking at one another they agreed from each other's faces that they must not wait to be arrested but should kill themselves at once. Cassius and some of the others already had their hands on the daggers under their robes and were drawing them out when Brutus, watching Laenas's expression and gestures closely, saw that the man was earnestly pleading a case, not making an accusation. He said nothing — too many outsiders were mingled in the crowd — but with a cheerful face he steadied Cassius. And after a little while Laenas kissed Caesar's hand and went his way, making it plain that all his talk had been about some private business of his own.

When the senators had gone in ahead to the chamber where they were to sit, the rest of the company gathered close around Caesar's chair as if they had some petition to bring him, and Cassius, turning his face toward Pompey's statue, is said to have invoked it, as though the marble could hear his prayers. Trebonius, meanwhile, engaged Antony in a long conversation at the door and kept him talking outside. As Caesar entered, the whole senate rose. As soon as he sat down, the conspirators crowded around him, and they put forward one of their own number, Tillius Cimber, to plead for his brother, who was in exile. The others joined their prayers to his, taking Caesar's hand and kissing his head and his chest. At first Caesar brushed their appeals aside; then, when they would not stop, he rose violently to his feet — and Tillius caught his toga with both hands and tore it down from his neck, which was the signal. Casca, standing behind him, drew his dagger and struck the first blow, a shallow wound near the shoulder. Caesar seized the handle of the dagger and cried out in Latin, "Casca, you villain, what are you doing?" — while Casca, in Greek, shouted to his brother, "Brother, help!" By now blows were coming at Caesar from many hands. He looked around for a way to break out — and then he saw Brutus with his dagger drawn against him. At that he let go of Casca's hand, covered his head with his robe, and gave his body up to their blows. They pressed in on him so eagerly, so many daggers hacking at once, that they cut one another; Brutus took a wound in the hand, and every one of them was smeared with blood.

With Caesar dead, Brutus stepped out into the middle of the chamber meaning to make a speech, calling on the senators to stay and take heart. Instead they bolted in terror and total disorder, jamming the doorway in the crush — though no one was chasing them. For the conspirators had firmly resolved to kill no one but Caesar, and to summon everyone else to liberty. Indeed, when they had been planning the deed, all the others had thought it essential to cut down Antony along with Caesar. He was an arrogant man, they argued, a would-be monarch, and one who by his easy familiarity had built up real influence with the soldiers — and on top of his naturally lofty and ambitious temper he now held the dignity of the consulship, as Caesar's colleague. But Brutus opposed the plan, insisting first on the plain injustice of it, and then holding out the hope that Antony might change. He refused to despair of so gifted and honorable a man, such a lover of glory: once Caesar was gone, Antony might well be stirred by the nobility of their attempt to seize the chance and join them in restoring his country's liberty. So Brutus saved Antony's life. But Antony, in the general panic, put on a plebeian's clothes and fled. Brutus and his party marched up to the Capitol, holding up their bloody hands and naked swords as they went and proclaiming liberty to the people. At first every street was full of shouting and cries, and the wild running back and forth of people caught up in the shock and passion of the moment only added to the tumult in the city. But no more blood was shed, and no shops were plundered, and so the senators and many of the people took courage and went up to the men on the Capitol. When a crowd had gathered, Brutus made a speech well calculated to win the people over and well suited to the state of affairs. They applauded and shouted for him to come down, and at that the whole party took confidence and descended into the forum. Most of them walked mixed in with the crowd, but many of the most eminent men escorted Brutus in the midst of them, with great honor, all the way from the Capitol, and set him on the rostra. At the sight of him the crowd — a jumbled mass, primed for a riot — was struck with awe, and waited for what he would say in order and in silence; and when he began to speak, they heard him out quietly and attentively. But that not everyone was pleased with the deed became clear soon enough: when Cinna began to speak and to accuse Caesar, the crowd erupted in sudden rage and abused him so violently that the whole party thought it best to withdraw again to the Capitol. There Brutus, expecting a siege, sent away the most eminent of those who had accompanied them up, thinking it wrong that men who had no share in the deed should share in the danger.

The next day the senate met in the temple of the Earth. Antony, Plancus, and Cicero gave speeches urging concord and a general amnesty, and it was decreed not only that the men should be free of all fear and danger, but that the consuls should consider what honors and dignities ought to be conferred on them. With that the senate adjourned; and when Antony had sent his son up to the Capitol as a hostage, Brutus and his company came down, and greetings and invitations passed back and forth through the whole gathering. Antony invited Cassius to dinner and entertained him; Lepidus did the same for Brutus; and the rest were taken in by their various friends and acquaintances. At daybreak the senate met again and voted thanks to Antony for having smothered a civil war in its cradle; then Brutus and his associates who were present received formal praise, and the provinces were assigned and distributed among them. Crete was allotted to Brutus, Africa to Cassius, Asia to Trebonius, Bithynia to Cimber, and to Decimus Brutus (Albinus) the Gaul that lies along the Po.

After this they took up the question of Caesar's will and the arrangements for his funeral. Antony asked that the will be read publicly, and that the body be given no mean or private burial, for fear of further inflaming the people. Cassius fought this fiercely, but Brutus yielded and gave his consent — and in doing so he seems to have made his second great mistake. Sparing Antony's life had already earned him some blame from his own party, as the setting up of a dangerous and formidable enemy against the conspiracy; now, in letting Antony manage the funeral, he fell into an error that was total and beyond recovery. For first, when the will revealed that Caesar had left seventy-five drachmas to every single Roman citizen, and had given the public his gardens beyond the Tiber, where the temple of Fortune now stands, the whole city was set ablaze with love for him and a passionate sense of what they had lost. And then, when the body was carried into the forum and Antony, as custom required, delivered the funeral oration in Caesar's praise, he saw the crowd moved by his words — and shifting into the register of grief, he unfolded Caesar's bloody garment and held it up, showing them how many places it was pierced, and the number of his wounds. After that there was nothing to be seen but chaos. Some screamed for the murderers to be killed; others — as had happened long before, in the days when Clodius led the people — tore the benches and tables out of the surrounding shops and heaped them together into one great funeral pyre, laid Caesar's body on it, and set it alight, there in the middle of a district ringed with temples and sacred precincts, so that the burning took on the look of a religious rite. As soon as the flames leaped up, the crowd surged in from every side, snatched half-burnt brands off the pyre, and ran through the city to set fire to the houses of Caesar's murderers. But the conspirators had fortified themselves well beforehand, and turned the danger back.

There was, however, a certain poet named Cinna, who had no part whatever in the conspiracy — he was, on the contrary, one of Caesar's friends. This man dreamed that Caesar invited him to supper, and that he declined, but Caesar pressed and urged him until at last, taking him by the hand, he led him into a deep, dark place, where Cinna was forced against his will to follow, in dread and bewilderment. After this vision he lay in a fever most of the night. Even so, in the morning, hearing that Caesar's body was to be carried out for burial, he was ashamed to stay away from the ceremony, and went out and joined the crowd — which Antony's speech had already whipped into fury. They caught sight of him, and mistaking him not for the Cinna he was but for the man who had lately denounced Caesar in a speech to the people, they fell on him and tore him limb from limb.

It was this, above all, together with the change Antony had worked in the city, that alarmed Brutus and his friends into leaving Rome for their own safety. They stopped first at Antium, planning to return as soon as the people's fury had burned itself out — which they expected would happen soon and easily in a crowd so unsettled, so easily swept by any sudden gust of passion, especially since they had the senate on their side. The senate, though it turned a blind eye to the men who had torn Cinna to pieces, made a strict search for those who had attacked the houses of Brutus's and Cassius's friends, and arrested them for punishment. By now the people, too, were growing unhappy with Antony, who they saw was setting up a kind of monarchy of his own. They longed for Brutus to come back, and they expected him to appear in person at the games and spectacles which, as praetor, he owed the public. But Brutus had intelligence that many of Caesar's old soldiers — men who had received lands and towns from him — were lying in wait, slipping into the city a few at a time, and he did not dare come himself. In his absence, though, the people got shows of the utmost magnificence and expense: he had bought up great numbers of wild beasts of every kind and ordered that none be returned or held back — all were to be spent freely on the public spectacles. He traveled to Naples himself to engage a large company of performers, and hearing of one Canutius, an actor everyone praised, he wrote to his friends to use every persuasion to bring him to Rome — for as a Greek, Canutius could not be compelled. He wrote to Cicero as well, begging him on no account to miss the shows.

This was how matters stood when everything was suddenly changed again by the arrival in Rome of the young Octavian. He was the son of Caesar's niece, and Caesar had adopted him and made him heir by his will. At the time of the murder he was pursuing his studies at Apollonia, where he was also waiting to meet Caesar on his way to the Parthian expedition he had planned. The moment he heard of Caesar's death he came to Rome; and to win the people's favor he took the name of Caesar for himself and paid out, to the letter, the money the will had left the citizens — and by that stroke he outmaneuvered Antony. By money and gifts, freely scattered among the soldiers, he gathered to his side a great number of the men who had served under Caesar. Cicero himself, out of sheer hatred of Antony, took the young man's part — and Brutus took this so hard that he lashed him in his letters. Cicero, he wrote, evidently had no objection to a master; what he feared was a master who disliked him; and all his speaking and writing in the young man's praise showed that his real aim was a comfortable slavery. "But our forefathers," Brutus wrote, "could not stomach even gentle masters." For his own part, he said, he had not yet finally decided between war and peace; but on one point he was fixed and settled — he would never be a slave. He was amazed, he added, that Cicero should dread the dangers of a civil war and not be far more afraid of a dishonorable and shameful peace; and that the reward Cicero sought for pulling down Antony's tyranny was the privilege of setting up the young Caesar as tyrant in his place. Such is the tone of Brutus's first letters to Cicero.

The city had now split into two armed factions, one gathering around Octavian and the other around Antony, while the soldiers sold themselves practically at public auction, going over to whichever bidder paid the most. Brutus despaired of anything good coming out of such a scramble. He resolved to leave Italy, traveled overland through Lucania, and came down to Elea on the coast.

From there it was decided that Portia should return to Rome. She was overwhelmed with grief at parting from Brutus, and did everything she could to hide it — but for all her self-command, a painting she happened upon betrayed her. Its subject was Greek: Hector parting from Andromache as he went out to fight, handing her their little son Astyanax while her eyes stayed fixed on her husband. The likeness to her own situation undid her. She burst into tears at the sight of it, and went back several times a day to look at the picture and weep before it. On one of these occasions, when Acilius, one of Brutus's friends, quoted the lines Andromache speaks to Hector in Homer —

  Hector, you are father and mother to me,
  and brother too, and my own dear husband —

Brutus smiled and answered, "But I could never say to Portia what Hector says back to Andromache:

  Go tend your loom, and give your maids their orders.

Her body may lack the strength for what only men's strength can do, but in spirit she fights for her country as bravely as any of us." The story comes from the memoirs of Brutus written by Bibulus, Portia's own son.

From Elea Brutus took ship and sailed to Athens, where the people received him with open delight — acclamations in the streets, public honors voted in his name. He lodged with a private friend and became a faithful listener at the lectures of Theomnestus the Academic and Cratippus the Peripatetic, throwing himself into philosophy so completely that he seemed to have set aside all thought of public business and to be entirely at leisure for study. All the while, unsuspected, he was secretly preparing for war. He sent Herostratus into Macedonia to win the commanders there over to his side, and he himself quietly recruited every young Roman then studying at Athens and kept them at his disposal. One of them was Cicero's son, whom Brutus praised everywhere, saying that sleeping or waking he could not help admiring a young man of so great a spirit and so fierce a hatred of tyranny.

At last he began to act in the open. Learning that several Roman ships loaded with treasure were coming that way from Asia, commanded by a friend of his, he went to meet him near Carystus. He found him there, persuaded him to hand over the ships, and then gave an unusually splendid dinner — it happened to be Brutus's birthday. When the drinking began and the cups were being filled with toasts to victory for Brutus and liberty for Rome, Brutus, wanting to stir the company further, called for a larger bowl. Then, holding it in his hand, with no prompting and no forethought, he suddenly recited this verse aloud:

  But deadly fate and Leto's son have killed me.

Some writers add that at the last battle he fought at Philippi, the watchword he gave his soldiers was "Apollo" — and they conclude from this that his sudden, unaccountable outburst was an omen of the defeat he suffered there.

Antistius, the commander of the treasure ships, gave him at parting five hundred thousand from the money he was carrying to Italy. And all the soldiers still left over from Pompey's army — men who had been wandering Thessaly since their general's defeat — flocked to him readily and joyfully. He also took from Cinna five hundred horsemen being led to Dolabella in Asia. Then he sailed to Demetrias and seized a great store of weapons that had been made on the dead Caesar's orders for the Parthian war and were now due to be shipped to Antony. Macedonia itself was put into his hands, surrendered by Hortensius the praetor, and all the kings and princes of the surrounding country came offering their services.

Then came word that Gaius, Antony's brother, had crossed over from Italy and was marching straight to join the forces Vatinius commanded at Dyrrhachium and Apollonia. Brutus decided to get there first and seize those troops himself, and set off at once with the men he had around him. The march was brutal — rough country, deep snow — but so fast that he left the servants carrying the morning's provisions far behind. Near Dyrrhachium, worn down by fatigue and cold, he fell into the condition called bulimia — a ravenous, fainting hunger that seizes both men and animals after hard labor, especially in deep snow. Perhaps the body's natural heat, driven inward when the cold takes hold, burns up all its nourishment at once; or perhaps the sharp, thin vapor that rises off melting snow cuts into the body and destroys the warmth as it escapes through the pores — for sweating seems to happen when the heat meets the cold at the body's surface and is quenched there. But I have discussed all this more fully in another place.

Brutus was growing faint, and no one in the whole army had anything for him to eat, so his servants were driven to turn to the enemy: they went right up to the city gates and begged bread from the sentries on duty. The moment the sentries heard what condition Brutus was in, they came out themselves, bringing food and drink with them. In return, when Brutus took the city, he showed the greatest kindness not only to those men but to all the inhabitants, for their sake.

Meanwhile Gaius Antonius reached Apollonia and summoned all the soldiers in the area to join him there. But they kept going over to Brutus instead, and suspecting that even the people of Apollonia leaned the same way, he abandoned the city and moved to Buthrotum — losing on the way three cohorts, cut to pieces by Brutus as they marched. He then tried to seize some strongpoints around Byllis that the enemy had occupied first, and was beaten in a pitched battle by young Cicero, to whom Brutus had given the command — an officer he used often, and with great success. Not long after, Gaius himself was caught in marshy ground, far from his supports. With the man in his power, Brutus would not let his soldiers attack. Instead he circled the enemy with his cavalry and gave orders that no one was to be killed — in a little while, he said, they would all be on his side. And so it proved: they surrendered, the men and their general together, and Brutus now had a large and formidable army.

For a long time he treated Gaius with every mark of honor and respect, and left him the insignia of his office, even though — as some report — letters kept arriving from Rome, from Cicero in particular, urging Brutus to put him to death. But when he found that Gaius was tampering with his officers and trying to stir up a mutiny among the soldiers, he put him aboard a ship and held him there under close guard. The soldiers Gaius had corrupted withdrew to Apollonia and sent word asking Brutus to come to them there. He answered that this was not the Roman custom: those who had given offense must come to their general themselves and beg forgiveness for it. They came, and he pardoned them.

As he was preparing to cross into Asia, news reached him of the upheaval at Rome. The young Octavian, backed by the senate against Antony, had driven his rival out of Italy and begun to grow formidable in his own right — standing for the consulship against all law, and keeping up great bodies of troops the commonwealth had no need of. And when he saw that the senate, unhappy with his conduct, was beginning to look abroad to Brutus, decreeing and confirming province after province under his command, he took alarm. He sent messengers to Antony proposing reconciliation and friendship; then he ringed the city with his troops and got himself chosen consul — though he was hardly more than a boy, barely twenty years old, as he says himself in his memoirs.

The moment he entered office he opened a judicial process against Brutus and his associates, for murdering the first man of the city — a holder of the highest offices of Rome — without trial or condemnation. He appointed Lucius Cornificius to accuse Brutus and Marcus Agrippa to accuse Cassius. No one appeared to answer the charges, so the judges were compelled to pass sentence and condemn them both.
