It is said that when the crier, following custom, called out from the tribunal in a loud voice summoning Brutus to appear, the people groaned aloud and the noblest citizens hung their heads in grief. Publius Silicius was seen to burst into tears — and for that, not long afterward, his name went down on the list of the proscribed. After this the three men — Octavian, Antony, and Lepidus — settled their quarrels completely, divided the provinces among themselves, and drew up the proscription list: the catalogue of those marked for slaughter, two hundred men in all. Cicero was among the slain.

When this news reached Brutus in Macedonia, he felt himself under a compulsion, and sent orders to Hortensius to kill Gaius Antonius — in revenge for Cicero his friend and for a Brutus who was his own kinsman, both proscribed, both dead. It was on this account that Antony, when he later captured Hortensius at the battle of Philippi, slaughtered him on his brother's tomb. As for Brutus, he wrote that he felt more shame at the cause of Cicero's death than grief at the event itself, and that he could not help blaming his friends at Rome. They were slaves, he said, more by their own doing than by that of the men who now tyrannized them: they could stand there, and watch, and endure things that ought to have been unbearable even to hear about.

He now moved his army — already very large — across into Asia, and ordered a fleet fitted out in Bithynia and around Cyzicus. Traveling overland himself, he made it his business to settle and secure the cities as he went, and gave audiences to the princes of every region he passed through. And he sent orders into Syria for Cassius to give up his planned expedition into Egypt and come to him. It was not to win an empire for themselves, he reminded him, but to free their country, that they were wandering like this with an army they had gathered for one purpose: destroying the tyrants. If they remembered that first resolve and meant to hold to it, they should not stray far from Italy, but hurry back and rescue their fellow citizens from oppression.

Cassius obeyed the summons and turned back, and Brutus went out to meet him. They met at Smyrna — the first time they had seen each other since parting at the Piraeus in Athens, one bound for Syria, the other for Macedonia. The sight of each other's forces filled them both with joy and confidence. They had fled Italy like the most miserable of exiles — no money, no arms, not a ship, not a soldier, not a city to rely on — and in this short time they had come together again so well furnished with shipping and money, with an army of horse and foot, that they were in a condition to contend for the empire of Rome.

Cassius wanted to show Brutus every bit as much honor as Brutus showed him, but Brutus generally beat him to it, coming to Cassius most of the time because Cassius was the older man and his health was the weaker. Men generally rated Cassius an expert soldier, but harsh-tempered and hot, a commander who preferred to rule by fear rather than love — though among his close friends he gave way easily to joking and would happily play the clown. Brutus, on the other hand, was esteemed by the people for his virtue, loved by his friends, admired by the best men, and not hated even by his enemies. For he was a man of singularly gentle nature and great spirit, untouched by anger or pleasure or greed, steady and unbending in holding to whatever he judged right and honorable. And what won him the deepest affection and the highest standing was the complete trust men placed in his intentions.

For no one had ever supposed that even Pompey the Great, had he beaten Caesar, would have submitted his power to the laws. He would have kept the management of the state in his own hands, soothing the people with the milder name of consul or dictator or some other title softer than king. And everyone was persuaded that Cassius — a man governed by anger and passion, and often carried beyond the bounds of justice for his own advantage — was enduring all these hardships of war and travel and danger to win dominion for himself, not liberty for the people. As for the earlier disturbers of Rome's peace — a Cinna, a Marius, a Carbo — it is plain that they set their country up as a stake for the winner, and all but admitted in so many words that they were fighting for empire. But even Brutus's enemies, we are told, never laid that charge against him. Many people, indeed, heard Antony himself say that Brutus was the only man who conspired against Caesar out of a sense of the glory and the apparent justice of the deed; the rest rose against the man himself, out of private envy and grudges of their own.

And it is plain from his own letters that Brutus relied not so much on his forces as on his own virtue. Shortly before the final engagement he wrote to Atticus that his affairs stood as well as fortune could make them: either he would conquer and restore liberty to the Roman people, or he would die and be beyond the reach of slavery. With everything else, he said, settled and certain, one question alone remained open — whether they would live or die as free men. He added that Mark Antony had paid a fitting price for his folly: he could have been counted alongside Brutus and Cassius and Cato, and had chosen instead to attach himself to Octavian — and even if the two of them were not beaten now, they would soon enough be fighting each other. In this he proved no bad prophet.

While they were at Smyrna, Brutus asked Cassius for a share of the great treasure he had piled up, because his own funds were all spent fitting out a fleet large enough to command the whole Mediterranean. Cassius's friends argued against it. "It is not right," they said, "that the money you keep so frugally, and gathered at the cost of so much resentment, should be handed to him to spend making himself popular and buying the favor of the soldiers." Cassius gave him a third of everything anyway. Then they parted, each to his own command.

Cassius took Rhodes and behaved there with no mercy at all — though at his first entry, when some of the people hailed him as lord and king, he had answered that he was neither king nor lord, but the destroyer and punisher of a king and lord. Brutus, for his part, sent to the Lycians demanding a supply of money and men. But Naucrates, their popular leader, persuaded the cities to resist, and they occupied a string of hills and small mountains to block Brutus's passage. Brutus first sent out a party of cavalry, who caught them at their meal and killed six hundred. Then, after taking the small towns and villages all around, he released every prisoner without ransom, hoping to win the whole nation over by goodwill. But they stayed stubborn, angry at what they had suffered and contemptuous of his kindness, until at last he drove the most warlike of them into the city of Xanthus and laid siege to it. They tried to escape by swimming and diving down the river that flows past the town, but were caught by nets let down across the channel, with little bells at the top that rang the moment anyone was taken. Then they made a sortie by night, seized some of the siege engines, and set them on fire; but the Romans spotted them and beat them back to the walls — and a strong wind was blowing, and it drove the flames onto the battlements so fiercely that several of the nearby houses caught fire. Brutus, afraid the whole city would be destroyed, ordered his own soldiers to help put the fire out.

But the Lycians were suddenly seized by a strange, unbelievable desperation — a frenzy that can hardly be described except as a violent appetite for death. Women and children, slave and free, people of every age and every condition fought from the walls to drive off the very soldiers who had come to help them, and with their own hands gathered reeds and wood and anything that would burn, spreading the fire through the whole city, feeding it with whatever fuel they could find and goading its fury by every possible means. When the flames had run through Xanthus and ringed it and blazed up on every side, Brutus, sick at heart over their calamity, rode around the walls on horseback, desperate to save the city, stretching out his hands to the Xanthians and begging them to spare themselves and their town. No one listened. They sought their own destruction by every means there was — not just men and women, but even boys and small children, with a hideous outcry, leaping into the fire, or from the walls, or onto their parents' swords, baring their throats and begging to be struck. After the city was destroyed, a woman was found who had hanged herself, her young child hanging at her neck, and in her hand the torch with which she had set her own house alight. So tragic was the sight that Brutus could not bear to look at it, and wept when it was merely described to him. He proclaimed a reward for any soldier who could save a Xanthian; and only a hundred and fifty, it is said, were found — saved against their wills. So the Xanthians, after a long stretch of years, as though the fated cycle of their destruction had come round at last, repeated by their own desperate act the calamity of their ancestors, who in the Persian war had burned their city and destroyed themselves in the very same way.

After this, finding the people of Patara resolved to resist and hold their city against him, Brutus was very reluctant to besiege it, dreading that the same madness might seize them too. But he held some of their women as prisoners, and he released them all without ransom. They went home to husbands and fathers of the highest rank and told them what a man Brutus was — how temperate, how just — and persuaded them to yield and put their city into his hands. From then on all the cities of the region came over to him, surrendering themselves, and found him kind and merciful beyond anything they had hoped. And the contrast told. Cassius, at that same time, was forcing every Rhodian to hand in all the silver and gold he privately owned — which raised eight thousand talents — and on top of that had fined the city another five hundred. Brutus took no more than a hundred and fifty talents from the Lycians, and having done them no other injury of any kind, marched away with his army into Ionia.

Through the whole course of this expedition Brutus did many memorable acts of justice, in rewards and punishments alike; but I will tell one in particular, because it gratified him — and the noblest Romans with him — more than all the rest. When Pompey the Great, stripped of his great power by Caesar, fled to Egypt and landed near Pelusium, the guardians of the boy king held a council on what to do, and could not agree — some were for receiving him, others for driving him away from Egypt. But Theodotus, a Chian by birth, then attached to the king as a paid teacher of rhetoric and admitted to the council only for want of better men, undertook to prove that both sides were wrong — those who advised welcoming Pompey and those who advised sending him off. In their situation, he argued, only one course was truly expedient: seize him and kill him. And he closed his argument with the proverb: "Dead men don't bite." The council came round to his opinion, and Pompey the Great — a lesson in how incredible and unforeseen events can be — was slain, thanks, as the sophist himself later had the impudence to boast, to the rhetoric and cleverness of Theodotus. Not long afterward, when Caesar came to Egypt, some of the murderers got their just reward and died the wretched deaths they deserved. Theodotus borrowed a little more time from fortune — time for a poor, despised, vagrant life — but he did not stay hidden from Brutus as he passed through Asia. He was seized and executed, and his death made him more memorable than his life ever had.

About this time Brutus sent word to Cassius to join him at the city of Sardis, and when Cassius was on the road, Brutus went out with his friends to meet him. The whole army, drawn up in formation, saluted each of the two men as imperator. But as usually happens in great enterprises, where many friends and many commanders are engaged, jealousies had been building between Brutus and Cassius — grievances, private accusations, things carried back and forth by other men — and they decided to settle all of it before they took up any other business. The moment they arrived, they withdrew into a room together and shut the door. Alone, just the two of them, they began first to air their complaints, then to argue hotly, then to accuse each other outright — and at last they were so carried away by passion that they fell to harsh words and finally burst into tears. Their friends standing outside were astonished to hear them so loud and so angry, and feared something worse might follow; but they had been ordered not to enter, and no one dared interrupt. No one, that is, except Marcus Favonius. He had been a devoted admirer of Cato, and he kept up the character of a philosopher not so much by learning or wisdom as by a wild, vehement manner all his own. Now he came charging at the door, and the attendants tried to hold him back — but stopping Favonius was hard work once his wildness had seized him. He was fierce in everything he did and would try anything to get his way. He was a senator, but he considered that among the least of his distinctions; what he really prized was a kind of Cynic's license to say whatever he pleased to anyone — rudeness so free and so badly timed that people who chose to take it as a joke could sometimes forgive it. This Favonius forced his way past the men guarding the doors, burst into the chamber, and in a theatrical voice declaimed the lines Homer gives to old Nestor:

  Take my advice — for I am older than you both.

At this Cassius burst out laughing. But Brutus threw him out, calling him an impudent dog and a counterfeit Cynic. Still, for the moment, the interruption put an end to their quarrel, and they parted. That night Cassius gave a supper, and Brutus invited the guests; and when everyone had taken their places, in came Favonius, fresh from his bath. Brutus called out that he had not been invited and told him to take the farthest couch. Instead Favonius shoved his way in and lay down on the middle one, the place of honor — and the evening passed in playful talk, with no shortage of either wit or philosophy.

The next day, on a complaint brought by the people of Sardis, Brutus publicly disgraced and condemned Lucius Pella, a man who had been a censor of Rome and whom Brutus himself had trusted with responsible posts — the charge was embezzling public money. The verdict stung Cassius badly. Only a few days earlier, two friends of his own had been accused of exactly the same crime; he had scolded them in private, cleared them in public, and kept them in his service. Now he accused Brutus of too much rigor, too much strict justice, at a time that called for policy and accommodation. But Brutus told him to remember the Ides of March — the day they had killed Caesar, a man who did not himself plunder and squeeze mankind, but who was the strength and shelter of the men who did. "If there were any decent excuse for setting justice aside," he said, "it would have been better to put up with the crimes of Caesar's friends than to let our own go unpunished. Then we could have been accused of nothing worse than cowardice. Now, after all our pains and all our dangers, we lay ourselves open to the charge of injustice." That was Brutus's purpose, and that was the rule he acted by.

About the time they were preparing to cross from Asia into Europe, it is said that a strange and wonderful sign appeared to Brutus. He was by nature a man of little sleep, and by discipline and a spare diet he had trimmed his hours of rest down to almost nothing. He never slept during the day, and at night only when all his business was done and there was no one left awake to talk with. But now, with the war begun, the whole weight of it on his mind, and the outcome pressing on him, he would take a short first sleep after supper and then spend the rest of the night on his most urgent affairs. If he could dispatch them early and save a little time, he read — until the third watch of the night, when the centurions and tribunes were accustomed to come to him for orders. So it was that one night, before the army left Asia, he was very late alone in his tent, a dim lamp burning beside him, the whole camp hushed and silent. He was deep in thought, turning something over in his mind, when he sensed that someone had come in. He looked up toward the door and saw a terrible, strange apparition — a monstrous, frightening figure standing beside him, saying nothing. Brutus asked it boldly, "What are you — man or god? And what business brings you to me?" The figure answered, "I am your evil genius, Brutus. You will see me at Philippi." And Brutus, not shaken in the least, replied, "Then I will see you."

As soon as the apparition vanished, he called his servants. They told him they had heard no voice and seen no vision. So he stayed awake until morning, then went to Cassius and told him what he had seen. Cassius, who followed the philosophy of Epicurus and often used to debate these matters with Brutus, answered him this way: "It is the opinion of our school, Brutus, that not everything we feel or see is real. The senses are slippery and deceitful things, and the mind is quicker and subtler still — it sets the senses in motion and stamps every kind of change upon them with no real cause behind it at all, the way an impression is pressed into wax. And the soul of man, which contains in itself both the stamp and the wax, can most easily, by its own workings, produce and take on every shape and figure. You can see this in the sudden changes of our dreams, where the imagination, once set off by the least thing, runs through a whole parade of emotions and appearances. It is the imagination's nature to be always in motion, and its motion is fantasy — mere conception. In your case, add this: your body, worn down and strained by constant toil, naturally works upon your mind and keeps it in an excited, unnatural state. As for supernatural beings — that they exist at all, or, if they did, that they would have human shape or voice or any power that could reach us — there is no reason to believe it. Though I confess I could wish there were such beings, so that in this most sacred and honorable enterprise of ours we might not have to rely only on our arms and horses and ships, numerous and powerful as they are, but could count on the help of the gods as well." With talk like this, Cassius soothed Brutus's mind. But as the troops were boarding the ships, two eagles flew down and perched on the two foremost standards. They crossed the water with the army, followed the soldiers all the way, and were fed by them — until they came to Philippi, where, just one day before the battle, both eagles flew away.

Brutus had already brought most of the places and peoples of that region under control, but now the army marched on to the coast opposite the island of Thasos, subduing any town or strongman that still held out. There they found Norbanus encamped in a position called the Straits, near Symbolum. They surrounded him so completely that they forced him to abandon the place, and Norbanus came within a hair of losing his entire army — Octavian was lagging far behind because of sickness, and only Antony's relief saved him, arriving with such astonishing speed that Brutus and his officers refused to believe the report that he had come. Octavian arrived ten days later and camped opposite Brutus, with Antony opposite Cassius.

The stretch of ground between the two armies is what the Romans call the Campi Philippi — the plains of Philippi. Never before had two Roman armies of such size come together to fight each other. Brutus's army was somewhat smaller in numbers than Octavian's, but in the splendor of its weapons and the richness of its equipment it was far the more magnificent, for most of the men's arms were of gold and silver, which Brutus had lavished on them. In everything else he had trained his commanders to frugality and self-restraint, but he believed that the wealth a soldier carried in his hands and wore on his body would put spirit into the man who craved glory — and would make the man who loved money fight all the harder to keep the armor that was his whole estate.

Octavian held his review and ritual purification of the army inside his own trenches, and handed out only a little grain and a bare five drachmas per soldier for the sacrifice they were to offer. Brutus — whether out of pity for that poverty or contempt for that meanness of spirit — first mustered and purified his army in the open field, as custom required, and then distributed great numbers of sacrificial animals to every regiment and fifty drachmas to every soldier. In the love of his men and their readiness to fight for him, Brutus held every advantage. But at the ceremony, they say, an unlucky omen befell Cassius: his lictor, his official attendant, presented him with the garland he was to wear at the sacrifice — upside down. It was said, too, that some time before, at a solemn procession, a golden image of Victory being carried before Cassius had crashed to the ground when its bearer slipped. On top of that, birds of prey circled the camp daily, and swarms of bees were found clustering at a spot inside the trenches — a spot the soothsayers ordered walled off from the camp, to quiet the superstition that was quietly beginning to infect even Cassius himself, and to shake his Epicurean philosophy, and that had already wholly seized and conquered the soldiers. Because of all this Cassius was reluctant to stake everything on an immediate battle. He argued for stretching the war out, since they were the stronger in money and supplies but the weaker in men and arms. Brutus, on the contrary, was as eager as ever to come to a swift decision — either to restore his country's liberty or to release all those multitudes of people from the misery of the levies and services and extortions the war laid on them. And when he saw his light cavalry getting the better of skirmish after skirmish, his confidence hardened into resolve. Then some of the soldiers deserted to the enemy, and others began accusing and suspecting one another, and many of Cassius's friends in the council came over to Brutus's opinion. But one man of Brutus's own party, named Atellius, opposed him, advising that they wait out the winter. When Brutus asked him how he expected to be better off a year from now, Atellius answered, "If I gain nothing else, at least I will have lived that much longer." The answer displeased Cassius intensely, and Atellius fell into general contempt for it. And so it was resolved to give battle the next day.

That night at supper Brutus was cheerful and full of hope, and talked philosophy with his friends before going to his rest. But Messala says that Cassius supped privately with a few of his closest companions and seemed thoughtful and silent, against his nature and his habit; that after supper he took Messala warmly by the hand and said to him — speaking in Greek, as he always did when he wished to show affection — "Bear witness for me, Messala, that I am brought to the same necessity as Pompey the Great before me: to hazard the liberty of my country on a single battle. Yet let us be of good courage, and trust our fortune — it would be wrong to distrust her, even if our counsels are bad." These, Messala says, were the last words Cassius spoke to him before saying goodnight; and he was invited to supper with Cassius the following evening, which was his birthday.

At first light the signal for battle — the scarlet coat — was hung out in both Brutus's camp and Cassius's, and the two generals met in the open ground between their armies. There Cassius said: "May it be as we hope, Brutus — may we win today and live all the rest of our lives happily together. But the greatest of human affairs are the most uncertain, and if the battle goes against us, we may find it hard ever to see each other again. So tell me now: what have you resolved about flight, and about death?" Brutus answered, "When I was young, Cassius, and knew nothing of the world, I let myself utter a bold philosophical opinion — I know not how. I blamed Cato for killing himself. I called it an irreligious act, and an unmanly one, to try to slip out from under the divine order of things — to refuse to accept bravely whatever evil comes, and to run from it instead. But now, in my own fortunes, I am of another mind. If Providence does not settle this undertaking as we wish, I will put no further hopes and no further preparations for war to the test. I will die content with my fortune. I gave my life to my country on the Ides of March, and I have lived a second life since then for her sake, in liberty and honor." Cassius smiled at these words, and embraced him, and said, "With these resolutions, let us go against the enemy. For either we shall conquer — or we shall have no cause to fear the men who do." After this they discussed the ordering of the battle with their friends. Brutus asked Cassius for command of the right wing, though it was generally thought that post belonged to Cassius, as the older and more experienced man. Even in this Cassius gave way to Brutus, and he placed Messala with the bravest of all his legions in the same wing. Brutus at once led out his cavalry, splendidly equipped, and brought his infantry up close behind.

Antony's soldiers were digging trenches from the marsh where they were camped out across the plain, to cut Cassius off from the sea, while Octavian's troops were to stand by in support — though Octavian himself could not be present because of his sickness. His soldiers were not expecting the enemy to offer a pitched battle at all — a few sallies, they thought, some darts and light weapons thrown to harass the men working in the trenches — and they took no notice of the army drawn up facing them, ready to fight. So they were stunned by the huge, confused roar that came rolling from the trenches. Meanwhile Brutus had sent out tickets to his officers with the password for battle written on them, and rode along the ranks himself, encouraging the men. But few of them ever heard the password before the fighting began; most of them did not wait for it to be passed down, but with one surge and one shout ran straight at the enemy. The charge threw the line out of order and the legions were pulled apart from one another. Messala's legion first, then the next beside it, swept clean past Octavian's left wing, barely grazing its edge and killing only a handful, and wheeling around behind it fell directly on Octavian's camp. Octavian himself, as his own memoirs tell it, had been carried out of the camp only moments before — one of his friends, Marcus Artorius, had dreamed a dream commanding that Octavian be carried out. He was believed dead, for the soldiers had riddled his empty litter with darts and pikes. There was great slaughter in the captured camp, and two thousand Spartans, newly arrived to reinforce Octavian, were cut down with the rest.

The rest of Brutus's line, which had not swung around the wing but engaged the enemy head-on, found them in complete disorder, overwhelmed them easily, and cut down three legions where they stood; then, swept along by the momentum of victory, they poured into the enemy camp on the heels of the fugitives, Brutus himself among them. But the defeated men saw, in their extremity, what the victors had failed to see. The right wing, hurrying off in pursuit, had torn away from the main body and left its flank exposed — and the enemy fell on that gap. They could not break the center, which met them with fierce, stubborn resistance; but the left wing, where Cassius commanded, in disorder and knowing nothing of what had happened on the other side of the field, they put to flight. They chased the fugitives all the way into Cassius's camp and sacked and wrecked it — with neither of their own generals present, for Antony, they say, had ducked away from the fury of that first charge and withdrawn into the nearby marsh, and Octavian was nowhere to be found after being carried from his tents — though some soldiers showed Brutus their bloody swords and swore they had killed him, describing his face and his youth. By now the center of Brutus's line had driven back its opponents with great slaughter. Brutus was plainly victorious everywhere on his side of the field, and Cassius was everywhere beaten. And this one mistake ruined everything: Brutus did not go to Cassius's relief, because he believed Cassius had won as he had; Cassius did not wait for Brutus's relief, because he believed Brutus had lost as he had. As proof that the victory was Brutus's, Messala points out that he took three eagles and many standards from the enemy without losing one of his own. But now, riding back from the pursuit and the plunder of Octavian's camp, Brutus was puzzled that he could not see Cassius's tent standing tall above the rest, where it had always stood — nor anything else looking as it should, for the enemy had torn it all down and looted it the moment they burst into the camp. Some of his men, sharper-eyed and longer-sighted than the rest, told Brutus they could see a great deal of gleaming armor and silver shields moving back and forth in Cassius's camp — and that neither the number nor the style of the armor looked like the guard that had been left there; yet there did not seem to be nearly as many dead bodies lying about as there should have been after the defeat of so many legions. That was what first made Brutus suspect Cassius's misfortune. Leaving a guard in the enemy camp, he called his men back from the pursuit and rallied them to march to Cassius's relief. And this is what had become of Cassius.

He had been angry from the very first, when Brutus's soldiers charged without the password and without the order to attack. Then, when they had won, he was just as displeased to watch them rush off after loot and plunder instead of wheeling to encircle what was left of the enemy. And then — acting by delay and hesitation rather than commanding boldly and with a clear purpose — he let himself be hemmed in by the enemy's right wing. His cavalry broke and bolted for the sea, and when he saw the infantry beginning to give way as well, he did everything a man could do to stop the rout and turn them back, snatching a standard from the hands of one fleeing soldier and planting it at his own feet — though by then he could barely hold even his personal guard together. In the end he was forced to withdraw with a handful of men to a small hill overlooking the plain. He himself, being weak-sighted, could make out almost nothing — only, and only with difficulty, the destruction of his camp. But the men with him saw a large body of horsemen riding toward the hill — the very riders Brutus had sent. Cassius took them for enemies coming to hunt him down; even so, he sent out Titinius, one of his companions, to find out who they were. When Brutus's horsemen saw Titinius coming and recognized a friend, a faithful servant of Cassius, those who knew him well shouted for joy, leapt down from their horses, and embraced him and shook his hands, while the rest rode circles around him, singing and cheering in their delight. And that was the cause of the worst mischief of all. For Cassius honestly believed that Titinius had been taken by the enemy, and he cried out, "Through too much love of life, I have lived to watch a friend captured by the enemy before my face." Then he withdrew into an empty tent, taking with him only Pindarus, one of his freedmen — a man he had kept ready for exactly such a moment ever since the disaster of the Parthian expedition, when Crassus was destroyed. From the Parthians Cassius had escaped with his life. Now he pulled his cloak up over his head, bared his neck, and held it out to Pindarus, commanding him to strike — with, men said, the same dagger he had used against Caesar. The head, at any rate, was found severed from the body. But no man ever saw Pindarus again, which led some to suspect that he had killed his master unbidden. Soon enough they discovered who the horsemen really were, and here came Titinius, crowned with garlands, hurrying toward Cassius. But when the cries and wailing of his grief-stricken friends told him of his general's tragic error and death, he drew his sword, bitterly cursed his own slowness for causing it, and killed himself.

As soon as Brutus was sure that Cassius's wing had been beaten, he hurried toward him — but he heard nothing of his death until he had nearly reached the camp. There he mourned over the body, calling Cassius "the last of the Romans," for the city, he said, could never again produce another man of so great a spirit. Then he sent the body away to be buried on the island of Thasos, so that a funeral inside the camp would not stir up disorder. He gathered the soldiers and steadied them; and seeing that they had lost everything they owned, he promised every man two thousand drachmas to make up for it. The words put heart back into them, and the size of the gift astonished them. They saw him off with shouts and praise, calling him the only general of all four who had not been beaten in the battle. And the facts bore out his confidence that he would win: with only a few legions he had overthrown everything that stood against him. If all his soldiers had actually fought — instead of most of them streaming past the enemy to get at the plunder — he would very likely have swept every part of the field.

Eight thousand men fell on his side, counting the army's servants, whom Brutus calls Briges. On the other side, Messala gives it as his opinion that the dead came to more than twice that number. So it was Octavian and Antony's men who were the more disheartened — until a servant of Cassius named Demetrius came to Antony that evening, carrying the robe he had stripped from the dead body, and the sword. At the sight of these the enemy took such heart that at first light they drew out their whole force and stood in battle order. Brutus, meanwhile, found both his camps wavering and in disorder. His own was full of prisoners and needed a heavier guard than usual; and Cassius's men were unsettled by the change of general — besides which, the beaten troops nursed a certain envy and resentment toward the part of the army that had done the conquering. He decided, therefore, to put his forces in battle order but to keep out of a fight. The captured slaves — a great number of them had been found mixed in among the soldiers, not without suspicion — he ordered killed. But most of the free men and citizens he released, saying that they were more truly prisoners with the enemy than with him: over there they were captives and slaves, but with him they were free men and citizens of Rome. Even so, he had to hide them and help them slip away secretly, because he could see that his friends and officers were bent on revenge against them. Among the captives were an actor named Volumnius and a clown named Sacculio. Brutus paid them no attention at all, but his friends dragged them before him and charged that even now, in this condition, the pair would not give up their jokes and their insolent talk. Brutus, his mind full of other things, said nothing to the accusation; but Messala Corvinus gave as his judgment that they should be publicly whipped on a stage and sent naked to the enemy's commanders, to show them what sort of drinking companions they liked to bring along on campaign. Some of those present laughed at that. But Publius Casca — the man who had given Caesar the first wound — said, "It is not right for us to be joking and making merry at the funeral of Cassius. And you, Brutus, will show what esteem you have for that general's memory by how you deal with men who mock him and speak of him shamefully." Brutus, badly discomposed, replied, "Then why ask me about it, Casca, instead of doing what you think fitting yourselves?" That answer was taken as his consent to the deaths of the two wretched men, and they were carried off and killed.

After this he paid the soldiers the reward he had promised. He rebuked them, though only mildly, for charging the enemy in disorder, without the password or any word of command; and he promised that if they fought bravely in the next engagement, he would give them two cities to sack and plunder: Thessalonica and Lacedaemon — Sparta itself. This is the one indefensible thing in all that is found fault with in the life of Brutus — though it is true that Octavian and Antony were far more cruel in the rewards they gave their own soldiers after victory. They drove out, one might almost say, all the old inhabitants of Italy, to put their soldiers in possession of other men's lands and cities. But then conquest and empire had been their whole purpose in the war, while Brutus, because of his reputation for virtue, was not permitted by the world either to win or even to save himself except with justice and honor — especially now that Cassius was dead, Cassius who was generally blamed as the man who had steered him toward his less merciful acts. As sailors, when a storm has smashed the rudder, fit and nail on some other piece of timber in its place — meeting the danger not well, but as well as necessity allows — so Brutus, at the head of so great an army in such uncertain times, with no commander equal to his need, was forced to use the ones he had, and to do and say much of what they advised. And what they advised was, in effect, whatever might bring Cassius's soldiers into better order. For those troops were headstrong and hard to manage — bold and insolent in camp for want of their general, but cowardly and fearful in the field, because they remembered being beaten.

Things stood no better with Octavian and Antony. Their provisions were running short, and their camp lay on low ground, so they expected a very hard winter. They were pressed close against the marshes, and the heavy autumn rains that fell after the battle filled their tents with mud and water, which froze at once in the cold. And while they were in this misery, news reached them of their disaster at sea. Brutus's fleet had fallen upon their ships as they carried a great reinforcement of soldiers over from Italy, and had destroyed them so completely that only a handful of men escaped the slaughter — and those few, starving, were driven to eat the sails and the rigging. The moment they heard this, Octavian and Antony were in a hurry to force a deciding battle before Brutus could learn of his good fortune. For it so happened that the fighting at sea and on land had fallen on the very same day; and yet by some mischance — not through any fault of his commanders — Brutus did not learn of his victory until twenty days after. Had he known, he would never have been drawn into a second battle. He had provisions enough for his army for a long time to come; his camp was well placed, sheltered from the winter weather and nearly unreachable by the enemy; and being absolute master of the sea, and victorious on land on the wing where he himself had fought, he would have been full of hope and confidence. But it seems the Roman state could no longer endure to be governed by many men; it required a monarchy; and so the divine power, wishing to remove from the scene the one man who could still resist the man destined to control the empire, cut off that good news from Brutus's ears. And it missed him by only a little. The very evening before the battle, a deserter named Clodius came over from the enemy and announced that Octavian had learned of the loss of his fleet, and that this was the reason for his haste to fight. But the story found no credit. The man was never even brought before Brutus; they simply set him down as someone with bad information, or a liar inventing tales to buy himself favor.

That same night, they say, the phantom came to Brutus again, in the same shape as before — but this time it vanished without speaking. Publius Volumnius, though, a philosopher who had carried arms with Brutus from the beginning of the campaign, makes no mention of the apparition. He records other omens: that the first eagle-standard was covered with a swarm of bees; that one of the officers had an arm that sweated oil of roses of its own accord, and though they dried and wiped it again and again, it would not stop; and that just before the battle two eagles met and fought in the open space between the two armies, while the whole field kept an unbelievable silence and every man watched the spectacle — until at last the eagle on Brutus's side gave way and fled. And the story of the Ethiopian is famous: meeting the standard-bearer just as the camp gate was opened, he was cut to pieces by the soldiers, who took him for an evil omen.

Brutus led his army into the field and drew it up facing the enemy, and then paused a long while before he would fight. As he was reviewing the troops, suspicions were stirred and accusations laid against some of them. Besides, he could see that his cavalry had no great appetite for beginning the action and were waiting to see what the infantry would do. And then, suddenly, Camulatus — an excellent soldier, one Brutus prized highly for his courage — rode close past Brutus himself and went over to the enemy. The sight grieved Brutus to the heart. Partly out of anger, partly out of fear of some greater treason and desertion, he immediately threw his forces against the enemy, at about three in the afternoon, with the sun already going down. On his own wing Brutus had the better of it. He pressed hard on the enemy's left, which gave way and retreated, and his cavalry charged in alongside the infantry when they saw the enemy in confusion. But the other wing was a different story. To avoid being surrounded — for the numbers were against them — the officers kept stretching the line, until it was drawn so thin at the center that it could not withstand a charge; at the first onset it broke and fled. After cutting through it, the enemy at once swung round and took Brutus in the rear. Brutus himself, in that peril, did everything an expert general and a brave soldier could do — by judgment and by his own hand, everything that might yet recover the victory. But the very thing that had been his advantage in the first battle told against him in the second. In the first, the beaten part of the enemy had been killed on the spot; but of Cassius's men who fled, few had been slain, and the survivors, cowed by their old defeat, infected the greater part of the army with their own dread and disorder. It was here that Marcus, the son of Cato, was killed, fighting among the noblest and bravest of the young men. He scorned to run and would not give the least ground, but kept fighting, declaring who he was and naming his father's name, until he fell at last, leaving his enemies in awe of his courage, upon a heap of the enemy's dead. And of the rest, the bravest died defending Brutus.

There was in the field a man named Lucilius, a good man and a friend of Brutus. He saw a party of barbarian horsemen ignoring everyone else in the pursuit and galloping at full stretch after Brutus alone, and he resolved to stop them, though it should cost him his life. Letting himself fall a little behind, he told them that he was Brutus. They believed him all the more because he begged to be taken to Antony — as if he feared Octavian but could trust Antony. Overjoyed with their prize, and thinking themselves wonderfully lucky, they carried him along through the night, first sending messengers ahead to tell Antony they were coming. Antony was delighted and came out to meet them; and everyone else who heard that Brutus had been taken alive came flocking to see him — some pitying his fortune, others sneering that out of too much love of life he had let himself become the prey of barbarians, a meanness unworthy of his former glory. When they came near, Antony stood still, considering how he ought to receive Brutus. But Lucilius, brought up before him, said with perfect confidence: "Be assured, Antony, that no enemy has taken Marcus Brutus alive, and no enemy ever shall — heaven forbid that fortune should ever win such a victory over virtue. He will be found, living or dead, in a manner worthy of himself. As for me, I am here by a trick I played on your soldiers, and I am ready to suffer any severity you choose to inflict for it." Everyone stood amazed at these words. But Antony turned to the men who had brought him and said: "I see, my fellow soldiers, that you are annoyed at being deceived, and feel yourselves insulted and injured by it. But you should know that you have taken a better prize than the one you were hunting. You were searching for an enemy, and you have brought me a friend. For to tell you the truth, I do not know how I would have treated Brutus if you had brought him in alive. But of this I am sure: it is better to have men like Lucilius as our friends than as our enemies." With that he embraced Lucilius, and for the moment put him in the care of one of his friends; and ever afterward he found him a steady and faithful friend.

By now Brutus had crossed a little brook that ran among trees under steep overhanging rocks, and since it was night, he would go no farther. He sat down in a hollow with a great rock jutting out in front of it, a few of his officers and friends around him. First, looking up at the sky, which was full of stars, he recited two lines of verse. One of them, Volumnius records, was this:

  Punish, great Zeus, the author of these ills.

The other, he says, he had forgotten. A little later, naming one by one all the friends who had been killed before his eyes in the battle, he groaned aloud — above all at the names of Flavius, the chief of his engineers, and Labeo, his lieutenant. Meanwhile one of the company, who was thirsty and saw that Brutus was thirsty too, took his helmet and ran down to the brook for water. Then a noise was heard from the far side of the river, and Volumnius, taking Brutus's armor-bearer Dardanus with him, went out to see what it was. When they came back a short while later, they asked what had become of the water. Brutus smiled — a smile that carried a great deal of meaning — and said to Volumnius, "It is all drunk; but more shall be fetched for you." So the man who had brought the first helmetful was sent again; he came close to being captured by the enemy, took a wound, and escaped only with difficulty.

Brutus reckoned that not many of his men had been killed in the fighting, and Statyllius undertook to find out for certain. He would dash through the enemy — there was no other way — and see what had become of their camp; if he found everything safe there, he would hold up a torch as a signal, and then return. He got through, and the torch was raised: Statyllius had reached the camp safely. But when a long time had passed and he did not come back, Brutus said, "If Statyllius is alive, he will come back." On the return, he fell into the enemy's hands, and was killed.

The night was now far gone. Brutus, still sitting, leaned his head toward his servant Clitus and spoke to him. Clitus gave no answer, only burst into tears. Then Brutus drew aside his armor-bearer Dardanus and had some words with him in private. Last of all he spoke to Volumnius in Greek, reminding him of their studies together and the discipline they had shared, and begged him to take hold of the sword with him and help him drive it home. Volumnius refused the request, and so did the others; and when someone said there could be no staying here — they must fly — Brutus rose and said, "Yes, indeed, we must fly; but not with our feet — with our hands." Then he gave each of them his right hand, and with a face full of pleasure he told them that it was an infinite satisfaction to him that not one of his friends had proved false. As for Fortune, he was angry with her only for his country's sake. As for himself, he counted himself far happier than the men who had conquered — not only as he had been a little while ago, but even now, in his present condition. For he was leaving behind him a reputation for virtue that no force can take away — a possession that none of the conquerors, with all their arms and all their riches, would ever be able to acquire; nor could they ever prevent posterity from believing, and saying, that being unjust and wicked men, they had destroyed the just and the good, and seized a power to which they had no right. Then he urged and entreated them all to look to their own safety, and withdrew with only two or three of his closest friends. One of these was Strato, whose acquaintance he had made when they studied rhetoric together. Him he placed nearest to himself; and then, taking hold of the hilt of his sword and directing it with both hands, he fell upon it and killed himself. But others say that it was not Brutus who held the sword but Strato, who — at Brutus's earnest entreaty, and turning his face away — held it steady while Brutus threw himself upon it with such force that it passed through his breast, and he died at once.

Messala, that friend of Brutus who was afterward reconciled to Octavian, once brought this same Strato to him at a leisure moment and said, with tears in his eyes, "This, Caesar, is the man who did the last friendly service for my beloved Brutus." Octavian received him kindly, and had good use of him afterward in his labors and his battles — at Actium Strato was one of the Greeks who proved their courage in his service. And of Messala himself the story is told that when Octavian once paid him this compliment — that although at Philippi he had been his fiercest enemy for Brutus's sake, at Actium he had shown himself his most devoted friend — Messala answered, "You have always found me, Caesar, on the best and justest side."

Brutus's body was found by Antony, who ordered the richest purple mantle he owned to be thrown over it; and when the mantle was afterward stolen, he found out the thief and had him put to death. The ashes of Brutus he sent home to his mother, Servilia. As for his wife Portia, the philosopher Nicolaus and Valerius Maximus write that she was determined to die, but was prevented by her friends, who watched her constantly; and that at last she snatched burning coals from the fire, shut them close in her mouth, and stifled herself — and so ended her life in a manner worthy of her birth and her virtue. Yet there is also a letter of Brutus in circulation, addressed to his friends, in which he grieves for Portia's death and reproaches them for neglecting her, so that in her illness she preferred to die rather than linger. It seems, then, that Nicolaus was simply mistaken about the time; for the letter — if it is indeed genuine, and truly Brutus's own — lets us understand Portia's sickness, and her love, and the manner in which her death came.

Antony

Antony's grandfather was the famous orator Antonius, whom Marius put to death for siding with Sulla. His father was another Antony, surnamed the Cretan — never famous or distinguished in public life, but a decent, kind man, and remarkable above all for his generosity. One story shows the whole character. He was not rich, and for exactly that reason his wife kept a tight rein on his good nature. One day a friend in need came to borrow money. Money he had none. So he told a servant to bring him water in a silver basin, and when it came he wetted his face as if he meant to shave, sent the servant off on some other errand — and handed his friend the basin, telling him to turn it into whatever he needed. Later, when a great hunt for the missing basin went up through the house, and his wife, in a very bad temper, was about to put the servants to the question one by one, he confessed what he had done and begged her pardon.

His wife was Julia, of the family of the Caesars, and in good sense and honorable conduct she was the equal of any woman of her time. It was under her that Antony was raised; after his father's death she remarried, to Cornelius Lentulus — the man Cicero put to death for his part in Catiline's conspiracy. Here, probably, is the first seed of the deadly grudge Antony bore Cicero. Antony even claimed that Lentulus' body was refused burial until Julia went and begged it from Cicero's wife. But that is plainly a mistake, for none of the men executed in Cicero's consulship was denied burial. Antony grew up a strikingly handsome young man, and then — the worst luck of his life — he fell in with Curio, a man wholly given over to his pleasures. To make Antony depend on him all the more, Curio plunged him into drinking and dissipation, and led him through spending so extravagant that at that early age he was already two hundred and fifty talents in debt. Curio stood surety for the whole sum; and when his father, the elder Curio, heard of it, he threw Antony out of his house. For a short while after that, Antony attached himself to Clodius, the most insolent and violent demagogue of the day, and ran with him through his career of riot and disorder. But he soon wearied of Clodius' madness, and grew nervous of the powerful enemies gathering against him, so he left Italy for Greece, where he spent his time in military training and the study of public speaking. He took most naturally to the so-called Asiatic style of oratory, then at the height of its fashion — a style that suited his boastful, swaggering temper very well, all empty flourishes and restless grabbing after glory.

After some time in Greece, Gabinius, a former consul, invited him to join a campaign in Syria. At first Antony refused — he had no wish to serve as a man in the ranks — but when he was offered a commission as commander of cavalry, he went along. His first action was against Aristobulus, who had persuaded the Jews to rebel. There Antony was himself the first man up the wall of the largest fortress, and drove Aristobulus out of every stronghold he held; then he beat him in a pitched battle against an army many times the size of his own, killed nearly all of them, and took Aristobulus and his son prisoner. When that war was over, Ptolemy came asking Gabinius to restore him to his kingdom of Egypt, with a promise of ten thousand talents as the reward. Most of the officers were against the venture, and Gabinius himself did not much like it, though the ten thousand talents tempted him sorely. But Antony, hungry for bold action and eager to oblige Ptolemy, helped talk Gabinius into going. Everyone agreed that the most dangerous stretch was the march to Pelusium — deep sand, no hope of fresh water, the road running past the Ecregma and the Serbonian marsh, which the Egyptians call the breathing-hole of Typhon, and which is probably water left behind by the Red Sea, or seeping through from it, since only a narrow neck of land divides the Red Sea from the Mediterranean there. Antony was sent ahead with the cavalry, and he did more than seize the passes: he captured Pelusium itself, a great city, took its garrison prisoner, and so made the march safe for the army and the road to victory easy for its general. Even the enemy got some benefit from his hunger for honor. When Ptolemy entered Pelusium, boiling with rage and spite against the Egyptians, he meant to put them all to the sword — and Antony stood in his way and stopped the slaughter. In all the many skirmishes and battles that followed, he gave proof after proof of his personal courage and his skill in command; once in particular, by swinging around and striking the enemy's rear, he handed the victory to the attackers in front, and won signal honors for it. And his decency toward the dead Archelaus was noticed just as widely. The man had once been his host and his friend; now, of necessity, Antony fought him bravely while he lived — but when he fell, Antony sought out his body and buried it with royal honors. The result was that he left a great name behind him among the Alexandrians, and every man serving in the Roman army counted him a most gallant soldier.

He had, besides, a noble and striking presence. A full, well-grown beard, a broad forehead, an aquiline nose — altogether a bold, masculine look that reminded people of Hercules as he appears in paintings and statues. Indeed there was an old tradition that the Antonys descended from Hercules through a son of his named Anton, and Antony liked to lend the story credit — by that resemblance, and by the way he dressed. Whenever he was to appear before a crowd, he wore his tunic belted low on the hips, a broadsword at his side, and a heavy, coarse cloak over everything. Even the things that others found insufferable in him — the bragging, the constant joking, the drinking in public, the way he would sit down beside the men at their meals or stand eating off the common soldiers' tables — made him the darling of the army. In matters of love he had the same easy charm: he won plenty of friends by helping them along in their affairs, and took their jokes about his own in perfect good humor. And his generous ways — the open, lavish hand with gifts and favors to friends and fellow soldiers — did a great deal to launch his rise to power, and once he was great, kept his fortunes standing long after a thousand follies had begun pulling them down. One example of that open hand is worth telling. He had ordered a payment of two hundred and fifty thousand to one of his friends — a decies, as the Romans call the sum. His steward, staggered by the extravagance, piled all the silver up in a heap where Antony would pass. Antony saw the heap and asked what it meant. "The money you ordered paid to your friend," said the steward. Antony saw the man's spite at once. "I thought a decies came to more than that," he said. "This is too little. Double it." But that belongs to a later time.

When the Roman state finally split into two hostile camps — the aristocratic party lining up behind Pompey, who was in the city, and the people's party looking to Caesar, who was off at the head of an army in Gaul — Curio, Antony's old friend, changed sides, devoted himself to Caesar, and brought Antony over with him. With the influence his eloquence won him among the people, and with a free flow of Caesar's money, Curio got Antony elected first tribune of the people, then augur. And the moment Antony took office he was worth a great deal to Caesar. First he blocked the consul Marcellus, who wanted to put the troops already assembled under Pompey's command and give him power to raise fresh levies; Antony countered with an order that the assembled troops be sent to Syria to reinforce Bibulus in his war with the Parthians, and that no one should enlist under Pompey. Next, when the senators refused to allow Caesar's letters to be received or read in the senate, Antony, by the authority of his office, read them out publicly — and to such effect that many changed their minds, since Caesar's demands, as he set them down in writing, looked nothing but fair and reasonable. At last two questions were put to the senate: should Pompey dismiss his army, and should Caesar dismiss his. A few voted for the first; for the second, all but a handful. Then Antony rose and put a question of his own: would it please them that both men give up their commands together? This proposal they seized on with real enthusiasm — they cheered him loudly and demanded a vote. But the consuls would not allow it. Caesar's friends then made some fresh offers, fair and equitable ones, but Cato opposed them fiercely, and the consul Lentulus ordered Antony out of the senate. So Antony was driven out with insults. He left cursing them, and, with Quintus Cassius, dressed as slaves, they fled in a hired carriage straight to Caesar. The moment they reached the camp they declared openly that there was no longer any order or justice in Rome — that tribunes were denied the right to speak, and that any man who stood up for common fairness was thrown out and lucky to escape with his life.

At that, Caesar put his army in motion and marched into Italy. That is why Cicero writes in his Philippics that Antony caused the civil war as surely as Helen caused the Trojan one. But this is slander. Caesar was not so soft or so easily swept away that the sight of Antony and Cassius, shabbily dressed and fleeing in a hired carriage, could carry him off into civil war against his country in a fit of momentary indignation — as if he had never thought of it or resolved on it long before. For a man who wanted a pretext for war, this made a fair and plausible occasion; but the true motive that drove him was the same that had once driven Alexander, and Cyrus before him, against all mankind: the unquenchable thirst for empire, the consuming ambition to be the greatest man in the world — which was impossible until Pompey had been pulled down. So as soon as he had advanced, occupied Rome, and driven Pompey out of Italy, Caesar decided to deal first with the legions Pompey had in Spain, then cross the sea and hunt Pompey himself with the fleet being built in the meantime. He left the government of Rome to Lepidus, as praetor, and the command of the troops and of Italy to Antony, as tribune of the people. Antony had the soldiers' hearts almost at once — he drilled with them, lived among them for the most part, and gave them everything his means allowed. But with nearly everyone else he was unpopular. He was too lazy to hear out the complaints of people who had been wronged; he listened impatiently to petitioners; and he had a bad name for meddling with other men's wives. In short, Caesar's government — which, as far as Caesar himself was concerned, looked like anything but a tyranny — got its bad reputation through Caesar's friends. And of those friends, Antony, who held the largest trust and committed the largest errors, was thought the most deeply at fault.

For all that, when Caesar came back from Spain he overlooked the charges against him — and in the war that followed, in every task he gave Antony, he never once had cause to complain of any lack of courage, energy, or soldierly skill. Caesar himself embarked at Brundisium, crossed the Ionian Sea with a small force, and sent the ships back with orders for Antony and Gabinius to embark the army and come over to Macedonia with all speed. Gabinius had no stomach for the sea in the rough, dangerous weather of midwinter, and wanted to march the army around by the long land route. But Antony was more afraid of what might happen to Caesar, hard pressed as he was by the numbers against him. He beat back Libo, who was blockading the mouth of the harbor of Brundisium, by swarming his galleys with a fleet of small boats, and with that opening he loaded twenty thousand infantry and eight hundred horsemen aboard and put to sea. The enemy spotted him and gave chase — and from that danger a strong south wind saved him, springing up and raising a sea so heavy that the enemy galleys could barely make way. But his own ships were being driven straight onto a lee shore of cliffs and rock walls dropping sheer to the water, with no hope of escape — when all at once the wind swung round to the southwest and blew from the land out to open sea. Antony, sailing now in safety, saw the whole coast covered with the wreckage of the enemy's fleet: the pursuing galleys had been driven onto it by the gale, and not a few had been smashed to pieces. Many men and much property fell into Antony's hands; he took the town of Lissus as well; and by arriving when he did, with a force that size, he gave Caesar great encouragement.

In the many engagements that now came one after another, there was not one in which Antony did not distinguish himself. Twice, when the army was in full flight, he stopped it, turned the men around, led them back into the charge, and won. Not without reason, then, his reputation in the camp stood next to Caesar's. And what Caesar himself thought of him showed plainly when the final battle came at Pharsalia — the battle that would decide everything. Caesar chose to lead the right wing himself, and gave the left to Antony, as the best officer he had. After the victory, Caesar, now named dictator, went off in pursuit of Pompey, and sent Antony to Rome as Master of the Horse — an office second in power to the dictator when the dictator is present, and, in his absence, first and very nearly alone. For once a dictator is appointed, every other magistrate except the tribunes ceases to hold any authority in Rome.

Dolabella, however, who was tribune then — a young man and hungry for upheaval — was pushing a sweeping measure to cancel all debts, and he wanted Antony, his friend and always ready to champion a crowd-pleasing project, to join him in it. Asinius and Trebellius argued the other side; and just at that moment Antony was seized by a terrible suspicion that Dolabella was carrying on with his wife. In his distress he put her away — she was his cousin, a daughter of Gaius Antonius, Cicero's colleague in the consulship — sided with Asinius, and went to open war with Dolabella, who had seized the forum, intending to force his law through. The senate voted that Dolabella should be put down by armed force, and Antony went down and attacked him, killing some of Dolabella's men and losing some of his own. The action cost him the affection of the common people; and with the better sort, and with all decent citizens, his whole way of living made him — as Cicero says — absolutely odious. They were disgusted by his drinking bouts at all hours, his wild spending, his gross affairs with women, his days spent asleep or walking off his debauches, his nights at banquets and theaters and at the weddings of comedians and clowns. The story goes that after drinking all night at the wedding of Hippias the comedian, he had to address the people in the morning; he came forward, still loaded from the night before, and vomited in front of them all, while one of his friends held out his toga for him. Sergius the actor was one of the friends with the most influence over him; so was Cytheris, an actress from the same stage, whom he doted on — when he made his official tours she rode with him in a litter, attended by an entourage in nothing inferior to his own mother's. And people were scandalized on every side: at the golden cups he carried with him on the road, fitter for a religious procession than a journey; at the pavilions pitched, and the lavish outdoor breakfasts spread by riversides and in groves; at chariots drawn by lions; at prostitutes and singing-girls quartered in the houses of sober fathers and mothers of families. And it seemed outrageous that while Caesar, out of Italy, slept in the open field and was toiling through the dangerous remnant of a hazardous war, others should use his authority to insult the citizens with their shameless luxury.

All this seems to have inflamed the party quarrels in Rome and encouraged the soldiers in outrages and plunder. Accordingly, when Caesar came home, he acquitted Dolabella; and being elected consul for the third time, he took as his colleague not Antony but Lepidus. When Pompey's house was put up for sale, Antony bought it — and then complained loudly when the price was demanded of him. He tells us himself that this, and his feeling that his services had never been properly rewarded, was why he did not follow Caesar and the army to Libya. And yet Caesar, by dealing gently with his failings, seems to have cured him of a good deal of his folly and extravagance. Antony gave up his old habits and took a wife: Fulvia, the widow of Clodius the demagogue — a woman not born for spinning or housekeeping, and not one to be content ruling a private husband. Her ambition was to govern a governor and command a commander-in-chief. Cleopatra, in fact, owed Fulvia a real debt for training Antony to obey a woman — he came to her hands tame, broken in already to taking orders from a mistress. He used all sorts of playful, boyish tricks to keep Fulvia amused. For example: when Caesar was on his way back after his victory in Spain, Antony went out with the rest to meet him. Then a rumor swept Italy that Caesar was dead and the enemy marching on Rome, and Antony turned back to the city. Disguising himself, he came to Fulvia by night, muffled up as a servant carrying letters from Antony. She, frantic, asked before she would even take the letter, "Is Antony alive?" He handed it to her without a word — and as she was opening it, caught her around the neck and kissed her. This one little story, out of many like it, I give as a sample.

When Caesar returned from Spain, everybody of any rank in Rome traveled days out to meet him, but no one was received like Antony: Caesar took him into his own carriage and rode with him the whole way, while behind them came Brutus Albinus, and Octavian, Caesar's niece's son — the boy who would afterwards take his name and rule the Romans so long. When Caesar was elected consul for the fifth time, he chose Antony as his colleague at once; but he intended to resign his own consulship in favor of Dolabella, and told the senate so. Antony fought it with everything he had, heaping abuse on Dolabella and getting as good back, until Caesar, unable to stomach the indecency of it, put the matter off. When Caesar later came before the people to proclaim Dolabella consul, Antony shouted out that the auspices were against it — and at last Caesar gave in and dropped the plan, much to Dolabella's fury. The truth is, Caesar seems to have been about equally disgusted with the pair of them. When someone was accusing them both to him, he said, "It's not these fat, long-haired men I fear, but the pale, lean ones" — meaning Brutus and Cassius, whose conspiracy would soon bring him down.

And it was Antony himself who, without meaning to, handed that conspiracy its fairest pretext. The Romans were celebrating the festival called the Lupercalia, and Caesar, in his triumphal robes, sat above the Rostra in the marketplace watching the sport. The custom is that many young men of the nobility and the magistracy, oiled for the running, race through the city with leather straps in their hands, striking in fun at everyone they meet. Antony was one of the runners. But he set the old ceremony aside: he had wreathed a diadem with laurel, and he ran up to the Rostra, was hoisted up by his companions, and moved to set it on Caesar's head — as though by that ceremony Caesar were declared king. Caesar made a show of refusing and leaned away from it, and the people applauded with a great shout. Antony pressed it on him again; again Caesar declined it. So the contest between them went on for some time — Antony's offers cheered by a handful of his friends, Caesar's refusals by the whole people at once. Which is a curious thing: men who submitted patiently to the fact of a king's power could not bear the name of it, as though the name alone were the death of their liberty. Caesar, thoroughly rattled by the whole scene, rose from his seat, pulled his robe back from his neck, and cried out that anyone who wanted could strike the blow there and then. The diadem ended up placed on one of his statues — and the tribunes Flavius and Marullus tore it down, and were escorted home by the people with shouts of applause. But Caesar resented it, and stripped them of their office.

All this put fresh heart into Brutus and Cassius. When they were choosing trustworthy friends for the enterprise, they debated whether to bring in Antony. The rest were for it, except Trebonius, who told them that on the journey out to meet Caesar he and Antony had shared lodgings and traveled together, and that he had dropped several careful hints, feeling him out. Antony had understood him perfectly, he said, and had not encouraged it — but neither had he said a word of it to Caesar. He had kept the secret faithfully. The conspirators then proposed that Antony should die along with Caesar. Brutus would not allow it: an act undertaken in defense of right and the laws, he insisted, must be kept unstained, pure of any injustice. But Antony's physical strength and his high office made him dangerous — so it was settled that when Caesar entered the senate house, and the deed was to be done, some of the party would keep Antony occupied outside with a conversation about some invented piece of business.

So the plan was carried through, and Caesar fell in the senate-house. In the first moments Antony pulled on a servant's clothes and hid. But when he learned that the conspirators had gathered on the Capitol and had no designs on anyone else, he persuaded them to come down, sending his own son up as a hostage. That evening Cassius dined at Antony's house, and Brutus dined with Lepidus. Antony then called the senate together and spoke in favor of an amnesty — a formal act of forgetting — and of granting Brutus and Cassius provinces to govern. The senate passed both measures, and voted besides that everything Caesar had enacted should remain in force. Antony walked out of that session with the highest reputation of his life. He had, it seemed, headed off a civil war, and he had settled questions of enormous difficulty and danger in the wisest and most statesmanlike way imaginable.

But those sober counsels did not last. The tide of popular applause swept them away, and with them came a thought that would not leave him: if Brutus were overthrown, Antony would beyond any doubt be the first man in Rome. As Caesar's body was being carried out for burial, Antony delivered the funeral oration in the forum, as custom required. He could see that his praise was working powerfully on the crowd, and so he began to weave in notes of pity, and of horror at what had been done. As he came to the end of the speech he lifted up the dead man's garments, soaked in blood and stabbed through in every part, and held them out for all to see, calling the men who had done this villains and bloody murderers. The effect was volcanic. The people would not hear of delaying the funeral. They heaped up a pyre of benches and tables right there in the market-place and set the body burning — and then, each man snatching a flaming brand from the fire, they ran to attack the houses of the conspirators.

At that, Brutus and his whole party abandoned the city, and Caesar's friends closed ranks behind Antony. Calpurnia, Caesar's widow, entrusted him with the best part of the estate, worth some four thousand talents; and he also got his hands on Caesar's papers — journals of everything he had done, drafts of everything he intended to do. Antony made excellent use of these. On their authority he appointed whatever magistrates he pleased, brought whomever he liked into the senate, recalled men from exile, freed others from prison — all of it, supposedly, "as Caesar had ordered." The Romans mocked the beneficiaries with the nickname "Charonites," men of Charon's ferry, since any of them asked to prove his title would have to appeal to the papers of the dead. In short, Antony's rule in Rome was absolute. He himself was consul, and his two brothers held high office beside him: Gaius as praetor, Lucius as tribune of the people.

While things stood so in Rome, the young Octavian — Caesar's great-nephew, his niece's son, and by his will his adopted heir — arrived from Apollonia, where he had been staying when his uncle was killed. The first thing he did was call on Antony, as his father's friend. He raised the matter of the money in Antony's keeping, and reminded him that Caesar's will had left seventy-five drachmas to every single Roman citizen. At first Antony simply laughed at such talk from so young a man. He told him he must be out of his mind — that what he needed was good sense and good friends, because the burden of being Caesar's executor would crush shoulders as young as his. That answer settled nothing. Octavian went on demanding the property, and Antony went on treating him insultingly in word and deed. He blocked him when he stood for the tribune's office; and when Octavian took steps to dedicate his father's golden chair, as the senate had decreed, Antony threatened to have him thrown in prison if he did not stop courting the people. At that the young man turned to Cicero, and to everyone else who hated Antony. Through them he was recommended to the senate, while he himself wooed the common people and gathered in Caesar's old soldiers from their settlements — until Antony took alarm, met him on the Capitol, and, after some words, the two patched up an agreement.

That night Antony had an ugly dream: he fancied his right hand had been struck by lightning. And a few days later came a report that Octavian was plotting against his life. Octavian tried to explain and was not believed, and the breach was as wide as it had ever been. Each man now raced through Italy, bidding high to enlist the old soldiers scattered in their settlements, and hurrying to be first to secure the troops still under arms.

Cicero at this point was the most influential man in Rome. He used every art he possessed to inflame the public against Antony, and at last persuaded the senate to declare him a public enemy, to send Octavian the rods and axes and the other marks of honor usually given to praetors, and to order Hirtius and Pansa, the consuls of the year, to drive Antony out of Italy. The armies met near Mutina, and Octavian was present and fought in the battle. Antony was defeated — but both consuls were killed.

Antony's flight was misery of every kind, and the worst of it was famine. But it was his character, in calamity, to be better than at any other time. Antony in misfortune came closest to being a virtuous man. It is common enough for people who have fallen into great disasters to see what is right and what they ought to do; what is rare is the strength, in such extremity, to obey that judgment — to do what it approves and avoid what it condemns. Plenty of people are so weak that they collapse into their old habits all the more, and let their minds go entirely. Antony, on this occasion, was an astonishing example to his own soldiers. Fresh from all that luxury and splendid living, he made no difficulty now about drinking foul water and living on wild fruit and roots. As they crossed the Alps, the story goes, his men ate the very bark of trees, and fed on creatures that no one before had ever been willing to touch.

The plan was to join the army on the far side of the Alps commanded by Lepidus, whom Antony counted on as a friend — he had done Lepidus many good turns with Caesar. He came up and camped close by; and when no encouragement of any kind was offered him, he resolved to push his luck and risk everything. His hair was long and wild, his beard unshaved since the defeat; and in that state, with a dark cloak thrown around him, he walked up to the trenches of Lepidus's camp and began to address the army. Some of the men were moved by the sight of him, others by his words — enough of them that Lepidus, not liking where this was heading, ordered the trumpets sounded so that Antony could not be heard. That only deepened the soldiers' pity. They resolved to confer with him in secret, dressed Laelius and Clodius in women's clothes, and sent the pair to him.
