Attack Lepidus's trenches now, they urged; a strong party inside would welcome him, and would kill Lepidus too if he wished it. Antony wanted no such thing. Instead, the next morning, he marched his army straight into the river that divided the two camps. He was the first man into the water; and as he waded toward the far bank he could see Lepidus's soldiers in great numbers stretching out their hands to help him and tearing down their own defenses to make way for him. Once inside the camp he was its absolute master — and yet he treated Lepidus with perfect courtesy, addressed him as "Father" when he spoke to him, and, though everything was now his to command, left him the honor of being called the general. That handsome treatment brought over Munatius Plancus, who lay not far off with a considerable force. So Antony recrossed the Alps in great strength, leading seventeen legions and ten thousand horse into Italy, besides six legions left in garrison under Varius, one of his familiar friends and drinking companions, whom everyone called by his nickname: Cotylon — "the Jug."

Octavian, meanwhile, had perceived that Cicero's heart was set on liberty, and stopped paying him any further attention; he was already using his friends to work out an understanding with Antony. The three of them — Antony, Octavian, Lepidus — met on a small island, and the conference lasted three days. The empire itself was settled easily enough: they divided it among themselves like a piece of family property. What gave them real trouble was agreeing on who should die. Each of them wanted to destroy his enemies and save his friends; and in the end, hatred of enemies beat respect for kin and love of friends. Octavian yielded, and gave Cicero up to Antony; Antony gave up his own uncle, Lucius Caesar; and Lepidus was given leave to murder his brother Paulus — or, as others tell it, surrendered his brother to the other two. I do not believe anything more truly savage or barbarous was ever transacted. In this exchange of blood for blood they were equally guilty of the lives they surrendered and of the lives they took — more guilty, in fact, where their friends were concerned, since those they killed without even the excuse of hatred. To complete the reconciliation, the soldiers crowding around them demanded that the pact be sealed with a marriage: Octavian was to marry Clodia, the daughter of Antony's wife Fulvia. That too was agreed. And then three hundred men were put to death by proscription.

Antony's orders to the men sent to kill Cicero were to cut off his head, and the right hand that had written the Philippics against him. When they were brought to him he gazed at them with delight, bursting out more than once into laughter for sheer joy; and when he had looked his fill, he had them fastened up above the speakers' platform in the forum. He thought he was insulting the dead. In fact he was only putting his own wanton arrogance on display, and his unworthiness to hold the power that fortune had given him. His uncle Lucius Caesar, closely hunted, fled to his sister. When the murderers broke into her house and were pressing toward his room, she stood in the doorway, spread out her arms, and cried again and again, "You will not kill Lucius Caesar until you have first killed me — me, the mother of your general!" And by making that stand she got her brother away and saved his life.

The triumvirate was hateful to the Romans, and Antony bore most of the blame — he was older than Octavian, he carried more authority than Lepidus, and the moment his affairs were settled he rolled straight back into his old luxurious, dissolute way of living. To the general scandal of his behavior one detail added special damage: he lived in the house of Pompey the Great, a man who had been admired as much for his temperance and his sober, citizen-like habits as for his three triumphs. People could not watch without anger as the doors of that house were shut against magistrates, officers, and envoys — shamefully refused admittance — while inside it was packed with actors, jugglers, and drunken flatterers, on whom Antony poured out most of the wealth that violence and cruelty were raking in. For the triumvirs did not stop at confiscating the estates of the proscribed, cheating the widows and families as they went, nor were they satisfied with laying on every imaginable kind of tax and levy. Hearing that sums of money, deposited by foreigners and Roman citizens alike, lay in the keeping of the vestal virgins, they went and took the money by force. When it became plain that nothing would ever be enough for Antony, Octavian finally demanded a division of the property. They divided the army as well, and marched for Macedonia to make war on Brutus and Cassius, leaving Lepidus in command of the city.

Once they had crossed the sea, opened the campaign, and camped facing the enemy at Philippi — Antony opposite Cassius, Octavian opposite Brutus — Octavian did nothing worth recording; all the success and all the victory were Antony's. In the first battle Octavian was completely routed by Brutus, his camp was captured, and he himself very narrowly escaped — though by his own account in his Memoirs, he had withdrawn before the battle even began, because of a dream one of his friends had. Antony, for his part, defeated Cassius — though some have written that he was not actually present in the engagement and only joined afterward in the pursuit. Cassius, not knowing that Brutus had won, was killed at his own entreaty and order by Pindarus, one of his most trusted freedmen. A few days later they fought a second battle; Brutus lost it, and killed himself. Octavian being sick, nearly all the honor of the victory fell to Antony. Standing over Brutus's dead body, he allowed himself a few words of reproach for the death of his brother Gaius, whom Brutus had executed in Macedonia to avenge Cicero. But then, saying that Hortensius was more to blame for it than Brutus, he ordered Hortensius slain on his brother's tomb — and he threw his own scarlet cloak, a garment of great value, over Brutus's body, and charged one of his freedmen with seeing to the funeral. When Antony later learned that this man had not burned the cloak with the corpse, and had pocketed a good part of the funeral money besides, he had him put to death.

Octavian was carried back to Rome so ill that no one expected him to live long. Antony set out for the eastern provinces to lay them under tribute, and entered Greece with a large force. Every common soldier had been promised five thousand drachmas in pay, so the taxing and levying were bound to be severe. To the Greeks, however, he showed at first a fair amount of reason and moderation. He indulged his love of amusement in civilized ways — listening to the scholars dispute, watching the games, undergoing initiation into the mysteries. In matters of law he was even-handed, and he took pleasure in being called a lover of Greece — above all in being called a lover of Athens, a city to which he gave very considerable presents. The people of Megara, wishing him to know that they too had something worth seeing, invited him to come and view their senate-house. He went, looked it over, and when they asked how he liked it, told them: "Not very large — but extremely ruinous." At the same time he had a survey made of the temple of Pythian Apollo, as though he meant to restore it; indeed he had declared to the senate that he intended to.

Leaving Lucius Censorinus in Greece, Antony crossed into Asia, and there laid his hands on the stored-up wealth of the East, while kings waited at his door and queens competed with one another over who could send him the greatest presents or look most enchanting in his eyes. So while Octavian in Rome was wearing out his strength amid sedition and war, Antony, with nothing to do amid the enjoyments of peace, let his passions carry him easily back down the old familiar road. A troupe of harpers and pipers — Anaxenor and Xuthus — the dancer Metrodorus, and a whole Bacchic carnival of Asiatic performers, far outdoing in license and buffoonery even the pests that had followed him out of Italy, came flooding in and took possession of his court. It was past all patience: wealth of every kind was being squandered on people like these. The whole of Asia was like the city in Sophocles, loaded at one and the same time

  with incense in the air,
  with songs of joy — and cries of despair.

When he made his entry into Ephesus, women dressed as Bacchantes went to meet him, with men and boys costumed as satyrs and fauns; through the whole town nothing was to be seen but spears wreathed with ivy, harps, flutes, and pipes, while the songs hailed Antony as Bacchus — the Giver of Joy, the Gentle. And so he was, to some. To far more he was the Devourer and the Savage; for he stripped people of worth and standing of their fortunes to gratify scoundrels and flatterers — men who would sometimes beg the estate of someone still living, pretending he was dead, get the grant, and take possession. He gave his cook the house of a citizen of Magnesia as the reward for a single highly successful supper. And at last, when he was moving to lay a second year's tribute on Asia, Hybreas, speaking on behalf of the cities, found the courage to tell him — bluntly, but in a style suited to Antony's taste — "If you can take tribute twice a year, no doubt you can give us two summers and two harvests a year." Then he put the rest in the plainest, boldest terms: Asia had already raised two hundred thousand talents for Antony's service. "If this has not been paid to you, ask your collectors what became of it. If it has been paid, and it is all gone — then we are ruined men."

The words cut Antony to the quick, for he was simply ignorant of most of what was done in his name — not that he was lazy, but that he trusted, frankly and completely, everyone around him. There was a great deal of simplicity in his character. He was slow to see his faults; but when he did see them, his repentance was intense, and he was ready to ask pardon of the very people he had wronged. When he made amends he was lavish, and when he punished he was severe — though his generosity always ran further than his severity. His mockery was sharp and insulting, but it carried its own antidote: he was perfectly willing to take as good as he gave, and enjoyed being laughed at almost as much as he enjoyed laughing at others. And this freedom of speech was, in truth, the cause of many of his disasters. He could not imagine that men who joked with him so boldly would ever flatter or deceive him in serious business. He did not understand how deliberately a parasite mixes boldness into his flattery, the way a confectioner cuts sweetness with something sharp so that it never cloys. The impudence at the dinner table was engineered precisely so that the yes-saying in council would look like conviction instead of servility.

With a temper like his, the last and crowning ruin came in the form of the love of Cleopatra — a love that woke and whipped to fury passions that had lain quiet and dormant in his nature, and stifled, and finally corrupted, whatever goodness and sound judgment still held out in him. He fell into the snare in this way. As he was preparing for the Parthian war, he sent orders commanding her to appear before him in Cilicia, to answer a charge that she had given great assistance to Cassius in the late war. But Dellius, who carried the message, had no sooner seen her face and registered the adroitness and subtlety of her talk than he understood that Antony would never dream of harming such a woman — on the contrary, she would soon stand first in his favor. So he set about paying court to the Egyptian queen at once, and gave her his advice: to go to Cilicia "in her best attire," as Homer would put it, and to fear nothing from Antony, the gentlest and kindest of soldiers. She put some faith in Dellius's words, but far more in her own powers of attraction. They had already won her Julius Caesar, and Gnaeus, the great Pompey's son — and those conquests she had made as a girl who knew nothing yet of the world. She would meet Antony at the age when a woman's beauty is at its most splendid and her mind at its full maturity. So she gathered a great store of money, gifts, and ornaments of value — everything so wealthy a kingdom could supply — but her surest hopes she carried in herself: in her own magic, her own spells.

She received letter after letter of summons, from Antony and from his friends alike, and took no notice of any of them. At last, as if in mockery of the whole proceeding, she came sailing up the river Cydnus in a barge with a gilded stern and outspread sails of purple, while oars of silver beat time to the music of flutes and fifes and harps. She herself lay stretched at ease beneath a canopy of cloth of gold, dressed as Venus is dressed in a painting, while beautiful boys, got up like painted Cupids, stood on either side to fan her. Her maids, costumed as sea nymphs and graces, steered at the rudder and worked at the ropes. Perfume drifted from the vessel to the shore, which was covered with multitudes — part following the galley up the river along either bank, part streaming out of the city for the sight. The market-place emptied until Antony was left sitting on the tribunal alone, while the word ran through the whole crowd that Venus had come to feast with Bacchus, for the common good of Asia. On her arrival, Antony sent an invitation to supper. She thought it more fitting that he should come to her; and Antony, wanting to show his good humor and courtesy, complied, and went. He found preparations magnificent beyond expression, but nothing so admirable as the lights: all at once, so great a number of branching lights were let down, arranged with such ingenuity — some in squares, some in circles — that the whole scene was a spectacle few things have ever equaled for beauty.

The next day Antony invited her in turn, ambitious to outdo her in magnificence and in cleverness alike. He was beaten in both, and so thoroughly convinced of it that he was the first to jest at his own poverty of wit and his rough soldier's manners. She saw at once that his humor was broad and coarse — more of the barracks than the court — and she met him in exactly the same key, instantly, without the least reluctance or reserve. For her actual beauty, it is said, was not in itself so remarkable that no one could be compared with her, nor the kind that struck everyone who saw her at first sight. But the contact of her presence, if you lived with her, was irresistible. The attraction of her person, joined with the charm of her conversation and a character that colored everything she said and did, was something bewitching. There was pleasure in the mere sound of her voice; and her tongue was like an instrument of many strings, passing from one language to another as she pleased, so that there were few barbarian nations she needed an interpreter to answer. To most of them she spoke herself — Ethiopians, Troglodytes, Hebrews, Arabians, Syrians, Medes, Parthians, and many others whose languages she had learned — which was all the more astonishing because most of the kings before her had scarcely troubled to learn even the Egyptian tongue, and several of them had let their Macedonian Greek go as well.

Antony was so captivated by her that he simply stopped functioning as a Roman commander. Consider what was happening in the world at that moment. His wife Fulvia was in Rome, waging his quarrels against Octavian with actual armies. Parthian troops under Labienus — the king's generals had made this renegade Roman their commander-in-chief — were massed in Mesopotamia, poised to pour into Syria. And Antony let himself be carried off to Alexandria to keep holiday like a schoolboy, playing and amusing himself, squandering the one commodity that, as the orator Antiphon says, is the costliest of all valuables: time. He and Cleopatra formed a sort of club with its own special name — they called it the Order of Inimitable Livers. The members entertained one another daily, each taking a turn, with spending so extravagant it defied measure or belief. Philotas, a physician from Amphissa who was then a medical student in Alexandria, used to tell my grandfather Lamprias about it. Philotas had gotten to know one of the royal cooks, and the cook, since Philotas was a young man, invited him to come see the sumptuous preparations for a supper. He was taken into the kitchen, where he marveled at the prodigious spread of everything — but what stopped him cold was the sight of eight wild boars roasting whole. "You must have an enormous number of guests," he said. The cook laughed at his innocence and told him there would be no more than twelve at supper — but every dish had to be served roasted to the exact turn, and if anything was mistimed by so much as a minute, it was ruined. "And Antony," said the cook, "may sit down to supper right now, or maybe not for an hour; maybe he'll call for wine first, or fall into conversation and put it off. So it isn't one supper we have to keep ready — it's many suppers, because there's no guessing his hour." That was Philotas's story, and he had more. He later became one of the medical attendants of Antony's eldest son by Fulvia, and was invited fairly often to the young man's table, along with his other companions, whenever the boy was not dining with his father. One day another physician at the table was talking loudly and making a nuisance of himself to the whole company, until Philotas shut his mouth with this piece of trick logic: "In some states of fever the patient should take cold water; everyone who has a fever is in some state of fever; therefore in a fever cold water should always be taken." The man was struck completely dumb, and Antony's son, delighted, laughed out loud and said, "Philotas, I make you a present of all of that" — pointing to a sideboard loaded with plate. Philotas thanked him warmly, but never for a moment imagined that a boy of that age could give away things of such value. Soon afterward, however, the whole service was brought to his rooms, and he was asked to put his seal on it. When he pushed it away, afraid to accept such a gift, the man who had brought it said, "What's the matter with you? Don't you know the giver is Antony's son, who is free to give it away if it were all solid gold? Still, if you'll take my advice, let us pay you its value in money instead — there may be some antique piece in the set, some famous bit of craftsmanship, that Antony would be sorry to lose." These are the stories my grandfather told us Philotas loved to repeat.

But to return to Cleopatra. Plato admits four kinds of flattery; she had a thousand. Whether Antony was in a serious mood or a merry one, she had at any moment some fresh delight or charm ready to meet it. She was at his side at every turn and never let him out of her grasp by day or by night. She diced with him, drank with him, hunted with him; when he drilled at arms, she was there to watch. At night she would go rambling with him through the city to knock at people's doors and windows and torment them, dressed as a servant-woman — for Antony went out disguised as a servant too. He often came home from these expeditions thoroughly insulted, and sometimes soundly beaten, though most people had a good guess who he was. The Alexandrians, for their part, rather liked it all. They joined in his fun good-humoredly and kindly, and said they were much obliged to Antony for playing his tragic roles for the Romans and saving his comedy for them. To catalogue all his follies would be trifling without end, but his fishing must not be forgotten. He went out angling one day with Cleopatra and had the bad luck to catch nothing while his mistress looked on. So he secretly ordered the fishermen to dive under the water and fasten onto his hooks fish that had already been caught — and he hauled these in so fast that the Egyptian saw through it. Pretending to be lost in admiration, she told everyone how marvelously skilled Antony was, and invited them to come out the next day and watch him again. So a crowd of them boarded the fishing boats, and the moment Antony let down his line, one of her servants beat his divers to the hook and fixed on it a salted fish from Pontus. Antony felt the line pull, drew up his prize, and — as you can imagine — the laughter was tremendous. "Leave the fishing-rod, general, to us poor sovereigns of Pharos and Canopus," said Cleopatra. "Your game is cities, provinces, and kingdoms."

While he was amusing himself with this boys' play, two dispatches arrived. One came from Rome: his brother Lucius and his wife Fulvia, after many quarrels between themselves, had joined together in war against Octavian, lost everything, and fled from Italy. The other brought news hardly better: Labienus, at the head of the Parthians, was overrunning Asia from the Euphrates and Syria all the way to Lydia and Ionia. So at last — barely — rousing himself from his long sleep and shaking off the fumes of the wine, Antony set out to attack the Parthians and got as far as Phoenicia. But then came heartbroken letters from Fulvia, and he turned back toward Italy with two hundred ships. On the voyage he took aboard friends of his who were fleeing Italy, and from them he learned that Fulvia had been the real cause of the whole war — a woman of restless spirit and plenty of daring, who had hoped that stirring up trouble in Italy would pry Antony away from Cleopatra. But it happened that Fulvia, sailing to meet her husband, fell sick on the way and died at Sicyon, which made a settlement all the easier. For when Antony reached Italy, Octavian showed no intention of pressing any charge against him, and Antony for his part shifted the blame for everything onto Fulvia. Their friends would not allow the moment to be wasted in picking that excuse apart; they made the reconciliation first, and then divided the empire between the two men, taking the Ionian Sea as the boundary — the eastern provinces falling to Antony, the western to Octavian, with Africa left to Lepidus. And they agreed that, whenever they did not choose to hold the consulship themselves, each in turn should hand the office to friends of his own choosing.

These terms satisfied everyone, but it was felt that some closer tie would be desirable, and for that, fortune supplied the occasion. Octavian had an elder half-sister — not of the whole blood, for his mother was Attia, and hers was Ancharia. He was deeply attached to this sister, Octavia, and she was, by every account, a wonder of a woman. Her husband, Gaius Marcellus, had died not long before, and Antony was now a widower by Fulvia's death — for though he did not deny his passion for Cleopatra, he disowned any talk of marriage; on that one point, reason was still fighting its case against the charms of the Egyptian. Everyone pushed to promote this new alliance, in the full expectation that once Octavia's beauty, honor, and good sense had won his affections — and it seemed certain they would — the whole arrangement would be held safely in the happy course of friendship. So, with both parties agreed, they went up to Rome to celebrate the wedding, the senate granting a dispensation from the law that forbade a widow to remarry within ten months of her husband's death.

Sextus Pompey, the son of Pompey the Great, held Sicily at this time, and with his ships — commanded by Menas the pirate and by Menecrates — he so infested the Italian coast that no vessel dared venture into those waters. Sextus had treated Antony with real humanity, having taken in his mother when she fled with Fulvia, so it was judged proper that he too should be brought into the peace. They met near the promontory of Misenum, by the mole of the harbor, Sextus with his fleet riding at anchor close by, Antony and Octavian with their troops drawn up along the shore. There it was settled that Sextus should keep the government of Sicily and Sardinia undisturbed, on condition that he sweep the seas clear of pirates and send a fixed quantity of grain to Rome every year.

With this agreed, they invited one another to supper, and the lot fell to Sextus to give the first entertainment. When Antony asked where it would be held, Sextus pointed to his flagship, a great galley of six banks of oars. "There," he said. "That is the only house Pompey inherits from his father." It was a jab at Antony, who was then living in the elder Pompey's confiscated house. Fixing the ship firmly on her anchors and laying a bridge of planks from the promontory to her deck, Sextus gave them a hearty welcome. And when the party had warmed up, and the jokes about Antony and Cleopatra were flowing freely, Menas the pirate leaned close to Sextus and whispered in his ear, "Shall I cut the cables and make you master not of Sicily and Sardinia only, but of the whole Roman empire?" Sextus considered it for a moment, then answered, "Menas, you should have done it without telling me. As it is, we must let things rest. I do not break my word." And so, after being entertained in turn by the other two, he sailed back to Sicily.

Once the treaty was completed, Antony dispatched Ventidius into Asia to check the Parthian advance, while he himself, as a courtesy to Octavian, accepted the office of priest to the deified Julius Caesar. In every affair of state and matter of consequence, the two men treated each other with consideration and friendliness. But it gnawed at Antony that in all their amusements — any contest of skill or of luck — Octavian kept winning. Antony had with him an Egyptian diviner, one of those who cast horoscopes, and this man — whether to please Cleopatra, or because his art genuinely told him so — declared openly that Antony's fortune, bright and glorious as it was, stood in the shadow of Octavian's, and advised him to keep as far away from the young man as he could. "Your Genius dreads his," he said. "Apart from him, your guardian spirit is proud and brave; in his presence it turns cowed and dejected." And events did seem to bear the Egyptian out. Whenever they drew lots for some trifle or threw dice, Antony lost; whenever they set their gamecocks or their quails against each other, Octavian's won. This nettled Antony in secret and made him trust the Egyptian's science all the more. So, leaving the management of affairs in Italy to Octavian, he left for Greece, taking with him Octavia, who had just borne him a daughter.

While he wintered in Athens, he received the first reports of Ventidius's victories over the Parthians: he had beaten them in battle and killed Labienus along with Pharnapates, the best general their king Hyrodes had. To celebrate, Antony gave a public feast throughout Greece, and at the games held in Athens he served personally as steward of the prizes. Leaving at home the insignia that are carried before a general, he appeared in public in a Greek gown and white shoes, with the steward's wands borne before him, and he did his duty in full — taking the combatants by the neck to pull them apart when they had fought long enough.

When the time came to leave for the war, he took a garland from the sacred olive tree and, obeying some oracle, filled a vessel with water from the spring of the Clepsydra to carry with him. In the meantime Pacorus, the Parthian king's son, marching into Syria with a large army, was met by Ventidius, who gave him battle in the country of Cyrrhestica, killed a great number of his men, and killed Pacorus among the first of them. This victory became one of the most celebrated feats of Roman arms, and it fully avenged the defeats under Crassus: after losing three battles in a row, the Parthians were forced to keep themselves within the bounds of Media and Mesopotamia. Ventidius chose not to push his luck further, for fear of stirring Antony's jealousy; instead he turned his forces on the peoples who had abandoned the Roman cause and brought them back to their old obedience. Among the rest, he besieged Antiochus, king of Commagene, in the city of Samosata. Antiochus offered a thousand talents for his pardon and promised submission to Antony's commands, but Ventidius told him he would have to send to Antony directly — for Antony was already on the march, and had ordered Ventidius to make no terms with Antiochus. He wanted this one exploit, at least, credited to his own name, so that people would not think every success was won by his lieutenants. But the siege dragged on and on; once the men inside saw their offers refused, they defended themselves stoutly, until at last Antony, finding he was accomplishing nothing, and ashamed and sorry that he had refused the first offer, was glad to settle with Antiochus for three hundred talents. After giving some orders for the affairs of Syria, he returned to Athens, and, paying Ventidius the honors he had richly earned, sent him home to receive his triumph. Ventidius is, to this day, the only man who has ever triumphed over the Parthians. He was of obscure birth, but Antony's friendship gave him the opportunity to show what he could do, and he made such glorious use of it that he confirmed the common observation about Antony and Octavian both: that they were more fortunate in what they did through their lieutenants than in their own persons. For Sossius, too, won great successes, and Canidius, whom Antony left in Armenia, defeated the people there along with the kings of the Albanians and Iberians, and marched victorious all the way to the Caucasus. By these means the fame of Antony's arms grew great among the barbarian nations.

Yet once again, on the strength of some unflattering stories, Antony took offense at Octavian, and set sail for Italy with three hundred ships. Refused entry at the port of Brundisium, he made for Tarentum instead. There his wife Octavia, who had sailed from Greece with him, asked leave to visit her brother. She was then heavily pregnant, having already borne Antony a second daughter. On her way she met Octavian, accompanied by his two friends Agrippa and Maecenas, and drawing those two aside, with urgent entreaties and many tears she told them that she, the most fortunate woman on earth, was in danger of becoming the most miserable. As things stood, every eye was fixed on her as the wife and the sister of the two great commanders; but if rash counsels prevailed and it came to war — "I shall be wretched," she said, "past all remedy. Whichever side wins, I lose." Octavian was overcome by her pleading and came on to Tarentum in a peaceable frame of mind, and the people there beheld a most stately spectacle: a vast army drawn up along the shore and as great a fleet in the harbor, with not one act of hostility — nothing but the greetings of friends, and every expression of joy and kindness, passing from one armament to the other. Antony entertained Octavian first, Octavian conceding even this for his sister's sake. At length they struck an agreement: Octavian would give Antony two of his legions for the Parthian war, and Antony in return would leave him a hundred armed galleys. Octavia then obtained, over and above this, twenty light ships from her husband for her brother, and a thousand foot-soldiers from her brother for her husband. So they parted good friends. Octavian went off at once to make war on Sextus Pompey for Sicily, and Antony, leaving in Octavian's care his wife and children — along with his children by Fulvia — set sail for Asia.

But the mischief that had lain quiet so long — the passion for Cleopatra, which better thoughts had seemed to lull and charm into forgetfulness — gathered strength again as he drew near Syria, and broke out in flame. In the end, like the restive, rebellious horse that Plato makes of the human soul, kicking away all good and wholesome counsel and breaking fully loose, he sent Fonteius Capito to bring Cleopatra to Syria. And when she arrived, the present he made her was no small or trifling thing: Phoenicia; Coele-Syria; Cyprus; a great part of Cilicia; the balm-producing side of Judaea; and the stretch of Arabia where the Nabathaeans reach down to the outer sea. Lavish gifts — and they deeply displeased the Romans. He had handed great governments and kingdoms to plenty of private persons before, and stripped many kings of theirs — Antigonus of Judaea he had actually beheaded, the first king ever put to death that way — yet nothing stung the Romans like the shame of these honors paid to Cleopatra. Their resentment grew sharper still when he acknowledged as his own the twin children she had borne him, naming them Alexander and Cleopatra, and adding as surnames the titles of the Sun and the Moon. But Antony, who knew how to put a fine face on the most disreputable conduct, would say that the greatness of the Roman empire lay more in the giving of kingdoms than in the taking of them, and that the way to spread noble blood through the world was to father a new line of kings in every place. His own ancestor, he said, had been born of Hercules that way — and Hercules had never confined his hopes of offspring to a single womb, never feared any law like Solon's or any audit of his procreation, but had freely let nature have her way in founding and beginning many families.

After Phraates killed his father Hyrodes and seized the kingdom, many Parthians fled the country — among them Monaeses, a man of great distinction and authority, who took refuge with Antony. Antony, seeing in his case a likeness to that of Themistocles, and comparing his own wealth and generosity of spirit to those of the old Persian kings, gave him three cities: Larissa, Arethusa, and Hierapolis, which used to be called Bambyce. But when the Parthian king soon recalled Monaeses, pledging his word and honor for his safety, Antony was quite willing to let him go — for he hoped by this to catch Phraates off guard, lulled into believing the peace would hold. All Antony asked of the king was the return of the Roman standards taken when Crassus was killed, and of whatever prisoners still remained alive. That done, he sent Cleopatra back to Egypt and marched through Arabia and Armenia. When his own forces had assembled and been joined by those of his allied kings — there were a great many of these, the most considerable being Artavasdes, king of Armenia, at the head of six thousand horse and seven thousand foot — he held a general muster. The count came to sixty thousand Roman infantry, ten thousand cavalry of Spaniards and Gauls who ranked as Romans, and thirty thousand more, horse and foot together, from the other nations. And these vast preparations — which alarmed even the Indians beyond Bactria and made all Asia shake — were rendered useless to him, we are told, because of Cleopatra. For in order to spend the winter with her, he pushed the war forward before its proper time, and everything he did, he did without clear thought — like a man with no real command of his own faculties, who, under the influence of some drug or spell, was forever glancing back toward her, his mind fixed far more on a quick return than on conquering his enemies.

Consider his mistakes. First: he ought to have taken up winter quarters in Armenia to rest his men, who were exhausted from marches totaling at least a thousand miles, and then moved at the first opening of spring to invade Media before the Parthians were out of their own winter quarters. He hadn't the patience to wait. He marched straight into the province of Atropatene, leaving Armenia on his left hand, and laid waste the whole country. Second: his haste was so great that he left behind the siege engines absolutely required for any siege — they followed the camp in three hundred wagons, and among them was a battering ram eighty feet long — none of which, once lost or damaged, could be repaired or replaced, since the provinces of upper Asia produce no trees long or hard enough for such work. Nevertheless he left them all behind as a mere drag on his speed, in the care of a detachment under Statianus, the officer of the baggage train. Antony himself laid siege to Phraata, a principal city of the king of Media, where that king's wife and children were. And when real need soon showed him the size of his blunder in leaving the siege train behind, there was nothing for it but to come up close and raise a mound against the walls — with enormous labor and a great loss of time. Meanwhile Phraates came down with a large army, and hearing that the wagons with the battering engines had been left behind, he sent out a strong force of cavalry. Statianus was caught by surprise; he himself and ten thousand of his men were killed, the engines were all broken to pieces, and many men were taken prisoner — among them King Polemon.

This great disaster at the very opening of the campaign badly discouraged Antony's army, and Artavasdes, the king of Armenia, deciding that Roman prospects were poor, withdrew with all his forces from the camp — though he had been the chief promoter of the war. The Parthians, emboldened by their success, rode up to the Romans at the siege works and taunted them freely. Antony, fearing that idleness would only deepen his soldiers' despondency and alarm, took all the cavalry, ten legions, and three praetorian cohorts of heavy infantry, and resolved to go out foraging — reckoning this the best way to draw the enemy into a battle on favorable terms. He marched a day's journey from camp, and finding the Parthians hovering around him, ready to strike while he was on the move, he had the signal for battle hung out in the encampment, but at the same time struck the tents, as if he meant not to fight but simply to lead his men home. So he marched them past the enemy, who were drawn up in a half-moon formation, with orders that the moment the legions were close enough to support them, the cavalry should charge. The Parthians, standing still as the Romans filed past, watched the army in open admiration: rank after rank passing at equal intervals, in perfect order and silence, pikes ready in hand. Then the signal was given, the horse wheeled sharply on the Parthians and charged them with loud cries, and the Parthians met them bravely, though at once they were too close for bowshot. But when the legions came on with a roar and a clashing of arms, the Parthian horses shied in terror — the men too — and they broke without holding their ground. Antony pressed them hard, in high hopes that this one victory might end the war. The infantry kept up the pursuit for six miles, the cavalry three times that distance — and yet, when they summed up the advantage, they had taken only thirty prisoners and killed a mere eighty men. Gloom and discouragement settled over them all at the thought of it: when they won, this was all their winning came to; but when they lost, as at the capture of the wagons, they lost ten thousand men.

The next day they got the baggage in order and started back toward the camp before Phraata. Almost at once they ran into scattered enemy troops on the road. Then larger parties. Then, at last, the whole Parthian army, fresh and in good order, calling them out to battle and charging them from every side, and it took hard fighting just to get back inside the camp. There Antony discovered something worse waiting for him. During a sally by the Medes, his men had panicked and abandoned the defense of the siege mound. He resolved to punish them by decimation, as it is called: the soldiers are divided into groups of ten, and out of every ten, one man — chosen by lot — is put to death. The rest he ordered to have their grain rations issued in barley instead of wheat, the food of the disgraced.

By now the war had turned grim for both sides, and the future looked grimmer still — for Antony most of all, because famine was closing in on him. He could no longer send out foraging parties without paying for every sack of grain in wounds and dead men. But Phraates had his own fears. The autumn equinox was past, the air was already sharpening toward winter, and if the Romans dug in and kept up the siege, he was afraid his own soldiers would desert him; Parthians will endure almost anything rather than winter in an open field. So he turned to a trick. He passed the word to those of his men who had struck up the most acquaintance with the Roman soldiers: when you meet them foraging, don't press them too hard — let them carry off some provisions. More than that: praise their courage. Tell them the king has good reason to call the Romans the bravest men in the world. Once this had done its work, the riders came in closer, walking their horses alongside the Romans, and began to complain about Antony's stubbornness. Phraates, they said, wanted nothing more than peace, and a chance to show how gladly he would spare the lives of so many brave soldiers — and Antony would not give him the smallest opening. Instead he sat there waiting for the two fiercest and cruelest enemies of all, winter and famine, and even with all the goodwill of the Parthians to help them, those two would be hard to escape. These reports reached Antony from many mouths, and he began to let himself hope. Still, he would not send a word to the Parthian king until he had put the question to these friendly talkers directly: were they speaking on their king's orders? They answered that they were, and urged him again to believe them. So he sent some of his friends to demand, one more time, the return of the standards and prisoners lost with Crassus — reasoning that if he asked for nothing at all, he would look pathetically grateful just to be allowed to slink away. The Parthian king's answer came back: never mind the standards and prisoners; but if Antony wished to retreat, he could do so whenever he pleased, in peace and safety. So after a few days spent collecting the baggage, he broke camp and marched. And here something telling happened. There was no man alive who could address a crowd of soldiers like Antony, no one better at carrying an army with him by sheer force of words — and out of shame and sadness he could not bring himself to speak. He handed the task to Domitius Ahenobarbus. Some of the soldiers resented it, as if he thought them not worth addressing; but most of them understood the real reason, and pitied him for it, and decided it was one more reason to give their general more respect and obedience than ever.

Antony had planned to go back the way he had come, through open, level country bare of trees. But a certain Mardian came to him — a man who knew Parthian ways inside and out, and whose loyalty to Rome had been proved at the battle where the siege engines were lost — and gave him different advice. Keep the mountains close on your right hand, he said. Do not march heavy-armed men across broad, open riding country against a huge army of mounted archers. That was exactly why Phraates had coaxed him away from the siege with soft promises: to cut him to pieces at leisure on the retreat. If Antony was willing, the Mardian would guide him himself, by a shorter route, with better supplies for the army along the way. Antony thought it over. He did not want to seem to distrust the Parthians right after making terms with them; but the shorter road through inhabited country was plainly the better one. So he asked the Mardian for some guarantee of good faith, and the man offered to be kept in chains until the army was safe in Armenia. For two days he guided the army in chains. On the third, when Antony had stopped thinking about the enemy altogether and was marching at his ease, in no particular order, the Mardian noticed that the bank of a river had been broken open and the water let out to flood the road ahead of them. He saw instantly whose handiwork this was: the Parthians had done it to slow their march. Be on your guard, he told Antony — the enemy is close. Antony had barely begun to form his ranks, posting the slingers and javelin men at intervals where they could dash out, when the Parthians came pouring in from all sides, fully expecting to surround the army and break it apart in confusion. The Roman light troops met them and were badly stung by the arrows; but the Parthians were entertained just as warmly with sling stones and javelins, took many wounds of their own, and pulled back. They rallied and came on again, until a battalion of Gallic cavalry charged and broke them, and they were seen no more that day.

The attack taught Antony what to do. He posted slingers and javelin men not only as a rear guard but along both flanks as well, and marched the army as a hollow square, with orders to the cavalry to charge and drive the enemy off — but never to chase them far when they fled. For the next four days the Parthians took at least as much damage as they gave, and their enthusiasm began to cool. Winter was getting on, they grumbled, and they pressed to go home.

But on the fifth day, Flavius Gallus, a brave and energetic officer with a substantial command, came to Antony asking for some light infantry from the rear and some cavalry from the van. With these, he said, he could strike a real blow. Antony gave them to him, and when the enemy attacked, Gallus beat them back — but instead of withdrawing as usual and retiring on the mass of the heavy infantry, he stood his ground where he was and fought on, dangerously exposed. The officers commanding the rear, seeing how far he was drifting from the army, sent messages calling him back. He ignored them. It is said that Titius the quaestor actually grabbed the standards and turned them around, shouting at Gallus that he was leading so many brave men to their destruction. Gallus shouted back, and ordered the men around him to stand firm; so Titius withdrew. Then, while Gallus pressed the enemy in front of him, a Parthian force swung in behind and closed the circle. Surrounded at last, he sent a messenger asking for help — and here the commanders of the heavy infantry, including Canidius, a particular favorite of Antony's, made what looks like a serious blunder. Instead of wheeling the whole army around, they sent small detachments; and when those were cut up, they sent more small detachments, and by this piecemeal mismanagement the rout would have spread through the entire army if Antony himself had not come up from the van at the head of the third legion, driven it straight through the mass of fleeing men, and faced down the enemy, stopping the pursuit cold.

Three thousand men died in that engagement. Five thousand more were carried back to camp wounded — among them Gallus, shot through the body with four arrows. He did not recover. Antony went from tent to tent to visit and comfort the wounded, and he could not look at his men without tears, without open grief. But they took his hand with shining faces and told him to go and look after himself and not to worry about them, calling him their imperator, their general, saying that as long as he was well, they were safe. And in truth, history cannot show a finer army behind any general of that age. In strength and youth, in patience under labor and hardship, it had no superior; and as for the obedience and affection the soldiers bore their general — the way every man of them, officer and private, great and small, valued Antony's good opinion of him above his own life — in that particular excellence, not even the Romans of old could have surpassed them. There were many reasons for this devotion, as I have said before: his noble family, his eloquence, his frank and open manner, his lavish and princely generosity, his easy way of joking with everyone. And now, above all, there was his kindness to the sick and wounded — visiting them, grieving with them, supplying whatever they needed — so that the sick and wounded were even more eager to serve him than the healthy and strong.

Yet that one victory had put such heart into the enemy that their weariness turned to contempt. They camped through the night close to the Roman lines, expecting to plunder the tents and baggage of an army about to disintegrate, and in the morning fresh forces streamed in, until their number is said to have grown to no fewer than forty thousand horsemen. The king had even sent the guards who attended his own person — a sign that he considered the victory certain. The king himself was never present at any battle. Antony prepared to address his soldiers, and called for a mourning robe, thinking to move them more deeply; his friends talked him out of it, and he came forward instead in the scarlet cloak of a general. He praised the men who had won the recent fighting and shamed the ones who had run. The victors shouted back promises of success; the others begged his forgiveness, telling him they were ready to undergo decimation or any other punishment he chose, asking only that he stop grieving over their failure. At that, Antony lifted his hands to heaven and prayed: if any judgment from the gods was coming to balance the great favors of his life, let it fall on his head alone — and let his soldiers have victory.

The next day they marched in better order, and the Parthians, riding up expecting a plundering party rather than a battle, met a storm of missiles from men who were anything but broken — fresh, resolute, and ready. It was the Parthians who began to lose their nerve. But at a place where the Romans had to descend a steep hill, they gathered and poured arrows into the column as it moved slowly down the slope. Then the shield-bearing infantry wheeled about, took the light troops inside their ranks, and the front rank knelt on one knee with shields planted before them; the rank behind held their shields over the heads of the first, and the next rank over those, layer upon layer, like the tiles of a roof or the banked seats of a theater — a sloping shell that no arrow could do more than glance off. The Parthians, watching the Romans sink to their knees, could only imagine the men were collapsing from exhaustion. So they put down their bows, took up their spears, and charged in close — at which the Romans leapt to their feet with a roar, met them hand to hand with their javelins, killed the foremost, and sent the rest flying. It went this way day after day, and the constant fighting shrank the marches to almost nothing. Then hunger set in. They could get only a little grain, and had to fight for what they got; and they lacked even the tools to grind it and bake bread, for nearly all the mills had been left behind — the pack animals were dead, or carrying the sick and wounded. In the camp, a quart of wheat sold for fifty drachmas, and barley loaves went for their weight in silver. They turned to vegetables and roots, and finding few of the familiar kinds, they were driven to try whatever they could pull from the ground — and so they came upon an herb that was death to eat. It first destroyed the memory and the mind. A man who ate it forgot everything in the world and gave himself over to a single task: moving large stones from one place to another, which he did with tremendous earnestness and industry, as if it were the most important business of his life. All through the camp you could see men stooped over the ground, digging up stones and carrying them from place to place. And then, at the end, they vomited bile and died — for wine, the one antidote, had run out. Watching them die by the score, with the Parthians still hanging on his heels, Antony was heard to cry out, again and again, "O, the Ten Thousand!" — in admiration of Xenophon's Greeks, who had an even longer road home from Babylonia, and a more powerful enemy, and made it back safe all the same.

The Parthians, unable to break the Roman army apart or crack its formations, and beaten as often as not, once more turned friendly with the foragers. They rode up with their bows unstrung and said they were going home; this was the end of their reprisals; only a few Median troops would follow along for two or three more days — not to trouble the Romans, but to protect some outlying villages. And they took their leave with salutes and embraces and a great show of friendship. The Romans filled up with confidence again, and Antony, hearing of it, leaned toward taking the road through the plains, since the mountain route was said to be waterless. But while he was preparing to do it, a man named Mithridates rode into the camp — a cousin of that Monaeses who had taken refuge with the Romans and received the gift of the three cities from Antony. He asked for someone who could speak Syriac or Parthian. Alexander of Antioch, a friend of Antony's, was brought to him. The stranger gave his name, explained that Monaeses was the man who wished to repay Antony's kindness, and then asked Alexander a question: did he see that high range of hills in the distance? He said he did. "Under those hills," said Mithridates, "the whole Parthian army is waiting for you. The great plains run right up to their feet, and they are counting on you to trust their promises, leave the mountain road, and take the level one. It is true that on the mountain route you will face thirst and the hardships you have grown used to. But if Antony goes through the plains, he should expect the fate of Crassus."

With that, he left. Antony, alarmed, called his friends to council, and sent for the Mardian guide, who said exactly the same thing. Enemy or no enemy, he told them, the plain had no reliable track, and losing the way in it was likely enough to rule it out by itself; the other route was rough and dry, but the dry stretch lasted only a day. So Antony changed his mind and marched out that night by the mountain road, ordering every man to carry his own water. Most of them had no vessels, so they made do, filling their helmets, or carrying it in skins. They were barely underway when word of the march reached the Parthians, who — against all their custom — pursued through the night, and at sunrise struck the rear guard, which was worn out with marching and lack of sleep and in no condition to put up a real fight. They had covered thirty miles in the dark, and to find the enemy on their heels at the end of such a march broke their spirit. Worse, fighting for every step of the road sharpened their thirst. The men at the front reached a river whose water ran cool and clear — and brackish, laced with minerals, so that whoever drank it was seized at once with cramps in the bowels and a thirst fiercer than before. The Mardian had warned them of this too, but they could not hold back; they shoved aside the men who tried to stop them and drank. Antony ran up and down the column, begging them for a little patience — there was a river of good water not far ahead, he said, and beyond it the country was too rough for cavalry, so the enemy could follow no further. Then he sounded the recall for the men still fighting, and ordered the tents pitched, so the soldiers could at least rest in the shade.

The tents were scarcely up, and the Parthians were withdrawing as usual for the night, when Mithridates appeared again and told Alexander to give Antony this advice: rest the army, but not a moment longer than necessary — then push hard for the next river. The Parthians would not cross it, but that far they were determined to follow. Alexander carried the message to Antony, who sent back a load of gold plate; Mithridates took as much of it as he could hide under his clothes and rode off. Acting on his warning, Antony broke camp while it was still daylight, and the whole army marched on with no interference from the Parthians at all — and yet, by their own doing, that night was the most wretched and terrifying of the entire retreat. Men began to murder and rob anyone suspected of carrying money. They ransacked the baggage train and stole whatever they found. Finally they laid hands on Antony's own equipment, smashing his fine tables and drinking cups and dividing the pieces among themselves. With such an uproar rolling through the whole army — men surging back and forth in the dark — the belief spread that the enemy had fallen on the column and cut off part of it, and Antony believed it too. He called for one of his freedmen among his bodyguard, a man named Rhamnus, and made him swear an oath: whenever Antony gave the order, Rhamnus would run his sword through him and cut off his head, so that he would neither fall into Parthian hands alive nor be recognized when dead. His friends stood around him in tears. Then the Mardian came up and gave them all their lives back. The air, he pointed out, had turned cool and moist — they could feel it in every breath — which meant the river he had promised was near; and the time it should have taken to reach it, reckoned against the hours they had marched, came out the same, for the night was nearly spent. At the same moment, others arrived to report that the whole uproar had come from nothing but the army's own violence and thieving. To quiet the tumult and pull the men out of their frenzy back into some kind of order, Antony gave the signal to halt.

Day was breaking, and calm and discipline were just returning to the column, when Parthian arrows began to drop on the rear guard. The light troops were ordered out to fight, and the infantry covered one another with their shields as before, roof-tile fashion, and held the enemy — who chose not to press any closer — while the head of the army, marching on at an easy pace, came in sight of the river. Antony drew up his cavalry along the bank, facing the enemy, and sent the sick and wounded across first. And by now even the men still skirmishing had time to drink in peace, for as soon as the Parthians saw the river, they unstrung their bows, told the Romans to cross freely, and paid them handsome compliments on their courage. They crossed unmolested, rested a while, and then pushed on — not quite trusting fair words from that quarter. Six days after that last battle they reached the river Araxes, which divides Media from Armenia — deep, violent in its current, and dangerous-looking to cross; and a rumor ran through the ranks that the enemy lay in ambush, waiting to strike the moment they were tangled up in the crossing. But when they had gotten safely to the far bank and stood in Armenia at last, they behaved like sailors sighting land after a storm at sea: they kissed the ground, they wept, they fell into one another's arms for joy. But then, marching through a country that overflowed with everything, they ate — after their long starvation — with such abandon that they came down with dropsy and dysentery.

Here Antony held a review of his army and found he had lost twenty thousand infantry and four thousand cavalry — and more than half of them had been killed not by the enemy but by disease. The march from Phraata had taken twenty-seven days, and in that time they had beaten the Parthians in eighteen battles — victories that settled nothing and won nothing lasting, because they could never pursue. And that fact points the finger plainly: it was Artavasdes who cost Antony the fruits of the whole expedition. If the sixteen thousand horsemen the Armenian had led away out of Media — armed in the Parthian style and trained to the Parthian way of fighting — had been there to run down the enemy whenever the Romans broke them, the Parthians could never have rallied so many times after so many defeats and come back fresh to attack again. The whole army, understandably, was hot to march into Armenia and take revenge. But Antony, thinking further ahead, made no mention of the desertion and kept up every courtesy as before; his army was worn out and short of everything, and this was no time for a new war. Later, however, when he entered Armenia again, he coaxed Artavasdes into a meeting with invitations and fair promises — then seized him, put him in chains, carried him down to Alexandria, and led him there in a triumph. This was one of the things that most offended the Romans back home: the proudest and most sacred ceremony of their country, handed over to the Egyptians for Cleopatra's sake.

All that, however, came later. For the moment, Antony was marching his army at desperate speed through the dead of winter, through one snowstorm after another, and the march cost him eight thousand men. With his numbers badly thinned he came down to the seacoast, to a place called the White Village, between Sidon and Berytus, and there he waited for Cleopatra to arrive. She was slow in coming, and the delay ate at him. He tried to shorten the time with wine and heavy drinking, but even so he could not sit through a meal; again and again he would jump up from the table and run down to look for her ship. At last she came into port, bringing clothing and money for the soldiers — though some say she brought only the clothes, and that Antony handed out his own money in her name.

About this time a quarrel broke out between the king of Media and Phraates of Parthia. It began, the story goes, over the division of the loot taken from the Romans, and it left the Median king in real fear of losing his throne. So he sent ambassadors to Antony offering to join him in a war against Phraates. This filled Antony with hope. The one thing he had lacked against the Parthians — the thing whose absence, he believed, had cost him the last campaign — was horsemen and archers; and here was someone begging him, as a favor, to accept exactly that. He began at once to prepare a return to Armenia, planning to meet the Medes on the river Araxes and open the war afresh.

But in Rome, Octavia wanted to see her husband, and she asked Octavian's leave to go to him. He gave it — not so much, most writers say, to please his sister, as to gain a respectable pretext for war when she was received with dishonor. She had no sooner reached Athens than letters came from Antony telling her of his new expedition and instructing her to wait for him there. She was hurt, and she was not blind to the real reason for this treatment; yet she wrote back asking only where he would like her to send the things she had brought for him. And she had brought a great deal: clothing for his soldiers, baggage animals, money, presents for his friends and officers, and two thousand picked men, splendidly armed, to serve as his personal guard. This message was carried to Antony by one of his friends, a man named Niger, who added to it the praises she so thoroughly deserved.

Cleopatra could feel her rival closing in, and she was afraid. Octavia had a noble character and the power of her brother behind her; if she were ever allowed to add the quiet charm of daily company and affectionate married life to those advantages, she would become irresistible, and hold Antony absolutely and forever. So Cleopatra pretended to be dying of love for him. She starved herself thin on a meager diet. When he entered the room, she would fix her eyes on him in rapture; when he left, she would seem to sink and half faint away. She took great care that he should catch her in tears — and then, the moment he noticed, she would hastily dry them and turn aside, as if the one thing she wished was that he should know nothing of it. All this theater was played out while he was preparing for Media, and Cleopatra's creatures worked busily to help it along. They scolded Antony for his cold, hard heart — letting a woman perish whose whole soul hung on him and him alone. Octavia was his wife, yes; but she had been married to him because it suited her brother's political business, and she had the honor of the title. Cleopatra, sovereign queen of many nations, had been content to be called his mistress; she did not shrink from the name or think it beneath her, so long as she could see him, live with him, have him. Take that away, and she would not survive the loss. In the end they so melted and unmanned him that, genuinely believing she would die if he left her, he put off the war and went back to Alexandria, postponing the Median expedition until the following summer — this although word had come that Parthia was in chaos with internal feuds. Some time later he did go into that country, and sealed an alliance with the Median king by betrothing one of his sons by Cleopatra to the king's daughter, still a very young girl. Then he returned, his mind now occupied with the coming civil war.

When Octavia came back from Athens, Octavian considered that she had been insultingly treated, and ordered her to live in a house of her own. She refused to leave her husband's house. More than that, she begged her brother — unless he had already resolved on war with Antony for other reasons — to take no notice of her injuries. It would be an unbearable thing, she said, to have it told of the two greatest commanders in the world that they had plunged the Roman people into civil war, the one out of passion for a woman, the other out of resentment about one. And her conduct proved she meant every word. She stayed in Antony's house as though he were living in it, and she took the noblest, most generous care not only of her own children by him but of his children by Fulvia as well. She received every friend of Antony's who came to Rome seeking office or on any other business, and did all she could to advance their requests with Octavian. Yet this honorable behavior did Antony damage she never intended: he was hated for wronging such a woman.

And the division of kingdoms he staged among his sons at Alexandria was hated just as much; it looked like a piece of theatrical arrogance and contempt for his own country. He assembled the people in the exercise ground and had two golden thrones set up on a platform of silver, one for himself and one for Cleopatra, with lower thrones at their feet for the children. Then he proclaimed Cleopatra queen of Egypt, Cyprus, Libya, and Coele-Syria, and joint ruler with her Caesarion, the reputed son of the first Caesar, who had left Cleopatra with child. His own sons by Cleopatra were to carry the title Kings of Kings. To Alexander he gave Armenia and Media — with Parthia thrown in, as soon as it should be conquered. To Ptolemy he gave Phoenicia, Syria, and Cilicia. Alexander was presented to the people in Median dress, with the tiara and the tall upright peak; Ptolemy in boots, mantle, and a Macedonian cap circled with the diadem — for this was the costume of the successors of Alexander the Great, as the other was of the Medes and Armenians. When the boys had saluted their parents, one was received by a bodyguard of Macedonians, the other by a guard of Armenians. Cleopatra, then and whenever she appeared in public, wore the sacred robes of the goddess Isis, and gave audience to the people under the name of the New Isis.

Octavian reported all this to the senate, and complained of it often before the people, stirring the public mind against Antony. Antony sent accusations back in kind. His principal charges were these: first, that Octavian had taken Sicily from Sextus Pompey and never given him his share of it; second, that he had kept the ships Antony lent him for that war; third, that after deposing their colleague Lepidus, he had taken for himself the army, the provinces, and the revenues that had been assigned to him; and last, that he had parceled out almost all of Italy among his own soldiers and left nothing for Antony's. Octavian's answer ran as follows: he had removed Lepidus from power because of Lepidus' own misconduct; whatever he had won in war he would divide with Antony the moment Antony gave him a share of Armenia; and as for Italy, Antony's soldiers had no claim to any of it — they had Media and Parthia, those splendid additions their brave service under their general had made to the Roman empire.

Antony was in Armenia when this answer reached him. He immediately sent Canidius toward the sea with sixteen legions, while he himself went with Cleopatra to Ephesus, where ships were streaming in from every quarter to form the navy — eight hundred vessels counting the transports, of which Cleopatra furnished two hundred, along with twenty thousand talents and provisions for the whole army for the duration of the war. Antony, on the advice of Domitius Ahenobarbus and some others, told Cleopatra to return to Egypt and await the outcome there. But she dreaded any new reconciliation that Octavia might manage, so she paid Canidius a large sum to plead her case with Antony. It was not just, Canidius argued, that a woman bearing so great a share of the war's cost should be robbed of her share of its glory; nor was it good policy to offend the Egyptians, who made up so large a part of the naval forces; nor, for that matter, could he see how Cleopatra was inferior in judgment to any one of the kings serving in the campaign — she had governed a great kingdom by herself for years, and she had lived long with Antony and learned the handling of great affairs. These arguments won — for such was the will of the fate that was steering everything into Octavian's hands. When all the forces had assembled, they sailed together to Samos and gave themselves over to festivities. Every king, prince, and governor, every nation and city between Syria, the Maeotid Lake, Armenia, and Illyria had been ordered to send or deliver munitions of war; and at the same time every stage performer was summoned to appear at Samos. So while nearly the whole world around them groaned and lamented, this one island echoed for days with piping and harping, with theaters filling and choruses competing. Every city sent an ox for the sacrifice, and the kings in Antony's train outdid one another in magnificent feasts and presents — until people began to ask themselves: what will they possibly do to celebrate the victory, if this is what they spend on the opening of the war?

When it was over, he gave the town of Priene to his performers to live in, and sailed for Athens, where fresh sports and play-acting swallowed his time. Cleopatra, jealous of the honors Octavia had received in that city — for the Athenians loved Octavia dearly — set out to win the people with every sort of attention. The Athenians voted her public honors and sent a deputation of citizens to her house to deliver them, and among the deputies went Antony himself, as a citizen of Athens; it was he who made the speech. And he sent orders to Rome to have Octavia put out of his house. She left it, we are told, with all of his children in her care except the eldest son by Fulvia, who was with his father — weeping, and grieving that she would be looked upon as one of the causes of the war. But the Romans pitied Antony more than they pitied her, and most of all those who had seen Cleopatra with their own eyes and could report that she had no advantage over Octavia in either youth or beauty.

The speed and scale of Antony's preparations alarmed Octavian, who feared he might be forced into the decisive battle that very summer. He was short of many essentials, and the taxes were bitterly resented: free citizens were being called on to pay a quarter of their income, freed slaves an eighth of their property, and there were loud outcries against him and disturbances all over Italy. This is reckoned among the greatest of Antony's blunders — that he did not press the war then. He gave Octavian time to finish his preparations, and gave the unrest time to burn itself out. For while the money was being demanded of them, people were mutinous and violent; once they had paid it, they held their peace.

Then Titius and Plancus, men of consular rank and friends of Antony, defected to Octavian. They had been badly treated by Cleopatra, having resisted harder than anyone her insistence on being present in the war; and they carried with them knowledge of the contents of Antony's will. The will had been deposited with the vestal virgins, who refused to hand it over — if Octavian wanted it, they sent word, he could come and take it himself. So he did. First he read it through alone, marking the passages that would serve his purpose best; then he summoned the senate and read those passages aloud. Many of the senators were scandalized by the whole proceeding: it seemed neither reasonable nor fair to call a living man to account for what was not to happen until after his death. Octavian bore down hardest on the clause about Antony's burial, for Antony had directed that even if he died in the city of Rome, his body, after being carried in state through the forum, should be sent to Cleopatra at Alexandria. Calvisius, one of Octavian's dependents, piled on further charges connected with Cleopatra: that Antony had given her the library of Pergamus with its two hundred thousand volumes; that at a great banquet, in front of a crowd of guests, he had stood up and rubbed her feet, to settle some wager or promise; that he had allowed the Ephesians to salute her as their queen; that many times, while seated on the tribunal giving public audience to kings and princes, he had received love notes from her written on tablets of onyx and crystal, and read them then and there; and that once, when Furnius — a man of great authority and one of the finest speakers in Rome — was pleading a case before him, Cleopatra happened to pass by in her chair, and Antony leaped up, abandoned the hearing in the middle of the cause, and walked at her side all the way home.

Calvisius, it must be said, was generally suspected of inventing most of these stories. Antony's friends went up and down the city pleading his cause, and sent one of their number, Geminius, to warn him not to sit still while he was stripped of his authority by vote and declared a public enemy of Rome. But Geminius had no sooner landed in Greece than he was marked down as one of Octavia's spies. At dinner he was made the standing butt of the jokes and seated in the least honorable places. He bore all of it well, only watching for a chance to speak with Antony. Finally, at supper, he was told to state the business he had come about. The rest of it, he answered, would keep for a soberer hour — but there was one thing he had to say, drunk or fasting: everything would go well if Cleopatra would return to Egypt. Antony flared up at that. Cleopatra cut in: "You have done well, Geminius, to tell your secret without being put to the rack." A few days later Geminius found his moment and slipped away to Rome. Cleopatra's flatterers drove off many more of Antony's friends with their insolence, among them Marcus Silanus and Dellius the historian. Dellius says he was in fear for his very life, and that Glaucus the physician tipped him off that Cleopatra was planning to have him killed. He had offended her by remarking that Antony's friends were served sour wine, while back in Rome Sarmentus — Octavian's little page boy, his delicia, as the Romans call such pets — was drinking Falernian.

As soon as Octavian's preparations were complete, he had a decree passed declaring war on Cleopatra, and stripping Antony of the authority he had surrendered to a woman to exercise in his place. Octavian added that Antony had been drugged out of his own senses, and that the generals Rome would actually be fighting were Mardion the eunuch, Pothinus, Iras — Cleopatra's hairdressing girl — and Charmion: for these, he said, were Antony's chief ministers of state.

These are the prodigies said to have announced the war. Pisaurum, a colony Antony had settled on the Adriatic, was swallowed by an earthquake. At Alba, sweat ran from one of the marble statues of Antony for days on end, and though it was wiped away again and again, it did not stop. While he was in the city of Patrae, the temple of Hercules there was struck by lightning; and at Athens, a violent wind tore the figure of Bacchus out of the sculptured Battle of the Giants and laid it flat on the theater below. Both omens struck home, for Antony claimed a connection with both gods: he professed descent from Hercules, and his imitation of Bacchus in his manner of living had earned him the name of the Young Bacchus. The same whirlwind at Athens singled out, from among many statues left standing, the colossal figures of Eumenes and Attalus — the ones inscribed with Antony's name — and brought them down. And aboard Cleopatra's flagship, which was called the Antonias, came the most ominous sign of all. Swallows had built their nest in the stern of the galley; then other swallows came, drove the first ones out, and destroyed their nests.

When the armaments gathered for the war, Antony had no fewer than five hundred warships, among them many huge galleys of eight and ten banks of oars, decked out as richly as if they were meant for a triumph. He had a hundred thousand infantry and twelve thousand horse. He had vassal kings in attendance: Bocchus of Libya, Tarcondemus of Upper Cilicia, Archelaus of Cappadocia, Philadelphus of Paphlagonia, Mithridates of Commagene, and Sadalas of Thrace — all of these with him in person. Out of Pontus, Polemon sent him substantial forces, as did Malchus from Arabia, Herod the Jew, and Amyntas, king of Lycaonia and Galatia; the Median king, too, sent troops. Octavian had two hundred and fifty war galleys, eighty thousand foot, and cavalry roughly equal to the enemy's. Antony's dominion stretched from the Euphrates and Armenia to the Ionian Sea and the Illyrians; Octavian's from Illyria to the western ocean, and from the ocean back along the Tuscan and Sicilian seas. Of Africa, Octavian held all the coast facing Italy, Gaul, and Spain as far as the Pillars of Hercules; Antony held the provinces from Cyrene to Ethiopia.

But by now he was so entirely an appendage of Cleopatra that although he was far superior on land, he wanted the victory won at sea — to please her; and this although he could not help seeing that for sheer lack of sailors, his captains were sweeping up every kind of man that miserable Greece could supply — travelers, mule drivers, harvest hands, half-grown boys — and even so the ships could not fill their crews, and most of them put to sea undermanned and wretchedly rowed. Octavian's ships were another matter: built not for size or show but for service — no towering parade galleys, but light, fast, and fully manned. He kept his headquarters at Tarentum and Brundisium, and from there he sent Antony a message: stop wasting time, and come. He would provide safe anchorages and harbors for Antony's fleet, and for his army he would pull back and leave him as much landing ground in Italy, inland from the sea, as a horse could cover in a single gallop. Antony answered in equally grand style: he challenged Octavian to single combat — though Antony was much the older man — and when that was refused, proposed that they fight it out on the fields of Pharsalia, where Caesar and Pompey had fought before. But while Antony lay with his fleet off Actium — where the city of Nicopolis now stands — Octavian saw his moment, crossed the Ionian Sea, and seized a place in Epirus called the Ladle. Antony's people were badly rattled, for their land forces were still far behind. "How terrifying," said Cleopatra, mocking them. "We may well be frightened, now that Octavian has got hold of the Ladle!"

At dawn the next day, Antony saw the enemy fleet standing in toward him, and feared his ships would be captured outright for want of soldiers to fight from their decks.
